[The Nature of Celestial Signs and Constellations]
Finally, observe that the author here understands by "images" the constellations of the eighth sphere, which are formed from many stars gathered together, and which create figures of living things. For there are two kinds of signs: One kind consists of those which are understood to be in the outermost or tenth sphere, and these are either the dodecatemoria of the zodiac, about which we have already spoken, or the twelve regions of the universal heaven, whose beginning is taken from the intersection of the vernal equinox. For example, the sign of Aries of the ninth or tenth sphere is the space of the heaven that extends 30 parts from the vernal intersection towards the North. The dodecatemorion of the heaven succeeding Aries is Taurus, to which the remaining dodecatemoria then succeed in order. The other kind of signs is to be understood in the eighth sphere, adorned with that infinite number of fixed stars, and these are not equal spaces of the zodiac, but constellations of stars impressed upon the heaven. I say constellations of stars, because "fidus" (constellation) and "stella" (star) do not mean the same thing, but just as in Greek "aster" and "astron" are different, so in Latin "stella" and "fidus" are distinct. A "stella" or "aster" is singular, like all the wandering stars and all the others that move alone, not mixed with others. But a "fidus" or "astron" is formed from the gathering of several stars, such as Aries, Taurus, etc., the greater and lesser bears, and whatever various kinds of things are believed to be received into the heaven. Nor indeed is it to be thought, as some have taught, that the position of the stars presents such figures to our eyes in the sky, or that lines connecting the lights of the constellation form images of living things. For what resemblance is there between the image of a celestial Aries and the figure of a terrestrial Aries, or between the taurine image and the figure of a terrestrial ox? I believe that the celestial constellations are circumscribed by the figures of inferior things and living beings primarily for this reason: that names could be given to the more prominent and observable stars, and that in such a great number of stars, many could be described more briefly by being encompassed by a single word, and finally, once perceived, could be more easily retained in memory. Therefore, these figures are voluntary, that is, not made by nature, but transferred by the will of artisans into the heaven, or rather from the heaven onto celestial globes. Since this is so, it is clear that the doctrine of the "stoicheiomatikoi," who engrave those seals and images of stars delineated by painters on celestial globes for healing diseases of body and mind, is empty, superstitious, and supported by no physical causes. Furthermore, even if that celestial position of the stars formed such images in the sky, nevertheless, since figures and all quantities in general, just as they have no efficacy whatsoever in themselves, so too they cannot receive any power of effecting from elsewhere, it would be manifest madness to attribute to engraved images the power of receiving celestial influence. But that we clearly see certain forms of things in this sublunar world agreeing with and changing with individual celestial bodies, and indeed with certain regions of the sky, this is to be judged prudently to happen not because of the figure, but rather because of the congruence of the temperature of the substances themselves, just as the magnet is affected more particularly by iron than by other things, so much so that it always and without any intermission turns towards the north; this happens not because of the figure, but rather because of the agreement of temperature, or some other hidden force of the north and the sympathetic nature of the magnet. By such a physical and experimentally proven reasoning, it should be believed that the learned and wise antiquity divided the entire heaven into effigies of things, and imposed names on the constellations that were consistent with their effects. Thus, Taurus received its name because it makes people laborious, with a raised and prominent forehead, and because it particularly affects animals of that kind among us more than others. Potanus narrates that the conjunction of Saturn and Mars in Taurus in his time caused the most horrible cold of winter and brought great calamity to oxen. And although a sheep is weaker in tolerating cold, yet the plague raged more violently among oxen because of the celestial sign to which the terrestrial ox is referred. If this conjunction had been in Aries, it would undoubtedly have raged more fiercely among sheep. Similarly, Leo and Canis received their names because they make people hot, choleric, fierce, cruel, prone to tyranny and injury, and rapacious. In the same way, all other images found their names from the property of their effect: such as Draco, Scorpio, Serpens from poison; Cassiopeia and Andromeda, because they portend prisons. Therefore, in astrological judgments, both universal and natal, the kinds of events are derived from the nature of the signs. For example, if a malefic threatens from Gemini, that evil is inflicted by a human, since that sign is human. Those whom chariots crush, whom quadrupeds tear apart, these are afflicted by unfortunate stars in their nativities, to whose images the forms of these things are subject. Ptolemy mentions these properties in the images in the first book, chapter "On Masculine and Feminine Animals," near the end. And in the fourth book, chapter "On the Quality of Death," and in many other places, he attributes much to them, calling them "morphoses" and "asterisms," and sometimes even "zoea." They are also called "ourania" and "stoicheia ourania," from which the "stoicheiomatikoi" took their name. Nor should it offend anyone that some authors have enumerated more, and others fewer, effigies, since such a distribution of the heaven was made by men not of one age, but of many centuries, in which these studies flourished. Therefore, it must be believed that these celestial images have been augmented, diminished, and finally changed as the matter itself demanded, due to various circumstances and forces discovered in the stars. Pliny in the second book, chapter 41, narrates that there are seventy-two signs or effigies into which the experts divided the heaven. But we should believe Ptolemy, who circumscribed one thousand twenty-two stars, notable by sight and effect, out of innumerable stars, with forty-eight images, of which twenty-one incline towards the North, and fifteen towards the South. The remaining twelve interweave the zodiac, which is why the author believes they precisely correspond with the dodecatemoria of the outermost sphere. For he writes thus: "The twelve parts of the Zodiac are called signs, and they refer to the images that are under the same Zodiacal circle." And although these do not disagree with Ptolemy, who also wants the dodecatemoria to be called by the adjacent forms of the signs, some quadrupedal, some terrestrial, some "hegemonikos," and others differently: nevertheless, in Ptolemy's time, when the first star of the Asterism of Aries was at the vernal intersection, they could only have a place, and by no means could they be accommodated without gross error to our times, in which the same first star of Aries has progressed from the apparent vernal equinox by 27 parts and a little more, as the calculation of the Prutenic tables shows. In the same way, the other constellations of the eighth sphere have made similar progressions into the succeeding dodecatemoria of the outermost spheres. The names of all celestial constellations, however, Joachim Camerarius...

[Planetary Powers and Dignities]
DIFFERENCES. For if they obtain the Midheaven, they especially indicate the native's fortune and estimation; if the Ascendant, they will indicate more about the body and mind. The same must be understood concerning the planets, whose cosmic dispositions are, first, those which are attributed to them simply by reason of their own powers and temperaments, which the author pursues in the second difference. Then there are other powers in the planets, which they acquire from communication and familiarity with signs of the same nature. For example, if the Sun is in Leo, its virtue will be increased by the temperament suitable to the sign of Leo. Thus Cancer is suitable for the Moon, Aries for Mars, Sagittarius for Jupiter. Similarly, the other signs are familiar to certain planets and are connected in many other ways. From this follow those planetary powers which they acquire by reason of the Sun and their own rays, and whatever else is explained in the third difference. The remaining planetary powers, which they receive with respect to the chart or figure of the heavens, and from the constitution of time, are accidental. In this way, in a nocturnal nativity, Jupiter, however, will be understood to be better tempered in a diurnal nativity. Similarly, it is accidental that in the Imum Coeli, or another abject place, they are unfortunate warriors; on the contrary, placed in the Midheaven, they are said to make one martial and fortunate in battle; placed in the ascending part of the heavens, they cause wounds to the head, and are said to decree something else for the native in other places. Having shown the order, let us now proceed to the exposition of the powers which the signs receive from the nature of the planets, or rather, which the planets receive from the nature of the signs. But since the power of any planet is increased or diminished more in some places than in others, for this reason these powers of qualities and weaknesses are distinguished by certain appellations. The most principal place, and more congruent than others to the nature and temperament of the planet, they call the house. This is followed by exaltation; then the triplicity; then the term or boundary. The last is the decan, which the Arabs, like many other things, seem to have invented contrary to nature and the opinion of Ptolemy. What each of these can do will be said below, after we have made each of them very clear with a broader exposition. First, however, we must explain the series of houses. The Sun and Moon have each obtained signs in which they have dominion and established rule. And the sign of Leo is masculine for the Sun, while the sign of the Moon is feminine, prudently—

[Planetary Dignity in Signs]
Although the Sun is said to rejoice in a place familiar to it by testimony, it is nevertheless said to rejoice if it is associated with other stars of the same sect. For example, if the Sun is in Pisces, in the bounds and house of Jupiter, it is said to rejoice not as if in its own place, but in the place of a friend. That such dignity of planets in signs is far weaker than all others, the matter itself indicates, and Ptolemy explicitly warns. For he says that in this way, although some communication of strength occurs due to similarity, it is nevertheless slight and of little efficacy. His words are these: "They say that it rejoices when the place itself is not familiar, but nevertheless to another of its own condition, which reason of congruence, although it is drawn from distant things, nevertheless some communication is thought to exist due to similarity." From what has been said, it is clear that Ptolemy and the author, according to the opinion of Dorotheus, have signified to us in one phrase a plainly diverse σωοικείωσιμ (familiarity) of stars with signs: the former as very weak, the latter as very strong; so much so that the author's joy does not so much agree with Ptolemy's joy as with the throne or seat that planets acquire in places where they are associated by two or more principal testimonies of familiarity. We will say more about this below.

[Second Difference: Nature of Seven Planets]
Since, with God's help, we have now completed what we proposed to discuss concerning the circle of the signs and its accidents, let us now pursue the intention of the seven planets, their natures, their existence, and what they signify. In all astrological treatises, the author proposes either the spaces and parts of the sky or the stars for contemplation. And the spaces of the sky are distinguished in various ways. First, by longitude, as in the dodecatemoria and 360 parts, etc. Then by latitude and declination into the sides of the world, one of which is examined by reason of the Ecliptic, the other by reason of the Equinoctial. Afterwards, there is a certain difference in the spaces of the sky by reason of their substance. Hence, some parts of the sky are more fortunate, brighter, smokier, colder, drier, wetter, more arid, more fertile, etc., than others. Finally, with the daily revolution of the world, individual parts of the sky sometimes obtain the ascendant, sometimes the midheaven, sometimes the descendant, sometimes the Imum Coeli, and by reason of their varied position in the world, they also vary their effects. And all these things were briefly and schematically expounded in the first difference, as was fitting, in the Isagoge. The second difference follows, in which, as order demands, he institutes a discussion of the power, efficacy, and significations of the stars. There are two kinds of stars: Fixed and Wandering. The wandering stars, which have their own peculiar motions, and these diverse, not common with the others, besides the two luminaries in such a multitude that exceeds number, only five have been noted in all ages, namely, Jupiter, Mars, Saturn, Venus, and Mercury. And they are commonly called by the Greek word Planeta, from πλανέω, that is, I wander or roam, not indeed because they wander and roam with an uncertain course, but because of the dissimilarity and variety of their motion. For now they are hidden, and now they reappear, sometimes receding from the Sun, sometimes approaching it. Now they precede it, now they follow it, sometimes they seem to move slower, sometimes faster, sometimes not to move at all, but to stand still. All other stars are called ἀπλανῶς, that is, non-wandering or fixed, adhering to the sky: so called from antiquity, not because they are entirely devoid of their own motion, but because, adhering to one eighth sphere, with that very slow motion, that is, they scarcely pass one degree from west to east in a hundred years, and thus carried together, they perpetually maintain the same distance among themselves, and constantly maintain the same relation to the Sun's Ecliptic, never changing their places, intervals, shapes, and magnitudes in their orbit, which is proper to the wandering stars, which now stand in this part of the sky, now in another, sometimes approaching these, sometimes those stars. In apogees they appear small and obscure, in perigees larger and brighter. And this is the main difference between wandering and fixed stars. For the planets acquire different distances among themselves at different times, and sometimes a northern, sometimes a southern relation to the Sun's Ecliptic. They also differ in radiance: For planets do not scintillate like fixed stars, except Mars: which, due to its darker and reddish light, seems to scintillate like a flame. As for the distance from us, the fixed stars are superior to the wandering ones, which the eyes also discern, especially when the wandering stars traverse the perigees of their orbits. Finally, the fixed stars always maintain the same magnitude. The magnitude of the wandering stars, however, varies with their approach to and recession from the earth. For example: Venus in apogee has an apparent diameter of 7 first scruples. In perigee, however, 10 scruples. Thus the Sun in its highest apogee occupies 32 scruples, in its lowest 33 scruples. The Moon in its highest apogee shows 31 scruples, in its lowest 35. Similarly, Jupiter and Saturn's apparent diameters decrease in apogees and increase in perigees by one scruple of the sky. And although each of the fixed stars always retains the same amplitude, not all have obtained the same magnitude, but are distinguished by a difference of six orders of magnitude. Stars of the first magnitude are those which have an apparent diameter of 8 scruples. Antiquity numbered 15 such among the fixed stars, such as Palilicium, Aldebaran, Spica Virginis, Capella, Fidicula, Sirius, Procyon, Fomalhaut, and the rest. Among the wandering stars, however, Jupiter and Saturn. Stars of the second magnitude have an apparent diameter of 6 scruples, and there are 45 such in the sky, and among the wandering stars, Venus and Mars. Stars of the third magnitude are reckoned at 208 in the sky, and their apparent diameter is 4 scruples. The apparent diameter of stars of the fourth magnitude is 3 scruples, of which the number in the sky is 474, and among the wandering stars, Mercury. Stars of the fifth magnitude are numbered 216, and have an apparent diameter of 2 scruples. Finally, the number of stars of the sixth magnitude is 50, whose apparent diameter occupies one scruple of the sky. To all these must be added 9 obscure and 5 nebulous stars. There are therefore in total 1029 stars observed in the sky by artisans from an innumerable multitude, of which five with the two luminaries are planets: the remaining 1022 they call fixed and adhering to the sky. Indeed, the greater and more powerful part of the fixed stars are circumscribed by 48 forms of animals and things, which the Greeks, as said before, sometimes call αστερισμός, sometimes μορφώματα, sometimes σχήματα, sometimes ζώδια. The rest, however, which are not contained in those images, but are adjacent to them, are called ἀμόρφωτοι αστέρες, that is, unformed stars. Moreover, although the efficacy of the fixed stars is very great, and they must necessarily be observed in all judgments, the author has passed them over in silence. Therefore, we will not hesitate to repeat and explain more clearly in this place those 48 celestial μορφώσφs, proposed above in verses. While reciting, we will also incidentally add their proper names, natures, places, and latitudes: both of those stars contained in these asterisms, and also of those which are adjacent to them, but only of the more significant ones, which are most observed because of their significant effects, and which are found everywhere in the books of poets and other good authors. We will begin, with Ptolemy, with the most powerful ones that fall into the zodiac, and then proceed from north to south. TWELVE STARS The constellations interwoven with the zodiac. In the asterism of Aries, its first star and the following one in the horn, are of the third magnitude, of the nature of Saturn and Mars. The first is today distant from the vernal intersection by 27 and a third parts, with northern latitude.

[The Sun's Influence and Significations]
The Sun is fortunate by aspect, and unfortunate by conjunction in one sign. It is masculine, diurnal, and produces heat and dryness. It signifies fathers if the nativity is by day. It signifies great kingship, the vital soul, light, splendor, intellect, beauty, purity, and faith. In terms of age, it signifies the end of youth. It participates with all planets in the disposition of years. In terms of professions, it signifies governance and rulership, the throwing of spears and hunting, and purification with every kind of cleansing that purifies bodies internally and externally. In terms of illnesses, it signifies hot and dry diseases appearing in the body. In terms of substance, it signifies much gold and all kinds of substances. In terms of the quality of the soul, it signifies sublimity, prudence, and what follows honesty, namely generosity, glory, and breadth of mind. In terms of sects, good culture and its like. It signifies the power of voice and the strength of swiftness. If Saturn is conjoined with it, it signifies stewardship and this kind of rulership. If Jupiter, it signifies rulership in faith and religion, and also a judge among men judging the oppressed or injuries, etc. If Mars, it signifies the leadership of an army and the investigation of wars. If Venus, it signifies kingship through women and through the obedience of the powerful. If Mercury, it signifies the counselors of kings, the work of books, inheritances of greater works. If the Moon, it signifies the work of ambassadors and the detection of counsels, and similar things. And some have said that the Sun signifies the image of a man's face, and specifically the right eye in men and the left eye in women. And some have said that it rules the heart, marrow, and thighs. And in terms of illnesses, the eating of flesh in the mouth, specifically damage to the mouth, and the descent of water into the eye. Its power is in the head. And the Indians said that when it is in the ascendant, it will be burning and will have a mark on the face. And in terms of colors, it has whatever appears as a foreign color, and in terms of tastes, acrid. And the quantity of its orb is 15 degrees. And in terms of days, it has Sunday, and in terms of nights, Thursday night. Its Firdaria years are ten. And its greater years are 120, its greatest are 1460, its medium are 39 and a half, or according to some, 69 and a half, and its lesser are 19. Its strength in the regions of the circle is in the east. And Messahala said that it signifies among the figures of men, one who has a color between yellowish-black, that is, brown, covered with redness, short in stature, curly-haired, bald, of beautiful body. It has an acute taste. And Dorotheus said that the figure of the Sun and Moon is the figure of the planets that are with them, and whichever is more dignified in their place. So if you want to know the figure of the Sun, know that it is yellowish, having a reddish part of the hair, its eyes somewhat yellowish. And in terms of parts, it has a part of future things, that is, a part of divination. And it is the significator of natures and the spirit of wisdom, elevation of perfection, also faith, sciences, and praises. And in terms of parts of the world, it has Zamarach and Cura Ferit, its boundaries, and the lands of the Romans.

[Venus's Influence and Significations]
Venus is fortunate, feminine, nocturnal, and is the significator of women, wives, and mothers, if the nativity is diurnal. It produces little heat and great humidity, or moderate cold and humidity. And in terms of age, it has youth or adolescence. And in terms of professions, instruments of games, also beautiful ornaments and figures, and games of dice and chess, and dances and leisure and fornication and fornicators, and children of fornication and much coitus, and all kinds of luxury, the composition of crowns and their use, and beauty and purity, also clothes and ornaments, gold, silver, and love, games, laughter, and joy, the use of various ointments and species, drinking and drunkenness, it believes all these things. It also signifies generosity, love, diligence, and affection, justice and houses of prayer. It also retains faith. And it signifies all kinds of professions, such as music, etc. If Saturn is conjoined with it, it signifies the sound of singing, by which the dead are sometimes lamented, or which builders sing when they build buildings. And if Jupiter is conjoined with it, it signifies the sound of readings or songs, which the masters of sects use at altars and their places of prayer in praise of Almighty God. And if Mars, it signifies the sounds used by seculars, and the songs of the common people, in which there is mention of blasphemy or battles, or in which there is mention of prison or labor, similar to these, such as a song in which there is mention of capture and binding, beating with whips. If the Sun, it signifies the sound of wood, by which one sings before kings and nobles. If Mercury, the sound that is exercised in the composition of verses. And if the Moon, it signifies the songs of sailors on ships. And in terms of illnesses, it has cold and humid diseases, which occur specifically and often in the genital members. And in terms of substance, that which is acquired for beauty, such as women's ornaments and their clothes, pearls, and paintings. And in terms of the quality of the soul, sweetness and friendship and eating, and similar things, and desires for drinking, eating, and coitus. And in terms of sects, the worship of idols, those in which eating and drinking are especially practiced. And some have said that it signifies the hips, the spine of the back, and sperm. And others have said that it signifies fatness and flesh, kidneys, and the womb, matrix, belly, comb, and navel. And in terms of colors, it has whiteness, and in terms of tastes, oiliness. And the quantity of its orb is 7 degrees. And in terms of days, it has Friday, and in terms of nights, Tuesday night. And its Firdaria years are eight, its greater years 82, its greatest 151, its medium 45, and its lesser 8. Its strength in the regions of the circle is to the right of the east. And Messahala said that it signifies among the figures of men, a white man, tending towards blackness, of beautiful body and hair, having a round face, and having small cheeks, beautiful eyes, and the blackness of its eyes is more than it should be. And Dorotheus said: it signifies a man having a beautiful face, beautiful eyes and much hair, and more white tinged with redness, thick, showing benevolence. And in terms of parts, it has a part of desire, and it signifies friendship, play, and love, patience, conjunctions of males. And in terms of parts of the world, it has Albiget and Aliemen, the lands of the Arabs.

[Mercury's Influence and Significations]
Mercury is mixed, masculine, diurnal, and inclines by its nature to that with which it is conjoined among the planets and among the signs. And it is the significator of younger brothers, it signifies servants and the love of concubines, it also signifies divinity and the oracles of prophets and credulity and work and honor or prayer. If it is in its own nature and no planet is conjoined with it, it signifies earthly things and the increase of things by growth, and in terms of age, youth, and progress in it. In terms of works, works that generate knowledge, preaching and rhetoric, and negotiations and estimation and geometry, and the disposition of things and philosophy and auguries, proverbs and writing, the science of versifying, and especially the work of numbers. If Saturn is conjoined with it, it signifies among the works of numbers, the work of measuring lands and inheritances and the number of buildings and looms. If Jupiter, it signifies the number of psalms and the number of divine books. If Mars, it signifies the number that is in the gifts of armies and fighters, and the number of blows, whips, and clubs. If the Sun, it signifies presiding over the number of kings, the substance of houses. If Venus, it signifies the number of strings, wood or lyres, the number of sounds and pipes. If the Moon, it signifies the number of dishes that are made for meals.

They differ in quality, like the Sun and Moon. (Each of the other remaining ones differs in the quality of its genesis, like the Sun and Moon.)

They differ in quality, as do the Sun and Moon. (Each of the other remaining ones differs in the quality of its generation, as do the Sun and Moon.)

[Sun and Moon's Quality of Generation]
The Sun and Moon differ in the quality of their generation. (Each of the other planets also differs in the quality of its generation, like the Sun and Moon.)

[Sun and Moon]

[Sun and Moon's Natal Quality]
They differ in the quality of their birth, like the Sun and the Moon. (Each of the others differs in the quality of its birth, like the Sun and the Moon.)

[Sun and Moon in Nativities]
Each of the remaining planets differs in the quality of the nativity, just like the Sun and the Moon.

[Ages of Man and Planetary Rulership]
They vehemently incite to Venusian allurements, so much so that at that time, burning with wretched desire and blinded by insane intemperance, they wander in error even with open eyes. Hence, from the end of 22 to the 41st year is the youthful age, that is, the age of youth, over which the Sun presides, and which, having cast off childish vanity, inconstancy, and vices, incites the mind to seriousness of actions, modesty of desires, and care for both family affairs and the estimation of honor. Afterwards, from the 41st to the 56th year, the virile age flourishes, that is, the age of virility, attributed to Mars, because it equally increases the cares and pains of mind and body, indeed now instilling a sense and thought of declining age, and urging one to laboriously accomplish some memorable undertaking before death. Then, from the 56th to the 68th year, the presbytic age extends, that is, the age of old age ascribed to Jupiter, who, having removed a laborious, turbulent, and perilous way of life, brings a more decent, dutiful, and peaceful way of life, which is full of counsel and consolation, but free from precipitation. Therefore, it makes one most studious of virtue, honor, and liberality with modesty and gravity. Hence, to the very end, what remains of life, all of it, the extreme, finished, and decrepit age, rightly occupies the properties attributed to Saturn. For then nature quickly fails, having lost the vigor of body and mind. The body is constricted by cold, deprived of nerves, dries up, and becomes languid for all desires and pleasures. The soul, however, is sad, miserable, exposed to offenses, morose in all things, because of the sluggishness of movements and strengths in that age. These are the properties of ages common to all humans, and for this reason, Ptolemy named them the proper natures of times considered in common and universally by nature. However, concerning the following three divisions of time, which Ptolemy called specific or particular, not because they are not general, I believe, and powerful, but because they are found to be peculiar and diverse in the particular nativities of humans, and because they cause that common nature of states, which we have just described, not to remain the same. The first is that which occurs through directions to the parts of the zodiac corresponding to the rays and bounds of the planets. The second is through years, months, and days from the changes of the signs, about which will be said in the fourth discourse. But concerning that division of times, which Ptolemy made last, through the ingress of the planets into the principal places of the nativity, the author, as far as I know, made no mention, unless we wish to understand it through annual conversions, or, as they call them, annual revolutions, which occur when the Sun returns to that point of the zodiac which it occupied at the time of birth. Let the diligent seek a fuller explanation of this passage from Ptolemy at the end of the Quadripartitum. It follows that Saturn signifies a melancholic temperament. He will produce this when he is the significator of both form and temperament. Ptolemy prescribes that the description of form and temperament should be taken universally from the ascendant and the parts succeeding it, and from the planets ruling the said place, and partly also from the place of the Moon in a similar manner. If, therefore, Saturn happens to rule in these places, he will produce a temperament and bodily form consistent with his nature. And if oriental, he will make them yellowish in color, fuller in body, with black hair, and somewhat bald, with large eyes, and of moderate stature, and finally with a more humid temperament.

[Eccentricity and Planetary Motion]
THIRD. For if it (the Sun) were to proceed everywhere uniformly, it would pass through equal halves of the zodiac in equal times, not unequal ones. The only cause of this diversity is that the Sun revolves in an eccentric orbit, whose center, as I said, is different from the center of the world. For if the Sun's deferent orbit were concentric with the world, then it would be cut into two equal hemicycles by the equinoctial, and therefore the Sun's constant, uniform, and regular motion would be measured in equal times. Since this does not happen, we must believe that the Sun is carried by its own motion in an eccentric orbit, elevated above the center of the world, and because of this, although it moves uniformly through its own orbits, it appears to us, who are fixed at the center of the universe, to make dissimilar and unequal progressions in the zodiac, appearing to pass through the apogees more slowly and the perigees more quickly. All these things are clearly illustrated in our grammatical figure, in which two lines intersecting at the center of the world, designated by the letter 'a', form equal angles, and therefore in the extreme periphery of the figure representing the zodiac, they receive two equal arcs, designated by the letters 'y' and 'd'. However, the arcs of the inner deferent corresponding to the same angles appear to be quite unequal and dissimilar. And the larger one is that which is designated by the letters 'e' and 't' around the apogee, because it is intercepted by lines extending further from the center of the earth; while the smaller one, which is marked by the letters 'i' and 'x' around the perigee, is enclosed by lines extending less far from the center. Furthermore, since the Sun, by its uniform motion, daily traverses equal spaces of its apogee, it follows that the larger arc of the apogee near the apogee, designated by the letters 'e' and 't', contains more diurnal courses than the arc near the perigee, designated by the letters 'i' and 'x'. To these equal and unequal arcs of the apogee, sustaining regular motion in equal and unequal times, correspond equal arcs of the zodiac, designated by the letters 'y' and 'd'. Therefore, the Sun, passing through the arc of the zodiac around the highest apogee, designated by the letters 'y' and 'd', will consume more days, and its course, having become slower, will appear to proceed more slowly than when it travels through the opposite and equal arc of the zodiac around the lowest apogee, designated by the letters 'q'. The same must be judged concerning the other wandering stars, namely, that the centers of their epicycles, carried uniformly in eccentrics, are carried unequally above the center of the earth, and thus appear to move more slowly around the highest apogees and to accelerate their course around the lowest ones, traversing unequal arcs of the zodiac in equal times. As for the motion of the stars in epicycles, it is sufficient here to observe that in the upper half of the small circles they progress through the consequent signs, and in the lower half of the same, they retrograde towards the antecedent signs. However, the Moon's motion in its epicycle follows not this, but the contrary path: for when it traverses the lower parts of the epicycle, it accelerates its course wonderfully through the consequent signs; but when it holds the highest parts, even if it seeks the antecedent signs, it never retrogrades quickly towards the antecedent signs due to the motion of the epicycle's center, but only becomes slow in its course. This is the rough theory of ascensions and descensions, progressions and retrogressions, which we must briefly and concisely touch upon as to how it is to be applied to the calculation of tables or rather Ephemerides. The arc of the zodiac calculated from the highest apogee of the eccentric to the center of the epicycle according to the order of the signs, in the theories of the planets and tables of motions, is called the 'equated anomaly of the eccentric', and commonly the 'true center of the planet'. How this center, which we say indicates the elongation of the epicycle's center from the highest apogee of the eccentric, is to be calculated from tables, has been amply indicated above, namely, by the subtraction of the apogee from the motion of longitude and the prosthaphaeresis of the eccentric. If you wish to calculate the same from the established motions of the common Ephemerides, proceed as follows: Extract the true motion of the planet from the ephemeris, and subtract from it the apogee, or as they call it, the 'auge' of the planet in the second signification, that is, the arc calculated from the vernal intersection to the highest apogee of the eccentric, and which is here ascribed by us to the planets in the tables, accommodating a whole revolution or twelve signs, as astronomical calculation requires, whenever subtraction cannot be performed: this done, the equated anomaly of the eccentric will remain, or as they call it, the true center, that is, the distance of the epicycle's center, or rather of the planet itself, from the highest apogee of its eccentric, for I have thought it safe here to neglect the difference between the true motion of the epicycle's center and the body of the planet. Furthermore, to return to the matter at hand, if that true anomaly of the eccentric is less than 180 parts or six signs, it will indicate the planet descending from the highest apogee of the eccentric; if greater, ascending from the lowest apogee to the highest. Finally, the number of parts 90 and 270 will show both mean longitudes, the former the first, the latter the second. APOGEES OF THE PLANETS. Planets, Signs, Parts: Saturn 8, 28; Jupiter 4, 6; Mars 6, 4; Sun 27, 3; Venus 2, 7; Mercury 6, 1; Moon 15, 28. The epoch of the apogees also, that is, the longitudes of the zodiac derived from the vernal intersection, and extending to the highest apogees of the stars, which we have added here, and which are established for the time of our example, that is, for the year of Christ our God 1527, can nevertheless be accommodated without error to the preceding or succeeding century in this practical calculation due to the very slow progression of the apogees. The perigees, however, are diametrically opposite to the places of the apogees, which is very useful for knowing the places where, by reason of their eccentrics, they recede furthest from the earth, or approach it most closely. For example, the Saturnian distance from the earth in our time is longest at 28 degrees of Sagittarius, and shortest at 28 degrees of Gemini. In the same way, one must judge concerning the distance or proximity of Jupiter, Mars, and Mercury. For the Moon's apogees are more unstable, and always traverse the zodiac in thirty days. The apogee, or its highest apogee, is with the Moon under the Sun at the beginning of the periodic month: on the seventh day, in the opposite parts of the Moon: on the fourteenth day, it is diametrically opposite to the Sun's parts with the Moon: on the 22nd day, it is again opposite the Moon: and then after almost every eight days, it is again conjoined with the Sun with the Moon. As for exploring the ascent and descent of planets in epicycles, it is easy for those who understand the calculation that supplies the places of the stars' longitude, which we discussed above, to judge this. For if the equated anomaly of the orbit, which they call the argument of the planet's equation, is less than 180 parts, it indicates the planet's descent from the summit of the epicycle to its perigee; if greater, it declares its ascent from the lower part of the epicycle to its highest point. For example, in our example, we found the equated anomaly above: Saturn indeed at 453.31 parts, Venus at 42.501 parts, and Mercury at 65.541 parts: which numbers indicate the descent of these three stars from the summits of their epicycles. On the contrary, Jupiter and Mars, since the equated anomaly of the former is 337.131 parts, and that of the latter is 183.91 parts, ascend to their apogees, having left the perigees of their epicycles. If you seek another and easier characteristic of this matter, the harmony of the stars, which they observe with respect to the Sun, will easily supply it to you. For the three superior planets, in conjunction with the Sun, and which is the same, around the mean directions, occupy the highest apogees of the epicycles: on the contrary, around the middle time of retrogradation, and diametrically opposite to the Sun, they hold the lowest apogee of the same epicycle. After conjunction, however, as the Sun recedes from them, they also begin to descend from the summit of the epicycle, and thereafter, descending, they observe such a proportion that they are always as many parts distant from the highest apogee of the epicycle as is the distance from the planet to the Sun. Therefore, the distance from the planet to the Sun according to the succession of the signs, which you can take from the ephemeris, will be able to inform you quite certainly of the elongation of the planet from the summit of the small circle. However, the case of the two inferior planets is different. For the stars of Saturn, Jupiter, and Mars can be distant from the Sun by the space of three, four, and six signs, while Venus and Mercury can be distant by two. The superior planets begin to retrograde from their morning station, the inferior ones from their evening station. But the characteristic...

[Planetary Almugea]
This calculation, on June 4, 14:00, 1555, made the Moon's latitude ascending southern, when it was then northern and descending. Let us now discuss the planets' distance from each other, namely what happens to the five planets in relation to the luminaries: we will say from what Ptolemy said about Almugea, that is, about seeing each other face to face, that is, when it is between a planet and the Sun: while the planet is occidental, that is, while it follows the Sun as much as there is between the house of that planet and the house of the Sun in signs, or when it is between the planet itself and the Moon, when it is oriental from the Moon, that is, while it succeeds the Moon as much as there is between the house of the planet and the house of the Moon in signs, that is, when the planet is as distant from the Sun afterwards as its house is distant from the Sun's house. Similarly, it is said of the Moon: because when a planet has succeeded the Sun, it will be from the Sun, namely Saturn in the 6th sign; and Jupiter in the 5th, Mars in the 4th, Venus in the 3rd, Mercury in the 2nd, then it is said to be in the Almugea of the Sun. Similarly, when it is between each planet and the succeeding Moon, this number of signs of each planet is said to be in the Almugea of the Moon.

[Planetary Security (Ductoria)]
Regarding ductoria, that is, the security of a planet, namely: how it is safe from rays. From this, the ductoria of a planet, that is, for the planet to be in its haim, that is, in its own part, and in some angle of the ascendant: and some of the luminaries similarly in a place similar to it, in a quadrant, namely in some angle, so that the planet is oriental from the Sun by day: occidental from the Moon by night. And every planet is said to be in its ductoria according to some, when there are 60 degrees between the oriental planet and the Sun.

[Planetary Combustion and Union]
And every planet, from which it is covered by the Sun's rays until it appears from under the rays, is called combust: and when it begins to enter the rays, it is said to have begun to be combust: and when it is hidden under the rays and is near the Sun by 12 degrees, it is called oppressed: and when it is with the Sun in one degree, and there are 16 minutes or less between them, its latitude similarly, it is called united: when it has passed the same union seeking an exit, until it is seen, it is called escaped.

[Planetary Orientation and Opposition]
And from which the three higher planets appear from under the rays, they begin to rise, that is, to appear in the morning before the Sun, this is when they are closer to the circle of the eastern hemisphere, until they come to opposition, they are called oriental dextri: from which they have passed opposition until they conjunct the Sun again, they are called occidental sinistri.

[Venus and Mercury Retrograde]
But Venus and Mercury, from which either of them separates from the degrees of the Sun in the middle of its retrogradation, they appear until they are combust by the Sun in their direct motion, they are called oriental: and from which they separate from the Sun in the middle of their direct motion until they are again combust in their retrogradation, they are called occidental masculine when they are in their rising, that is, when they are oriental they are called dextri, and they are said to be in their setting, that is, when they are occidental, they are called sinistri, they are said to be feminine, then they are stronger.

[Aspects and Latitudes]
For example, if the Sun is at the beginning of Capricorn, with a southern descending latitude of 7 parts, and Venus is at the beginning of Cancer, having a northern ascending latitude of likewise 7 parts, Mars will be perfectly and equally opposed to Venus. However, stars distant by 180 parts of longitude, if they do not have contrary latitudes, that is, if one is not in the northern part while the other is in the southern part, are in a partile diameter of longitude and not of latitude or equality, even if they have equal and diverse latitudes, that is, one ascending and the other descending. Furthermore, the author seems to hint at the other corporeal conjunctions that Ptolemy contemplates with a single word, which younger astrologers observe: Trines, although they desire contrary, do not desire equal latitudes. Sextiles require neither equal nor contrary latitudes, but at least tending unequally to one side. Finally, quadrangular corporeal conjunctions admit none of these, but absolutely demand that both stars, or at least one of them, be in the Ecliptic, and entirely devoid of latitude. All these things are most easily understood from the speculation on aspects by Johannes Blanchinus, who, in a large circle inclined above the Ecliptic and passing through the body of a star, however much latitude it has, takes the intervals of radiations or aspects. Both poles of this circle exist in the circle of latitude described through the poles of the Ecliptic and the center of the star, and are distant from the poles of the Ecliptic by as much as the latitude of the star itself, or its distance from the Ecliptic. Since all great circles in the sphere intersect each other mutually in two parts, it is clear that for stars having any latitude, both quadrangles remain perpetually in the Ecliptic, as if they lacked latitude, while both trines and sextiles, along with the diameter, fall outside the Ecliptic not in one way, but variably. For if the latitude of the star is northern, both of its sextiles will remain in northern latitude, with the diameter and both triangles extending to the southern side. Conversely, if the latitude of the star is southern, both of its sextile aspects will also remain in the southern part of the world, with the opposition and its collateral triangles transmitted to the north: a summary of which we have provided for you in a grammatical form. We have also added numbers, which indicate the latitudes of trine and sextile radiations, which are derived from stars not less than eight parts distant from the Ecliptic. As in the example we just gave, Mars holding the beginning of Capricorn with a southern latitude of 7 parts leaves both sextiles in the south, distant from the Ecliptic by 3.29 1/2, 37 2/3 parts, with all other rays, except the squares, which fall exactly on the Ecliptic, cast to the north. And the latitude of the opposite radiation is as much as that of Mars itself, namely 7 parts north. The trines, however, are 3.29 1/2, 37 2/3 parts, also north, which the inscribed numbers show, and furthermore, they also show that trines and sextiles are almost as distant from the Ecliptic as half the latitude of the stars.

[Mutual Deflux and Application]
The DIFFERENCE is that in the 24th part it proceeds, in the 25th part it retrogrades. Here, they meet each other, and signify a duplication of effects, which usually occur through such an application. Mutual deflux, however, occurs when the planet that is in more parts is direct, and the one in fewer parts is retrograding, and it signifies the frustration of significations, as if Mars were in the 9th part of Taurus proceeding, and Venus in the 8th part of Taurus retrograding. These are the general modes of applications and defluxes, and they are obtained by division. For either both planets are found direct, or both retrograde; or finally, one of them is direct, and the other retrograde, and no more cases can be found, in all of which, as often as they are conjoined within the orb of light, they will make an application; but as often as they are conjoined before the lighter planets leave the orb of light of the heavier ones, they will make a separation or deflux, as we have said. In these conjunctions and all subsequent ones, you will be greatly helped in judging them most easily and briefly by a compendium that generally embraces all aspects of all planets, both to themselves and to the other parts of the figure, which you can describe for yourself in the nativities of individuals or the beginnings of other things in this way: Draw about thirteen lines in length, and in the spaces at the top, starting from Aries, describe the twelve signs of the zodiac. Then draw seven transversal lines to the previous ones, encompassing spaces for the number of the seven planets. Finally, in the first and uppermost space, depict the character of that planet which has completed fewer degrees of its sign, and this under the sign in which the planet of the fewest parts is found. The next space should be occupied by that planet which, after the first, has traversed fewer parts of its sign. By the same reasoning, the subsequent spaces are to be assigned to the remaining planets, so that the last space is allotted to the one who has traversed more parts of its sign than all the others. With these things thus arranged, if you have noted the epochs of each planet in the margin, and inscribed all the aspects on both sides of the planets in the appropriate sign spaces, you will obtain a perfect Astronomical Table of Rays.
