[Moon's Application to Planets]
The Moon applies to a planet when it is in the same sign as that planet, or in an adjacent sign, and is moving towards a conjunction, opposition, or square aspect with it. This application is considered stronger when the Moon is in a cardinal sign, and weaker in a fixed sign. The application is also stronger when the Moon is in a sign of its own nature, such as a watery sign for the Moon, or an airy sign for Jupiter. The application is further strengthened when the Moon is in a sign where it has dignity, such as exaltation or triplicity. Conversely, the application is weakened when the Moon is in a sign of its detriment or fall. The application is also considered stronger when the Moon is swift in motion, and weaker when it is slow. Finally, the application is stronger when the Moon is free from combustion, and weaker when it is combust.

[Dedication to the City Council of Cologne]
ENARRATIO ELEMENTORVM ASTROLOGIAE, IN WHICH, BESIDES ALCABITIUS, WHO PRODUCED THE ARABIC DOCTRINE IN A COMPENDIUM, AN EXPOSITION, AND A COLLATION WITH PTOLEMY'S PRINCIPLES, REJECTING FORTUNE-TELLING AND ABSURD AND COMMONLY ACCEPTED OPINIONS, THE TRUE ORIGIN AND USE OF THE ART'S PRECEPTS ARE SUFFICIENTLY DISCUSSED IN THE MOST CELEBRATED COLOGNE ACADEMY FOR STUDENTS OF PHILOSOPHY, PROPOSED BY VALENTINE NABOD, ORDINARY PROFESSOR OF MATHEMATICS TO THE MOST AMPLE SENATE OF COLOGNE. ART LOVES FORTUNE AND FORTUNE LOVES ART. ARNOLD BIRCKMAN COLOGNE, At the heirs of Arnold Birckmann. | M. D. LX. With the Grace and Privilege of His Imperial Majesty. Digitized by Google Digitized by Google: TO THE MOST SPLENDID AND ORNAMENTED MEN, SIR ARNOLD A SIEGEN, KNIGHT, SIR HERMANN SUDERMAN, SIR JOHN PIEL, SIR CONSTANTINE A LYSKIRCHEN, SIR GODFREY HITTORPFF, SIR PHILIP GEIL, OFTEN AND AGAIN CONSULS AND MOST VIGILANT RESPECTIVE PROVOSTS OF THE RENOWNED UNIVERSITY OF COLOGNE, AND TO THE MOST AMPLE SENATE OF THE SAME FLOURISHING REPUBLIC, HIS REVERED LORDS, VALENTINE NABOD OF SILESIA SENDS GREETINGS. I believe, most ample and most prudent men, that it has been rightly handed down by great men that among all living beings, only humans, divinely animated by divine minds, are placed in the middle of the universal globe, which is called earth, so that from there, turning their eyes to all things enclosed by the outermost boundaries of the world, they might recognize the wisdom and goodness of God through diligent scrutiny of the various natures that each part of the world contains, and might choose things useful for living rightly and blessedly. For nothing in the nature of things is so subtle or abject that it lacks innate powers and duties implanted by nature, which, when more deeply examined, not only confirm divine care for human affairs in the minds of the good, but also bring their own aids to life. What is commonly held as more vile than plants and roots of the earth? For they are so common and everywhere at hand that they are trampled underfoot even by those unwilling. But their virtue and the sympathy and reconciliation with our bodies, after being moderately known and cultivated by diligent and skilled men of nature, gave birth to the most beautiful and most useful art of healing for the human race, by which the fragile life and health of mortals are especially preserved: By its aid, in human bodies, the equitable temperament of humors suitable for life is preserved, and indeed atrocious and most severely raging diseases are so manifestly overcome that one should not be ashamed to believe that some herbs were divinely ordained and divinely shown to humans for nourishing and preserving certain body parts, and others for expelling certain diseases. Such and similar virtues are also inherent in all other things of nature, which if men could observe and understand more closely, they would undoubtedly approach a blessed life. And although far more natures are contained in individual parts of the world than can be comprehended by anyone's intellect as a whole, and confined to definite genera and forms, since men do not even perfectly grasp the entire power of one kind of thing: nevertheless, those very things that can be known by labor and diligence, and which have been comprehended by many brilliant minds and handed down by an artful method, open a great path to virtue and the recognition of divine goodness and wisdom, and have a manifest use in all of life. Monstrous men, who despise the arts discovered by our ancestors with admirable and truly divine ingenuity and handed down to posterity, and the study of further contemplating nature, insult God and harm human society.

[Astrology's Medical and Financial Utility]
If anyone thinks these things are too sublime, and disagrees with this opinion, let him know that astronomy contributes not a little, but indeed very much, to the medical art. Although physicians may primarily think these things refer to general circumstances and constitutions, such as in which places, Italy rather than Germany or Gaul, and for what condition of people, men more than women, or bilious more than phlegmatic, dangers of diseases are to be foreseen: they rarely apply them to singular and weaker causes, even though these are sufficiently effective in affecting bodies to which they properly adhere. However, since there is no difference between general and singular in terms of this power, they are rightly and usefully observed in the particular constitutions of each individual. For just as those who hide in subterranean places or apply other cooling things feel less the common heat of the Sun, so too it is doubtless that immoderate heats, which arise particularly in the bodies of individuals from each one's own cause, can be mitigated by contrary remedies. Finally, this diligence in removing bad natural effects of the heavens, to pass over in silence public and common afflictions, such as those derived from defects of the luminaries, also seems to have no small importance in avoiding and alleviating unexpected human misfortunes. For if a fall from a height, or danger from waters, or wounds are foreseen, by avoiding high places, contentions, ships, and rivers, we will be able to escape unharmed. As for wealth, glory, friends, and goods of this kind, although astrology itself is not a bestower, as Ptolemy rightly judges, nor is any other part of philosophy, yet since it teaches to foresee abundance or scarcity of future things, it can rightly be considered more useful and liberal than all others in economic and political matters for making profit: for in this matter, it requires much virtue of mind, but very little use of the body. Pliny writes that Democritus, who first understood and showed the connection of the heavens with the earth, when his wealthy citizens scorned this care, foresaw a scarcity of oil from the certain rising of the Pleiades in the future, and bought all the oil in the whole region at a low price, because of the hope of olives, to the astonishment of those who thought poverty and quiet were most dear to his studies. And when it appeared, a great cause of wealth flowed, and the anxious and eager repentance of the owners restored the price, content that he had thus proven that wealth would be easy for him (when he wished). Aristotle relates that Thales of Miletus gave a notable example of making money by the same reasoning, having foreseen an abundance of olives, whose words I have not been reluctant to transcribe here, so that his authority might add some weight to this commemoration. We must collect the reasons, recited here and there, by which some have made money: in this matter, we will be most useful to those who value and honor the method of making money. And of this kind is that of Thales of Miletus, which can be considered as a kind of invention for acquiring wealth. And although it is attributed to him alone for his supreme wisdom, it is nevertheless a general precept for making money. For when his poverty and the study of philosophy were objected to him as useless, and he himself had perceived from astrology the future abundance of olives, in winter, before they began to bloom, he is said to have bought all the olives in the Milesian and Chian territory at a low price, because no one then offered more, and at least by giving earnest money, since he did not have much money at hand: but when the time appeared, with many buyers suddenly converging, he sold them for as much as he wanted: and thus, having collected a large sum of money, he showed that philosophers can easily make money if they wish, but that their studies are not directed to that end. For so many and so great, and many other reasons, which it would be too long to pursue, since this science benefits men, and cannot be rightly reprehended by any means, as many have attempted, it is to be embraced with a grateful heart, and guarded as a gift of God not common. But someone may say: If the events of things are to be assigned to the eternal motion of the stars and an inviolable law, as you assert, then one of two absurdities, and a third with either, must be believed. For either chains must be placed on God, according to the opinion of the Stoics, which so connect him to the heavens and other inferior causes, that he cannot act freely or otherwise than as he is led by them: or certainly it must be established that God, after he once constituted and ordered the series of all natural causes, and attributed to them the perfect and supreme power of accomplishing all future outcomes, retired to perpetual leisure, just as an architect, having completed a ship, retires free from cares, and leaves it to be cared for and governed by sailors in the future, which is akin to the opinion of the Epicureans. Finally, if we accept either of these as true, a greater absurdity will be admitted, than if we believe that the stars in any way cause bad inclinations and vices in men, for we are absurdly compelled to say that God, who created the stars, is the cause of such evils. These things may seem plausible to the common and less cautious, but to those moderately trained, the defect of all parts is clear. And first, by the assiduous and necessary action of the stars, neither the freedom of God to act, nor the providence of God, which assiduously attends to created nature, is taken away, but rather both are affirmed, and confirmed in the minds of sound men, as can be sufficiently established from what the Physicists have handed down concerning necessity, and Ptolemy concerning divine and natural fate. Indeed, what can be pronounced concerning the eternal essence and virtues of God are altogether and absolutely, as they say, necessary, nor do they ever admit the implication of contradiction. For since God proceeds from himself and from no preceding cause, therefore he deserves to be called simply and without any imagination of contradiction omnipotent, intelligent, beneficent, just, freely acting, etc., through all eternity. But the condition of subsequent things, which have flowed from the first cause, and have at some time begun to exist, is different, far less constant, and finally the connection is weaker: for the power of acting inherent in all bodies of the world cannot be perpetual without God the creator and preserver of nature, nor can it ever interrupt the action once undertaken from itself. Celestial bodies, for example, just as they could not complete their periods at equal and constant times, nor observe eternal laws of motion, unless their power were always preserved intact and whole by divine power and goodness: so again, constantly circumducted by fatal necessity, they cannot inhibit or change their innate, or rather divinely implanted, course from themselves, nor according to the will of men or any other creature, but only by divine command. Thus, devouring fire retains its perpetual power of burning not from itself, but by the benefit of God.

[Dedication: The Divine Art of Celestial Observation]
DEDICATION. Those who condemn useful disciplines or mock them with foolish words, conversely, give due honor to God and assist the commonwealth in its proper place, who either learn useful disciplines themselves or ensure their propagation through others' generosity. But no part of erudition is more divine, nor more learned, nor more worthy of man, than a sober discussion of the heavens' revolutions and the effects of the stars. For since that exalted and most splendid, clear, illustrious, and star-filled celestial mass, which we observe to be equipped with such a variety of regular, certain, and ordered motions, is a divine and most ample work, as it were, a great book, in which God, the best and greatest, has expressed the traces of the greatness of His wisdom, and goodness, and His other innumerable virtues, and indeed, by that eternal motion of the celestial bodies, proceeding in an unchangeable order and law, as if with His own finger, He has described the future outcomes of all subject things, that is, regions, peoples, and cities, and indeed, of individual men, and the natural path of their fates, no discipline can truly be conceived as nobler, nor more admirable, nor more conducive to accumulating the goods of mind and body, than that which instructs us to read and understand this book of God. For since men, as Aristotle pleased, naturally desire the knowledge of things, what can be more desirable? What more suitable for acquiring tranquility of mind? What, finally, more convenient for fulfilling natural desire? Than the knowledge of this divine book, which embraces the causes of all natural events, by which we are made contemplators not only of present or near things, but also of the most remote and future, and of the most excellent things of all nature, that is, celestial and divine things. Moreover, the knowledge of numbers, geometry, and universal cosmography, the vehement study of the medical art, natural philosophy, and dialectic, and finally, the love of truth and the assiduity of labors, and many such things that this science contains, and without which it is never exercised with true praise and honor as if imperfect, have great importance for the illumination of the mind and the instruction in the knowledge of various things. What? That the studies of all virtues and arts can be better augmented and more rightly governed, with a foreknowledge of the natural inclination of genius, and the aptitude or inaptitude (or ἀδυναμία) implanted by the stars for managing affairs. For just as we see musicians to be inept, poets ungrateful, and soldiers unfortunate, who, with a natural inclination resisting, try to sing or versify or fight, and such things, which artists perform with great grace, sweetly and almost without labor, as if by natural instinct, laboriously imitate: so those who study philosophy, who study the art of healing, who study jurisprudence, and such faculties, if they are aided by the benefit of nature, will achieve all the main points of their art more easily and rightly and quickly, and will exercise them with greater dexterity, than those who, in a way, with nature resisting, strive for them with great diligence and for a long time. This, therefore, is what Horace prescribes: "You will say or do nothing against Minerva's will." And what the author of the Centiloquy prescribes, saying: "ἐπ τηδεία ψυχὴ πρὸς γνῶσιμ απλέομ ἐπιτυγχάνε το ἀληθῆς, ἢ ὁ ἄκρως ἀσκήσας τιὼ ἐπιςήμηp," that is, "a soul apt for knowledge attains more truth than one who has exercised himself excessively in science." These things, however, are to be understood in such a way that their place and praise are left to the arts and disciplines. For although nature is more powerful in most things, yet if it is not stimulated and strengthened by exercise and study, it will never alone produce a perfect habit of anything. And that doctrine contributes greatly to perfection, is clearly shown by the distinction that can be observed among ingenious people themselves: some of whom, who are governed by art, handle affairs aptly, clearly, and in a convenient order: while others, who are not restrained by the limits of precepts, following the affections of their own nature alone, are accustomed to often bring up irrelevant things, to distinguish related things, and to weave together things that are minimally coherent. For just as a diligently cultivated field, equally fertile as another, produces abundance, but neglect, besides a lack of good things, produces wild growth: so good talents are accustomed to excel through doctrine, and degenerate through negligence: and this is what the author of the Centiloquy teaches in another sentence, saying: "Gon Jυχή, inquit, συνεργεῖ τῇ ὀρανία ενεργεία απερ ὁ ἄρις Ο γεωργός συνεργεῖ τῇ φύαφτγῆς διὰ τ αροτριάσεως καὶ ανακαθάρσεως," that is, "a wise mind assists the celestial power, just as a good farmer assists the nature of the earth through plowing and clearing." It is therefore useful in some way to foresee happy or unhappy inclinations from the position of the heavens, so that it may be understood what each person can usefully and commendably achieve or not achieve in any profession, in acquiring honors, and in similar actions familiar to human nature. But also, we are greatly helped in this matter to avoid dangers, which often follow immoderate disturbances of the mind. For if someone has foreseen that impulses to furious anger, to inconstancy, to wandering lusts, and other vices forbidden by laws, arising from a bad mixture of celestial light, he will be able to avoid the penalties of laws and many severe evils, which sometimes those whom blind impulse leads with loose reins, as it is said, "ὃς ἐπιθυμία ήνιοχεῖ μαργοις χαλινοῖς," must incur, by sobriety, circumspection, by accustoming the heart to repress anger and to subdue desires. Therefore, the saying of the same author of the Centiloquy is worthy of praise: "δυναται ο ίπιςήμωμ πολλὰς ἀποτρέψαθαι ενεργείας την αςέρωμ, ὅτε ὀξιμ εἰδήμων ης φύσεως αὐτῆν, καὶ προπαρασκευάζει ἑαυτόμ πρὸ το συμπτώσεως τη ἀεργφών," that is: "He who is knowing can avert many effects of the stars, when he knows their nature, and prepares himself before their occurrence." And all these things that have been said about helping good inclinations and repressing bad ones, not only pertain to accumulating mental endowments, but also to preserving the goods of the body: and if they have somehow collapsed, how they ought to be recovered, they are very conducive. For there is no science other than this by which it can be better and more rightly understood what the constitution of each body is, and what the temperament of its qualities, and what things are most suitable to use to suppress the dyscrasia of the humors. As if, considering the position of the stars, it is found that an acute fever will arise from the vital part of the sky through the inauspicious radiation of Mars' transit, then by using colder foods and drinks for a longer time, we will be able to calm the western heat of Mars, if not abolish it entirely, at least for the most part: since nature is so constituted that humidity and burning force are minimally compatible, and do not remain in the same matter: So if Saturn is understood to bring illness to the body by exciting his nature, that is, cold and dry humors, we will try to temper that body with moistening and warming things. Hence the "ἰατρομαθηματικόμ στωτάξιωμ μέθοδο" was born among the Egyptians, who, as Ptolemy relates, when they had most increased the power of this art, were accustomed to everywhere combine astronomical previsions with the art of healing, so that from there they might hunt for the causes of present and future illnesses: and from here, taking remedies that are congruent or adverse to individual humors, they would remove present diseases with infallible remedies as much as possible, and avert future ones with "ταῖς προφυλακαῖσιμ" or, as they call them, preservative remedies. For unless the property of the surrounding sky and temperaments is entirely foreseen, they rightly believed that remedies often fail, since the same things are not suitable for all bodies and diseases. Nor did the father of medicine, Hippocrates, think differently about these disciplines. For in the book he inscribed "πολὶ ἀἐρωμύδάτων τόπων," when he considered the observation of changes, which arise from the "πρόδοις" (risings) and occurrences of the stars, necessary for a physician.

[Occasion for Writing]
The diction and context of the speech are barbarous, but the meaning and the matter itself, which alone we will always look for in this author, are better. Although, he says, there are not wanting those who have written introductions to Astrology, most of them have either not comprehended necessary matters, or have so obscured them by the prolixity of their discourse, that while they profit the unlearned reader nothing, they seem to have wasted all their labor; or finally, having no regard for order and method of teaching, they have confusedly heaped up precepts. For this reason, he says he was compelled to write a Methodical Introduction to Astrology, which should contain all necessary precepts, and in which nothing useless or superfluous or wrongly placed should be found. And so on.

[Divine Will and Astrological Necessity]
DEDICATION. It retains, and when applied to materials susceptible to penetration, it cannot but divinely suspend their necessary inflammation and dissipation. Similarly, all other things comprehended in the world, whether they are understood to be in the ether or in the elements, or whether one is compared to the other, can only be shown to cohere by this kind of physical, as they call it, bond of necessity. Thus, it is easy to understand that through the fatal, that is, as far as nature is concerned, necessary agreement of inferior matter with the motions of the heavens, the freedom of the divine will and its presence in all things are established and confirmed. Therefore, those who dream that either Stoic necessity is imposed on God by the decrees of the stars, or that eternal idleness is given to Him, greatly err. For the necessary commotion of the heavens, and the predictions of future events and actions, which are derived from the motion, position, and mutual connection of the stars, as if from powers divinely attributed to nature, are not simply asserted to be true, but insofar as God, the Lord of nature, does not oppose natural actions, just as the predictions of the art of healing are asserted to be true. For just as physicians, for example, observing a person subject to continuous defluxions, in whose body the lung, burdened with putrid humors and injured by an aposteme, like a perforated bellows, does not adequately supply the heart with copious and effective spirit, if they say that such a person will soon be subject to death due to increased heat and suppressed humidity, they do not deny God, the Lord of nature, His power and miraculous actions, by which He can, beyond and above the common course of nature, even restore life to the dead when He wills, let alone heal and preserve those afflicted by illness. EPISTLE: They do not deny, but rather demonstrate the effects and outcomes that will proximately follow from the naturally acting forces inherent in causes by God, and this with sufficient certainty, especially in this kind of disease, which has raged so much that it seemed worthy of being noted by the Poet thus: 'You see how the blood, released from the soft lung, leads with a certain limit to the Stygian waters.' In the same way, a prudent Mathematician, at the first rising of someone, observing Saturn passing through Libra, in which it especially rejoices, and occupying the seventh or sixth house of the heavens, which are conducive to health, and hostilely striking from a square aspect the body of the Moon traversing Cancer, if the Moon and Cancer, which are wonderfully reconciled to the brain and chest, are afflicted in this way, predicts an immoderate cold born in the brain, tightness of the chest, an abundance of noxious phlegm, and indeed, due to the weak heat and dryness of the star of Saturn descending to the lungs, ulceration of the lungs, difficulty in breathing, trembling of the heart, and finally, a fatal and lethal desiccation of the entire body. We must judge him to be expounding a series of naturally acting causes, and by no means infringing upon divine power, by which God, the Lord of created nature, can inhibit its actions, stop the course of the heavens, heal bodies subject to otherwise incurable vices and diseases, and do many other similar things. Through these, it is undoubtedly declared that most things in nature, which are necessary with respect to our spirits, if referred to the power of God, are truly contingent, and the effects decreed by the stars are by no means, as many have thought, sanctioned by such a necessary law that no other force can oppose them, but that they simply proceed to accomplish their primary intention and outcome. For besides the fact that God can command all subsequent causes, and can intensify, relax, or finally completely change or abolish their action at will, human foresight and diligence themselves, as I have said, can avoid or at least mitigate many great and serious calamities aroused by natural actions. Hence, experienced physicians, prudently distinguishing lethal diseases from curable ones, indeed impede and avert the procreating causes of the latter with natural and contrary remedies; but the former, when they see them arising from many, great, and insurmountable causes, they either leave to God to cure, or permit them to pass into the dissolution of the body to which they adhere. To this dissolution, not infrequently, even other lighter and curable diseases lead, when either their nature is not recognized by physicians, or the contrary force of remedies, when shown, is foolishly neglected; for then lighter evils, following their nature without any impediment, acquire strength and destroy. We ought to look for the same method and the same end in astrology, by the benefit of which not only the proximate causes of present diseases are shown, which is proper to physicians, but also the subsequent effects of superior and more powerful causes, which not infrequently suspend their actions for a long time, are defined. For if we have foreknown events that will arise from great, powerful, and many concurrent causes, and are therefore inevitable and completely unsubmissive to any things of contrary forces, then, as if deserted by the nature of things, we will flee to the invocation of God and pray that He, since He sits above the whole nature of things and above the stars, is bound by no bond of necessity, and is accustomed to bring help to those who invoke Him, even those already rejected by the rest of nature, may deign to benignly remove or at least mitigate our calamities. But as for other less effective causes, since they also, if unknown or neglected, bring about the necessary intentions of their nature, if we strive to avert them, when foreseen, with opposing remedies having a contrary force, we will avoid many calamities, and we will make many evils, which if left to their nature would be intolerable, to be borne and overcome. Since we have sufficiently shown that this diligence is useful and almost necessary for both body and mind, it remains for us to absolve God, the creator and constant ruler of nature and the stars, which, besides the seeds of virtues, we have said also engender the flames of more serious vices in humans, from all contagion and pollution. Many good men ask how it can be that if vicious inclinations or crimes arise from the stars, God is not the cause of human transgressions? For since God is the fount of goodness, and made the stars very good, and ordained them for one perpetual and constant effectuation of good things, how can it be that the cause of evil inclinations naturally descends from heaven? I think that in turn, it should be asked of them, since nature tends to one and the same and unchangeable mode of action, how it can be that wine, one and the same in substance and quality, drunk by many at one and the same time and in equal, but nevertheless somewhat immoderate measure, produces in some joy and hilarity, in others sadness and melancholy, in others fury, a desire to quarrel and fight, and finally in others laziness and profound sleep? Here I think they will immediately answer that the cause of the diversity of such actions is not to be sought in the active virtue of the wine, but in the subjects permeated by the pure wine. For since men rarely agree in temperament, and the very crasis, once it has assumed its qualities at the beginning according to the surrounding heaven, never changes into another state, and whatever is added to the body in subsequent times for the sake of nourishment and augmentation, it so reconciles and tempers to itself that it passes into that first peculiar state of its own, sanctioned by heaven before; therefore, what is surprising? The abundance of temperaments, wonderfully increased by the easily alterable wine assimilated to them, has indeed produced the well-known verse: 'The blood-filled temperament is mixed with wine,' declaring with excellent clarity the effects congruent with each nature: the sanguine producing hilarity, the bilious quarrels, the melancholic tears, and finally the phlegmatic sleepiness and such effects. Well, now they certainly fall in line with our opinion. For what difference does it make, I ask, as far as distinguishing these natural actions is concerned, whether there are two subjects, or indeed 72?

[Author's Intention]
In all arts there are two kinds of teaching. One they call 'Toli', by which we show a thing most simply and in plain words, without any mention of causes. The other, 'Si ou', which by accurate disputation investigates the causes of the precepts. Just as when in the art of healing, abstinence from wine is prescribed for those with fever, it is a precept of the first kind: but when the reason is added, that the drinking of wine increases the inflammation of bilious matter, it pertains to the latter kind. In the same way, when in the Spherical Elements the zodiac is defined as a great circle, cutting the equinoctial circle at oblique angles, so that its middle part, which they call the ecliptic, recedes from the same equinoctial by a maximum distance of about 32 parts, under which all the planets move by their proper motions from west to east: such a simple and bare description of the zodiac refers to the 'Toli' genus of teaching. But when practitioners, by more accurate disputation, give the causes of its obliquity, namely that it is necessary that such an obliquity of the zodiac be believed, because experience and many consenting observations have found and explored that the sun never declines from the equinoctial circle by more than 32 parts: and that this was so ordained by God, the author of nature, so that the sun with the other wanderers, for the life of living beings and other nascent things, might wander to the sides of the world, and not, if they were perpetually carried around under only one parallel by the conversion of the first motion, leave the middle earth scorched by the excessive heat of direct rays, and the other parts also barren and perpetually rigid with ice: they differ in the genus of teaching: for they endeavor not only to simply describe the zodiac, but also to show the cause of its obliquity. Thus in introductory books of Astrology, when, for example, the simple distribution of the zodiac into 12 signs is taught, with the causes concealed: it is 'Toli' doctrine. But through the 'Tudi ok' doctrine, the reasons and causes of such a distribution are more diligently sought, namely that the zodiac was first divided by the ancients into twelve parts, neither more nor fewer, because in the annual space, the moon, having completed its period, overtakes the sun twelve times, and is conjoined with it once in each of the twelve parts of the sky: or because out of 48 celestial images to be explained hereafter, only twelve fall into the path of the sun. From these it can be understood that the scope of this work and the entire intention of the author is, by the 'Tõ õlu' genus of teaching, omitting accurate disputation about the causes of the precepts, and neglecting the refutation of inept discourses by which some think this whole philosophy can be undermined, to simply expound the bare precepts of Astrology, as is customary and ought to be done in introductory books. For the 'rõ diõli' of astrological precepts, and the fortification and defense of the entire art against rivals, he says, have been sufficiently expounded in Ptolemy's Quadripartite Construction, and in certain works written by himself, of which I have not yet had the opportunity to see a copy.

[Human Nature and Celestial Influence]
But does it not happen that what was once a primary and most excellent form, having been abolished, assumes a far worse condition? It must be admitted, however, that such a condition is inherent in human nature: for man, having received from the Creator an immortal body and a soul full of divine light, through disobedience was not only stripped of his pristine perfection, but also miserably wounded and infused with poison, and made subject to death, than which nothing is more terrible. Since these things are so, if the heavens sometimes generate evil inclinations in men, we ought to attribute this evil not to the stars, which in the sound and uncorrupted nature of man could have produced nothing but what is good, but to the depraved nature of men due to great wickedness. This can easily be shown to happen not only in the stars but also in innumerable other things. Wine, than which nothing is better for nourishing strong bodies and for increasing and preserving innate heat, is destructive to those suffering from violent fevers; but this is not due to its nature, which is good, and, as I said, a friend to good health, but because, when added to foul matter inflamed by preternatural heat due to putrefaction, its own useful heat increases the conflagration. And hemlock, which in a vitiated and insufficiently warm nature kills by coagulating the blood with intense cold, could have been, in an integral nature uncontaminated by any defect, no less suitable as food than lettuces are now suitable for our bodies. Therefore, just as we see gold and silver purified and strengthened by a stronger fire, while other metals are consumed and dissipated, for no other reason than that the former are perfect and the latter imperfect, impure, as it were, diseased bodies: so it is fitting to think and believe that the natural action of the stars, in the untainted and pure matter of our bodies and souls, would undoubtedly produce all good effects, and that they inflict evil impulses, sometimes perverse inclinations, diseases, brevity of life, and such plagues, for no other reason than the impurities and defilement of our nature. I have discussed these matters at greater length and with a coarser Minerva, so that it might be easily understood by all that this part of philosophy, which they call divinatory, is not, as is commonly supposed, a superfluous and curious investigation of vain or illicit things, but a certain physical or natural treatise, in which the more potent effects in the elements, the crases or temperaments of mixed bodies are indicated, and referred to celestial causes, just as physicians are accustomed to consider the same things, except that they, content with particular bodily constitutions and more proximate causes, that is, those inherent in the matter itself, often neglect the more potent celestial causes. But indeed, it has been abundantly said above that both proximate and remote true causes are everywhere usefully observed. There are great books of childish and ridiculous accusations collected by Pico and others in reprehension of this science, to which some, either from slowness or malice of nature, so applaud that they think that from this, besides the utility of predictions, the whole action of the heavens and the comprehension of the whole art can be shaken as impossible. Here they achieve so little and are so inept, that it is no fault to pass over in silence and safely despise all those things which they ignorantly conceive and object to the subversion of the energy of the heavens and the comprehension of the whole science, since they can scarcely be admitted without fault to a discussion of serious matters. For those who sophistically or petulantly contradict either the immovable principles of the arts or manifest experience, are rightly judged unworthy with whom good and truth-loving men should have dealings. For it is not consistent that, while the small and fragile bodies of this sublunar world are universally endowed with certain powers and actions by the benefit of God, those vast bodies full of celestial light should lack powers or be idle. And besides, Ptolemy and Aristotle have sufficiently demonstrated that celestial bodies are the universal causes of the changes and motions that occur in the mass of the elements; the coarser and more evident effects of celestial light in the elements and in the bodies of animals and plants also fall within the common senses. For who is there who does not observe that the Sun, besides the four seasons of the year, also brings about a manifest difference of dryness, heat, humidity, and coldness by its daily revolution: and that according to the Moon's relation to the Sun, humors in growing things increase and decrease. The life of farmers, fishermen, and shepherds is rude and without letters, yet a more ingenious observation of the actions of nature appears in them than in some who have grown old in letters and are puffed up with a false persuasion of philosophical knowledge. For they know that when the Moon is waxing, shells should be sought full of juice, but when it is waning, matter should be yielded that is dry and therefore least susceptible to worm corrosion; and with similar diligence, taught by use and daily experience, they observe and adapt to the Moon's motion the more suitable times for sowing, planting, admitting cattle to mating, incubating domestic birds, and similar rural operations. But these excellent interpreters of nature are not ashamed to declare that they perceive no significant effect of the luminaries or other stars, and that there is no action of the stars. Those also, who, conceding the action and influx of the stars on the adjacent matter of the earth, deny that there is an art or science by which the times of future effects can be defined, although they might seem saner, we nevertheless think they need some instruction in the same school of farmers, or rather of sailors, who from the Moon's ascent to the zenith or the highest point of the sky, readily foreknow the access of marine waters, and through the same Moon's descent from the meridional circle to the cardinal points, the reciprocation of those waters and their reflux to the ocean, and know how to predict them to others with precision, although they are rude in calculations, and those marine tides, due to the more rapid disjunction of the luminaries, do not observe fixed times. But if these rural matters still seem too subtle and incomprehensible to anyone, I do not see why he should not be instructed by the school or examples of brute animals. For some animals are known to habitually observe and presage the more significant effects of the heavens. Hence the wisest poet Hesiod, the ant, because it is a small creature

[Division of the Work and Order of Teaching]
And the division in all things, and especially in handing down arts, is most necessary: for it brings light to things, and wonderfully aids memory, so that without it hardly any understanding or memory can exist. Order, moreover, is the arrangement of many conclusions, which constitute a certain discipline, in suitable and appropriate places. For since that from which a discipline is composed is manifold, order is necessary, otherwise confusion results, so that young people, because of the difficulty, become disgusted with the art, or at least are led to its knowledge later. Therefore, the author rightly, before the exposition of the precepts, indicates the order he will follow in expounding them. Furthermore, it is easy to judge how much the author's order differs from or agrees with Ptolemy's order.

[The Wisdom of Nature and Astrology]
This little creature adapts its operations to the change of lunar splendor by a wonderful natural instinct, the same, that is, to name the knowing, and to mark it with this bare epithet. And it is commonly known that many kinds of birds sense changes in the weather, and because of this, before winter, they fly in flocks to warmer places, and when spring approaches, they return for nesting. Now, if brute animals and uneducated men, ignorant of the powers of the stars and the places in the heavens where they are, can foresee and observe more significant effects proceeding from more efficacious causes, it is an injustice to deny the same sagacity to artists, and madness not to attribute greater skill and dexterity in judgment. Therefore, it is manifest from what we have briefly said that studious and skilled men, who, instructed in the philosophy of natural things, have learned to deduce consequent effects from antecedent causes, and moreover have a thorough understanding of the nature of celestial places and stars, and finally are so instructed in calculation that they can infallibly place before their eyes the position of the entire heaven at any given time and the certain epochs of the stars, can judge and predict, by physical consideration, without entering into any futility of opinions, the proper state of the air surrounding us, and the proper temperatures of mixed bodies, and what follows from these in natural order. But it has been shown that the doctrine of previsions is felicitous, admirable, a demonstration of divinity, and useful both in political and economic matters, and indeed for acquiring, preserving, and increasing private goods of all kinds. Therefore, it seemed to me not undeservedly most worthy that, freed as much as possible from the sorceries and other innumerable trivialities with which it is commonly overwhelmed, insofar as it agrees with natural actions, to use Ptolemy's word, "according to fitting philosophy," I should propose it to liberal minds for observation and study. And since it is the most excellent of all, and lies buried under the calumnies of so many sycophants, I commend it to men outstanding in wisdom and authority, and to the guardians of the liberal sciences, especially since this argument is by no means alien to the Mathematics, which you wished me to publicly illustrate in your most celebrated Republic's Academy, with the proposal of an honorable annual stipend from the public treasury. And no one will easily deny that the artful method of previsions, by which more important and serious unforeseen events are commonly foreseen, is most fitting for the providers of great things. But since in the doctrine of numbers, figures, and celestial motions, and in all other disciplines, we are accustomed, by what I believe to be a good and useful custom of the schools, to propose the very beginnings of precepts to youth, so that from there there may be easier access to more subtle discussions of things, therefore, desiring to promote and adorn this part of Physics as well, we have endeavored, explaining Alcabitius's Isagoge (because we knew that nothing in this genre had been produced more correctly by others), to discuss all the precepts that seemed necessary for acquiring and practicing the true art, not so artfully and perspicuously, but, as it is said, with a coarser Minerva. This writer is an Arab, and his interpreter a Spaniard, whose language, even if it is not so polished as to delight the learned, yet since it easily surpasses the others who formerly translated Arabic writings into Latin, I thought his zeal should be tolerated and taken in good part. Moreover, since this instruction could not only instruct the reader to understand Ptolemy, but also and especially the doctrine of all Arabs, it was necessary to introduce empty lots, decanates, novenaries, duodenaries, serdaries, and such things dissenting from physical doctrine, and not even approved by him, along with the true ones. But we, who have been concerned not so much with many writers of this kind as with a diligent investigation of the true and natural art, having cursorily viewed and then rejected these similar things that deviate from the goals of true art, have sought nothing but a clear explanation of the precepts of sincere art and a comparison everywhere with Ptolemy's opinion. But as for the other idle and empty opinions, even if we have often shown that they should be cut off as alien to the art, nevertheless, since we well understood that the writings of others should be emitted intact, we did not cast them out, and we thought that they too could or should exist usefully for economy or be seen by the incautious, so that from them the true may be more easily distinguished from the false, the physical from the vain, and what Ptolemy calls "number, lot, and the inexplicable" may be better understood. For just as in all other disciplines, for the sake of sharpening and strengthening judgment, it is useful to compare one or another of the more important writers, so we believe that this comparison of the Arabs with Ptolemy's doctrine can also bring some acuteness to young people. It seemed easier to write a new work of this kind, but it is more useful to adapt the first institution to the common treatment of Greek materials. And although these things, which I have added here for the sake of helping the studies of youth, I acknowledge are by no means so polished that they could be committed to the censure and judgment of the multitude without any fear: nevertheless, since the most excellent bookseller, Arnold Birckman, a most honorable citizen of your republic, with the excellent old man Master Conrad Tegemann, Canon of St. Ursula, whom, besides his remarkable knowledge of sacred literature, I found to be not only not ignorant of Mathematics and all this philosophy, but also conscious of many other admirable actions of nature, with other most studious and by no means common men of nature, did not cease to exhort me that they should be published in print, I did not think I should resist them in a matter which I could not have denied in any way even to Antonio Rinco alone, a man eminent by name of nobility and lineage, and besides being intensely studious of the most beautiful virtues and best arts, with whom I so long sojourned. From him I have often, and you especially, when the satiety of other business seizes us somewhere, conversing about the heavens, about the stars, and other admirable things of nature, understood that it was most troublesome to him that the most excellent art of astrology had no sincere, but either obscure or vainly filled, treatment of principles: by which he seemed to me to signify how much good judgment he possessed and how desirous he was of solid virtue. It is a great thing, a lineage from ancestors, but a greater praise to adorn the splendor of one's birth with excellent doctrine, whose impetus, even if it is from heaven, as I said, since it is known that both bodies and minds are propagated from parents in offspring by some similarity, therefore I have sometimes congratulated his father, John Rinco, a most distinguished man (who both derives his origin from an ancient and most noble family, and because of the wonderful dexterity of his genius, with which he cultivated liberal studies from an early age, has long borne the insignia of Doctor of both laws, not for the sake of any gain, but at least that he might be an ornament to the city and the Academy, in which he shines in a wonderful way, since I saw him now old and in advanced age, in which however he is in firm health and maintains military vigor, greatly loving and cultivating all good arts and uniquely admiring the most excellent things in nature) not without gratitude for an heir who can resemble not only his father's noble family but also his father in virtue. Therefore, I hope that skilled and candid readers will take it in good part that I, in treating a most noble subject, have complied with most noble men and friends who were my authors, that to the most beautiful doctrine of the motions of celestial bodies, which is constructed from certain and perpetually established demonstrations of numbers and geometry, I should add the immediately following part of physics, in which the powers, actions, and effects of the stars of heaven are explained by an admirable and undoubtedly divinely shown reason, and that I should publish these things concerning the first principles of the art, now collected by me for the benefit of youth. But your prudence, most ample and splendid men, which in the selection of persons
