[Dedication to the Republic]
DEDICATION. ...to the most beautiful image of the blessed Republic, which you are very capable of expressing, I have decided to dedicate this work of mine, both for other reasons and especially for the remarkable generosity which I have experienced from you in the past and continue to experience, since whatever burden or duty I bear and sustain in your most celebrated Academy, according to my humble ability. Nor indeed was I afraid that this willingness of mine to please would not seem suitable or pleasing enough to you, since we all see that you, occupied with the greatest and most difficult matters of the Curia, seriously inspect literary matters, and augment the celebrity of the Academy with suitable persons and scholars for all philosophy, and for as many languages as are publicly exercised, and finally for all other faculties: and for this reason we are led to the opinion and confidence that it is impossible for you to judge the acceptance of those arts unworthy by your authority, whose assiduous practice in the city you believe contributes greatly to the admiration of the Republic. Hence it is fitting for us to estimate the loftiness of your minds and the depth of your counsels. For by thus providing for the Republic, you show not only that you understand that it pertains to the duty of governors both to defend the bodies of citizens and to imbue and instruct their minds with the best disciplines, but you also declare it clearly by your actions. For sailors can scarcely save ships caught in a whirlpool or long vexed by ocean waves: But you, in the space of a war raging like a fiery fury throughout Europe, in which we have seen the homes of many either devastated or burned, have been able to preserve your city and citizens unharmed. And about three years ago, in a time of great scarcity, when a long famine afflicted neighboring places, we ourselves saw that the grain exported from your granaries sufficed for your citizens, and saved many of your neighbors who would have perished from hunger. Such and similar deeds, remarkably performed daily in the Republic, even if they bring you exceptional and greatest praise, are nevertheless contained by the celebration of all men. But the benefits which you transmit through the preservation of the Academy to citizens, to neighbors, and finally to regions everywhere surrounding, are difficult to conceive entirely in the mind, and certainly cannot be sufficiently praised by any speech. Concerning these great virtues, to which all who sit at the helm should be seriously invited and diligently exhorted, I only admonish you who already possess these parts, that you strive to retain them intact hereafter. I also pass over the generous rewards by which, so that the University may always deservedly be called the guardian of all arts, you seriously restore collapsed professional positions, as can be sufficiently confirmed by my own example. For as I was passing through here, when I saw the form of the school, and heard all parts of erudition, except for the mathematical disciplines, which in most schools either negligence or the superfluous care of philosophers disputing about trifles had extinguished, beautifully sounding, I began here to expound the beginnings of these arts, which the school seemed to lack, for the sake of studious youth, and at the same time I signified that my labor, if it were judged useful to the school by you, would not be wanting. As soon as you rightly understood this, not only was a position in the school, with an annual salary, assigned to me by you, but also a most honorable gift was bestowed for the burden which I had voluntarily undertaken in the school for a time: for which I now thank you, whenever an opportunity arises, and at the same time I ask that you be pleased to commend me and my studies to you hereafter. But now I will end the letter with such a clause: If some governors, by the mere defense of the walls, the city, and the citizens, have been able to acquire the favor of many and the praise of extraordinary virtues, it is not easy to say how much favor, gratitude, and praise your citizens owe to you, who maintain a most peaceful city among quiet republics, and foster the only alumna of German academies among German academies. Farewell happily. Given in your republic, in the year of the saving birth 1560, at the vernal equinox of July and August CAESARS, that is, on the 25th day of March, on which CHRIST GOD in the womb of the blessed Virgin Mary, born of the royal lineage of David, assumed human nature. Errata. In the fourth verse, read ardentem for occidentem. Also read priusque for priusquam. Also read τρόποι for Jinpis.

[Planetary Rays]
But when a planet is in some sign, its rays will be in those signs that aspect that sign in the same degree and minute. For example, if Mars is in the first degree of Aries, and in the first minute of the same degree, its rays will be in the first degree of the sign Libra, and in the first minute of the same degree: and thus understand the other aspects, as is most clearly evident in this figure.

[Preface to the Reader]
It is the duty of the Astronomer to discover the rising of the stars, in what places the luminaries and other stars are held, and how they are connected or disjoined among themselves, and similar things. But what Ptolemy says, that those who are born with the Moon and Mercury disjoined, with Saturn by day, or Mars by night ascending to the horoscope, and beholding the aforesaid configuration, often become epileptic, pertains to the conjecture of Astrology. The chief writer in both arts is Claudius Ptolemy, who has handed down these arts more elegantly and truly than others: yet he, because of the subtlety and profundity of the matters, and especially in this part, his excessive brevity, will not have benefited much the unlearned reader who has not first learned the first elements from introductory books, of which the principal ones are: in Astronomy, those of Proclus and George Purbach; in Astrology, those of Alcabitius, whose brief and somewhat obscure sayings, for the sake of the beginners in this art, we will endeavor to explain more broadly, and illustrate with examples, when the matter so demands. But our other and principal aim in this commentary will be to diligently compare these teachings of Alcabitius, which serve especially for understanding the traditions of the Arabs, with the opinion of Ptolemy, which I see approved by all true practitioners. We hope, moreover, that this labor of ours will be neither ungrateful nor unfruitful to diligent young students. For from these our instructions, they will not only learn all the necessary precepts of this philosophy, but also be able to distinguish the true from the false and superstitious, the 'unreasoned', and easily understand in what respects the Arabs followed Ptolemy, in what they departed from him, and what absurdities they finally invented. And so on. He begins with an invocation in the manner of the ancients, who even in prose, like poets, are accustomed to begin with an invocation. Demosthenes, in his speech against Aeschines for Ctesiphon: 'First, indeed, O men of Athens, I pray to all the gods and goddesses that as much goodwill as I have always had both towards this city and towards all of you, so much may be granted to me by you in this present contest.' And Lycurgus in Leocrates: 'I pray to Athena and the other gods and heroes established throughout the city and country.'

[Ptolemy's Astrological Principles]
PREFACE. to judge. Universally in astrological treatment, either the parts, that is, the longitude, latitude, and substance and position of the heavens are considered, and the forces of all these are examined; or the proper powers and effects of the stars are explored; or finally, these two are compared with each other. Ptolemy, therefore, in his Quadripartite Construction, first explains what the inherent and proper nature of each star is in itself, and then, as if in the second place, he sets forth their adventitious nature by comparison with the Sun. Thirdly, he establishes the general constitution of the signs, which they derive from the temperament of the four seasons of the year. Hence some are called vernal, some aestival, some autumnal, and finally some hibernal signs. He then adds the nature of the four angles of the heavens, to which both stars and signs should be equally compared. Fourthly, he recites the constitutions of the signs derived from the peculiar property and inherent nature of each, by which means some are called tropical, some equinoctial, some bicorporeal, others again masculine, others feminine, and finally others quadrupedal, others human, others terrestrial, or otherwise denominated. Fifthly, he enumerates the properties of the signs which arise from their mutual collation. For this reason, some are called commanding, some obedient, some in diameter, square, trine, sextile, and other ways beholding each other, and finally some are called unconnected signs. Having thus explained the qualities of each separately, in the sixth and last place, he institutes a comparison of the stars to the signs, and teaches that some parts of the heavens agree with the Sun, others with the Moon, others with the other planets by house, exaltation, trigon, and bounds. Finally, he states that the dispositions of individual stars are to be examined in four ways. First, from the proper nature of each star; secondly, from the nature of the sign in which it is constituted; thirdly, from its configuration to the Sun; fourthly, from its configuration to the angles. His words, with which he concludes the first book containing the principles of the art, are these: "From all these things it is evident that the quality of each star is to be examined from its own natural peculiarity, and from the dodecatemoria surrounding it, and from its configurations to the Sun and the angles." Ptolemy expounded astrological precepts in such an order, which this author here (Digitized by Google) left, and somewhat changed, but in such a way that this change does not bring any significant inconvenience to the reader. And indeed, if the truth must be told, this method of teaching, which is proposed here, also has the advantage of facility and clarity. For in the first place, he shows the varied distribution of the zodiac, and briefly and concisely explains the general constitutions of the signs, which they derive from the four seasons and half-years. The next step, and more consistent with Ptolemy's method, would have been to explain the proper natures of individual stars. But the author, having deferred these to a later place, namely to the second difference, immediately compares the signs themselves with the stars and the stations of the heavens, and teaches how those general constitutions of the signs are promoted or diminished by the presence of the stars and by reason of their diverse positions in the heavens. For example, the power of the sign Leo, which is hot, is intensified by the presence of Mars or the Sun. Conversely, it is diluted if it is mixed with the body or ray of Saturn. Similarly, the power of cardinal signs is greater than that of succedent signs. And again, the virtue of these is found to be greater than that of cadent signs. Hence it is clear that the nature and constitution of one and the same Leo, or even of another sign, does not always remain the same due to the mixture of different stars and by reason of various positions to the Meridian and the setting point, but varies in many ways. And this is what the author says, that he will write in the first difference about the essential and accidental being of the signs. For he calls the general constitution of the signs and their inherent and innate property, as well as the increases or decreases of powers which they receive from the Sun and stars, the essential power of the signs. Ptolemy, however, calls it "energeia kata to kosmikon," that is, disposition according to the world; but the author calls the intensifications or remissions of qualities which happen to the signs by reason of different stations of the heavens, accidents, while Ptolemy calls it "energeia kata ten genesin e allon archen," that is, the disposition which occurs to the signs in a nativity or in another beginning of things. And this is to be understood not only of the signs, but also, and much more, of the stars in a similar way. Therefore, just as in the first difference of the signs, so in the following ones (Digitized by Google)

[Collection of Light]
8. DIFFERENCE. But if there is a collection of light, when two planets are not plainly conjoined, but both are conjoined to some third planet: then that third planet collects the light of both, and receives their nature, and projects it to the parts of the zodiac which it aspects by radiation, in which places any planet existing receives the light of the aforesaid two. For example, let the Sun be in Aries and Venus in Virgo, not seeing each other by any aspect, but Mars, traversing Cancer, sees the Sun from Aries and Venus from Virgo. In this case, Mars collects the lights of the Sun and Venus, and projects them to the places of their rays, or even to the planets existing there. That is, Mars, as a medium, conjoins the Sun and Venus, who are not conjoined by themselves, and renders or attributes their light both to places and to stars which it aspects. Again, if 7. In another way, the return of light, or the conjunction of two unconjoined stars, is said to occur if some third planet mixes with two planets that do not touch each other with rays, so that separating from one, it carries its light to the other to which it is conjoined. But this case does not differ from the translation of light, of which we spoke a little earlier. It follows that 8. He teaches that the conjunctions of planets are impeded in two ways. First, when three planets traverse one sign, if the slower planet is between the two lighter ones: then that middle planet prohibits their light from being communicated. For example, if Jupiter is in the 1st part, the Moon in the 11th part, and Saturn in the 5th part. In this case, Jupiter seeks the Moon, but Saturn prohibits this conjunction, who, being in the middle and closer, repels Jupiter and himself receives the Moon. If such a conjunction of the lighter planets would be bad, then the prohibition is useful and helps; but if good, it harms. Secondly, when two planets are in one sign, if the lighter of them is conjoined to the heavier, and a third planet strikes the same heavier planet with an equally near ray, that is, if the ray of the aspecting planet and the body of the planet existing in the same sign are equally distant from the heavier planet, then the one who is conjoined by body prohibits the aspecting planet from the aspect. But if the rays of the aspecting planet approach closer to the heavier planet, the society of the planet existing in the same sign being repelled, the conjunction of the aspecting planet with the heavier planet will be perfected. As if Saturn is in the 10th part, Mars in the 6th part of the same sign, Jupiter in the 6th or 11th part, casting his rays to the 6th part, then Mars is conjoined to Saturn by body, being repelled from Jupiter's aspect. On the contrary, if Jupiter is placed in the 7th part, irradiating the 7th part with a trine, he prohibits Mars from Saturn, and himself conjoins with Saturn, which is especially true if the one who prohibits is faster in motion than the one who is prohibited. But if the one who is prohibited is faster than the prohibitor, and will reach the body of the heavier planet sooner, the opposite seems to be true, since in this matter, everything that is potentially such, is understood to be actually such. 9. Pulsation first occurs when a planet is in some dignity, that is, in the house, exaltation, triplicity, or term of another planet, and the aspecting planet is said to pulsate, that is, to send the nature of that dignity to the lord himself, whose dignity it is: for the lord of the place is always understood to receive his virtue from the aspecting planet: as if the Sun is in Aries, aspecting Saturn traversing Virgo with a trine, then the Sun will send the nature of the house of Saturn to Saturn himself, the lord of the place. Similarly, if a light planet existing in one of its said dignities aspects another heavier planet having no dignity in the place where the light planet is: then the light planet transmits its own nature to the heavier and peregrine planet: as if the Moon is in the 7th part of Taurus, and Saturn in the 7th part of Virgo. Here the Moon sends its strength or virtue of exaltation to Saturn, although Saturn has no dignity in the Moon's place. 10. Reception occurs when a planet existing in its dignity aspects another who obtains dignity in the same place: as if Mars is in Aries, and constituted in his house and exaltation, sees Venus in Pisces, then Mars communicates both natures, namely his own and that which he obtains by reason of the house, to Venus. But if the inferior planet exists in the dignity of the superior, and vice versa the superior in the dignity of the inferior, there is a mutual and perfect reception, as in Aries and Libra, they receive each other by mutual reception. For Mars is in the house of Venus, and Venus in the domicile of Mars. Now follows 11. The return or rather restitution of virtue occurs when an inferior planet aspects a superior planet that is combust or retrograde, who, being weak, remits to the inferior planet the virtue which it had sent to him: as if the Moon is in Taurus, and aspects Mercury, combust or retrograde, in Capricorn. In this case, the Moon commits the nature of its exaltation to Mercury: but because Mercury is combust, he cannot retain the nature committed to him, therefore he returns to the Moon the light which he had received from her. When, however, the restitution is useful and when it is detrimental, is clearly evident in the text. Then follows 12. Refranation from a conjunction or aspect occurs when an inferior planet seeking the conjunction of a superior planet, is already retrograde, or because before it is conjoined partilely with the heavier planet, it will become retrograde. As if the Moon is in the 12th part of Virgo, and Saturn in the 5th part of Virgo. Here Venus is direct, but if reaching the 8th or 9th part, before the conjunction with Saturn, she begins to retrograde, she will be refranated in such a case from the conjunction of Saturn, and this, which at first sight seemed a simple application, will be changed into a mutual separation, if, namely, Venus retrograding, Saturn proceeds direct. For there is a mutual deflux, when the planet which is in more parts is direct, as was said above: but the one which holds fewer parts is retrograde. The same judgment applies to other configurations, if, namely, a planet goes to an aspect, but before it reaches it, begins to retrograde. This follows 13. When three planets are in the same sign, of which one is heavy, and the other two are lighter, so that one of the lighter planets has passed the heavy one, and the other seeks the conjunction of the same, but before it is conjoined to it, the one who passed the heavy one becomes retrograde, and by retrogradation is conjoined to the first heavy one: then passing the same, he also conjoins with the lighter one, and in this way destroys the conjunction of the former with the heavy one: as if Jupiter is in the beginning of the sign, Mars in the 8th part, and Saturn in the 10th part, retrograding. Here Jupiter seeks Saturn, but before he reaches him, Mars, having become retrograde in the 10th part, is first conjoined, and then meets Jupiter, and thus destroys Jupiter and Saturn. The author calls this contrariety; Cardan calls it perturbation. Perturbation, he says, occurs when two planets apply to each other, as to their natural motion: but there is a good third planet: a planet in more parts than both, who, before the first two are conjoined, either because he is already retrograde, or because he will become retrograde a little later, conjoins himself to the slower, and interposes himself between the first two, and thus perturbs the conjunction of the first planets. 14. Frustration from a conjunction or aspect occurs when a light planet seeks the conjunction of a heavy planet, but before it is conjoined to it, the heavy planet enters another sign, at the beginning of which are the rays of another planet, which aspects that sign. These rays, first meeting the heavy planet, then the light one, can frustrate the conjunction of the light planet with the heavy one in two ways: for example, let Mars be in the 29th part of Leo, and Jupiter in the 19th part of Leo, seeking the conjunction of Jupiter, but before he comes to him, Jupiter enters Virgo, at the beginning of which we imagine the rays of Saturn, who, first receiving Jupiter, then Mars, frustrate the conjunction with Jupiter. 15. The meaning is: When a light planet seeks the conjunction of a heavy planet, but before it reaches it, another planet is conjoined to that heavy planet by retrogradation, and thus cuts off the light of the first from the heavy planet: as if the Moon is in the 29th part of Taurus, Jupiter in the 24th part of Virgo, and Mars in the 1st part of Virgo, retrograding, looking at Saturn very closely from Virgo. In this case, Jupiter seeks the conjunction of Saturn, but before he completes it, Mars, by retrocession from Virgo, is conjoined partilely to Saturn, and thus cuts off the light of Saturn from Jupiter. Here the third planet, namely the retrograde one, or its rays, are placed at the beginning.

[Zodiacal Quarters and Planetary Order]
The first quarter, from the beginning of Aries to the end of Gemini, is hot, humid, vernal, youthful, and sanguine. The quarter from the beginning of Cancer to the end of Virgo is called hot, dry, aestival, juvenile, and choleric. The quarter from the beginning of Libra to the end of Sagittarius is called cold, dry, autumnal, melancholic, and signifies the beginning of the diminution of middle age. The quarter from the beginning of Capricorn to the end of Pisces is called cold, humid, defective, senile, hiemal, and phlegmatic. Among all the planets that are carried in these signs (not that they are in them, but because they are carried beneath them, that is, directly below them), Saturn is higher and closer to the circle of the signs and slower in its course, then Jupiter, then Mars, then the Sun, then Venus, then Mercury, then the Moon, which is closer to the Earth and swifter than all in its course. Certain significations are also indicated by the Head and Tail of the Dragon with the planets, as we will explain in the following sections. First, the length of the zodiac is divided into 12 parts, which the Latins call signs, and the Greeks call dodecatemoria, so that their number corresponds to the number of synodic months in a year. For the Moon, in a space of 29 days, always overtakes the Sun with its speed, and is conjoined with it twelve times each solar year, that is, once in each dodecatemorion. The fact that some years show us 13 lunations is not truly due to the annual time, but to the 11 days by which the Sun's circuit exceeds the twelve conjunctions of the luminaries, which cause intercalations. Moved by this reasoning, the Arabs attributed 354 days to the annual period. For 29 days, in which the luminaries are conjoined, collected twelve times, make 354 days. But the Egyptians and C. Julius Caesar, neglecting the monthly course of the Moon, concluded the limits of the year by the periodic motion of the Sun. However, even these do not agree in all respects. For the Egyptians and Persians ended the solar year in only 365 days, while Julius ended it in 365 days and a quarter of a day, that is, six hours. But leaving aside this variety of years, which is explained elsewhere, let us proceed to the following divisions of the zodiac. It seemed most useful to divide it into smaller arcs than the signs, if they were to distribute it into as many parts as the Sun traverses in its daily circuits. But indeed, since the number of days in a year, which is 365, was not at all suitable for calculations, astronomers divided not only the zodiac, but also all celestial circles in general into 360 parts, which number is close to the number of days in the tropical or turning year, and consists of many component or multiple parts, such as a half, a third, a quarter, a fifth part, a sixth, two-thirds, three-quarters, and others that follow, and for this reason easily admits all kinds of calculations. Now, if we divide 360 parts by 12, we will find that each dodecatemorion or sign of the zodiac consists of thirty degrees. For the younger astronomers call the thirty equally divided parts of each sign degrees, while the ancient Latin writers call them parts, and the Greeks call them moirai. And indeed, this division of one sign into thirty parts also corresponds to the motion of the Sun and Moon. For since the period of time in which the Moon, having departed from the Sun, again overtakes it, comprises almost 30 days, therefore the twelfth part of the sky, which the Sun has meanwhile traversed by its motion, is understood by nature itself to be cut into as many parts, which the more recent (astronomers) not absurdly call degrees from the Sun's daily progress, as I said. From these it is clear that in the more important divisions of the zodiac, namely into twelve signs, and the thirty parts of the signs, wise antiquity followed nature itself as its guide. Moreover, what the author adds, that each part is again divided as if whole into sixty minutes, and one first minute into sixty secondary minutes, one second into 60 third minutes, and similarly thereafter to fourth, fifth, sixth, and other minutes that follow in order, that mathematical division proceeds as calculation demands, this is established not by nature, but by art. The Greeks call these minute parts of the sky first, second, third, fourth, fifth, sixth, seventh, and so on. But to proceed beyond the first minutes in assigning motions is a matter of curiosity, not necessity, since the minute parts of the sky are entirely sufficient. Nor am I unaware that to extract the true motions of the stars from tables, even the following smallest minutes are usefully observed. In this sexagesimal system, that is, the number of sixty, the ancients also looked for the same computational convenience as in the number 360, which itself is composed of six sextants of a circle or sixty degrees, as was said. Therefore, they preferred the sexagesimal system to all other numbers. For among the smaller numbers composing a whole circle, none has so many multiple parts as sixty. For first, it consists of a half, that is, 30; then of a third, that is, 20; thirdly, of a quarter, that is, 15; fourthly, of a fifth part, that is, 12; fifthly, of a sixth, that is, 10; sixthly, of a tenth part, that is, 5; eighthly, of a fifteenth part, that is, 4; ninthly, of a twentieth part, that is, 3; and finally, of a thirtieth part, that is, 2. Therefore, practitioners not only attenuate the degrees of a circle as if whole into the smallest sexagesimal parts by perpetual sexagesimal division, as was said, but also continuously amplify the integers themselves with the same sexagesimal system, forming sexagesimals, that is, sexagesimal groups of integer parts: just as a sextant of a circle, which contains two dodecatemoria, or 60 parts, constitutes one first sexagesimal. And sixty first sexagesimals, that is, ten whole circles, make one second sexagesimal, and so on, ascending further, as the use of calculation may require. In such a numbering system, the degree or other form of things is placed in the middle, to which the calculator's thought assigns the force of an integer. Sexagesimals are placed on both sides, and to the right precede the sexagesimals collected from integers; to the left, however, follow the sexagesimal parts of the minute scruples. But the greater use and broader treatment of this matter is found in the logistics of astronomical scruples.

[Saturn and Journeys]
What is said about Saturn signifying long journeys, prisons, bonds, slowness, and affliction, and similar impediments to travel, should be understood thus: Saturn threatens these evils if he hostilely aspects the places of journeys (such as those of the Moon, Sun, Mars, and the Part of Fortune, according to Ptolemy) when they are in the seventh, ninth, or third house, and succeeds them. Ptolemy's words in Book 4, Chapter on peregrinations, are these: "If benefics aspect or succeed these aforementioned places, they decree honorable, glorious, and profitable journeys, and swift and unimpeded returns. But malefics decree laborious, harmful, dangerous, and impeded returns." These mentioned places, that is, those held by the Moon, or the seventh, ninth, or third house, if aspected by benefics and succeeded by them, decree honorable, clear, and profitable journeys, and swift and expeditious returns. But malefics decree laborious, harmful, dangerous, and impeded returns.

[Constellations and Astrological Characters]
Camerarius embraced these verses, which I have transcribed here for their elegance. Learn, studious boy, the names of the ancient labors, the starry ones, and recognize the shining signs of the heavens. They call those signs by which you see those temples distinguished, but they finally gave the number four and eight. The Great Bear holds the cold of the North, through which a huge Serpent glides like a river. Arctophylax follows this, but also the gem of the Crown, then Genu Nixus, after which Lyra, then Volucris. Then Cepheus and Cassiopeia, then Vector and Perseus, raising his scythe-like sword against the Gorgon. From here Deltoton, and Andromeda, from here you stand Pegasus, but the Dolphin receives you, this one the Arrow, this one the Eagle, and Ophiuchus. The Ecliptic follows these with its sloping orbit, in which are Aries, Taurus, Gemini, Cancer, Leo, Virgo, Libra, Scorpio, Sagittarius, Capricorn, Aquarius, Pisces. But the South holds these images under its axis: armed Orion, the Dog in which Sirius burns. Who is Procyon? And Lepus? And Jason's Argo is added, and Hydrus, and Centaurus, and the Altar unknown to us, then the more familiar Pisces, Cetus, and the waters of Eridanus. The former verses seem to express only 19 images, but two signs double the number. For Pegasus here signifies the section of the horse, or the first horse, and Pegasus or the second horse. Similarly, the word Ophiuchus is to be attributed to Ophiuchus and the serpent of Ophiuchus. The latter eleven verses also seem to name the stars. But Centaurus doubles the number. For the wolf or beast that Centaurus carries is a distinct constellation from Centaurus; Hydrus, however, triples the number. For on its back it carries the Crater and the thirsty Corvus. To these must be added the Southern Crown, which some call γρανίσκομ, that is, a small heaven, others call it retam ixionis. In this entire treatise, the appellations of the signs, degrees, and minutes, of the planets, as well as the aspects or radiations, and the intersections which the plane of the moon forms with the plane of the sun, will very often be used. Therefore, we here subjoin the significations of the characters, by which we can henceforth express all these things concisely and without tiring the reader. CHARACTERS OF THE TWELVE SIGNS OF THE ZODIAC. Northern. Aries, Taurus, Gemini, Cancer, Leo, Virgo. κριός. ταύρο. δίδυμοι. κάρκιν. λέωμ. παρθένα. Southern. Libra, Scorpio, Sagittarius, Capricorn, Aquarius, Pisces. ζυγός ἤχαλαί. σκόρπιο. τοξότης. αιγόκερω. υδροχόο. ἰχθῦς. CHARACTERS OF THE SEVEN WANDERING STARS. Saturn, Jupiter, Mars, Sun, Venus, Mercury, Moon. Φαίνωμ ἢ κρόνο. Φάεθωμ ἢ ζεὺς. πυρόεις καρης. ἵλιθ. ἀφροδίτκι φωσφόρο. ἕρμας ἢ τίλβωμ. σελήνη. CHARACTERS OF THE SPACES OF THE HEAVENS. Sig.
