[Planetary Sects and Religions]
Regarding sects, Cardan noted these few things in his Supplement to the Ephemerides. He says the Jewish law is from Saturn, the Christian from Jupiter and Venus, and the Mohammedan from Mars and the Sun, according to how they rule. Hence, it guards justice, but with great impiety and cruelty. Idols are from Mercury and the Moon. And each law is overcome by its opposite: Saturn is overcome by Jupiter by authority and reason; Jupiter and Mercury are overcome by not listening to reasons and acting against authority; Mars and the Sun are overcome by Saturn through cunning, and Venus through lust; finally, the Moon and Mars are overcome by Jupiter and Venus through authority, dignity, and truth. These are general principles and apply to all peoples. Furthermore, Albubather and other Arabs teach that the future quality of each religion is to be inferred from the ninth house and the natural constitution of Jupiter as the significator of religion.

[Differences in Ascensions and Stature]
DIFFERENCE & Conjunction, synod. Sextile Aspect, hexagon. Square Aspect, tetragon. Trine Aspect, trigon. Opposite Aspect, diameter. Dragon's Head, anabibazon. Dragon's Tail, katabibazon. Part of Fortune, that is, Lot or Part of Fortune. And from these, the equinoctial circle not only divides the zodiac into its principal signs, etc., but also the entire globe of the world into two equal parts, one of which is called boreal and the other austral, from the winds erupting from these regions. Therefore, six signs of the zodiac, namely, Aries, Taurus, Gemini, Cancer, Leo, Virgo, and also other stars, whatever incline into the boreal part from the equinoctial, are called boreal. The remaining six dodecatemoria of the zodiac, namely, Libra, Scorpio, Sagittarius, Capricorn, Aquarius, Pisces, and whatever other stars have receded to the south from the equinoctial, are called austral. Now, in order that the reason may be understood why some signs are called direct and others oblique or tortuous in their ascensions, it must be assumed from spherical principles that ascension is called the arc of the equinoctial co-rising with a given arc of the Ecliptic. There are two types of ascensions of signs, namely, direct and oblique. And in tables of directions or ascensions, direct ascension is called the arc of the equinoctial which co-ascends with a given arc of the zodiac in a right sphere. In Greek, anaphora epi tes orthos sphairas. Oblique or climatic ascension is called the arc of the equinoctial which rises with a given arc of the Ecliptic in the position of an oblique sphere. In Greek, anaphora epi tes enklimenes sphairas. The author here does not mean such ascensions of signs, which take their differences by reason of the right and oblique sphere, but speaks in general of the direct and oblique rising of signs, which is common to both the right and oblique sphere. According to this reasoning, we call ascension direct when a greater arc of the equator ascends than of the Ecliptic, and the parts of the Ecliptic consume more time in rising than the parts of the equator. Conversely, we call it oblique when fewer degrees of the equator than of the Ecliptic are carried above the Horizon, and the parts of the Ecliptic require less time in rising than the parts of the equinoctial. In the same way, the signs themselves are made some direct, which, while ascending, occupy a little more time; others oblique, which are carried upwards in a shorter time. And although in both the right and oblique sphere, some signs are found to have direct ascensions and others oblique ascensions, there is nevertheless some dissimilarity in their number. For in the oblique sphere, there are six signs of direct ascensions and as many of oblique ascensions. The direct ones are those which extend in the descending half of the zodiac from the beginning of Cancer to the end of Sagittarius, namely, Cancer, Leo, Virgo, Libra, Scorpio, Sagittarius. The oblique ones are the remaining ones, which extend in the ascending half of the zodiac from the end of Sagittarius to the beginning of Cancer, namely, Capricorn, Aquarius, Pisces, Aries, Taurus, Gemini. But in the right sphere, only four signs, of which two on each side touch the solstitial points, namely, Gemini, Cancer, Sagittarius, and Capricorn, ascend directly; the remaining eight ascend obliquely. For this, we have made you a compendium in the following description. Direct Signs in the Oblique Sphere: Descending Half. Tortuous Signs in the Oblique Sphere: Ascending Half. Signs in the Right Sphere: Direct: Gemini, Cancer, Sagittarius, Capricorn. Tortuous: Aries, Taurus, Leo, Virgo, Libra, Scorpio, Aquarius, Pisces. The tables of ascensions also clearly demonstrate that any given sign located in the descending half of the oblique sphere draws more parts of the equinoctial with it in rising than any sign placed in the other ascending half. And similarly, in the right sphere, the four signs contiguous to the solstitial points consume more time in ascension than the other eight signs. We have appended examples of this, first for the position of the right sphere, in which both poles lie on the horizon; then for the position of the oblique sphere and the altitude of the pole calculated for 51, 52, and 53 degrees, from which you will easily judge, in both situations of the sphere, not only which signs are direct and oblique, but also which have equal anaphoras. The anaphoras of the signs calculated for the right position of the world. Signs: Parts, Scruples. Aries, Taurus, Gemini, Cancer, Leo, Virgo, Libra, Scorpio, Sagittarius, Capricorn, Aquarius, Pisces. The anaphoras of the signs calculated for three oblique positions of the world. Pole: 51, 52, 53. Signs: Parts, Scruples. Aries, Taurus, Gemini, Cancer, Leo, Virgo, Libra, Scorpio, Sagittarius, Capricorn, Aquarius, Pisces. And tortuous. However, what the author wrote here, that six signs ascending in a direct path rule over the other six ascending obliquely and tortuously, namely, Cancer rules over Gemini, Leo over Taurus, Virgo over Aries, and others similarly rule over others which are equally distant from the tropical points, cannot be confirmed. For Ptolemy teaches us that these are not ruling or obedient, but beholding and of the same power. Similarly, those which the author calls concordant in their course because of equal anaphoras (which, whether they are in both situations of the sphere, the preceding descriptions of anaphoras sufficiently declare) Ptolemy transmits as ruling and obedient signs. But we here and everywhere will always prefer Ptolemy's opinion as superior to all others, and below, in its proper place, in his chapter on competent signs, we will clearly explain the entire doctrine of ruling and obedient signs. For we see no reason by whose aid we might dare to excuse the author in this, that either having abandoned Ptolemy, he subscribed to the opinion of others here, or he himself, by his own authority, ought to have changed these most elegant and most proper names of the parts of the zodiac transmitted by Ptolemy. Moreover, not contemptible physical reasons, and the very practice of the art, which we see approved by artisans, admonishes and exhorts us to keep the direct and oblique signs separate and distinct from the ruling and obedient ones. For it is known that illustrious writers judge some events from ruling and obedient signs, and others from direct and tortuous signs. From this it follows that events arising from the former do not at all correspond to the latter, and similarly, those effected by the latter cannot be accommodated to the power of the former without error. For example: One of the causes that declare the bodily quantity of those born is the rectitude or obliquity of the ascending signs, not the dominion or obedience of the signs. And conversely, one of the causes that generate benevolence and love towards others in the native is the ruling and obedient signs, and not, as the author seems to think, the rectitude or tortuosity of the signs. But in order that less learned readers may understand how the rectitude or tortuosity of the signs helps to form the stature of the body, and may be admonished by an easy example of the use of these distinctions, by which experts distribute the zodiac into various and distinctly named parts, I will here subjoin the 52nd statement of the Centiloquium, which, although it is by Hermes, we always see printed and circulated under Ptolemy's name. "The lords of the nativity of tall people are in the heights, and their horoscopes are in the beginnings of the signs. But those of short stature are found in the low places. To these, inquire whether the signs are direct or oblique." Pontanus thus interpreted these words: "The lords of the nativity of tall people are in the heights, and their horoscopes are in the beginnings of the signs. But those of short stature are found in the low places. To these, inquire whether the signs are direct or oblique." According to this opinion, there are three most powerful causes producing the tallness or shortness of the human body. The first is the lord of the nativity, or rather the lord of the ascending sign, placed in its orbs. For if it surveys the highest part, it confers tallness; but if it traverses the lowest part, it confers shortness of stature. The second is the number of degrees of the horoscoping sign; if its first parts pass the eastern horizon, then because of their strength, they produce tallness; if the extreme parts, because of exhausted strength, shortness; if finally the middle parts, by their moderate power, they produce a moderate stature. The third cause is the rectitude or obliquity of the horoscoping sign: the former, by prolonging the time of rising, also makes the body somewhat long; the latter, by shortening the time of ascending, shortens the body of the native. Example: When Titius is born, let Libra ascend, which is a direct sign. For with its thirty parts, in any altitude of the pole, more than...

[Significations of Planets]
Concerning garments, colors, tastes, parts of the human body, and innumerable such forms of things which agree with certain stars due to a similar temperament, much has been said above. We will also explain at the end of this difference to which stars days, nights, and hours are attributed.

[Body Stature and Ascendant]
Examples of anaphora indicate that thirty parts of the equinoctial rise. Let the beginning of Libra also ascend, so that its first or second, or even third, fourth, fifth, or sixth degree touches the eastern horizon. Finally, let Venus, the Lady of the geniture, be carried in the highest of her orbs. With these three causes concurring, as I have said, at Titius's birth, we will declare Titius to be of tall stature, even if he were born of short parents. For it is not doubtful that the good stature of parents, as well as the region in which people are commonly taller, also contributes much to tallness of stature. Conversely, if at Titius's birth, the last parts of the sign occupy the eastern horizon, and the rising sign itself is moreover oblique, like Aries or Pisces, and finally, if the Sun or Saturn, having obtained dominion of the geniture, is found depressed to the lowest parts of its orbs, we will not doubt that Titius will also be of shorter body and contracted stature. These are the extreme states of bodily stature. Mediocrity, however, which is produced when these do not all concur at once, is left to the sagacity of the prudent Physician to judge, according to whether more or fewer of those causes have concurred. The dikodiasotes or lord of the geniture, who it is, and by what reasons it is to be chosen, will be explained below in a more opportune place. In the meantime, by reason of long and short ascensions, one half of the zodiacal circle, which extends from the beginning of Leo to the end of Capricorn, is called the maximum half, because while it ascends, it occupies the most time. This same half is also called the half of the Sun, because Leo is of a solar nature, and in this half the Sun exercises that power which the other planets have in their own bounds. The other half, which extends from the beginning of Aquarius to the end of Cancer, since it shortens the time of rising the most, is called the minimum half. It is also called the half of the Moon, because just as the Sun holds principality in the maximum, so the Moon holds it in this minimum. For Cancer is familiar to the Moon. In all the other parts of this half, the Moon reigns just as the planets do in their own bounds, about which more will be said below. The bounds of the signs of the Zodiac, from which the principal causes of rising and setting flow, are of different qualities: some are general, others are derived from the familiarity of the Sun and Moon and other stars, others arise from their mutual collation, and finally, others are simply and without any mixture inherent and peculiar to each, as Ptolemy's opinion has been stated above. That he distinguished these in this way is apparent from his own words: "The general constitutions have the same ratio with the times. But in each there are other properties, some of which are taken from the familiarity of the Sun, Moon, and other stars; others are simple, which are inherent in each dodecatemory by itself, and which arise from their mutual collation." In exactly the same way, Alcabitius, after completing the division of the zodiac, explains the qualities and properties of its parts. And first, he insinuates the general qualities and natures of the signs by a certain proportion corresponding to the four seasons of the year, the four ages, and finally, the four humors of living beings. For in all these, the first part is humid and hot, with humidity predominating. Similarly, the second part is hotter than dry; the third is drier than cold; the fourth is colder than humid. But since the zodiac, inasmuch as it is a circle, has neither beginning nor end, it can be asked where its first part should be established and begin. We respond that it must be estimated and taken from the agreement of the qualities inherent in the effects with the qualities inherent in the more potent efficient causes. The oblique circle or zodiac is the efficient cause of nascent things. The seasons of the year and the humors of living beings are what are produced by it. For the Sun, traversing the zodiac, excites the qualities of the seasons of the year, each of which agrees with the humors of certain ages. Just as spring agrees with childhood, in which blood abounds; summer with youth and the choleric humor; autumn with decline and the melancholic humor; and finally, winter with old age and the phlegmatic humor. Since this is so, it is clear that each age of animals, and each humor, agrees principally in the order and power of qualities not only with certain times of the year, but also with those portions of the zodiac that produce such times. For from whatever cause each such thing exists, it must abound in that kind of quality. Therefore, the first age, in which animals abound in humidity and are soft and tender, agrees not only with the season we call spring, but also with that part of the zodiac which is the efficient cause of springtime, in order and nature. Such is that which extends from the beginning of Aries to the end of Gemini, since, when traversed by the Sun, it excellently moistens the air and the bodies enclosed in the air. Because of this great similarity of qualities equally dominating in the said portion of the sky, in springtime, and in the first age of animals, this first, vernal, childish, and sanguine quadrant of the zodiac is congruously named. For all these, just as they constitute the beginnings of things, so also they agree in heat and humidity; but they abound much more in humor than in heat. Furthermore, the first age of man (for this is principally discussed here), which they call childhood or rather the age of growth, extends from birth to the 21st year. In the same way, the subsequent seasons of the year and subsequent ages must be accommodated in order to the remaining subsequent quadrants of the zodiac. The second age, called the age of strength, extends from the 21st year to the 35th year, but in robust individuals to the 40th year, and because of its heat and dryness, it agrees with summer. Hence the portion of the zodiac that produces summer is called the second quadrant, hot and dry.

[Orbs of Planets]
The orb of a star is the space of the sky that the star affects circularly and from all sides, not so much by its ray or visible light, but by a hidden power and efficacy. The half or semi-diameter of this luminous orb, according to the tradition of the Arabs, is 15 parts for the Sun, 12 parts for the Moon, 9 parts for Saturn and Jupiter, 8 parts for Mars, and 7 parts for Venus and Mercury. However, Ptolemy shows these limits of rays somewhat differently, namely, the semi-diameter of the Sun extends 17 parts in all directions, the Moon 12 parts, Jupiter 12 parts, Saturn 10 parts, Venus 8 parts, and Mars 7 parts. This consideration is useful. For aspects are said to last as long as the orbs last, and according to them, both applications to conjunctions and separations from them are taken, as will be more clearly shown below in the doctrine of applications and defluxions. Many also call a planet combust when it is less than half its orb distant from the Sun, or precisely that much before and after.

[Ages, Seasons, and Temperaments]
The first age is dry, summery, youthful, and choleric. In all these, however, heat dominates. The third age of wisdom begins at age 35 and extends to age 50; in robust individuals, it progresses from age 40 to age 60. This age is compared to melancholic humor and the autumn season due to the decrease in heat and the dominance of dryness. This makes the portion of the zodiac that creates autumn called the fourth part: cold and dry, autumnal, melancholic, and deficient. The fourth age of debility universally begins between ages 50 and 60 and lasts until death. It is compared to phlegm, winter, and the last quarter of the zodiac, which is therefore called cold and humid, with redundant coldness, phlegmatic, wintry, and senile. This similarity of temperaments in ages and seasons of the year agrees with the opinion of physicians. For what Ptolemy makes the spring season humid and hot, and Galen temperate, they do not in fact disagree, since spring is humid in substance, but temperate in quality. Similarly, autumn is dry in substance, although it appears humid in quality, etc. As for the temperaments of the ages, Hippocrates widely stated that bodies that grow have a great deal of innate heat, which, as Galen teaches, is humid and hot according to its substance, as it inheres in an airy and watery substance. They call the subsequent age consistent, in which, although heat is intensified and sharpened according to quality, the innate heat is nevertheless diminished due to the underlying substance, in which, because of the impurity of added nourishment, the innate moisture is reduced, and the earthy parts are prominent. For similar reasons, the third age is drier, and old age is cold like winter. Since all these agree with the quadrants of the zodiac regarding temperaments, the nature or general constitution of all the dodecatemoria is clearly evident. For the three signs of each quadrant, which follow each other in order from any of the cardinal points, always agree in temperament. Just as the signs Taurus and Gemini found in the vernal section are hot and humid; those that follow in the aestival section, namely Leo and Virgo, are hot and dry. Hence, those placed in the autumnal section, namely Libra and Scorpio, are dry and cold. Finally, Sagittarius and Capricorn, which make winter, are humid and cold. But the middle ones of these, that is, the fixed signs, are especially such. Therefore, Taurus is very hot and very humid, and for this reason, the most temperate of all signs in the sky. Similarly, Leo is very hot and very dry, very bilious and very spirited. Likewise, Scorpio is very cold and very dry, but drier than cold, very pernicious and very inimical to human nature, for which reason poison is rightly attributed to it. Finally, the sign of Aquarius is very cold and very humid, and for this reason, very sluggish and very timid. Furthermore, the cardinal signs, that is, the first signs of each quadrant, excel greatly in one quality, while the other is diminished. Hence, in the dodecatemorion of Aries, humidity far surpasses heat. In Cancer, heat surpasses dryness; in Libra, dryness surpasses coldness; in Capricorn, coldness easily surpasses humidity. In the same way, in the last signs of the quadrants, which will be called bicorporeal and common below, one quality is strong, but in reverse order, and where one quality does not so excellently and excessively suppress the other. Therefore, in the dodecatemorion of Gemini, heat exceeds humidity; in Virgo, dryness exceeds heat; in Sagittarius, cold exceeds dryness; and finally, in Pisces, humidity exceeds cold. This is the general constitution of the twelve dodecatemoria, from which it is clear that the entire northern half of the zodiac is hot, and the southern half is cold, as the author intended. (There follows an admonition about the order and position of the planets, in which, following Ptolemy, he places those superior to the others that complete one period in a longer time. Hence, the star of Saturn, since it completes one revolution in its equal motion in 29 years, 162 days, and almost 7 hours, and appears smallest due to its great distance from Earth, it is consistent that it is the highest and encompasses the largest circle. The star of Jupiter traverses the entire Zodiac in 11 years, 315 days, and almost 174 hours, and therefore occupies a lower orbit than Saturn. Mars follows this, completing one revolution in one year, 320 days, and 222 hours. The Sun, leader and moderator of the others, placed in the middle, traverses the Zodiac in 365 and a quarter days. First Mercury, then Venus, are placed below the Sun, each of which completes the entire Zodiac in the same amount of time as the Sun. For the lines of the mean motions of Mercury, Venus, and the Sun are always conjoined, for which reason they are called isodromoi, homodromoi, and xandromoi to the Sun. The newest star and most familiar to Earth, and found by nature as a remedy for darkness, is the Moon, which completes one period in 27 days and almost 8 hours. This is Ptolemy's opinion on the order of the planets, from which some dissent. Indeed, all agree on the order of the three superior planets. The Moon is also, by everyone's opinion, the lowest, because it deprives us of the Sun's aspect and has a more manifest parallax, which they commonly call the diversity of aspect. Therefore, the controversy remains regarding the position and order of Mercury, Venus, and Mars. Plato and Aristotle place Mercury immediately above the Moon, then Venus immediately above Mercury, then Mars above Venus, and finally, as before, ascending in order, Jupiter and Saturn. Those who advocate this opinion offer this reason: If, they say, Mercury, Venus, and Mars were situated below the Sun, they would obscure it no less than the Moon (if not entirely, at least some part of it) whenever they were conjoined with the Sun and placed between the Sun and our sight; since this has never been observed in any age, they therefore believe that the stars Mercury, Venus, and Mars are placed above the Sun. However, this objection, by which they imagine an obscuration of the Sun by the intervention of Mercury or Venus, can be easily refuted. And first, it is evident that the Moon is occulted by the intervention of the Sun: For the body of the Moon, which shines only when illuminated by rays, since it is opaque and dark by its nature, and so dense that it hardly allows the passage of incident rays, and strongly reflects all received light, it is not surprising if it obstructs our view by its interposition. But since Venus and Mercury are not opaque, but very luminous bodies, they cannot obscure the Sun at all. Secondly, Mercury, Venus, and Mars mostly wander outside the ecliptic, and can rarely be so conjoined with the Sun that they fall into a line drawn from our sight through the center of the Sun, and thus be placed between our aspect and the Sun. Finally, even if this also happens, and as it can happen, an observable conjunction of one of these occurs: nevertheless, since the apparent smallness of these stars is such that they cannot deprive us of any part of the Sun that can be perceived by the senses, those who subject the Sun to these stars for no other reason than that they do not deprive us of its sight in conjunctions with the Sun, do not seem to be heard. Furthermore, although the Moon can be shown to be 6644 times smaller than the Sun: nevertheless, due to its low position, it appears of such magnitude.

[Planetary Years and Fridaria]
We have described all the planetary years mentioned by this author in the following table. The years of Fridaria: Sun 10, Moon 9, Saturn 11, Jupiter 12, Mars 7, Venus 8, Mercury 13. Maximum years: Sun 1461, Moon 520, Saturn 465, Jupiter 427, Mars 284, Venus 551, Mercury 480. Major years: Sun 120, Moon 108, Saturn 57, Jupiter 79, Mars 66, Venus 82, Mercury 76. Medium years: Sun 69, Moon 66, Saturn 43, Jupiter 45, Mars 40, Venus 45, Mercury 48. Minor years: Sun 19, Moon 12, Saturn 5, Jupiter 13, Mars 12, Venus 15, Mercury 18. What the Arabs mean by Fridaria years will be explained in the third to last chapter of the fourth difference. But the Arabs call maximum years the duration of time during which the effects of planets last in universal and great world changes. Through major, medium, and minor years, the Arabs are accustomed to deriving the spans of life in human nativities, as we will see below in the fourth difference, in the chapter on Alcocodes. Furthermore, Ptolemy nowhere mentions any of this kind of years contained in this place, undoubtedly indicating by his silence that the observation of minor years is useless. However, he seems to make mention of major years in the first apotelesmatic chapter on boundaries, but in such a way that he also leaves them to be despised.

[Planetary Houses and Dignities]
28. The apparent diameter of the Moon, when it is most distant, is 29 minutes, but when it is closest, it is almost 36 minutes. The apparent diameter of the Sun, when it is most distant from the Earth, fills 31.5 minutes of the sky, and when it is closest, 34 minutes. Now, from a comparison of the Moon's closest apparent diameter to the Sun's apparent diameter, wherever it may be, it is clear that the body of the Sun can be completely obscured by the intervention of the Moon's body, even if not for a long duration. Conversely, the apparent diameter of Venus, according to the observations of skilled astronomers, barely equals a tenth part of the Sun's diameter. And since the planes of two orbs relate to each other in the duplicate ratio of their diameters, the ratio of the apparent solar plane to the apparent plane of Venus is as 100 to 1. Therefore, if Venus were to cover the Sun, it would cover only one hundredth of its apparent part, which could not be noticed by the senses of the eyes. Regiomontanus, in the beginning of the 9th book of Ptolemy's Epitome, defends him thus: It is not consistent that the space between the Sun and Moon should be empty of stars, since it is greater than the interval extended from the center of the Earth to the orbs of the Moon. For the minimum distance of the Sun from the center of the world contains 1070 Earth semidiameters, and the Moon at its highest is distant from the center of the universe by almost 64 of the same Earth semidiameters. Subtracting these from the Sun's distance leaves 1006 Earth semidiameters, which is 865,160 German miles. It is not likely that such a vast interval between the two luminaries should be entirely devoid of stars. 9. Concerning the existence of stars in the signs, when the orbs of the luminaries and other planets are understood to be affixed to the sky, etc. Therefore, when we say, for example, that the Sun is in Aries, it is not to be understood as if the Sun's highest heaven, approaching, is carried among the fixed stars, but rather that it moves under that part of the zodiac which is called Aries. The author of the Sphere teaches that planets exist in the signs in four ways. But the true and most exact place of the Sun or planets lacking latitude in the ecliptic is indicated by a line extended from the center of the Earth through the center of the star to the outermost heaven. As for those stars that have latitude and move outside the ecliptic, their true places are most certainly shown by that part of the periphery which extends through the pole of the Zodiac and the center of the star to the ecliptic; for that point of contact in the Zodiac is the true place. 29. the focus of that star according to longitude. In this way, the polar star, as they call it, of the third magnitude, although it is 66 degrees away from the ecliptic, is now referred to 20 degrees 52 minutes of Gemini, according to the Prutenic calculation. 10. The Dragon is a figure that the paths of the Sun and Moon create. For the plane of the Moon's orbit intersects the Sun's ecliptic along the world's diameter, so that one part of it deviates 5 degrees to the north from the ecliptic, and the other to the south. This inclination of the Moon's orbit in opposite directions is called the winding path of the Dragon, from a certain similarity to huge serpents whose belly is looser and wider than their head and tail. One of these intersections, where the Moon ascends to the northern region, is called the Dragon's Head; the Greeks call it anabibazon, that is, the ascending or rising node, whose usual character is ☊. The other intersection, where the Moon descends to the south, is commonly called the Dragon's Tail; the Greeks call it katabibazon, that is, the descending or lowering node, and its usual character is ☋. The northern limit (boreum poras) is the midpoint of the northern lunar semicircle. The southern limit is directly opposite the former, similarly the midpoint of the southern lunar semicircle: the common people call these the bellies of the Dragons. With these things being so, it is manifest that between both limits and the nodes themselves, quadrants of a circle are encompassed, and the Moon, when positioned in the nodes, is entirely deprived of latitude, and attains its maximum latitude at the limits. Finally, it must be known that these points of the Moon's nodes and limits are not fixed, but daily move from east to west by 3 minutes, so that a reason can be given why lunar eclipses always occur when the Moon is not at its closest. CONCERNING THE HOUSES OF THE PLANETS. 1. The planets also have certain powers in these signs, some by nature, some by accident. Those that are by nature are these: House, Exaltation, Term, Triplicity, Face. We will treat those that are by accident in a convenient place. The Houses are these: Aries and Scorpio, houses of Mars; Taurus and Libra, houses of Venus; Gemini and Virgo, houses of Mercury; Cancer, house of the Moon; Leo, house of the Sun; Sagittarius and Pisces, houses of Jupiter; Capricorn and Aquarius, houses of Saturn. 2. The seventh sign from the house of each planet is said to be its detriment. 3. And if two signs are the house of one planet, they are said to be concordant in the Almantica, that is, in the circle which is broad in the middle and tightly bound, and is meant to signify the Zodiacal circle. 4. The signs in which planets are said to rejoice when they enter them, and their lords, according to Dorotheus, are these: Saturn is said to rejoice when it enters Aquarius; Jupiter in Sagittarius; Mars in Scorpio; Venus in Taurus; Mercury in Virgo. 1. Having set forth the division of the Zodiac and the general constitution of the signs, as the order explained above requires, it now wishes to show other properties of the signs derived from the familiarity of the luminaries and other wandering stars. But perhaps someone here will criticize me, saying that this is a new exposition or one alien to the author's meaning. For since the Author here prefaces that he will speak of the powers of the planets, both inherent and accidental, he does not seem to want to say in what ways the general constitution of the signs is intensified or remitted. But our exposition is not without reason. For he who discusses how the virtue of the planets is intensified by kindred qualities and the familiarity of suitable signs, or diluted by contrary ones, at the same time tacitly treats the intensification and remission of the qualities inherent in the signs. For those temperaments that are associated with similar ones make the quality of both concurring more efficacious, as: because the Sun and Leo agree; therefore, when the Sun passes through Leo, it increases the heat and dryness of the sign, and the power of the star in turn aids the kindred quality of the sign of Leo. From this, it is clear that this treatment of the association and agreement of the planets with the signs pertains to increasing or decreasing both the power of the signs and the power of the planets. However, I do not see how the author can make the familiarity of the planets with the signs partly natural and partly accidental. For since he admits that the stars are naturally reconciled to the signs by house, exaltation, trigon, and terms, what reason can remain by which the stars are accidentally associated with the signs? None. Since this is so, therefore, in the last chapter, which he titled 'Concerning the Accidental Powers of the Planets,' he could justly compare not the stars to the signs, but the temperament of diurnal and nocturnal time. The sex of the signs is also not accidental, but natural. But the twelve stations themselves and the quadrants of the world, which either the daily motion of the heavens or the places of the luminaries, as will appear below, create, can have nothing in common with the signs. Let us therefore hold to the method explained by us above, and to be repeated here again for its great utility, namely, that we must observe the disposition of both the signs and the stars, both cosmically and natally. And so that he may first speak of the signs, certain general qualities are found in them, such as those already explained in the first chapter. These same qualities are then increased, as will now be said, by the association of the planets when their temperaments agree, or they are diluted and weakened when the qualities are contrary. If you add to these the properties of the signs peculiarly inherent in each, and generated from mutual comparison, you will have all the cosmic constitutions of the signs. The remaining power or weakness of the signs, which accrues by reason of the position of the heavens, is called 'according to birth' or 'fortuitous disposition.' For the daily motion of the heavens, with its ineffable swiftness, varies the position of the heavens not only every hour, but every moment of time, and leads the celestial signs to other and other places. From this it follows that the effects and significations of one and the same sign, affected in the same way by its nature, are diverse.

[Saturn's Strength in Regions]
That which says the strength of Saturn is intensified in the northern region does not agree with Ptolemy's statements. For it has been demonstrated above that the north agrees with Jupiter, just as Venus agrees with the south, Saturn is tempered in the east, and Mars in the west.

[Messahella and Dorotheus on Body Shape]
The sayings of Messahella and Dorotheus that follow are to be referred to defining the figure of a native's body, which we mentioned above.

[Planetary Domiciles and Qualities]
It was wisely appointed that each of the two luminaries, according to the quality of its genus, should preside over a sign congruent to its sex. The other planets, namely Mars, Jupiter, Saturn, and Mercury, each reside in two signs, one masculine and one feminine: one is placed in the descending half, the other in the ascending half. But the domiciles of Mercury are closest to the luminaries on both sides, which in turn touch the habitations of Venus, these are surrounded by the seats of Mars, these by Jupiter, and finally by Saturn, a summary of which we have described for you in the following figure. Behold how aptly and wisely ancient wisdom established their domiciles for each planet. For the Sun and Moon, as they approach our zenith most closely, and strike our regions more directly than the other signs, provide hospitality to the two luminaries, which easily surpass the other planets in magnitude, clarity, and effects, and this not indifferently, but a masculine, diurnal, and hot sign receives the Sun, which abounds in the same qualities: while Cancer, being feminine, nocturnal, cold, and humid, fosters the Moon of the same nature. Each domicile also seems to have been conveniently attributed to the luminaries, lest the power and might of the luminaries, which is indeed the chief of all celestial bodies, should be distracted by too many commands and rendered weaker. That the kingdoms of the other wandering planets were assigned with equal prudence can easily be shown.

[Planets and Regions]
As for regions, they first ascribe the first climate to Saturn with his houses and signs. The second to Jupiter with his signs. The third to Mars with his signs. The fourth to the Sun with his sign. The fifth to Venus with her signs, which inclines Italians to luxury and effeminacy. The sixth to Mercury with his signs. The seventh to the Moon with her sign.

[Planetary Detriment and Exaltation]
Nor did they wish Capricorn and Aquarius to be familiar to Saturn without cause. For since Saturn is the greater infortune, coldest and darkest in temperament, and holds the highest and most remote orb, it is most consistent with truth that he should primarily show his dominion and power in signs most remote from the domiciles of the luminaries, malefic and coldest, and opposite to the houses of the luminaries, which aspect is notably evil. Capricorn is opposed to the domicile of the Moon, that is, Cancer: and Aquarius to the domicile of the Sun, that is, Leo. Next in order, Jupiter follows Saturn, who is the greater benefic, and thus Sagittarius and Pisces conveniently host him, both of which are Jovian in nature, since the sign of Pisces is fertile, and Sagittarius moves the winds. Moreover, they behold the houses of the luminaries with the best, that is, a trine aspect. But Jupiter sees Pisces, and Mars follows Jupiter in order as the lesser infortune. Therefore, he is familiar with Aries and Scorpio, which strike the houses of the luminaries with a square, that is, a moderately evil aspect. But Scorpio strikes Leo, and Aries strikes Cancer. For the same reasons, Venus, the lesser benefic, has Taurus and Libra, which strike the houses of the luminaries with a sextile aspect, which is moderately good. Moreover, since the star of Venus is more fertile than all others, it is rightly considered an associate of fertile signs. Taurus is a fertile sign among animals, the sign of Libra is fertile in seeds. The remaining two signs, namely Gemini and Virgo, which do not see the houses of the luminaries, but touch them on both sides, are left, because Mercury never departs more than 27 degrees from the Sun. Hence it is that he never aspects the Sun with any aspect. The maximum elongation of Venus from the Sun is about 48 degrees, and thus she can only be conjoined to the Sun by a sextile aspect, and that a platic one. But it is necessary to observe not only the conciliations of stars and places, but also the places adverse to those with which they are familiarly conjoined. For just as the virtue of stars, if united with a kindred quality of places, becomes far more efficacious: so on the contrary, as Ptolemy says, if they are found in alien places, and contrary to their condition, that is, which are opposed to those signs in which they are hosted or exalted, or in some other way increase their strength, their contrary and dissimilar virtue is greatly weakened and dissipated by the constitution of the signs. Ptolemy's words on this matter are these: "When they are found in places alien and of a contrary sect, much of their proper power is weakened, producing another nature according to the dissimilar mixture of the surrounding signs." Therefore, after it has been briefly indicated which planets gather their domestic strengths in which signs, there are now subjoined the signs in which, as if driven into exile, they are exiled, and suffer no small loss of their strengths. The seventh sign, says the author, from the house of each planet, that is, the opposite sign, is called the detriment of the planet: others call it exile. Ptolemy generally calls it "the alien place and of a contrary sect." For example, the seventh sign from the house of the Sun, that is, from Leo, since it is the sign of Aquarius, therefore the sign of Aquarius is the detriment of the Sun. Thus the Moon in the sign of Capricorn, opposite to the Cancerian dodecatemorion, suffers detriment. Similarly, Mars in Libra and Venus in Aries dissipate their domestic strengths. This detriment will be considerably increased if, moreover, the nature of the sign is contrary to the strengths of the planet. As when Mercury is in Sagittarius, then not only is he exiled from his house, but also weakened by the contrary intemperance of the sign, he contracts his powers for doing good. On the contrary, when Mercury is in Pisces, although he is exiled from his house just as in Sagittarius, yet the kindred nature of the sign assumes the power to harm due to immoderate coldness. For although Pisces is a watery sign, yet because of its familiarity with the Moon, it is cold and humid. In the same way, planets exert their strengths in their exaltations, and contract them in opposite places, as Mars, as we shall say, is exalted in Capricorn, and cast down in the opposite sign of Cancer. And so it must be judged concerning the opposite signs of the other dignities. But in triplicities and terms, since this detriment is not significant, but rather brief, astrologers have therefore neglected the opposites of these places, and only consider the opposites of houses and exaltations.

[Parts of Strength and Stability]
What is to be thought of the parts of strength and stability, and all other strong parts, will be explained below in the fifth Difference. From what we have extensively noted here about Saturn, the diligent reader will easily grasp the descriptions of the other subsequent planets. For the method of explanation is everywhere the same and equal, and once understood in the consideration of one planet, it will not be difficult to adapt it as an example to the descriptions of the others.

[Concordant Signs and Zodiacal Form]
From the foregoing, it is clear that signs are called concordant in transit when they have equal anaphoras in any climate. But since the two signs, which are said to be ruled by one planet, have unequal ascensions, it is quite clear that the author here understands something different by the concordance of signs in the zodiac than by their concordance in transit. Perhaps he calls the two signs hosting one planet concordant in the Zodiac because they recede equally from the domiciles of the luminaries: for Taurus and Libra are the houses of Venus, and they are equally distant from Leo and Cancer, the houses of the Sun and Moon, on both sides. By the same reasoning, you see the domiciles of the other planets equally removed from the houses of the luminaries in the figure described a little earlier. But when he says that such signs are called concordant in the Almantica, that is, in a circle which is wide in the middle and narrow at the ligature, it is a notation of the zodiac, which you will easily understand from the following description of the Zodiac. From the common section of the solstitial colure and the ecliptic, count 6 or 8 parts on each side (for Venus usually departs almost 8 parts from the Sun's limit), and at the ends of these arcs, imagine two lines from the center of the world, which, together with the solstitial colure revolved around the poles of the ecliptic, will describe the form of the Zodiac, which, emerging from the center of the earth at its narrowest part, occupies a sufficiently wide and ample space in the outermost orb. This latitude of the ecliptic itself, that is, the plane in which the Sun perpetually moves, divides it into two equal parts. By ligature, therefore, he understands the junction of the equator with the ecliptic, which, like the Horizon, meridian, or other circle, is understood to be a flat extremity without any latitude. When he says: "It is wide in the middle," he understands the thickness of the zodiac, which, since it is sufficiently wide not only at the extreme but also in the middle of the sky through which the Sun runs, cannot form any certain ligature or intersection with the equator.

[Moon's Phases and Qualities]
The last part of the narration about the Moon, which begins there (And some said that it signifies childhood from the beginning of the month, up to 7 days, etc.), is worthy of annotation, and especially what follows there (And some said that the Moon, indeed, at the conjunction, up to half of its first light, will be of a humid nature, etc.). For the former of these parts expresses the opinion of others; the latter, however, expounds Ptolemy's view on the different configurations of the Moon and other planets to the Sun. Ptolemy's words on this matter are these: "The Moon and the three wandering planets, namely Saturn, Jupiter, and Mars, receive more or less of their inherent powers according to their configurations to the Sun. For the Moon, from its first rising until the increment of the first quadrature, when it shines as if halved, is most effective of humidity; from there until the full moon, of heat; then from the full moon until the diminished half, of dryness; finally, from the second quadrature until its occultation, of coldness. The wandering stars, when morning stars, until the first station, have most humidity; from the first station until they rise at night, they heat more; from the nocturnal shining until the second station, they dry more; finally, from the second station until their setting, they cool more." From these words, it is clear that the Moon and the three superior planets (for Ptolemy excludes Venus and Mercury from this comparison, because they never come to opposition with the Sun) have four states with respect to the Sun. The first state begins from their rising, when they cast off the Sun's rays, and extends to the first quadrature. From here, the second state lasts until the opposition, from which more certainly...

[Planetary Joys and Dignities]
The last sentence of the clause is that, besides the Sun and Moon, each planet has two houses, and of these, one is more familiar to it, and is called its joy. That such and so great a dignity is principal and primary, there is no doubt. But for Ptolemy, on the contrary, joy is the planet's dignity, the last of all others. For even if a star were in places...

[Planetary States and Qualities]
The third state is derived to the second quadrature. Finally, the last state ends in occultation. Hence it is clear that stars moving with the Sun and oppressed by its rays, as if placed outside all states, obtain no powers. If, therefore, we wish to join to this comparison that space also, in which the stars are held enveloped by the Sun's rays from occultation until the first shining, we will find that there are five states of them with respect to the Sun, one of which, namely the last, is that in which they can do absolutely nothing, and therefore it is rightly omitted by Ptolemy. The difference of the four states is also most worthy of consideration. In the first state, indeed, the stars are illustrious. For having recently emerged from the Sun's rays, they become conspicuous to all, and obtain a pure and sincere substance in their plates, just as recently born bodies consist of a more humid and purer substance than those of adults. Therefore, then representing infancy and childhood, consistent with their nature, they will produce substances and impressions more humid than hot, and especially the Moon. For when, as Ptolemy teaches, it increases humors due to its proximity, it will certainly moisten most, when it has been moved closest to the earth in the quadratures. It will also have moderate heat, because in such a state it draws moderate light from the Sun: and so, due to the superabundance of humor easily suppressing heat, it will putrefy most. For this reason, I think the first quarter is called childish by some. For even in the bodies of these, we see that the substance of pure humidity easily overcomes heat. Stars that have attained the second state are hotter, both because of the absorption of humidity and because of the increase in heat. For since the stars primarily heat by the light drawn from the Sun, they will heat most when they shine with increasing light. The age that follows childhood also heats more vehemently, and the abundance of childish humors diminishes more and more. In the third state, dryness dominates. But someone may say, how will the three superior planets, shining with full and ample light in opposition to the Sun, and moreover traversing the perigee of the small circle, becoming closer to the earth, dry out, since it has been said that stars draw vapors and moisten more, the closer they are to the earth? The Moon also, moving from the opposition of the Sun to the second quadrature, and at the same time hastening from the apogee of the eccentric to its weirdo? We answer that stars placed in opposition to the Sun become drier according to quality, not according to substance. Who doubts that bodies declining in vigor, and especially obese ones, have a greater abundance or quantity of humidity than they had before in childhood, and yet they are said to be dry, not indeed according to quantity, which is greatest, but according to quality. For in the former age, humid and concocted parts dominate dryness and crudity: in the latter, however, dry and crude parts easily suppress innate humidity. Thus the substance or quantity of light of the three superior planets, placed in opposition to the Sun, is most ample and illustrious, but dry according to quality. Similarly, the Moon is to be judged. But if it is dry, why then, and especially in the quadrature following the fullness, does it moisten most, and by moistening, does it putrefy most, all of which are confirmed by experience, that at no time does the plague rage more than in the last quarter, and at no time do the tides of the ocean usually overflow more? We answer that the body of the Moon is by itself more humid than all the others, and because of its very swift circuits, it does not age so much, and for the most part perpetually preserves its nature of humidity. Therefore, in the second quadrature, approaching the earth most closely, it will moisten most, and because of the decrease of light and heat, it will putrefy most, and it will carry away the ocean with great impetus, because then it agrees most with the element of waters in both qualities, namely humidity and coldness. Finally, in the fourth state, there is no doubt that the stars are cold: for then they are removed from the earth, and approaching the Sun more closely, they turn less light towards us. And they said 19. Towards the end of this narration, he adds the opinions of some about the sympathy of the planets with the qualities of the soul. Ptolemy teaches that two planets, namely Mercury and the Moon, principally agree with the qualities of the soul, and indeed to Mercury he attributes the intellectual and rational part of the soul: but to the Moon he attributes the duller and moral-governing part of the soul. Indeed, the intellect has imagination, ratiocination, memory, and the other qualities of the soul that pertain to cultivating genius and illuminating the mind, with all of which Mercury principally agrees and sympathizes, it is clear why, when Mercury is afflicted, genius itself and the mind itself are disturbed by dull darkness. But the duller part of the soul is nothing other than the obedience of the lower powers and will to the judgment of the intellect. It therefore has justice, fortitude, temperance, and the other moral virtues that are contained under these categories: with all of which the Moon agrees, it is clear why, when the Moon is afflicted, men arise who deviate from common custom and the practice of life. But why did they want the Moon to preside over the lower powers, and Mercury over the higher part of the soul? Because, as was said above, the Moon presides over the brain, from which the nerves and organs of sensation are derived, and therefore it not undeservedly governs those virtues that depend on the senses and are closer to them. Mercury, however, since it has the spirits of the brain, which are the organs of the mind's actions, therefore dominates more than all those things that proceed from the mind. In addition to these, every other planet is also the source of peculiar qualities of the soul. Saturn, therefore, is the source of that power which is called retentive, from which it generates a firm memory, perseverance, and tolerance of labors. Jupiter has probity and simplicity. Mars has fortitude, promptness, audacity, anger, and cruelty. Ambition and pride are familiar to the Sun. Venus loves luxury and elegance. Just as individual planets principally agree with certain members: so also these qualities of the soul originate from different parts of the body, and are never brought into action unless they are first stimulated and aroused by the nerves and spirits of the brain. Therefore, Ptolemy wisely considered not only the places, but also the rulers of these places, since they themselves arouse their qualities in the soul. 20. The last clause, which begins, there (And because we said in this chapter, etc.) I can hardly guess what it meant to signify. And I do not know whether this obscurity should be attributed to the interpreter or rather to the writer. I think, however, that the meaning is that from the mutual mixture of the stars, many other temperaments arise, so that the exposition of the words is: Just as the different positions of the planets to the Sun vary their powers, as was said a little earlier: so in the same way, mutual inter se comKk

[Exaltation and Debilitation of Planets]
Just as the Sun is exalted in the 19th degree of Aries, so it falls in the 19th degree of Libra, and so on for the others. Ptolemy, however, places the entire sign of Aries as the exaltation of the Sun, and the entire sign of Taurus as the exaltation of the Moon, and similarly for the rest. These are the 'hypsomata', the exaltation or altitude of a planet among astrologers, not understood as a local position or distance from the earth, or from another point, etc., by which imagination Pliny was deceived (Book 2, Chapter 16), but rather as that part of the zodiacal sign which, when the stars have reached it, they show the greatest change in the air and in other inferior things. For example, when the Sun first enters the 19th part of Aries, it immediately shows us manifest spring. The reason for these altitudes is this: The Sun is exalted in Aries because, by ascending through Aries into the northern semicircle, it causes evident increases in both days and heat in bodies. For the opposite reason, it lies in its debility, since, as we said before, as much virtue and energy as the kindred quality of the signs adds to the stars, so much of their own strength is taken away by the dissimilar constitution of contrary or opposite places. Since each of the planets is exalted in that place where another of a different nature and efficiency suffers the contrary, and the Sun is the author of clarity and heat, while Saturn is the author of darkness and cold: therefore, just as with houses, so the places of Saturn's altitude are deservedly opposed to the Sun. Therefore, Saturn's altitude is in Libra, and its debility is in Aries. For where heat is intensified, there cold must be remitted; and conversely, where heat diminishes, cold increases. When the luminaries conjoin in Aries, then the Moon first appears, and with increased light, it shines forth in Taurus, the principal sign of its triplicity; therefore, Taurus is said to be its altitude, and Scorpio its debility. But Jupiter is exalted in Cancer, because it is the mover of the northern and more fertile winds. Therefore, being carried closest to the north, it shows its greatest strength in Cancer and Pisces, and is debilitated in Capricorn. And Mars, by its nature fiery, is exalted in Capricorn, opposite the sign of Jupiter's altitude, and by reason of Cancer being most remote from our zenith, and thereby tempering Mars' ardor. For the power of Mars, the more moderate it is, the greater it becomes. For the opposite reason, it is debilitated in Cancer. Moreover, since the powers of Jupiter and Mars in action are plainly dissimilar: for one, for example, is the author of justice and humanity, while the other is the author of injustice and ferocity, for this reason they have rightly obtained adverse places of altitude. But Venus, the mistress of humidity and pleasures, shows her greatest strength in Pisces, and is debilitated in Virgo. Furthermore, Mercury, which is rather dry, the giver of intellect, learning, and other mental goods, and altogether of contrary efficiency, has thus obtained an altitude and debility contrary to Venus: an altitude indeed in Virgo, where autumnal dryness impends; and a debility in Pisces. Haly says that Mercury is exalted in Virgo because when the Sun is in Leo, Mercury begins to appear in Virgo in the evening, which is taken from the similarity of the Moon. This is the reason for exaltations and debilities. But nevertheless, these and many other things in astrology are based more on experience than on demonstrations. A doubt follows, whether planets are exalted universally throughout entire signs, or whether exaltations are to be observed in certain parts. Hermes leaves the signs enumerated here to the altitudes of the planets, but he establishes that each planet is most exalted in the 15th part of its sign, which certainly does not seem to lack cause. For it is consistent with reason that the power of a planet is greater if it exists in the middle of a sign congruent with its nature, than if it is at its beginning or end. I see younger astrologers following Ptolemy, who teaches that planets are exalted in whole signs, conceding, however, that they are most exalted in the parts described here by the author. Regarding Triplicities: We distinguish triplicities as follows: All three signs that appear to agree in one nature form a triplicity, and are called by the same name, that is, Triplicity. Therefore, Aries, Leo, and Sagittarius form the first triplicity, because each of these signs is fiery, masculine, diurnal, namely hot and dry, choleric, and bitter in taste. This triplicity is also oriental. Its lords are the Sun by day, and Jupiter by night, and Saturn is a participant by day and night. The second triplicity consists of Taurus, Virgo, and Capricorn, because these are feminine, nocturnal, earthy signs, namely cold and dry, melancholic, and acrid in taste. This triplicity is meridional. The lords of this triplicity are Venus by day, and the Moon by night, of which Mars is a participant by day and night. The third triplicity consists of Gemini, Libra, and Aquarius, because these signs are masculine, diurnal, sanguine, namely hot, humid, airy, and sweet in taste. This triplicity is occidental. The lords of this triplicity are Saturn by day, and Mercury by night, of which Jupiter is a participant by day and night. The fourth triplicity consists of Cancer, Scorpio, and Pisces, because these are feminine, nocturnal, northern, watery, phlegmatic, cold, humid, and salty in taste. The lords of this triplicity are Venus by day, and Mars by night, of which the Moon is a participant by day and night. Table of Houses, Exaltations, Joys, Triplicities, Detriments, and Falls of the Planets. Signs, Houses of the Planets, Exaltations, Joys, Triplicities, Detriments, Falls. Aries 19, Taurus 3, Gemini 21, Cancer 3, Leo 15, Virgo 27, Libra 19, Scorpio 3, Sagittarius 21, Capricorn 28, Aquarius 15, Pisces 27. The connection of three signs, which behold each other by trine aspect and have a similar nature, is commonly called a triplicity; the Greeks call it 'trigonon', Pontanus 'triangularity'. But someone might say, if this is true, what we asserted a little earlier, namely that the general constitutions of the signs agree with the temperament and qualities of the four seasons of the year, and that always three signs of each quadrant, following each other from one of the cardinal points, as far as their crassness is concerned, agree, it is in no way possible for signs placed in different quadrants, such as are proposed in trines, to have the same nature and similar power of action. I respond that the signs of one trine are not truly of the same nature in themselves, as far as the temperament attributed to the signs above is concerned, but they are nearly so, and as the author rightly says, whether he said it by chance or knowingly, they appear to be of the same nature, both because of their gender and similarity of figure, and the trine ray, they come together in a certain familiarity and society, and truly and most importantly, because they obey the same lords, whom he calls 'trigonokrators', that is, Triangulators, and are governed and ruled by them. For just as a master can accustom or compel three servants, one German, for example, another French, and a third Spanish, whose not only nationality, but also temperaments and customs are diverse in themselves, to agreement and friendship, or at least to a uniform and consistent handling of all affairs, so also three signs forming one trine, because of the obedience they equally render simultaneously to the same stars of the same sect, are compelled to one nature and the same power of action, even if they differ in their inherent nature and customs. There are four trines, one of which the Arabs call fiery, another earthy, a third airy, and a fourth watery. The fiery trine is formed by Aries, Leo, and Sagittarius. For these signs, besides beholding each other by trine radiation, agree in two ways. First, by reason of similarity of form, for all are wild, quadrupedal, and fierce; then by nature, for all wish to be of the fiery nature, that is, hot and dry, masculine, diurnal, increasing choler or red bile, and bitter in taste. Hence, if at the time of birth or at the time of a question, one of these luminaries illuminates the ascendant, they say it mitigates the cold of winter, but causes immoderate heat in summer, especially if the lords or triangulators of the ascendant sign are present, or have occupied the trine elsewhere, or finally have strongly struck the ascendant from some angle of the figure. But in nativities, such a position inclines the native to fire and choler.

[Eastern Triplicity and Ptolemy's View]
The Arabs say that this first triplicity is Eastern. But Ptolemy is more to be believed, whose precepts are proven by arguments drawn from nature itself and by experience. Ptolemy teaches that this trigon is northern because of Sagittarius, the house of Jupiter, participating in some way with the west because of Aries, the house of Mars. So that the reason for this distribution may be clear, it should be known that Ptolemy attributes to each planet its own suitable region of the world: for example, to Saturn the east, which part, being diurnal and hot, reduces Saturn's cold to a certain temperateness. For a similar reason, he assigns Mars, the driest planet, to the humid and nocturnal western region. The north is assigned to Jupiter, because fertile winds usually break forth from this part. The star of Venus is familiar to the southern region because of its humidity. From this distribution, the luminaries are first excepted, because they are the general causes of winds; then Mercury, because he is perpetually present with and serves the luminaries. And since, according to Ptolemy's opinion, two planets preside over each trigon, as will soon be clear, it was entirely fitting to attribute not one, as the Arabs do, but exactly as many regions of the world to each trigon, from which the assigned rulers would be understood to excite their winds. As in the first trigon, Jupiter and Mars rule. Therefore, those parts from which Jupiter and Mars draw winds must be attributed to the first trigon. But since the Sun is, as we have said, the general mover of all winds, and cannot be attributed particularly to any one wind, Mars, being very similar to the Sun in nature and having a house in this trigon, is substituted in the Sun's place. Hence, a wind mixed from the boreal and occidental is collected, which Ptolemy calls Borrholibycus. For Jupiter brings northern winds, and Mars western winds, because this region is feminine, and Mars himself is of the sect of the Moon, which rules the west, just as the Sun rules the East. Ptolemy's words are these: "This trigon is primarily northern, because Jupiter exercises dominion there. For he is the author of blasts, and fertile, like the Aquilonian winds. But because of the house of Mars, Aphricus is mixed with it, and this trigon becomes Borrholibycus, that is, mixed from the north and Aphricus, because Mars excites Aphricus winds due to his familiarity, and because the western part is feminine." Ptolemy and the Arabs also disagree in the number and order of the triplicities. Ptolemy, since the seven planets were to be distributed conveniently among the four trigons, therefore assigned two planets to the first three trigons, and at least one to the fourth, as will be clear. The Arabs, however, make two principal lords for each trigon, one diurnal and the other nocturnal; and besides these, they add a third, participating in such dominion at both times. As in the first trigon, the Sun and Jupiter principally rule, to whom Saturn is added as a third participant in the dominion. But in nativities and the beginnings of other things, the diurnal lord is to be considered first in diurnal nativities, and the nocturnal lord in nocturnal nativities; then the remaining and participating lord. Therefore, if it is a diurnal nativity, and Leo is placed in the ascendant, for the nourishment of the native, according to the opinion of the Arabs, the lords of the Leonine triplicity should be considered, and in the first place the Sun, because it is a diurnal nativity, and afterwards Jupiter with Saturn as participant. Ptolemy, however, does not disagree with the Arabs in the ordering of the lords of the first triplicity, except that he postponed Saturn as a participant in both, and not without reason. For since Saturn is in detriment in Aries, it is not fitting for him to rule in the same trigon. Therefore, if three lords are absolutely required for each triplicity, the opinion of those who, rejecting Saturn, assume Mars as a participant seems more correct, which Ptolemy, however, rightly removes from the dominion of this diurnal triplicity because he is a nocturnal planet. For the dominion of a triplicity is nothing other than a certain power arising from the dignity of the house, exaltation, and sect, which the barbarians call Hayz. That is, in choosing the lords of the trigons, three things must be observed. First, that in diurnal trigons there are diurnal planets, and nocturnal planets in nocturnal trigons. Second, that masculine planets are referred to masculine trigons, and feminine planets to feminine trigons. Third, that since in every trigon there is one movable, one fixed, and finally one common sign, the ruler of the fixed sign is preferred to the others. Whatever planet has the greater testimony of suitability in these three is admitted to the office of triplicity ruler. Thus, because of the first two reasons, Mars is expelled from the first trigon, but because of the last reason, the Sun, the ruler of Leo, is preferred to Jupiter, etc. The earthy trigon is formed by Taurus, Virgo, and Capricorn, which signs, besides their gender and triplicity coherence, similarly agree in form, which is that of gentle and domesticated animals, except that to Virgo, because of Mercury's great power in that sign, human form is attributed, and then nature: for they are generally said to be cold and dry, melancholic, feminine, nocturnal, and acrid in taste. They also say that in the conjunction or opposition of the luminaries, if any of these, and especially Capricorn, illuminates the horoscope, the rigor of winter is greatly increased, especially if Saturn is found in that trigon.

[Southern Triplicity and Venus's Dominion]
This trigon is also southern according to Ptolemy's opinion, because of Venus's dominion in it, whose humidity and heat agree with the temperament of the southern region, participating in the east because of Capricorn, the house of Saturn, who, being of the Sun's sect, is judged to be the mover of eastern winds. The Moon, however, since she is a general producer of winds like the Sun, and cannot be attributed particularly to any one wind, is excluded from this particular administration, and Saturn is substituted for her because of his house, which he possesses in this trigon. We have already warned above that Mercury is also excluded everywhere with the luminaries for a certain reason. Therefore, these signs, because of Venus and Saturn, agreeing with the south and east, move winds mixed from the southern and eastern regions, which Ptolemy therefore calls Notapelioticus. Ptolemy's words are these: "This trigon is primarily southern, because of the dominion of Venus, whose star excites the winds of that part, because of the efficiency of heat and humidity. But when it also admits the subsolanus because of Saturn's dwelling in Capricorn, this trigon is constituted Notapelioticus, that is, mixed from the south and subsolanus, so that it is opposite to the first. For Saturn excites such winds, that is, eastern, since he is familiar with the east because of his solar condition." Ptolemy likewise assigns diurnal dominion here to Venus, nocturnal to the Moon, but neglects Mars as a participant.

[The Airy Trigon]
The third difference: The airy trigon consists of Gemini, Libra, and Aquarius. Their form is human and vocal. Their nature, in general, is hot and humid, sanguine, masculine, diurnal, and sweet in taste. If at that time, or if any of the luminaries ascend the horoscope from these signs, it is said to announce winds in that lunation. This trigon, according to Ptolemy, is oriental due to Saturn's maximum dominion in it, participating with the north because of Jupiter, who, as far as sect is concerned, agrees with Saturn. For it is diurnal and masculine. Therefore, the winds that arise from this triangularity will be between the north and the east, which they call borrascliolicus. Ptolemy's words on this matter are: "This trigon primarily agrees with the subsolanus because of Saturn. But by reason of mixture, it becomes borrapelioticus, that is, mixed from the north and subsolanus, because by name of condition it is reconciled to Saturn." The order of the trigon lords is the same in Ptolemy, except that he, as in all other trigons, neglects the third participant.

[The Watery Trigon]
The watery trigon connects the signs Cancer, Scorpio, and Pisces, whose form is that of aquatic and mute animals. For although Scorpio does not live in water, it lives in humid places. As for their nature, all are generally said to be cold and humid, phlegmatic, feminine, nocturnal, and salty in taste. Therefore, if any of these signs occupies the horoscope at the synod or diameter of the luminaries, they assert that rain is portended in that lunation, and indeed on that day when the Moon touches the horoscopant part, before the opposition if it was a conjunction figure, but before the conjunction whenever you judge from the opposition figure. Abraham Avenezra considers this to be most true, and if the horoscopant sign was one of the cardinal points of that earth figure. He calls the earth figure the theme of the world's revolution, which varies differently according to places. The same Abraham wrote: "If at the hour when the luminaries immediately precede the ingress into any of the aquatic signs, the horoscope is cast, and the luminaries themselves are in the angles, there will be excessive rain and heavy showers in that year."

[Watery Trigon Winds]
Furthermore, this trigon, according to Ptolemy, is western due to the dominion of Mars and the Moon, participating with the south because of Venus. Therefore, it also stirs up such winds, namely those collected from the western and southern regions, which they call notolybicus, that is, Austriaphricus. And Ptolemy says: "This triangle is primarily Aphricus because of the dominion of Mars and the Moon. But because of Venus, it becomes Notolybicum by mixture, that is, Austriaphricus." The Arabs want Mars and Venus to be the principal lords of this triangle, assigning Mars to the day and Venus to the night, with the Moon participating with both. In this, they differ from Ptolemy not in the number or diversity of stars, but only in their arrangement. Ptolemy established Mars alone as the principal triangulator for both day and night, and Venus and the Moon as participants, Venus by day and the Moon by night. His words are: "The fourth triangle, Cancer, Scorpio, and Pisces, is left to Mars, who alone remains and claims his right because of his domicile. However, Venus and the Moon are joined to him as co-administrators, both because of their condition and because of the feminine signs, the Moon by night and Venus by day, etc."

[Trigon Lords Table]

Cancer, Scorpio, Pisces: Mars, Venus, Moon. Arab Triangulators: Day, Night, Participant. Aries, Leo, Sagittarius: Sun, Jupiter, Saturn. Taurus, Virgo, Capricorn: Venus, Moon, Mars.
