[Movable, Fixed, and Common Signs]
Four of these signs are also called movable, namely Aries, Cancer, Libra, and Capricorn. And four are fixed, namely Taurus, Leo, Scorpio, and Aquarius. The remaining four, namely Gemini, Virgo, Sagittarius, and Pisces, are common. They are called movable, fixed, or common because when the Sun enters one of them, time moves, that is, it changes; or it is fixed, that is, it perseveres in the same state; or it becomes common, that is, half of that one time will be like half of the other. For example: When the Sun enters the sign of Aries, time changes, that is, winter turns into spring. And when it enters Taurus, the same spring season is fixed. But when the Sun enters Gemini, the time becomes common, that is, half spring and half summer, and so on for the others. The present division and difference of the dodecatemoria is derived from the general constitution of the signs, which we explained in the first chapter above. For just as the four states of the seasons are not perpetually similar to each other, but each is distinguished by the increase, consistency, and decline of temperatures, so also the signs of each quarter, creating such a variety of time, have received their names from mobility, stability, and mediocrity. For example: Just as the entire spring season is distinguished into new, adult, and declining spring, so also the entire vernal quarter is divided into three signs of different appellations, the first of which is movable, the second fixed, and the third common. Similarly, one must judge concerning the remaining three quarters. For universally, the first four signs of the four seasons of the year are called movable: so called because the seasons of the year are changed by them. As when the Sun enters Aries, winter changes into new spring; Cancer, spring into summer; Libra, summer into autumn; Capricorn, autumn into winter. Tropical signs, according to Ptolemy, are subdivided so that some are equinoctial, namely Aries and Libra, which received their name from the thing itself, since at their beginning the Sun, being constituted, causes the equinox throughout the whole earth; others are tropical, namely Cancer and Capricorn, to which likewise the name is given from the thing itself. For the Sun, being constituted in them, turns away from us in Cancer, but towards us in Capricorn, and besides these, causes the vicissitude of days and nights. For when the Sun enters Cancer, the night, which was previously diminishing, now begins to increase; conversely, when the Sun reaches Capricorn, the days, which were previously continuously decreasing, now gradually begin to increase. Hence, from the remaining eight places of the signs, Ptolemy calls four sterea, that is, firm or fixed, and four disoma, that is, bicorporeal. Fixed are those that follow the tropical and equinoctial signs, namely Taurus, Leo, Scorpio, and Aquarius, so called because in these the state of time is made firm and more vehement, not indeed because in Taurus the time is by its nature more temperate, or in Leo more ardent, but because human bodies, having already experienced that quality in a movable sign, feel the same as if unchanged and simpler due to the very delay in the fixed sign. Bicorporeal are those that are numbered after the fixed signs, namely Gemini, Virgo, Sagittarius, and Pisces, so called because they contain two bodies. For Gemini has two infants, Pisces as many animals, Sagittarius is composed of the body of a horse and a man. But the sign of Virgo, because it adheres to Libra, is called disomous. But the reason for this etymology is born from the accommodation of celestial images to the dodecatemoria. These same are called common because, being placed between the firm and movable signs, they share common natures with them.

[Differences in Signs]
They have differences, both those that precede and those that follow. Hence it is clear that the signs of each quarter are distinguished by such a reason and manner, just as the general constitution of each season of the year varies. If, however, all things are to be referred more curiously to the order which we proposed above from Ptolemy, it must be said that in this chapter certain simple properties of the signs are enumerated, peculiar to each dodecatemory by itself, or as Ptolemy says, the powers of the dodecatemories themselves, pure and unmixed. There are those who think that this division is taken from the mobility, stability, and moderate duration of things, which are begun by the ascendants themselves, or are signified by the planets placed therein. Hence those who have given precepts concerning elections, think that all things generally, which we wish to be fragile and weak, should be begun when the Moon is passing through some movable sign: but in more stable things and those that will endure longer, attention should be paid to the fixed signs: finally, in things that will last fewer years, indifferent and common signs are to be observed. But we do not care about such things. CONCERNING THE ASPECTS OF THE PLANETS. Signs are also said to aspect each other, that is: Every sign aspects the third before it, and the third after it, which is the eleventh. And this aspect is called sextile, and it is an aspect of affection and moderate friendship. And it is also called sextile because it holds the sixth part of the circle, that is, 60 degrees. For example: A planet that is at the beginning of Aries aspects the one that is in Gemini before it, and the one that is in Aquarius after it, which is an aspect of friendship. And it aspects the fourth before it and the fourth after it, that is, the tenth. And this aspect is called square, because it holds the fourth part of the sky, that is, 90 degrees. And it is an aspect of discord and moderate enmity.

[Schema of Aspects]
DIFFERENCE SCHEMA OF ASPECTS OR CONFIGURATIONS. A *오오 日 B an ६ This entire chapter is consumed with the treatment of the properties of the dodecatemories, which arise from their collation with each other, that is, from the mutual aspects of the signs. Aspects are intervals computed in the Zodiac, from which the stars communicate the greatest power. And although any star diffuses innumerable rays orbicularly: nevertheless, experience has shown that five of these, which are constituted by whole signs and whole parts of the entire circle, surpass the remaining multitude in efficacy. First, the sextile or hexagonal aspect is that which contains the sixth part of the Zodiac, that is, two signs or 60 degrees: as if the beginning of Cancer is A, from which 60 degrees are taken, contained between A and C. And if the line AC is drawn, it will be a sextile ray, which is noted!

[Aspects to Angles and Stars]
The angles of the houses of the sky, or to other wandering stars, are compared. If the comparison is made to the angles of the sky, then the dexter (right) aspects, which are carried against the order of the signs, are stronger than the sinister (left) aspects. For the stars are carried to the centers of the sky not by their own motion, but by the motion of the firmament. On the contrary, if the comparison is made to other stars, then the sinister aspects are to be preferred to the dexter, because the stars approach other stars not by an alien motion, but by their own motion. Ptolemy insinuated this distinction of rays to us in the treatise on life, and in book 4, chapter on dignity and honors. 6 Finally, by another division of aspects, one is called 'Platicus' when it is only from the signs, and the signs themselves aspect each other, etc. The other is 'Patilis', when from star to star as many parts of the sky are counted as constitute the configuration itself: that is, when the rays of the star are projected into similar parts of the aspecting sign. For example, if the Sun is in Aries 15°00', its square sinister ray will be in Cancer 15°, and dexter in Capricorn 15°. And its trine dexter in Sagittarius 15°, sinister in Leo 15°. Finally, the opposition in Libra 15°. This is to be understood of the minute parts of the sky: so that if the Sun were in Aries 9°47', all the said rays would fall into the 9°47' of the said signs. There are also terms or bounds of the planets in the signs: Because in each sign five planets have terms, arranged through different degrees. For from the beginning of Aries up to the sixth degree of the same Aries, is the term of Jupiter: and from the sixth up to the twelfth, the term of Venus: and from the twelfth up to the twentieth, the term of Mercury: and from the twentieth to the twenty-fifth, the term of Mars: and then to the end of the sign, that is, from the twenty-fifth to the thirtieth, the term of Saturn. [Nature and Distribution of Planetary Terms]
Terms or bounds, for it matters not by what name you call them, are peculiar parts of the sky, which in each sign are distributed for certain reasons among the five wandering stars, namely Saturn, Jupiter, Mars, Venus, and Mercury. For the two luminaries lack bounds, and in their place, they occupy the two halves of the zodiac, as has been said above. The Greeks call them τὰ ὅρια, and the lord of the bounds ὁριοκράτορα or τήν ὁρίων ἀρχήν. Since the degrees of the bounds are unequal in number and distributed to the planets in a somewhat unstable order, so that it is difficult to comprehend them by memory and very difficult to retain them: therefore, it seemed more useful and convenient to the author to present the planetary bounds depicted concisely in a table, rather than explained in a long discourse. However, their rationale seems to be as follows. The beginning of each sign is universally seized by that planet which has surpassed the others in the number of dignities in the same sign. But if this does not happen, and several planets have individual powers in the sign: then the triplicity ruler is always preferred to the lord of exaltation, and here to the lord of the house. However, this preeminence of dignities does not advance the malefics. For these are preferred only in the chariots of the Sun, and if they are deprived of this, they are not only expelled from the beginnings but also from the middle places of the signs and driven to the extremes. This is so true that Mercury in Aquarius holds the first parts solely by reason of the trine, while Saturn, having both the trine and domicile there, is not only repelled from primacy but also pushed to the extreme parts. And Mars occupied the beginning of Cancer for no other reason than that he is the sole lord of its trine; but of Scorpio, because of the trine and domicile. In Libra, however, Saturn was preferred to Mercury due to excessive power, having obtained exaltation there in addition to the trine. The beginnings of all other signs, as well as the intermediate places and those succeeding the beginnings, are free from the dominion of the malefics and are subject to the other planets by reason of the prerogatives already mentioned. For example, in the sign of Pisces, since Mars could not hold primacy solely by reason of the trine, Venus presides over the first parts due to exaltation; Jupiter, the lord of Pisces, follows her closely; then Mercury, who is completely peregrine. The malefics hold the extreme parts, and Mars, being the lord of the triplicity, precedes Saturn. In the sign of Aries, Jupiter occupies the first place due to the trine. Here, although neither Mercury nor Venus rule for any reason, yet due to power and beneficence, Venus is preferred to Mercury. Mars, the lord of Aries, has the penultimate place, with Saturn again pushed to the extremes. In Taurus, since Venus has the trine, she rightly presides over its first parts. Again, Jupiter and Mercury, although they do not rule here in any way: yet Mercury is preferred to Jupiter, because he has exaltation and domicile in the sign of Virgo, which is bound to Taurus by triplicity affinity, while Jupiter is completely alien to this trine. The malefics administer the extreme parts, with Saturn preceding Mars due to Capricorn. And so it must be judged for the rest. But why is Mercury preferred to Venus in Capricorn, when she exercises the right of the triplicity there, while he seems almost peregrine? Because, as we have already said, Mercury, due to Virgo, has almost greater power in that trine than Venus herself, to whom at least Taurus offers hospitality in addition to the trine; or because the ancients frequently experienced some significant power of Mercury at the beginning of Capricorn. It is also possible that Venus was substituted for Mercury, and Mercury for Venus, by scribes due to the similarity of the figures. Again, it can be asked, why does the right of the triplicity precede all other powers in the distribution of terms? To this hesitation, we must respond that the distribution of terms is almost like the division of judicial and other public offices in a polity. Therefore, just as in a well-constituted city, by the harmonious consent of the senate, suitable persons are placed in charge of the reins of government: so here too, the planets, exercising power in the signs of triplicity, which signifies consent, harmoniously divide among themselves the more important terms or bounds of dominion. Although the triplicity rulers are preferred to the other lords in this matter: nevertheless, they yield to those who have a throne in the signs, that is, more than one jurisdiction, and on that account, like tyrants, always occupy the best part by a certain violence. This is the reason for the order. However, we omit here the discussion of why the dominions of the terms are contained in an unequal number of parts, because it is more perplexing and involved in many ambiguities; and because I believe that the ancient Egyptians established

[Triplicities vs. Terms]
Some, however, prefer triplicities to terms, because the lords of triplicities are stronger in nourishment: because they signify nourishment, and because there is no discord in triplicity, as there is in terms when they are preferred. And some prefer terms to triplicities, because the lords of terms are stronger in direction. There is hesitation as to whether the power of trigons or bounds is greater. We respond by distinction: If a general comparison of these dignities is made, universally trigons precede terms. But if we compare them to certain matters, we will find other cases in which trigons are to be preferred to terms, and others in which they are to be postponed. As in the matter of nourishment, triangles are preferred to terms, and these in turn are preferred to triangles in the ordering of general chronocrators. He himself says the same thing, but in a more confused order, as it appears. Some, he says, prefer the right of triangularity to terms in two ways. First, and specifically with respect to certain matters, such as in the question of nourishment. For when the question, whether it is granted from heaven for the native to grow or not to grow, is proposed for explanation, then greater force must be attributed to triangles than to bounds. Indeed, the lords of triangles, as the Arabs please, signify nourishment. Second, and generally, as concerning a more evident, and therefore more certain cause, there is greater consensus among astrologers regarding the order of triangulators than regarding the order of chronocrators, since, as was seen in the preceding chapter, there is great contention as to which of the planets should precede in the power of the bounds in the signs. Again, he says that some specifically prefer bounds to triangles, because when we order the arbiters of times through progressions and transits of aphetic places, the efficacy of the stars holding the bounds is found to be greater than that of the triangulators. What directions and other progressions are, and how they are accomplished, we will explain below in a more opportune place.

[Decans and Planetary Strengths]
The faces of the signs are called Decans. There are three Decans for each sign, each of which exercises authority over ten parts of the 30 parts of any sign. The order of the Decans begins with Aries and Mars, and continues in order to the end of the zodiac according to the natural order of both signs and planets. For example, the first Decan is [Mars], the second [Sun], the third [Venus]. When this order is followed, the first Decan of Taurus is [Venus], the second [Mercury], the third [Moon]. Following this order, [Saturn] is admitted as the first Decan in the sign of Gemini, and similarly the following [planets] are placed over the following Decans. The method by which, given any degree of a sign, one can determine by calculation, without the aid of a table, which Decan the given degree obeys, is as follows: Count the number of signs from the beginning of Aries up to the beginning of the proposed sign. Then multiply the number of complete signs by three. Finally, to the number that arises from the triplication of the whole dodecatemoria, add the complete Decans of the proposed sign, along with the incomplete Decan whose lord is sought. This done, divide the sum by 7. And from what remains after the division, the first unit is assigned to Mars, the second, if present, to the Sun, and so on according to the natural order of the planets, until the progression reaches the last unit, that is, the lord of the incomplete Decan in question. For example, if it is proposed to investigate which Decan rules the 15th part of a sign, 6 must be multiplied by 3 (for from the beginning to the beginning, six complete signs are counted), which makes 18. To this, add 2 for one complete Decan and one incomplete Decan, making 20 Decans, which, divided by 7, leaves 6. Now, if the first unit of the remaining number is assigned to Mars, the second to the Sun, the third to Venus, the fourth to Mercury, the fifth to the Moon, the sixth incomplete Decan will finally reach Saturn, which the table also shows. Here are assigned certain co-workers or benefactors, who are three deities applied to each Decan. Also, certain images that arise with each Decan, called by the Greeks 'planatellonta', of which Ptolemy makes no mention, and, as I believe, he counts all of this chapter among the 'irrational arrangements and unreasoned causes'. Therefore, leaving these aside, let us proceed to those things that are not prepared for the sake of vain ostentation, but are confirmed by physical causes. The first chapter is about the virtues of the planets. And because now, with God's approval, we have discussed the powers of the planets in the signs, which are house, exaltation, triplicity, term, and face: Now therefore let us discuss their virtues or strengths in these themselves. For the lord of the house has five strengths, and the lord of the exaltation four, the lord of the triplicity three, the lord of the term two, and the lord of the face one. Understand therefore from this number the strengths of the planets. For those who abound more in number, abound more in strength. And some prefer the term to the triplicity, that is, they want the lord of the term to be stronger than the lord of the triplicity. But we have already explained above, in what each is valid. Some also gave such a comparison concerning this matter, saying that when a planet is in its house, it is like a man in his house and in his dominion. And when it is in its exaltation, it is like a man in his kingdom and glory. And when it is in its term, it is like a man among his parents and relatives and people. And when it is in its triplicity, it will be like a man in his honor, and among his helpers and ministers. And when it is in its face, it will be like a man in his mastery. These are the universal essential powers of the planets in the signs, and the figures of the signs follow these. And because the author has now established the universal familiarities of the stars and places, which they call the essential powers or dignities of the planets in the signs, as five in number, namely house, exaltation, trigon, term, and face. Since we have explained what each of these is, it is next to be said how great the force and power of each is, that is, how much strength and power each power can or cannot confer on the planets. And this is what the author says, that he will now discuss the virtues or strengths of the planets placed in these dignities themselves. Since the house is the noblest of all dignities, he therefore assigns five strength points to it. Next to this is the exaltation, and for this reason it is endowed with four points of strength. Then follows the trigon, distinguished by three points of power. After that, the dignity of the term, marked by two points. The last is the face, and for this reason signified by one point. This order of dignities is arranged in numbers so that we can more easily estimate and judge the magnitude of the strength or weakness of the stars, which they acquire in any place of the sky by their 'synoikeiosis' (familiarity), and thus more correctly choose the victorious and predominant planet. For we can declare that a star which we find to collect a greater number of strengths or powers in some place of the sky, overcomes the other stars in dominion in that place. For example, in the first six parts of Libra, when Saturn obtains 5 powers for its house, 4 for its exaltation, 3 for its trigon, and two for its terms, and thus easily surpasses all other wandering stars with a total of 14 powers: therefore, there is no doubt that Saturn is the ruler and victor in such a place. This order of dignities is general, and for this reason it should not be thought that it is observed everywhere consistently. For these dignities are accustomed to change their strength and powers in many ways with respect to the matters to be explained. Indeed, in matters concerning life, the house has more power than others; in honors and magistracies, the exaltation; in fortune and friends and popular glory, the trigon; in singular and proper matters, the term. And with this admonition, we have also provided a clear solution to the hesitation that the author raises here about the prerogative of trigons and terms. Furthermore, what the author says at the end of the chapter, that these are the universal essential powers of the planets in the signs, agrees with Ptolemy's doctrine and words, if you remove the face which he despised. For he also, as soon as he familiarized the stars with the dodecatemoria,

[Ptolemy on Dignities and Carpenta]
He shows that the familiarity of the house, exaltation, trigon, and terms are to be reconciled, rejecting faces, dodecatemoria, and other such subtle and empty divisions of rulerships. He concludes this same treatise with similar words, saying: "The familiarities of the stars and the dodecatemoria are such," that is, the familiarities of the stars and places are almost in this manner. The difference, however, is in this, that the author said all, but Ptolemy said almost all, indicating by the particle "almost" that besides those already mentioned, there are still other familiarities of signs with stars. These are three, which he immediately adds in the same place, namely, the carpentum, the persona, and the joy, which we explained above when discussing the domiciles of the planets. The persona will be discussed below in the third difference. As for the carpentum, we read that Ptolemy wrote about it in this manner: "And they are said to be in their own splendors, and on thrones and in their own chariots, when two or more of the aforementioned modes of familiarity are found to be associated with the places in which they are found. For then their powers are especially increased for action, due to the similarity and cooperative nature of the dodecatemoria's innate familiarity, and they are said to be enthroned and to shine." That is, they are said to be placed in splendors, and as if carried in royal chariots and carpenta, and to sit on thrones, and to be adorned with such excellences, when they have a right to those places in which they are found, by two or more of the modes of familiarity already explained above. For when their powers are especially increased for action, due to the similarity of the dodecatemoria, as far as innate properties are concerned, and the cooperative action of the same, then they are said to sit on their own thrones and to shine. From these words of Ptolemy, it is clear that the carpentum is not a simple dignity, but one composed of other simple dignities. For he wants a planet to achieve carpentum only when it is in a place related by two or more modes of association. For example, when Mercury is within the first seven degrees of Virgo, it obtains three simple dignities, namely, house, exaltation, and terms. Therefore, placed in such a place, it is said to be carried in a carpentum and to sit on its own throne. Thus, Jupiter in the first twelve degrees of Sagittarius, where it possesses house, trigon, and terms, as if in its own throne or carpentum, triumphs, etc. Ptolemy says that the power of planets sitting on their own thrones, that is, their faculty of action, is excellently increased for two reasons. The first is the similarity of properties innate to both signs and planets, which doubles the planet's power of action, and this is the efficient cause. The second is the cooperative nature of the dodecatemoria, that is, the proper power of the signs, which helps to perfect the action of the present stars. And this is the material cause of the effects. From this, it follows that signs empty of planets are not idle, but by their inherent power, they foreshadow those effects which the stars related to them accomplish. But now let us conclude this section with a brief repetition of what we have said, namely, that according to Ptolemy, there are seven kinds of dignities, or modes by which stars are reconciled to places in the sky: namely, house, exaltation, trigon, and terms, persona, joy, and carpentum. Of these modes, the first six are pure or simple; the last, however, is composed of simple ones. Finally, of the simple ones, the first four, concerning whose prerogative among themselves we have already spoken, are principal. The other two are less principal, with persona preceding joy. From this, it follows that the carpentum is to be considered the first and most noble of all, while joy is the last and weakest of all. Concerning that similarity, by which the author, according to the common opinion of astrologers, institutes a comparison of the house to dominion, and of exaltation to kingship, etc., there exists not only the judgment of the most celebrated man Cardano, but also a most beautiful exposition of all these dignities, explaining what each can do in any matter. Therefore, we have taken care to include his entire passage on this matter here. He says, "It must be seen what each of these can do, and not like Haly, who compares house to house, exaltation to kingdom, and explains the powers of dignities in such a way that you know as much after reading what he writes about them as you did before. Therefore, one must pay attention, since this is a most excellent tradition, and discovered by us through long observation and subtle reasoning. Nor should we be less grateful to Haly, who, besides what he could do, provided us with the cause for inquiring into the truth. Therefore, a carpentum is when a planet is, as it were, in all its joy and in all its dignity. For one born with such a significator is loved and honored by his own and by strangers, everything is deferred to him, believed, he leads a tranquil life, arranges everything with a certain ease, so that he truly deserves to be called fortunate. But when a planet is in its house, one born under its disposition will have perpetual power, and without noise, people will spontaneously obey him: they lead a tranquil life, and show a certain perpetual efficacy of that planet: if it is Mercury, of study and negotiation; if it is Venus, of grace and charm; if Jupiter, of probity and administration; if the Sun, of dignity and majesty; if Mars, of audacity and promptness, fortitude, anger; if Saturn, of moroseness, stability, cunning, obstinacy, solicitude; if the Moon, of desire for journeys, and handling other people's affairs. But if you refer to general constitutions, you will see the same similarity. For if Saturn is in its houses and rules, the times will generally be cold and dry, with a fixed and strong coldness: and if it is summer, the times will be perpetually temperate. If Jupiter, the time will be serene, pleasant, beautiful, broad, with moderate and pleasing winds. And if Mars, the time will be very arid and very dry; if the Sun, the time will be very hot, with few rains however; if Venus, everything will be mild, sweet, rainy; if Mercury, everything will be varied and inconstant and changeable, so that it never remains in the same tenor for three continuous days; if the Moon, the time will be cloudy, rainy, inconstant and heavy. If anyone asks, whence is this from Ptolemy? I answer, Ptolemy taught us this expressly, that the action of the Sun is to be imitated in all things. Therefore, when it is in Leo, it causes the greatest heat and rules equally. But Leo is the Sun's house, indeed, its carpentum and throne (but the ratio of trigons should hardly be counted among the parts of the carpentum, since it depends on the house and exaltation), therefore the Sun's power in Leo proceeds from its domicile. By the same reasoning, exaltation itself is understood. For the dignity of exaltation in private nativities brings new and not perpetual powers, and more among strangers than among one's own, and produces a restless kind of life. Therefore, exaltation is not to be compared to the house in terms of tranquility, or duration, or ease. But because it is new and unusual: Defense

[The Nature of Signs and Their Meanings]
72. DIFFERENCE. It brings unexpected and almost unhoped-for advantages, and is considered more illustrious by most people. For humans tend to admire the unexpected more, even if weaker and smaller, than the usual and greater things. Who would not marvel more at an inheritance of four thousand crowns received from a stranger, than at a son succeeding his father to thirty thousand? And this is the reason why many have preferred altitude to house. Therefore, in general constitutions, when the Sun enters Aries, its exaltation, it moves the whole world, everything sprouts, everything is cheered, yet it does not heat as in Leo, nor even as in Cancer or Taurus. And to say it in one word, the house makes a thing quiet and perpetual, the exaltation in change and a greater appearance. The trine, however, has more virtue in dividing regions of the earth than in nativities. In nativities, it signifies strength and efficacy, and the mandates of that person are received by people, if he presides, or his counsels, if he is an associate. The terms declare more powerful forces, but limited by times, such as embassies, annual magistracies, and singular affairs. In times, however, changes are not general, but those that end in a few days or months. Hence, terms, because they receive an end, are called days. Qualities, however, polish both mind and body, they do not create them. Hence, he who has the lord of manners in the terms of Mars or Saturn, may indeed be otherwise good, but with rude, rustic, and unkempt manners. In bodily affections too, the term alleviates or increases evil, it does not create it. The person makes it appear and represent a species, not that it is. Joy, however, alleviates or exacerbates the significations, but is barely felt. This is Cardan's view. CONCERNING THE ESSENCES OF THE SIGNS, AND what they signify in themselves. Because in the signs there are certain signs that are called rational, namely, Virgo, Gemini, Libra, and Aquarius, the first half of Sagittarius, because their images in the circle are figured as images of humans. These are also called signs having beautiful voices. These also thrive when they are in the East. And some are called winged, namely Gemini, Virgo, Pisces. And some are quadrupedal, like Leo, Sagittarius. Some of them are domestic, like Aries, Taurus, Capricorn, these thrive when they are in the South. Virgo, however, and Capricorn and Aquarius thrive when they are in the North. And among the signs, some are tortuous and vicious, namely, Aries, Taurus, Cancer, Scorpio, and Capricorn. And some of them are said to have many offspring, namely Cancer, Scorpio, and Pisces, and these thrive when they are in the West. And some of them are sterile, like Gemini, Leo, and Virgo. And some have few children: like Aries, Taurus, Libra, Sagittarius, Capricorn, and Aquarius. And some are said to be very luxurious: namely Aries, Taurus, Leo, and Capricorn. And among the signs, some are said to have a medium voice, which are formed in the images of bleating, bellowing, and roaring animals, like Aries, Taurus, Leo, and Capricorn, the last part of Sagittarius. And some are voiceless, namely those formed in the images of voiceless animals: namely Cancer, Scorpio, and Pisces. This title: Concerning the essences of the signs, and what they signify in themselves, seems to indicate that these differences of the signs, which are discussed in this chapter, should be referred to the simple and naturally inherent properties of the dodecatemoria. But if we wish to examine the matter more accurately, we will find that they are not entirely simple, but rather derived from the fixed stars, and from the connection of the images of the eighth sphere. And this is the reason why, at the end of the preceding chapter, after the completed discussion of the dignities of the planets in the signs, or rather of the reconciliation of the planets with the natural temperament of the dodecatemoria, it says that the figures of the signs follow, or as it is said more clearly in Greek, τὰ μορφώματα τοῦ ζῳδίου, that is, the appellations accommodated to the dodecatemoria from the adjacent forms of the signs. And immediately at the beginning of this chapter, it says that the dodecatemoria of Gemini, Virgo, Aquarius, and the first half of Sagittarius are called human because the images connected to these signs are figured in the likeness of a human image. This was especially true in the time of the ancients and Ptolemy, when, as has been copiously explained above, not only did the head of the starry Aries coincide with the vernal intersection, but also the entire image of the same Aries was joined to the Arian dodecatemorion, and in the same way the subsequent images in the eighth sphere coincided entirely with the dodecatemoria following in the outermost sphere. But in our time, since the first star of Aries has receded 17 parts and a little more from the vernal equinox, and all the other constellations have made similar progressions εἰς τὰ ἐπόμενα, it is clear that, having abandoned the dodecatemoria they once occupied, they have almost entirely migrated into the succeeding dodecatemoria. For example, the image or sign of Aries of the eighth sphere has for the most part been transferred into the dodecatemorion of Taurus of the outermost sphere, and similarly the Taurine image of the eighth sphere has proceeded into the dodecatemorion of Gemini of the outermost sphere, and the image of Gemini into the Cancerian dodecatemorion. Therefore, the dodecatemorion of Gemini, for example, which was once called a human sign with the added image of Gemini, can no longer be correctly called human, but rather quadrupedal; and the Cancerian dodecatemorion, once named reptilian from the connected image, will now be more correctly called human because of the ingress of the image of Gemini. For there is no doubt that such a translation of the things themselves must follow the translation of the appellations that the dodecatemoria assume from the images of the eighth sphere. This must be observed more diligently, lest by abusing these appellations as if they were constantly inherent in the dodecatemoria, we fall into a very gross error. Haly wrote these words to the same effect: Many things, he says, the ancients told you about the signs, but it is fitting that you accept from their sayings that which agrees with Ptolemy's opinion, because he alone is the one who follows the natural thing. For he holds that each sign has its separate power, because of the fixed stars existing in each sign. And for this reason, it is fitting that you inspect.

[Ptolemy's Dodecatemoria and Fixed Stars]
FIRST. You will judge which stars belong to which sign, and according to this, you will judge. And when you have done this, you will naturally have followed this art, and you will be in agreement with Ptolemy's opinion. For it is fitting that you do this, and dismiss all other sayings, and not lose yourself, like one who wants to take many paths and does not know which is certain. And thus you will not go astray, like one who does not know what is truth and what is not. But Ptolemy's words, by which he teaches that the appellations of the dodecatemoria are derived from the adjacent figures of the fixed stars, are these: "But they also applied other appellations to the dodecatemoria, taken from the figures of the stars, for example, quadrupedal and terrestrial, and ducal, and fertile, and so named. Which appellations, since they are evident from their figures, we deem it superfluous to enumerate here. The qualities of such forms, insofar as their powers are useful in judgments, have been expounded by us." By this text of Ptolemy, we are usefully and very timely admonished that the enumeration of appellations, which the places of the heavens take from the adjacent images of living things, by which the entire machinery of the world is distinguished, is by no means necessary: since it is easy to understand the powers of each from its form or figure: especially, however, since, as he himself testifies here, insofar as they can or should be usefully considered in judgments, it has been sufficiently expounded in its proper places. Ptolemy did this in Book 1, Chapter on the powers of the fixed stars, and throughout all the books of the Apotelesmatics. Haly is of the opinion that it is superfluous to enumerate these differences of the signs, because they are inconstant, and are changed over longer periods due to the transit of figures into succeeding places. For the same reasons, the author also, as it seems, distinguishes at least twelve principal figures, which are interwoven with the zodiac, in this chapter as an example with various appellations. "Human or anthropomorphic signs" are those in which human effigies are found, such as Gemini, Virgo, Aquarius, and the middle part of Sagittarius. The author and Haly also include Libra among the human signs, because of its proximity to Virgo, I think. Why Gemini and Virgo are called "winged," that is, having wings, the painting of the globes sufficiently indicates. But the "gills" of Pisces are like wings. However, I do not remember having read in Ptolemy that the figures of Pisces and Gemini are called winged, who, when he speaks of winged creatures, for example, names Virgo and Sagittarius with a hen. "Quadrupedal" signs are Taurus, Leo, and the last part of Sagittarius. And two of these, namely Leo and Sagittarius, are "wild" or untamed: the rest, however, are "tame" or domestic. The author adds that human signs are also called vocal, and the same in the east: but quadrupedal signs exert greater power in the south than in other quadrants, with Virgo, Aquarius, and Capricorn being more in agreement with the North. In general, however, the figures as well as the appellations of all signs took their origin from their natural effects, which they impress upon the subjects and those things that particularly agree with them. As in general constitutions, human signs signify events that will turn out for the human race, and those of other figures for similar brutes and similar things: which Ptolemy expounded more fully in Book 3, Chapter 7, on the kind of events. In nativities also, human signs bestow sociability, gentleness, and civil custom upon the natives. On the contrary, those that have the names of wild beasts, such as Leo, engender inhospitality, savagery, insolence, and rapacity. But those that are called domestic, indeed add gentleness to the native, but with a certain humility and abjection. To this belongs that saying of Hermes: "Whoever does not have the rulers of his nativity or the ascendant in human signs, will be alienated from humanity even to himself." In the same way, Cancer, Scorpio, and Pisces are called tortuous, vicious, and morbid signs, because they inflict upon the natives humps, lameness, dislocations, ulcers, and such calamities. Ptolemy calls them "afflicted signs": "especially," he says, "when the Moon is in the nodes or flexures, or in the afflicted signs, such as Cancer and Pisces, there occur bodily defects of humps, lameness, and dislocations." And there a little further down, where he speaks of judgments of diseases from the signs of the eighth sphere: "Specifically," he says, "Cancer, Capricorn, and Pisces, and generally all creeping and aquatic signs, particularly cause diseases of ulcers that consume flesh and creeping skin diseases, scrofula, fistulas, leprosy, and such diseases." Similarly, "fertile signs," that is, those having many offspring, such as Cancer, Scorpio, and Pisces, have found their name from their effect. He says that these are strong in the west, while "sterile signs" are Aries and Leo. Furthermore, he seems to wrongly enumerate Gemini among the sterile signs, since their image is "two-bodied," and for this reason, according to Ptolemy in Book 4, Chapter on children, it multiplies the number of children. But the six remaining signs, namely Taurus, Virgo, Libra, Sagittarius, Capricorn, and Aquarius, since they have a middle nature in this matter, are not improperly called signs of few children. Similarly, since the signs of men are called vocal: but Cancer, Scorpio, and Pisces are mute: the rest, namely Aries, Taurus, Leo, Capricorn, and the last part of Sagittarius, are not undeservedly left to a middle nature. These are the differences of the twelve signs interwoven with the zodiac. The principal ones of the rest that are situated outside the Zodiac, whose use Ptolemy shows in their proper places, are almost these: "Signs of birds," such as the eagle and the hen: "Aquatic signs," such as the dolphin and Pisces: "Shipwrecked signs," such as Argo and the ship: "Poisonous signs," such as Scorpio and serpents: "Mutilated signs," such as Taurus and the head of Medusa: "Bound signs," such as Andromeda and Cepheus: "Ducal signs," such as both the northern and southern crowns. All these can be reduced to a compendium in this way. Some images are of animals, others of things. Of things, such as ducal, like both crowns: and shipwrecked, like Argo. Furthermore, of animals, some are flying in the air or winged, such as the eagle and the hen: others are swimming in the waters: and of these again, some are called marine, such as the dolphin: and others are called fluvial, such as the fish.

[Planetary Influence on Body Parts by Sign]

In the previous chapter, the agreement of the signs with the body parts was shown; in this one, it wants to indicate the parts of the body that the wandering stars affect as they pass through the signs. For example, if Saturn is placed in the sixth house of the nativity, which signifies illness, threatening infirmity to the native, then according to this rule, if Saturn is in Aries, the chest will be in danger; if in Taurus or Gemini, the belly; if in Cancer, the genitals and all that follows them, etc. For greater ease of use, we have included a summary in the following figure. The reason for this division, by which parts of the human body are distributed to the planets as they pass through all the signs, appears to be as follows. To each planet, its own house, which first occurs after Aries in the subsequent signs, is assigned the first part of the human body, namely the head. If the same planet then passes through the sign next to that house in the subsequent signs, it will also affect the second part of the human body, and the part closest to the head, that is, the neck, and similarly, as it travels through the subsequent signs, it will take care of the subsequent parts of the human body, whose order has been explained above. For example: since Capricorn is the house of Saturn, and indeed the one that first occurs when counting from Aries in the subsequent signs, therefore Saturn, when placed in Capricorn, will take care of the head; in Aquarius, the sign immediately following Capricorn, the part closest to the head, namely the neck; in Pisces, the shoulders and arms; in Aries, the chest; and in such an order, in the subsequent signs, it will affect the subsequent parts of the human body. Not only to the twelve signs of the zodiac and the seven planets, but also to the twelve stations of the sky described below, some have been accustomed to distribute the parts of the human body in a completely similar manner, and just as Aries is the first among the signs, so the ascendant, the first of the twelve houses, is considered to occupy the first part of the body, namely the head. For this reason, to the second house, the second part of the body, namely the neck, is attributed, and similarly to the remaining subsequent houses, the remaining subsequent parts of the body. This seems to be derived from the 74th aphorism of the Centiloquium, whose words are these: "Whoever has Mars ascending will certainly have a scar on his face." Therefore, what the ascendant, the first house of the natal chart, can do for the head, the first part of the body, I believe it has been judged that the second house can similarly achieve and exercise for the neck, the second part of the body, and the subsequent houses for the subsequent parts. These are the reasons why we see most astrologers insist on detecting and pronouncing the places of diseases and defects determined by the stars. But Ptolemy, as we can clearly see from his words in Book 3, Chapter on bodily defects and diseases, of his Apotelesmatics, thought somewhat differently and made conjectures about the places and qualities of future diseases and defects from more serious causes. "Here," he says, "for a general understanding, we must look at the two centers of the horizon, that is, the eastern and western, but especially at the one that is itself moving and at the one that is not fixed to the eastern center, observing how the maleficent stars are configured to it. For if they are positioned in the ascending degrees of the aforementioned places, either bodily or quadratically, or diametrically, or if such a place or both are afflicted, then bodily affections are to be suspected in the natives, especially if either of the luminaries, or both, are angular in the manner we have said, or diametrically opposite. For then, not only if one of the malefics is ascending, but also if one of the luminaries is ascending, being angular itself, it is sufficient to dispose to one of the aforementioned conditions, whatever the places of the horizon and the signs indicate together with the affection, and the natures of the stars, both those that cause harm and those that are harmed, and also those that are configured with them. For each of the parts of the zodiac that contain the afflicted part of the horizon will indicate the part of the body." S T

[Great Conjunctions and Ptolemy's View]
Thus far, he has explained those things which contribute to understanding the nature of the celestial spaces and the power of celestial bodies. In the following, he will enumerate all the names of astrologers, including the aphetic and anaretic places, and the profections, directions, and other revolutions of the aphetic places. The first chapter is named after the six great conjunctions, which, I believe, received their name from the rarity and magnitude of the accidents they produce, such as wars, plagues, earthquakes, floods, and similar effects, which are believed to occur with great delay and evidence. Indeed, the first and greatest of all, the conjunction of Saturn and Jupiter, celebrated at the beginning of Aries, is said to return every 960 years and generally dispose of the same times. Similarly, the second conjunction, which is the transit of the conjunction of Saturn and Jupiter from one trigon to another, occurs every 240 years. The third, which is the conjunction of Saturn and Jupiter at the beginning of Cancer, occurs every 30 years. The fourth, which is the conjunction of Saturn and Jupiter in any sign, occurs every 20 years. The fifth place is held by the true return of the Sun to the point of the vernal equinox, or, as they commonly say, the Sun's ingress into the first point of Aries. The last and least of these is the synod or diameter of the luminaries. Besides these, there are many other smaller and lesser conjunctions, nearly 120, a catalog of which Haly and Pontanus enumerate in the aforementioned 50th chapter of the Centiloquium. Furthermore, that the author says the greatest conjunction of Saturn and Jupiter returns every 960 years, and the second every 240 years, is very variable, and can only be true if we assume that the conjunction of Saturn and Jupiter returns every 20 years, and proceeds annually by 2425 parts, that is, by 8 signs and two and a half parts. For example, if the conjunction of Saturn and Jupiter first begins at the beginning of Aries, then after 20 years from the root, according to this thesis, the second conjunction of Saturn and Jupiter will be in 2 degrees, 30 minutes of Leo. The third, after 40 years from the root, in 5 degrees, 2 minutes. The fourth, after 60 years, in 7 degrees, 30 minutes. The fifth, after 80 years, in 10 degrees. The sixth, after 100 years, in 12 degrees, 30 minutes of Scorpio. The seventh, after 120 years, in 15 degrees of Sagittarius. The eighth, after 140 years, in 17 degrees, 30 minutes of Capricorn. The ninth, after 160 years, in 20 degrees of Aquarius. The tenth, after 180 years, in 22 degrees, 30 minutes of Pisces. The eleventh, after 200 years, in 27 degrees, 30 minutes of Aries. The twelfth, after 220 years, in 36 degrees, that is, in 6 degrees of Leo. Therefore, after 240 years from the root, this great conjunction will migrate from the fiery trigon to the earthy trigon, for it will be at the end of 30 degrees of Pisces, that is, at the beginning of Taurus, and likewise in this earthy, as they call it, triangularity, it will remain for 240 years, and in the same bodies, Saturn and Jupiter will similarly conjoin twelve times. Therefore, if you multiply 12 by 20, you will again get 240 years, after which the great conjunction will pass to the beginning of Gemini, and again, after exactly 240 years, it will come to the beginning of Cancer, and will remain in its trigon for 240 years; and finally, after a total of 960 years, it will return to the beginning of Aries, that is, to the fiery triangularity. It will therefore remain in each trigon for 240 years. And since there are 4 trigons, multiply 4 by 240, which will be 960 years, after which the greatest conjunction, changing from the watery trigon, will return to Aries, from where it first departed. But it appears that this hypothesis of astrologers is false. For the minor conjunction of Saturn and Jupiter occurs not every 20 years, but every 19 years and 315 days, 19 hours. And the process is by 243 degrees, 59 minutes, 18 seconds, 9 minutes, 19 seconds, that is, by 8 signs, 2 degrees, 59 minutes, 18 seconds, 9 minutes, 2 seconds. And so in one and the same nature of the trigons, as if from one to another, not, as the author seems to want, by 2 degrees, 30 minutes, 14 seconds, but proceeding by almost three parts. From which it is clear that the two superiors in one trigon do not, as is written here, meet twelve or thirteen times, but at least ten times, and thus that great conjunction in one and the same trigon does not persist for 240 common years, but at least 198 and a little less than one year. But we believe that a more extensive discussion and deeper inquiry into this matter neither pertains to our purpose nor to the true substance of the art; since the doctrine concerning great conjunctions, although both the learned and the unlearned vulgar equally rush into it, can be proven to be partly plainly erroneous, and partly not of such great importance as is thought, by arguments of fact and the authority of Ptolemy, who, at the beginning of the second book of the Apotelesmatics, explaining the general prognostic parts of astronomy, transferred both the foreknowledge of regions and cities, to which the significations of great events are directed, and their times, to the sole eclipses of the luminaries, making no mention of great conjunctions. His words are these: "For the general examination of cases, two things are especially taken into account: the dodecatemoria of the zodiac, and furthermore the familiarity and sympathy of the stars with each of the climes, and the significations of effects that occur in their proper parts at the proper time, according to the conjunctions of the Sun and Moon, and according to the transits of the wandering stars around their risings and stations. Therefore, before all things, we will explain the natural causes of the aforesaid sympathy, at the same time briefly recounting the bodily and moral peculiarities of all nations, which are not alien to the natural constitution of both the dominant stars and the places of the zodiac to which they are subject." And further down he says again: "There is no prior or more powerful cause of such cases than that which is derived from the eclipses of the Sun and Moon, and the transits of the stars at that time." But someone might say that this text of Ptolemy rather extols and strengthens the doctrine of great conjunctions than abolishes it, since the power of producing great and general events is attributed not only to the eclipses of the luminaries, but also to the transits of the wandering stars? I answer that, although Ptolemy, in general mutations, attributes power to the superior planets equally with the rest of the multitude of astrologers, he does not do so to the same extent, nor in the same way as they themselves think about their power. For they primarily observe the conjunctions of Jupiter and Saturn for discovering the times and places of great events, to which something is signified, and extend their effect for many centuries. But Ptolemy, on the contrary, considers the times and places.

[On Compotent Degrees]
DIFFERENCE. Fortunate. Unfortunate. Fortunate. Unfortunate. 4 7 26 4 7 4 25 1 15 4 26 2 13 4 27 2 20 2 25 2 7 2 24 14 24 18 12 28 12 ++ 25 22 ++ 26 m 15 P 25 ++ 13 p 26 ++ 9 X 26 p X 28 21 14 X 9 X 13. ON COMPOTENT DEGREES. And two degrees, which are of the same longitude from the head of the movable signs, are called compotent, that is, of the same strength, and partners or participants in virtue, as the 20th degree of Capricorn or Cancer with the 10th degree of Sagittarius or Gemini, the 20th degree of Aries or Libra with the 10th degree of Pisces or Virgo. Parts equally removed from the four cardinal points, generally called by the common people compotent degrees, or degrees of the same strength and power, the Greeks were accustomed to call τὰ ἀντίσκια μέρη. These are distinguished by Ptolemy in such a way that parts equally removed from the tropical points on both sides are called βλέποντος καὶ ίime μoντo, that is, beholding and equally powerful: but parts receding equally from the equinoctial points are properly and learnedly called προςκατοντσο καὶ ἀκής or, that is, commanding and obedient. The words of Ptolemy concerning commanding and obedient places are these:

[Ptolemy on Commanding and Obedient Places]
FIRST. They are: προςάποντα καὶ ἀκέοντα Λέγονη τμήματα τὰ κατ' ἴσίω διάςασιμ ἀχὶ τῷ αὐτε όποτορα του ισημερινών σημείωμ ακμαν libr.t.capt Ειλόμλια, δια τὸ ἐμοῖς ἴσοις χρόνοις αναφορες δέτε᾽ ἴωμ πραλ πυρί προς λάλωμ. τότωμ δὲ τὰ μεν οὐ τους θερινῷ ἡμικυκλίῳ προς άοσοντα και σαυτόνο λίτη, τα δ' ἐμ το χειμερινῷ ὑπακοντα: 21ὰ τὸ κατ' ἐκεῖνο μή γινό τωμα- μενου τόμ Ο μείζονα ποιεῖμ τνυκτὸς τὸ ἡμέραμ, κατὰ το δεές καόντωμ λάζω: that is, Commanding and obedient places are said to be those which are at an equal distance from one or both of the equinoctial points, and this is because they rise in an equal space of time and describe the same parallel circles. Moreover, those which are in the summer part of the sphere are called commanding, and those which are in the winter half are called obedient, because the Sun makes the days longer there, and the nights longer here. In this text, Ptolemy teaches us both the causes of familiarity between commanding and obedient places, and why the latter are subject to the former. He states one cause of familiarity to be that such places, being at equal intervals from the equinoctial points, have equal anaphoras or ascensions, as they call them, which is clear and known from the first spherical institutions, and the use of the anaphora table described by us below easily indicates the same. For example, in our altitude, that is, of the city of Agrippina, which is 51 parts, both dodecatemoria, namely and X, when equally receding from the vernal intersection, in their rising draw with them from the equinoctial 13 parts, 21 scruples, which converted into time make 0 hours, 57 scruples. Thus and rise equally with 17 parts, 25 scruples, that is, one temporal hour, and 14 scruples. In the same way, we must judge concerning the remaining dodecatemoria equally removed from the intersection, whether vernal or autumnal. He states another cause of association between the said parts to be that they describe equal parallel circles, which is equally easily established. For if four points are taken equally removed from the equinoctial intersection on both sides, the lines connecting the assumed points will be both equal to each other and equally removed from the diameter of the ecliptic, and will be equidistant from it by the 14th of the third element of Euclid, and will delineate parallel circles entirely equal to each other during one revolution of the world. But why are the northern places more powerful and nobler than the southern?

[Commanding and Obedient Signs]

He answers that since day is nobler than night and more suitable for action, therefore the places of the zodiac signs which bring an increase of days are deservedly called commanding, and the remaining ones in which the diurnal light is overcome by diurnal darkness are called obedient. Thus Aries commands and is more powerful than Pisces, and Taurus than Aquarius, and the rest than the rest, which we have described in the following table. For example, the first part of Aries, and the last of Pisces, the second part of Aries, and the penultimate of Pisces: Likewise the tenth part of Aries, and the twenty-first of Pisces, the twentieth part of Taurus and the eleventh of Aquarius, and so on with the rest, which are equally removed from the vernal or autumnal intersection, mutually command and obey each other. For such an association of such parts we can correctly begin either from Aries and Pisces, or from Virgo and Libra: so that you might understand this more clearly, we have also included a figure of this matter.
