Ptolemy described to us places that are beholding and equally powerful with these words: ἰσοδιωαμεῖμ φασίμ ἀλλήλοις μέρη, τὰ τῷ αὐτῶ καὶ όπος. τορα την τροπικόμ σημείωμτὸ ἴσομἀφεςῶτα, λὰ τὸ καθ' έκατέρο πόντωρ. καὶ ἰσοδυ αὐτῶν τε ο γινομένα, τις τε ἡμορας ταῖς ἡμέραις κὶ τὰς νύκτας ταῖς νυξί, κή, το διας ήματα τῇ οικείωμ ὡρῶμ ἰσόχρονα ἀποτελεῖθη, ναμόντων ταῦτα δε καὶ βλέπαμἄλληλα λέγεται, διά τε τα προφρημένα, καί ἐπειδήπερ εκάτερομ αὐth in to thì cὐτῇ μέρωμα ορίζοντ ἀνατέλλει, καὶ εἰς τὰ αὐτὰ καταδύς, that is: They say that parts are of the same power which are equally distant from one or both of the tropical points, because when the Sun passes through those parts, days become equal to days, and nights to nights, and finally hours become equal to hours. These are also said to behold each other, both for the reasons we have just mentioned, and because both rise and set in the same parts.

[Causes of Equal Power]
DIFFERENCE. from both tropical points, because when the Sun passes through those parts, days become equal to days, and nights to nights, and finally hours become equal to hours. These are also said to behold each other, both for the reasons we have just mentioned, and because both rise and set in the same parts. From these words, three causes are evident, by which parts equally removed from the tropical points, beholding each other, acquire equal power. The first is that they make days and nights equal: the second, that they maintain equal times for the hours: the third, that they always rise and set in one and the same place of the horizon. For example, when the Sun is in the 27th part of Cancer, the same amount of light and darkness is produced as when the Sun is in the fourth part of Gemini, and this in any position of the sphere. Similarly, the temporal hours to be explained below, of the 27th part of Cancer, both diurnal and nocturnal, are equal to the diurnal and nocturnal temporal hours of the fourth part of Gemini. Finally, whether the Sun is in the 27th part of Cancer, or in the 4th part of Gemini, it rises and sets in one and the same point of the horizon, if indeed the said points are constituted in one and the same parallel circle. Now, as for what remains, we propose to examine the beholding and equally powerful signs in the table. (Beholding M I X ++ p καμμια ip) What we warned about a little earlier in the discussion of commanding and obedient places, we repeat here, namely that all these things which are said about the equipollence of entire dodecatemoria, are to be referred not so much to the dodecatemoria themselves, as to parts and scruples of parts, the grammatical ἀπόδαξιμ of which we have presented to the eyes. HATEFUL AND BEHOLDING. HATEFUL AND BEHOLDING.

[Antiscia and Inconjunct Places]
The antiscia of the zodiacal places are arranged in the manner we have described. For ease of use and to resolve all obscurity, we will now describe a table of parts and minutes, from which both commanding and obeying signs, as well as parts of signs and minutes of parts that mutually behold each other, can be easily derived. For example, if Saturn is in the 20th part, 10th minute of Aries, and its obedient and beholding or equivalent place is to be found: first, from the previous descriptions of the figures, it is clear that the sign of Aries commands the sign of Pisces, and the same Aries beholds Virgo. Now, to recognize the corresponding parts to the parts in which Saturn resides, first enter with part 20 into the following table of parts, and opposite part 20 you will find part 11 written. Then also, send the 10 minutes into the table of minutes, and 51 minutes will appear. Therefore, you will declare that in part 20, minute 10 of Aries, it commands part 11, minute 11 of Pisces, and beholds part 11, minute 1 of Virgo. For once the part of one is known, the part of the other will never be unknown, which is most worthy of observation. Julius Firmicus and the author are of the opinion that no part projects antiscia into the 30th part of the dodecatemory, which did not seem to be consistent with the truth. For who does not understand that the middle point of the first degree of Gemini almost beholds the middle point of the thirtieth degree of Cancer? Therefore, with one part moved, I have described the antiscia of the parts here a little differently than Firmicus. TABLE OF ANTISCIA. Parts. | Minutes. ---|--- 1 | 30 | 1 | 60 | 16 | 45 2 | 29 | 2 | 59 | 17 | 44 3 | 28 | 3 | 58 | 18 | 43 4 | 27 | 4 | 57 | 19 | 42 5 | 26 | 5 | 56 | 20 | 41 6 | 25 | 6 | 55 | 21 | 40 7 | 24 | 7 | 54 | 22 | 39 8 | 23 | 8 | 53 | 23 | 38 9 | 22 | 9 | 52 | 24 | 37 10 | 21 | 10 | 51 | 25 | 36 11 | 20 | 11 | 50 | 26 | 35 12 | 19 | 12 | 49 | 27 | 34 13 | 18 | 13 | 48 | 28 | 33 14 | 17 | 14 | 47 | 29 | 32 15 | 16 | 15 | 46 | 30 | 31 We must also observe that two commanding and obeying signs, namely Taurus with Scorpio, and Scorpio with Taurus, are said to command and obey hatefully, because besides this testimony of conjunction, they strike each other with a square radiation. For a similar reason, two combinations of beholding signs, namely Taurus with Leo and Scorpio with Aquarius, are said to behold each other hatefully, which observation contributes greatly to prognostication. From this, it is clear that those born under fixed signs generally harbor hostile enmities, and these are far more intolerable if those signs are commanding and obeying, for these are accustomed to generate dissimilarity of manners, that is, tyranny in the commander and infinite tolerance in the obedient. Here, however, as everywhere, the condition of men must be considered. For if the commander is nobler, he will openly attack the obedient with force and oppression; if of humbler fortune, he will attempt the same, not with violence, but with fraud and deceit, as we have sometimes heard of princes being deceived and seduced by their counselors. However, a more tolerable hostility will exist between those whom beholding signs, which signify similarity of manners, have disjoined. For if those signs are both summer signs, both will be born impatient of injuries and delighting in command; if winter signs, both will be dissimulators. These things being so, it is clear that beholding places by themselves are joined neither by a bond of concord nor discord, but only determine a similarity of manners, such as equal tolerance or impatience. For if intuition were a bond of concord, with the hatred of square radiation repelled or certainly mitigated, it would create a certain mediocrity of consent. Therefore, the intuition of signs creates a similarity of manners between two; but discord or concord, a suitable or hostile figuration of places. Nor should it be overlooked that beholding and commanding signs with obeying signs are more powerful, and diffuse their virtue much more widely near both solstices, where the Sun's declination varies very gradually, than around the equinoxes, where the declination suddenly and rapidly increases or decreases. For, as Pliny says, the position of the zodiac near its middle is more oblique, but near the solstices it is more direct. Therefore, the 10th part of Cancer, although precisely conjoined with the 21st part of Gemini, nevertheless almost agrees with the tenth part of the same sign of Gemini, and much more with the remaining intermediate parts. For it can be estimated from the figures of the antiscia delineated by us a little earlier that the parallel circles of such points differ by almost only one degree. On the contrary, near the equinoxes, where the parallels of individual parts are widely disjoined, they can do little unless parts correspond to parts. There is also a manifest use of inconjunct places in judgments; since these are plainly left by the author, we will therefore also adduce their definitions and notes from Ptolemy, lest any precept necessary for a full knowledge of the art be lacking in these instructions. The words of Ptolemy, by which he explained inconjunct and alien places to us, are these: "Unconnected and alienated sections are called those which have no simple relation to each other of the enumerated associations. These are those which neither command nor obey, nor behold each other, nor are of equal power, nor describe any of the four figures we have enumerated above, that is, neither diameter, nor trine, nor square, nor sextile, but those which are numbered second or sixth. For two continuous signs are averted from each other, and by mutual contact form one angle rather than any figure. But those which are numbered sixth divide the whole circle into unequal parts, while other figures divide it into equal parts." These are Ptolemy's words, which, to understand correctly, you must have at hand the nine ways we have hitherto explained chapter by chapter, by which the parts of the zodiac are connected to each other. For first, those places cohere by command, that is, when they exist in two lines parallel to the equinox. Secondly, by equal power, that is, when one and the same line parallel to the equinox terminates them. Thirdly, when they are disjoined by diameter. Fourthly, if they embrace each other by trine. Fifthly, if they form a square figure. Sixthly, finally, if they are associated by sextile: of which ways, the three latter, since they double the number, as shown above in the chapter on aspects, make nine bonds by which each place of the zodiac is coupled with other places, of which twelve are found in the whole zodiac. When this is so, two places will remain unconnected, that is, excluded from all coupling. Aries, for example, since it commands Pisces, and beholds Libra, and finally is coupled by diametrical, square, and trine radiation, and sextile to Gemini and Aquarius, it leaves at least two other signs unconnected, namely Taurus and Virgo, to which it coheres by no bond at all. Similarly, the same must be judged for all other movable and common signs. For fixed signs, although they can be coupled with at least eight other signs, are plainly deserted by the others. This happens because two signs, connecting themselves more firmly by a double bond, are deprived of two couplings. For example, Taurus with Scorpio, and Aquarius with Leo, cohere by a double bond, namely intuition and square. Similarly, Taurus with Leo, and Scorpio with Aquarius, are connected by imperial power besides square radiation. For this reason, we said a little earlier that the former are called mutually beholding with great hatred, and the latter commanding and obeying not without indignation. Therefore, those signs which are joined to other signs by no plain bond, such as the nine we have already explained, Ptolemy calls unconnected and alien, such as those which are numbered second or sixth. For the second are tortuously disjoined to different parts, and forming at least one angle, they enclose no figure, nor are they in a straight line as in a diameter.

[Luminaries and Friendship]
15. If they generate maximum hatred due to a square radiation. If by chance we are deprived of this testimony of familiarity, we should see whether the Sun and Moon have exchanged their places, that is, whether the place of the Sun in Paul's nativity is the place of the Moon in Peter's nativity, and conversely, whether the place of the Sun in Peter's nativity is the place of the Moon in Paul's. This uniquely promotes benevolence. For example, if when Paul was born, the Sun passed through Sagittarius and the Moon through Scorpio, and when Peter was born, the Sun passed through Scorpio and the Moon through Sagittarius, we would judge that there would be a very close familiarity between Paul and Peter due to the exchanged signs of the luminaries, and indeed, a wonderfully stable one due to the obedience of the luminary signs, which, as this aphorism states, increase benevolence and generate a certain conformity of manners. For both the sign of Pisces and Scorpio are obedient. But if such an aspect of the Sun's places, or the Moon's places, or an exchange of luminaries, were to occur from Libra and Scorpio, it would similarly be signified by a trine radiation, but not with such equal benevolence. For the love of him whose significators occupy Cancer, an imperious sign, is joined with a certain command and pride; but the significators of the other, Scorpio, are found in the obedient sign of Scorpio with subjection and servitude. In the same way, if the signs of the horoscopes in both nativities are either the same, or trine, or sextile, or finally if they have exchanged places, they will signify friendship. But since each native has a single ascending sign, how can an exchange of horoscopes occur? I answer that in such a comparison, the Part of Fortune must be assumed. For just as the ascendant is the Sun's, so the lunar lot is the horoscope. But just as friendship is judged from the συμφονία (harmony) of the said places, so conversely, hatred is judged from the ἀσυμφονία (disharmony) of the same places. For if square or opposite, or ἀσύνδετα (unconnected) or unconjoined signs are possessed without any exchange of luminaries, they will announce hostilities, hatreds, and enmities. They also refer the seventy-eighth statement to the use of antiscia: πολλάκις ἐνοργεῖ ὁ ἀς προ να τόπωυκ ἔχε εἰ οργφαμ, καρδθ ἀπροσδόκητη προσφορωμ. τους Hννωμοίῳ. That is, a star often exercises powers where it has no powers, bringing unexpected gain to the native. For example, if the 7th degree of Scorpio culminates, and Jupiter is in the 24th degree of Taurus. Here Jupiter, although it does not aspect Scorpio, nevertheless helps the native to attain magistracies through the hidden protection of the antiscium. For the 7th degree of Scorpio and the 24th degree of Taurus are of the same potency. But if Taurus hostilely strikes this antiscium of Jupiter, it will disturb the administration of the magistracy. Conversely, Venus will help Jupiter, and will bring about a successful administration, and will bring support to that antiscium from benevolent places. Therefore, if ever the stars do not see each other, it must be considered whether they are joined to each other by associated radiation through the antiscium. For when the antiscium has emitted in such a way that they are joined by trine, square, or sextile radiation through it, the stars themselves thus determine, as if they were constituted in a simple arrangement. If you diligently attend to all these things, you will be greatly helped in explaining the fates of men.

[Accidental Being of the Circle]
CONCERNING THE ACCIDENTAL BEING OF THE CIRCLE. But since, with God's help, we have already set forth the essential being of the circle of Signs, let us now set forth the accidental. For the circle is figured at every hour with such a figure, which is divided into four parts, which are divided by the circle of the hemisphere and the circle of the meridian, that is, the circle of the midheaven, which makes midday. And each of these parts is divided into three unequal parts, according to the ascensions of the ascending sign. And in this way the circle is divided into twelve parts, which are called houses: they are also called cusps and towers, whose work is expounded in the book of Ezibi, that is, in the book of the course of the stars. The beginning of the division is also the horoscope: that is, the ascendant, whose beginning is above the circle of the eastern hemisphere, then the second house follows and the third, and the other houses up to the twelfth.

[General Figure of the Celestial Magistracy]
From the division of the zodiac, which is perpetual and unvarying, we now pass to its accidental division. Hitherto, we have distinguished the zodiac, or rather the entire machinery of the world, into four quadrants perpetually congruent with the times of the year, into twelve regions of the signs, into ascending and descending halves, hot and cold, and many such stable parts, divided by circles or rather by hemicycles drawn through the pole of the zodiac. And finally, we have shown the familiarity and agreement of the zodiac's quadrants and signs with other things. Now, however, we must similarly distinguish the same machinery of the world into halves, quadrants, and twelve regions, but differently, that is, into inconstant parts, and continually variable due to the first motion, by the office of the meridional circle and the horizon and the hemicycles ending in the intersections of these circles, and dividing not the zodiac, but rather the equinoctial equally. And similarly, we must explain the familiarities or agreements of the quadrants and those twelve unstable stations of the heavens with the things subject to them. And this distinction agrees with Ptolemy's doctrine. For he also, having explained the natural texture of the times of the year, adds the nature of the accidental parts of the heavens and times according to the same example and manner: "Similarly," he says, "the four places and angles of the horizon, one having more of the dry towards the east," etc. These things being so, for the sake of learning and aiding memory, this part will be conveniently divided into two members, the first of which is where the author explains the accidental division of the zodiac, which continually varies with the diurnal revolution of the heavens; the second, where he enumerates the familiarities or significations of the quadrants and the twelve places of the heavens, which accompany this accidental, as he calls it, and mutable division of the zodiac. But the author very briefly touches upon the first member, which ought to contain the method of distributing the heavens, and refers the whole matter of constructing the celestial domiciles to the canons of the tables. Therefore, I think I shall do a worthwhile deed if I describe more fully in this place all the variety of this distribution for young students, and show the complete method of forming themes or pictures of the houses, and the way of extracting the places of the planets from the tables of ephemerides and motions. But before all else, certain circles omitted by the author of the Sphere must be described, the knowledge of which is not only necessary for the distinction of the celestial domiciles, but also for understanding the method of directing, as they call it. Vertical circles are those which, drawn through the vertex of the place and through each part of the horizon, divide it into 360 parts, and are called by some circles of rectitude, because they show in what region of the world any star rises or sets, and how far it is at any time from the east or west, from the south or north. Most people call them azimuth circles by an exotic name. It must be observed here that the meridian also belongs to the number of vertical circles, whose use is greatest in astrology above all others; but next to it in dignity among the vertical circles is that which, intersecting the meridian at right angles and drawn through the points of rising and setting, divides the horizon into four equal parts, whence, because of its manifold use, it is almost uniquely called the vertical circle by its proper name. Therefore, whenever the name of the vertical circle occurs absolutely, understand the vertical circle which is drawn through the rising and setting and through the vertex of the place. But understand the circles of altitude by this description: The vertex of any place is distant from its horizon by 90 degrees. If, therefore, planes equally distant from the horizon are understood to be drawn through the divisions of each part, circles of altitude will be formed, the first and greatest of all being the horizon itself. Each of the others, however, is smaller the closer it is to the vertex. Thus, all drawn together, intersecting the quadrants of the meridian and the other vertical circles at 90 degrees, and being intersected by them in turn at 360 degrees, they weave a kind of hemispherical net. Most people call these circles almicantarath by a foreign name. The office of these circles is to show the true distance and altitude of the Sun and other stars from the rising or setting in the vertical circles. For the altitude of a star or of any point noted in the sky is the arc of the vertical circle drawn through the center of the star, falling between the horizon and the star itself. A star obtains its meridian altitude when it reaches the meridian circle; but when it is absent from the meridian, it is said to have an eastern antemeridian altitude; if it has passed it, a western postmeridian altitude. The circle of position is that which is drawn through the center of a given star and through the intersections of the meridian and the horizon. This is sometimes called the horizon or boundary of the star, and has no small use in the progressions of Aphetic places. These things being known beforehand, let us proceed to the description of the horoscope. The horoscope is properly said to be that which observes the reason of the hours. But here we call the horoscope the natal star, or rather that part of the Zodiac which rises by the revolution of the heavens at the moment when someone is born, or when any other thing begins. It is sometimes taken, as here, for the picture of the heavens, which the Greeks call schema, that is, it is taken for the whole heaven figured in a flat form and distributed into twelve parts or places, which have received various appellations from astrologers. For they call them domiciles, habitations, towers, parts, hospices, mansions, and commonly twelve houses in the figure of the revolution of the heavens. Although not one but many and diverse methods are read among astrologers by which the entire heaven is variously divided into 12 parts, nevertheless all agree in this, that they imagine six great circles intersecting each other in two opposite places, whose common diameter extends from south to north, distinguishing the entire heaven and the whole machinery of the world into 12 parts, so that whatever is in the world is entirely comprehended in one of these 12 parts, which we call houses. But the extremities of the diameter, in which the said six circles intersect each other, I find to be constituted differently by different authors. For the author, as it appears, and those who preceded Campanus in teaching about dividing celestial domiciles, thought that these two points were the vertices of the world, in which the heaven, revolved daily with ineffable speed in the space of 24 hours, is the most potent cause of the said domiciles. For greater clarity, imagine two great circles, one of which is the meridian, and the other drawn through the point of the ecliptic ascending above the horizon and its opposite, so that by their mutual intersection at the poles of the world, they divide the heaven into four portions. Thereafter, each of the portions of the equinoctial, intercepted by the said two great circles, must be equally divided into three parts, and through the points of section and the poles of the world, four circles must be drawn, which, with the previous two, constitute the twelve domiciles of the heaven according to the author. In this method of equalizing, the horizon circle is counted among the said six circles only in a right sphere, never in an oblique sphere: which Abraham Avenezra also noted. Moreover, as the author warns, this also results in the same six circles always dividing the ecliptic into twelve unequal parts, and only making opposite domiciles equal; indeed, in this way the ecliptic is separated into four equal quadrants only at a certain hour of the day, namely when the beginnings of Aries and Libra lie on the horizon. Others again judged these points of section, which are common to the six circles, to be the intersections of the meridian and the horizon. But not even here do all agree among themselves. For Campanus and Gazalus distinguish the domiciles.

[House Division Methods]
In this manner, the quadrants of the vertical circle passing through the ascendant and descendant, intercepted between the horizon and the meridian, are divided into three equal portions. Then, through the points of the sections and through the common sections of the meridian and horizon, four circles of positions are imagined to be drawn, which, with the ascendant and meridian, divide the entire sky into twelve equal spaces, and this is according to the ratio of the parts of the vertical circle, and not according to the parts of the zodiac or the equator. Horontius, otherwise a distinguished artisan, defends this method of equalizing houses and thinks it should be retained. On the contrary, Regiomontanus, in Problem 14 of his tables, rejects it as alien to the mind of philosophers and constructs another fabric of celestial domiciles differing from the two preceding methods. First, he divides not the vertical circle, but the equinoctial itself into four equal parts by the meridian and the oblique ascendant, each of which quadrants he again cuts into three equal parts, and through the points of the sections he draws four circles of positions, which, meeting at the sections of the horizon and meridian, divide the entire sky into twelve houses of the sky. This method of Regiomontanus is, as it were, intermediate between the first and second. For in that he divides the equinoctial and not the vertical circle into twelve equal parts, he agrees with the first method and contradicts Campanus, but inasmuch as six circles meet and intersect not in the poles of the world, but in the common sections of the ascendant and meridian, to that extent he dissents from the first method and agrees with Campanus's reasoning. Finally, here the spaces of the sky are unequal in every oblique horizon, as in the first, but each house retains its invariable magnitude, as in the second. Therefore, he not unreasonably calls this method of equalization rational, because it rationally avoids all the inconveniences that could be objected to the extremes. Although this method of the most learned Regiomontanus is more erudite than all the others and simply more consistent with the truth, and finally approaches most closely to Ptolemy's opinion, there were nevertheless those who did not approve of it much, such as Johannes Schonerus and the other distinguished artisans of our age, who prefer to this and all other methods another, and in our order, fourth fabric of equal domiciles, as they call them, which is to be imagined in this way. First, the zodiac is separated by the ascendant and a great circle passing through both poles of the zodiac and the vertex of the place into four quadrants. Afterwards, each quadrant of the ecliptic is cut into three equal parts: which done, if you understand five circles to be drawn through the points of such sections and the poles of the zodiac, those very circles, with the one that passed through the vertex, will divide the entire zodiac and the whole world into twelve equal parts or houses, to which all astrologers are said by many to have accommodated their judgments. That this is true cannot be denied, if it is understood of Firmicus, Albohali, Albumasar, Albubater, Bonatus, and similar authors. But that the supreme Ptolemy, the prince of all astrologers, nay, among so many writers on celestial matters, the only astrologer, in his quadripartite construction approved this equal equalization of houses, it is certain that this is done with insufficiently firm conjectures, the principal of which I will recite here. And first, to begin with those things that are thought to be most firm, the figure of equal sides, which they think can happen nowhere else than in the equalization of houses, which is done by equal degrees, assigning 30 degrees of the zodiac to each house, scarcely supports their opinion. But this is not sagaciously enough, nor consistent with Ptolemy's opinion. For the very words of Ptolemy, which are extant in the third apotelesmatic, chapter on those who are not nourished, openly speak that the figure is to be understood otherwise. He says, "If one luminary is in an angle, and one of the malefics either conjoins or opposes it, and this partially and according to an isosceles figure, no benefic being configured, and the ruler of the luminaries being detained in the places of the malefics: what is born cannot be nourished, and will soon die." In these words, by an isosceles figure, he understands an exact conjunction of bodies or rays, both according to longitude and latitude, so that in conjunction the latitude is the same, but in opposition of the Sun, the malefic is below the Ecliptic, but in opposition of the Moon, the malefic has an equal latitude, but opposite. For example, let Saturn be in the 12th part, with a northern latitude of 3 parts, and the Moon opposite Saturn in the 12th part, with a southern latitude of 3 parts. It is now manifest that Saturn and the Moon with their places in the ecliptic make two isosceles and equal triangles, in which one side of the isosceles is the fourth part of the ecliptic, the other the fourth part of the great circle passing through the centers of the Moon and the malefic and the poles of the zodiac. Similarly, when the latitude is equal on the same side, we imagine an isosceles figure in the quadrants of the ecliptic and the circle of latitude because the arcs of the said circles drawn from their common intersection, and cut off by the circle passing through the poles of the zodiac and the point of conjunction, are equal to each other: which the annexed diagram clearly shows. Moreover, Schonerus wrote that an isosceles figure is formed if the Sun is in the ascendant, the Moon in the fourth house, the infortune in the seventh, striking the Sun by diameter, and the Moon by square. But Ptolemy did not speak of an equilateral triangle, that is, of a figure of three equal sides, such as is depicted in this configuration, but of an isosceles figure, of which only two sides are equal, such as we showed a little earlier. Moreover, Ptolemy in this place made no mention of the square configuration, which is added here by Schonerus. Finally, the following words in the same place clearly state that Ptolemy understood by an isosceles figure only an exact conjunction of opposite bodies or rays: "But if this position does not form a triangle of two equal sides, that is, if the malefics do not harm the luminaries by the same longitude and latitude, but only apply their rays to the places of the luminaries from nearby, etc." From these it can be clearly seen that the equal distribution of houses in the zodiac is by no means confirmed by an isosceles figure. It remains to be seen.

[Ptolemy on Aphetic Places]
We will present the opinion of the other place, from which the same is defended by the authority of Ptolemy. This passage is found in Book 3, Chapter on Aphetic Places, whose words are these: "First, indeed, it must be considered that there are Aphetic or prorogating places, in which it is absolutely necessary for him to be found who wishes to obtain the jurisdiction of governing life, as around the horoscope from five parts previously emerging above the horizon up to the 25 remaining succeeding. And he who touches these thirty parts with right hexagonal rays, which is called the place of the good daemon, and is the eleventh in order. And he who touches with tetragonal rays, namely the zenith above the earth, and he who touches with trigonal rays, which is called God, and he who touches with opposite rays, which is cast into the setting. So far Ptolemy, from whose words it is rightly gathered that the five degrees preceding the cusp and the 25 immediately following hold the virtue of such a house: but what is added about the zodiac, namely, that each house contains qualitatively 30 parts of the zodiac, does not proceed. For Ptolemy here makes no mention of the zodiac: nor does he speak of its parts, but of the equinoctial parts, so that each house is understood to be bounded by 30 equinoctial parts. And this is the reason why he immediately adds that the eleventh house always sees the horoscope with a sextile aspect, the ninth with a trine, the midheaven with a square, which would be absurd if this text of Ptolemy is understood of an equal distribution of domiciles in the ecliptic. For in many places, with the sign Gemini ascending, the midheaven occupies, and thus it does not look at the horoscope from a square, but from a trine aspect of the ecliptic. On the contrary, with the ascendant falling into the ninth house, it will not see the horoscope from a trine, but from a square. Therefore, Cardanus rightly teaches concerning Ptolemy's opinion that two kinds of aspects are to be understood, one in the zodiac, which must be taken exactly in the ecliptic from a similar part and minute, as said above. The other, which Ptolemy understands here, is in the equinoctial, in which, as in the zodiac, the configurations of the twelve signs are to be imagined. Many obscure passages of his agree with this opinion, as when he says that the trine of malefics from signs of short ascensions also destroys when afflicted, that is, when the two circles of position, impacted into the ecliptic through the extremities of the triangular figure, enclose such an interval from the equinoctial that it represents the form of a square aspect in the same equinoctial: then such a triangular aspect of malefics, encountering the giver of life, destroys the native. Similarly also these words, which precede the former passage: "sometimes also the hexagon, afflicted by the long-lived parts of the dodecatemoria, destroys," are to be understood of a double radiation and of the transformation of the hexagonal aspect in the ecliptic into a square aspect in the equinoctial, otherwise neither the trine of short ascensions nor the hexagon of long ascensions would kill. All these things, therefore, which are adduced from Ptolemy, sufficiently show that they are alien to the equal distribution of domiciles which is established in the zodiac, and openly testify that Monteregius approaches most closely to Ptolemy's opinion in the equation of celestial domiciles, so far is it from being able to be accommodated to understanding his opinion. It also seemed wonderful to me how Ptolemy is understood to denote the circle drawn through the vertex and poles of the zodiac by these words, which are found in Book 4, Chapter on Children: "If the reason for marriage also follows the reason for children, it is necessary to observe those things that are present at the vertex," etc., where by "the place at the vertex" is shown the midheaven, that is, the zenith or the tenth house, and rather the meridian than the circle drawn through the poles of the zodiac is indicated. We have not made mention here of those things which are adduced from the Centiloquium, since it is certain that this writing is falsely attributed to Ptolemy, and in most places it entirely contradicts his doctrine, which he left to us expounded with his own genius in the Quadripartitum. But now I will proceed from the section of the sky to the canons for constructing domiciles, after having first explained the order and names of the cusps for the sake of the less learned. The first house is that whose cusp or beginning first emerges, and according to the method of Monteregius it is then drawn out through thirty equinoctial parts, which Firmicus and the rest of the crowd count in the zodiac. The Greeks call it "anatole," that is, the rising part, because it ascends from the lower to the upper hemisphere: also calling it the horoscope. The circle of position below the earth, distant from the horizon by 30 parts, defines the first house and begins the second. Therefore, know that the house which succeeds the first below the horizon is the second house. This is followed by the third, which is bounded at the angle of the notes, and so on, going against the motion of the primum mobile, the remaining houses are numbered up to the twelfth house, because the order of the signs is such, and the motion of the planets proceeds in this way. Four of these twelve houses are called "katra," that is, the cardines of the sky, namely "anatole," the eastern cardo, "dysis," the setting or western cardo: "mesouranema," the midheaven or tenth house: "hypogeion," the imum medium coeli or fourth house. Ptolemy, however, specifically calls the summits of the first lines initiating the domiciles, centers: but the spaces completing these four principal stations of the sky, he usually calls angles. The four that follow the cardines, namely the second, fifth, eighth, and eleventh, are called "epanaphora," that is, succeeding: The remaining four, namely the third, sixth, ninth, and twelfth, are called "apoklinontes," that is, cadent. All these are shown to the eyes in the general figure of the sky, whose "katagraphe" I add for the sake of the less learned: Having taken the first square of just magnitude, if a second square is inscribed, whose angles touch the midpoints of the four sides of the first square, four triangular figures will be formed, each of which is cut in half by straight lines extended from the angles of the first square to the midpoints of the sides of the second. Finally, a third square is described in the second no differently than the second was inscribed in the first, and around the third square will appear twelve triangular forms, which we call celestial domiciles. Similar

[Calculating Celestial Figures: Monteregius' Method]
A similar and equally valid scheme is when two squares, one inscribed within the other, are divided by two diagonals proceeding from opposite angles of the outer square, as follows. These are the FIRST. Most people in our time use these methods for projecting the heavens onto a plane. There are also many other less common forms of depicting the heavens, whose delineation we have omitted for that reason. Having explained these things, we will proceed to the astrology of constructing celestial charts for any moment in time and for any latitude of a region. And first, we will bring back to calculation the method of Monteregius, since he alone is the one who agrees with Ptolemy's opinion and truth, and therefore must be known above all by students. On the construction of the celestial figure according to the rational method of John Monteregius from the tables of directions: - The action of celestial light is admirable, as it not only affects, governs, and nourishes bodies already born and perfected in the sublunar world, but also confers upon all things that arise under a certain celestial disposition, from the very beginning, that power which governs and preserves them until the end of their duration, so that from the beginning of generated things, the duration of their existence is cast, and such will be the future event as was the position of the heavens at their origin: and that from the beginning of motion, the remaining series of future motions until the end can be explained by those skilled in nature. Since the heavens, due to their ineffable speed, significantly change their position at every moment in time, it is easy to judge why astrologers, in explaining future events according to the natural order of things arising from the position of the heavens, diligently seek out the moments in time when these things began. For unless the time of the origin of things is accurately told to us, we will never be able to arrive at the true position of the heavens, nor at the cause that infuses a preservative power into things produced at that time. Therefore, in constructing true celestial charts, the first concern is that we do not deviate from the true time, that is, not only from the year, month, day, and hour, but also from the minute and second of time when the thing itself began. However, since the diversity of civil and mathematical time can entangle and mislead the incautious and less educated minds here, we will first prescribe how to reduce political or common time to Astronomical time, so that it agrees with the calculation of the tables. Mathematicians, for reasons to be explained elsewhere, take the beginning of the day from the meridian circle, that is, from that point where the Sun holds the meridian circle either above or below the earth, and from there they count 24 continuous hours until the Sun repeats the same place. In Ephemerides, motions are also calculated in this way, so that the positions of the stars assigned for each day are always to be understood as referring to completed Mathematical days. For example, in the year 1527, on May 21, at 3 hours and 47 minutes, someone was born in Valladolid, under latitude 41 degrees, and longitude 8 degrees, 20 minutes. The opposition of the luminaries immediately preceding the birth was on May 15, according to which the motion of the Sun in Stöffler's Ephemeris is assigned as 3 degrees, 6 minutes, 11 seconds. This motion is to be understood as referring to the completed Mathematical day, that is, that at that point of the meridian of that day, which is also civilly called the fifteenth of May, the center of the Sun is found in the said minute of the sign of Gemini. For Stöffler, in his Ephemerides, calculated the positions of the stars not for midnight, but for midday or the middle of the diurnal light. Furthermore, the time of the opposition of the luminaries, which falls on the same day, is noted as 11 hours, 11 minutes, which again is to be understood as referring to completed time, that is, that this Mathematical day is entirely completed, and moreover, 11 hours and 11 minutes have passed. If the same opposition had been around the 23rd hour, it should be understood that it would have been one hour before midday of the next day, which civilly refers to May 16. For civilly or in common life, days are often begun from the meridian circle, but not from midday, as in our ephemerides, but they begin at midnight and extend to the middle of the following night. Such are also the calculations of motions in the Prutenic Tables, in which the Epochs of Christ are set for midnight of the day preceding the Kalends of January. Therefore, those who, following the example of the Romans, begin the day from midnight, and use not a full clock, but twelve hours repeated twice, you will adapt their hours to Astronomical time begun from midday, if you add twelve post-meridian hours to the ante-meridian hours or those elapsed after midnight, and diminish the number of civil days by one. As if someone were born in the year of salvation 1527, on May 21, at 9 hours, 47 minutes before midday, then for such civil time to be adapted to the time of the ephemerides, 12 hours must be added to the 9 hours and 47 minutes, so that 21 hours, 47 minutes result, which time of hours and minutes follows the completed twentieth day of May. The same is true if in such a case you subtract twelve hours from the common or political time. As if from May 21, 9 hours, 47 minutes, you subtract 12 hours, there will remain, as before, May 20, 21 hours, 47 minutes. However, if hours counted from midday to midnight are offered, they agree with Astronomical hours and do not admit any further commutation. As happened in the assumed nativity, which, as we said so long ago, was in the year 1527, on May 21, at 3 hours, 47 minutes after midday. That time does not need our reduction, for May 21 is offered as completed at midday, and moreover, hours along with 47 minutes. Such a simple commutation of time is not only sufficient for constructing the figure of the heavens, but also useful for extracting the motions of the planets both from the usual ephemerides and from most tables of celestial motions, which they call resolved. But if it is desired to collect the motions of the stars from the Prutenic Tables and from the Epochs of Christ, which, as I said, are set for midnight: then, conversely, we must convert Astronomical hours, begun from midday, into hours calculated from midnight, by subtracting twelve hours from the time transformed to the beginning of midday in the manner I have already explained, so that the astronomical hours counted from the middle of the night remain. If, however, subtraction cannot be made from the hours, then an entire natural day, that is, 24 hours, as is customary in physical calculation, must be accommodated, and the number of days diminished by one. And conversely, if time calculated from midnight is offered, and it is to be translated to the beginning of midday, then 12 hours must be added to the proposed time, and if from such a composition more than 24 hours arise, twenty-four hours must be discarded, and the number of days increased by one. If by chance you wish to convert hours counted from sunset, which the Athenians once used, into Astronomical hours calculated from midday, then combine your hours calculated from sunset with the semi-diurnal time, which must be found by the doctrine of ascensions, and the Mathematical time begun from midday will be formed. And if from such an addition more than 24 hours exist, remember again that 24 hours must be discarded from the aggregate, and the number of days increased by one. Conversely, if time calculated from midday is to be converted into time flowing from sunset, subtract the arc of semi-diurnal time from the astronomical hours, and whenever subtraction cannot be made, assume 24 hours, and diminish the number of days by one, and the time counted from sunset will remain. Finally, you will convert the time counted from sunrise into our said Astronomical time, if you add to it the semi-diurnal arc along with a full 12 hours, again discarding 24 hours if necessary, and increasing the number of days by one. But conversely, if those astronomical hours are to be translated into hours counted from sunrise, you will subtract the semi-nocturnal arc of time along with a full 12 hours.

[Calculating Astronomical Time]
From these, subtract 12 hours, adjusting again, if the matter so demands, by 24 hours, and with the number of days diminished by one, the time calculated from sunset will remain. As for the reason for such time commutations, it is readily apparent to those who understand that those who begin their movements from noon encompass more time backward than those who begin from sunset, or from midnight, or finally from sunrise. However, the common or civil times, when they have been translated into astronomical time adjusted to noon according to these precepts, will no longer require any further correction, as far as constructing the figure of the heavens is concerned. For the figure of the heavens is conformed to the equated time that agrees with civil time. But if astronomical time is taken from tables of movements or ephemerides, which usually happens when we wish to know the ascendant of an eclipse, or of another luminary or the conjunction of other stars: then for the true constitution of the chart, the natural days' prothaphairesis, or as it is called, the equation, must be added to it or subtracted from it. Similarly, for the meridians, the time found in the table of regions must be added to it or subtracted from it, as indicated by the A or M assigned there. And so far concerning the determination of true time, which must first be observed in the construction of the celestial figure. The second precept is that for this very astronomical time, we derive the true place of the Sun from the tables of movements or ephemerides: where also the difference of the meridians must be considered, if your location and the locations of the Ephemerides differ by a notable longitude. This difference, as will be explained more clearly below, must be added to the time for which you have decided to extract the Sun's place: if your location is more westerly than the location of the tables or Ephemerides, or subtracted from it if it is more easterly. As in our example, since the location of Valladolid is longitude 8 degrees 20 minutes, it is 24 degrees 40 minutes, that is, 1 hour 38 minutes, more westerly than the location of Ulm, for which Stoffler's Ephemerides are adapted, whose longitude is 33 degrees 0 minutes. Therefore, this difference must be added to 3 hours 47 minutes, so that 5 hours 25 minutes result. If one wishes to pursue the matter more scrupulously, 9 minutes must still be added due to the equation of days, so that 5 hours 35 minutes result, which is the time adapted in all ways for the Sun's place, indeed, for the places of all the stars to be derived from the Ephemerides. Furthermore, when we have found the true place of the Sun, we will immediately extract the corresponding arc of the equinoctial from the tables of right ascensions, and this will suffice if the Sun is precisely on the meridian, that is, if no hours or minutes of time are attached after the completion of the days at noon. But if the Sun has passed some time from noon, then the arc of the equinoctial, which the hours and minutes of the afternoon hours converted into degrees and minutes of the equinoctial make up, must be added to the right ascension of the Sun's place, so that the number of degrees and minutes resulting from such an addition shows the right ascension of the Midheaven or the cusp of the tenth house. Moreover, the hours and minutes of hours multiplied by 15 degrees pass into equinoctial times. For in every equal hour, 15 degrees of the equinoctial rise everywhere. This, however, is to be understood as physical multiplication. But if you prefer to follow the common method of calculation, multiply the hours by 15 degrees, and divide the first minutes of the hours by 4, so that the degrees of the equinoctial corresponding to such time result: since, as has been said, 15 times correspond to one hour of time from the equinoctial, and for this reason, one degree of the equinoctial corresponds to four first minutes of time. As in our example, since we must transform 3 hours and 47 minutes into degrees of the equinoctial, we will multiply 3 hours by 15, and 45 parts will result. We will divide 47 minutes by 4, and 11 parts 45 minutes will result, which, combined with 45 parts, produce 56 parts 45 minutes, which is the arc of the equinoctial corresponding to 3 hours and 47 minutes of time. Thirdly, you will collect the oblique ascension of the remaining five eastern houses, which succeed the tenth house up to the fourth, if you add 30 parts to the ascension of the Midheaven five times in order. For example, if you add 30 parts to the ascension of the MC, the sum will be the oblique ascension of the eleventh house. If you again add 30 parts to this, you will collect the oblique ascension of the twelfth house, and so on, by continuous addition of 30 parts, the oblique ascensions of the succeeding cusps are to be derived. Fourthly, the places of the Ecliptic, which correspond to these ascensions, are to be derived from the tables of ascensions, of which peculiar ones serve almost every place, by the ninth problem of Monteregius. But the cusp of the tenth house is always taken from the tables of right ascensions, for the meridional circle acts as the right horizon. The table constructed for the latitude of the proposed place serves the cardine of the rising heaven. Finally, the rational table of houses, placed near the end of Monteregius' tables, indicates the tables serving to derive the cusps of the remaining houses. In its area, two distinct numbers are found, the former showing the elevation of the pole above the circle of position of the eleventh and third houses, the latter showing the same above the circle of position of the twelfth and second houses. Therefore, seek the number of the elevation of the pole, or what is the same, the number of the latitude of the given place in the left side of the rational table, and opposite it in the area you will find numbers that indicate from which tables and for what latitude they were constructed, the principles of the remaining houses are to be derived. For example, the latitude of the place of birth, which we use in the exemplary exposition, is 41 parts. Therefore, the table of oblique ascensions constructed for the latitude of 23 parts and 29 minutes will serve the eleventh and third cusps. The twelfth and second will be adapted to another table made for the latitude of 36 parts 58 minutes. But since the tables of oblique ascensions are constructed only for whole degrees, therefore for 23 parts and 29 minutes, we will simply assume 23 or 24 parts, and for 36 parts 58 minutes, simply 37 whole parts, due to the proximity of the numbers, for ease of use without manifest error. If we wish to execute everything diligently and elaborate it to the letter, by consulting the end of the ninth problem of Monteregius' tables, we will know what the calculator needs to do, adjusting his calculation to the altitude of the pole not precisely in degrees, but in minutes of degrees. But this diligence is necessary only for accurately constituting the horoscoping part, since the tenth house is not accustomed to having any number of the exalted vertex attached, and for the remaining cusps placed outside the cardines, a calculation made approximately abundantly suffices. However, once the beginnings of the six eastern houses are known, the principles of the remaining six western houses will no longer be hidden, since each eastern house looks diametrically opposite to its counterpart in the western part. Therefore, if we describe similar parts and minutes in the opposite houses of the opposite signs, we will complete the entire celestial chart. But all these things, so that they may become clearer by example, for the time of the assumed birth, namely, for the 21st day of May, at the third hour after noon.

[Aspects and Their Nature]
FIRST. 53. as you see in the diagram, with six lines emanating from one center, which, when drawn in a circle, divide it into six equal parts. This aspect is considered moderately good, because the signs contemplating each other at this interval agree in sex and nature. 2. This is closely followed by the square, that is, the square aspect. And it looks at the breast: so called because it comprehends the fourth part of the Zodiac, that is, three signs or P.90., which you see described in the figure, etc., by the straight line AD, and marked with a tetragon, which, inscribed in a circle, receives three signs on each side. This aspect is judged moderately bad, because the two signs at such an interval disagree in sex and nature. 3. The square is followed by the trine, that is, the trine aspect, which contains the third part of the Zodiac, that is, P.120.: and it is described in the diagram by the straight line AE, and marked by an equilateral triangle A, which, when transferred to the inner circle, receives 4 signs on each side. This aspect is also judged good, partly because of its efficacy, partly because the three signs distant by such an interval agree in sex and nature. 4. The trine is followed by the diameter, that is, the diametral or opposite aspect, which contains half of the circle, that is, six signs, P. 180.: and it is described in the diagram by the straight line AF, and marked by two opposite orbs. This aspect is considered egregiously bad, not because of the conflict of natures in the signs, but rather by reason of opposition: for those things which are opposite and most distant, are most inimical to each other, and in every genus are contrary to each other. There are therefore two causes of disagreement in aspects: one, and most principal, is the conflict of signs in nature and sex: the other, and principal, is the greatest distance. Thus in the square ray, since the distance is moderate, and the contrariety is only in the nature and sex of the signs, the disagreement is less. And when the radiations do not differ greatly, they do not disagree, but agree moderately because of the similarity of natures and sex in the dodecatemoria: but opposite signs, since they differ most by 15. of the third element of Euclid, therefore, even if they agree in nature and sex, they nevertheless disagree most because of the greatest distance: just as both are masculine and diurnal, and both are feminine and nocturnal, and yet they are opposite and disagreeing, G3 - Digitized by Google 54 DIFFERENCE disagreeing, the cause of which disagreement is their greatest distance, and not diversity or contrariety in sex and nature, which is absolutely true: for it is manifest that opposite signs, which agree in sex and nature, ought to be believed to fight most and more than squares. What some have handed down, that opposite signs disagree with each other because they are composed of two square aspects, in which way the diameter also consists of two square radiations, namely and, then and, does not proceed. For what disagrees with one, rather agrees with it than disagrees. Moreover, since the opposite radiation also consists of two agreeing ones, namely hexagonal and trigonal: according to this reasoning, the opposition of signs ought to be judged most harmonious, which is absurd. Haly thinks that opposite signs are harmonious for two reasons. The first is, that when one ascends, the other descends, and vice versa: the second, that when it is in one of them, it makes a time contrary to that when it is in the other: just as when it is in, it makes a humid time, in dry. But from the latter cause, this absurdity would follow, that and would be disharmonious, since is no less a vernal sign than the sign II, to which it is opposed. Therefore, the reason previously explained, for strengthening the doctrine of radiations, is more potent, and agrees with Ptolemy's opinion. His words on this matter are these: "The trine and sextile aspects are considered harmonious, because they are composed of homogeneous dodecatemoria, that is, either all feminine or all masculine. But the square and opposite aspects are disharmonious, because they are constituted by dissimilar places." From these words it is also gathered, that the agreement of signs in hexagonal and trigonal aspects, and the disagreement in square and opposite radiations, is not to be understood of temperament and mixture, as the Arabs wish, but only of the sex and nature of the signs, so that signs and II, although joined by hexagonal radiation, are not of the same temperament, Digitized by Google FIRST. 55 temperament, but one is humid, the other dry. Similarly, trine signs agree not in temperament, but in nature and sex, as said above. For all are masculine and diurnal, but the sign is humid and hot, and is hot and dry, is dry and cold, etc. But if someone objects to Ptolemy, that signs which are masculine and diurnal, are also hot and dry: and those which are feminine and nocturnal, are also cold and humid, is not this rightly thought? To this objection we must answer, that such a consequence is by no means necessary, since the condition of time and sex does not arise from the primary qualities. For the diurnal and nocturnal state proceeds from the masculine sex: but the masculine and feminine sex itself is derived from a more efficacious or inefficacious action. Finally, this efficacy flows from the principle of the most robust motion, which exists near the equinoctial. Ptolemy testifies to this in book 1, chapter on masculine and feminine signs, with these words: "The equinoctial circle, described through V and, produces the first and most powerful motion of all." From these, therefore, in continuous series, feminine signs are subjected to masculine, that is, and this virtue is communicated to II and. For these thus look at each other from both principles, namely and a convenient aspect, that is, * and also from themselves. These things being so, it is clear, first, that the sex and diurnal nocturnal condition of the signs derive their origin from motion and not from qualities. Secondly, that not all masculine and diurnal dodecatemoria are rightly said to be hot and dry, nor feminine and nocturnal, humid and cold. 1. Conjunction is numbered among the aspects, which is marked by a single orb, so that the coitus of two bodies in the same line may be signified. From what has been said, it is also clear that the harmful and threatening aspects are the square and opposition: the benevolent ones are the trine and sextile: and the conjunction is indifferent, that is, good with good, bad with bad. As for their strength, the conjunction is the most robust and efficacious of all, after the opposition, because, as appears in concave mirrors, lines passing through the center are the most valid of all, and because Digitized by Google 56 DIFFERENCE that those proceeding from fewer sides are always more efficacious than those from many sides. The third place is held by the square and trine, which are considered almost equal in power. The last and weakest radiation is that which comes from the sextile. Ptolemy adduces two other reasons for radiations, one Geometrical, the other Musical, which I now omit for brevity's sake, and because more faith is given to radiations from experience than for any reasons. All rays, moreover, except the opposite ones, are double, sinister and dexter. Sinister are those which exist before the sign from which we begin, that is, which tend according to the order of the signs: As Hexagon AC, and square AD, and trine AE. Dexter are those which are behind the sign from which we begin, that is, which tend against the order of the signs: As sextile AB, and the rest which descend to the right. Hence it is clear, first, that ray AC is called sinister with respect to A, and dexter with respect to B. Secondly, that stars placed in the upper hemisphere illuminate the horoscope with sinister radiation: but in the lower with dexter radiation, whether they see it from a sextile, or a square, or a trine. [Calculating House Cusps]
Let us establish a celestial figure for the 47th minute of the 3rd hour after noon on May 21, 1527, for a region with a latitude of 41 degrees. First, from what has been said, it is clear that the political time defined by us requires no reduction, since, besides the 21 completed days of May, it counts 3 hours and 47 minutes from noon, and therefore it perfectly agrees with the astronomical time sought. With these things thus established, the next step is to extract the Sun's position corresponding to the said time from the tables of motions or ephemerides, and its right ascension from the tables of right ascensions. Having done this, we will find the Sun to be at 8 degrees, 53 minutes, 28 seconds of Gemini, and its right ascension to be 67 degrees, 10 minutes. To this, we now add the arc of the Equator corresponding to the 3 hours and 47 minutes elapsed from noon, namely 56 degrees, 45 minutes. By doing this, we will produce the right ascension of the Midheaven or the Tenth House, approximately 123 degrees, 55 minutes. Continuously adding 30 degrees five times to this, we will find the oblique ascensions of the remaining five eastern houses as follows: Midheaven: 123 degrees, 55 minutes, Pole 39 degrees, 30 minutes. Eleventh: 153 degrees, 55 minutes, Pole 24 degrees, 30 minutes. Twelfth: 183 degrees, 55 minutes, Pole 37 degrees, 30 minutes. First: 213 degrees, 55 minutes, Pole 41 degrees, 30 minutes. Second: 243 degrees, 55 minutes, Pole 32 degrees, 30 minutes. Third: 273 degrees, 55 minutes, Pole 24 degrees. Now, what remains is to extract the parts of the ecliptic corresponding to these house ascensions from specific latitude tables that serve each. First, by entering the right ascension of the Tenth House, having 123 degrees, 55 minutes, into the area of the table of right ascensions, we will find that it corresponds to approximately 1 degree, 39 minutes of Leo on the left side of the table, assuming a proportional part, which should always be done whenever the ascension number is found exactly in the table area. Similarly, by entering the oblique ascension of the First House, having 213 degrees, 15 minutes, into the table of oblique ascensions constructed for the natal location, that is, for a latitude of 41 degrees, we will find that it corresponds to 26 degrees, 44 minutes of Libra, which should be written on the eastern horizon or on the cusp of the First House. Furthermore, we have previously warned that in this example, the table constructed for a latitude of 24 degrees serves the ascensions of the Eleventh and Third Houses without obvious error. Therefore, in it, for the oblique ascension of the Eleventh, which is 153 degrees, 55 minutes, you will find 6 degrees, 17 minutes of Virgo corresponding. And for the oblique ascension of the Third House, which is 273 degrees, 55 minutes, in the same table, 23 degrees, 26 minutes of Sagittarius correspond. Finally, since the table of oblique ascensions constructed for a latitude of 37 degrees serves the oblique ascensions of the Second and Twelfth Houses, you will find in it, first, for the oblique ascension of the Twelfth House, which is 183 degrees, 55 minutes, 3 degrees, 13 minutes of Libra corresponding. And finally, for the oblique ascension of the Second House, which is 243 degrees, 55 minutes, in the same table, 21 degrees, 55 minutes of Scorpio correspond. You now have calculated the beginnings of the six eastern houses originating from the Midheaven, to which, in the corresponding parts of the opposite signs, the beginnings of the remaining six western houses must be placed. For example, in our case, since 1 degree, 39 minutes of Leo falls on the Midheaven, the beginning of the Fourth House will be placed at 1 degree, 39 minutes of Aquarius. And since we found 26 degrees, 44 minutes of Libra on the cusp of the First House, the Seventh House, which is opposite the ascendant, will begin at 26 degrees, 44 minutes of Aries. Similarly, the beginnings of the remaining four houses, that is, the Fifth, Sixth, Eighth, and Ninth, must be established diametrically opposite the Eleventh, Twelfth, Second, and Third, as is evident in the following description of the celestial chart, to which we have also added the Part of Fortune and the longitudes of all wandering stars. How these are to be extracted from ephemerides for the meridian of any city, we will discuss shortly. Although we should have omitted the more exact circle of tables here, as in the first rudiments of calculations, we will briefly touch upon it, since it is absolutely necessary for establishing exact stellar epochs.
