Jupiter 4 minutes. Saturn 2 minutes. Mars 52 minutes. Venus 1 degree, 46 minutes. Dragon's Head 1 degree, 25 minutes, 2 seconds. Dragon's Tail 6 degrees, 5 seconds. Part of Fortune 1 degree, 4 minutes, 46 seconds. Ascendant 23 degrees, 5 minutes.

[Erecting a Celestial Figure]
On erecting a celestial figure from the tables of Monteregius' directions according to the equal house system, from which the judgments of the Arabs are said to depend. You should have the right ascension of the Midheaven from the preceding doctrine, and to it add 90 degrees, and you will complete the oblique ascension of the rising cardinal point, from which, from the table of your climate, extract the corresponding degree of the ecliptic, as has been said, and assign it to the cusp of the First House. To the Second House, assign a similar degree of the following sign, and so on, proceeding without omitting any sign until the figure is completed. But from the right ascension of the Midheaven, the corresponding place of the Ecliptic must be extracted from the table of right ascensions, which, designated at the top of the figure, is called the Heart of Heaven. The same celestial chart and for the same place to be erected according to the equal house system.

[Constructing a Rational Figure]
ON THE CONSTRUCTION OF A CELESTIAL FIGURE according to the rational method of Monteregius from the tables of the equation of the twelve houses. First, it must be seen that if the proposed time is civil, it should be transformed into astronomical time in the manner explained a little earlier. Second, the Sun's place for the completed day's meridian must be extracted from the tables of motions or the ephemeris. Third, inquire about the said place, or rather the completed degree of the Sun, in the table of the houses of the sky for the elevation of your location, or for the nearest lesser or greater latitude to your location, under the title of the tenth house. Having done this, you will find the position of the sky and which parts of the zodiac are at the beginnings of the individual eastern houses at the time of the meridian. Therefore, if your time precisely coincides with the meridian, the beginnings of the said six houses will immediately present themselves to the right, and nothing further will remain to be investigated from the table. But if hours or minutes of hours are attached to the completed day at the meridian, then in the same place in the table, describe the time placed on the left side, to which you always add individual minutes of hours for every 15 minutes of degrees of the Sun (for 15 minutes of the equator rise in the space of one minute of time). Fourth, to the time described from the table, add your time in hours and minutes, for which you have decided to construct the houses of the sky: diligently observe this, that for every six added hours, you always add individual minutes of time for the sake of the equation of days, for one revolution of the sky is completed in approximately 24 hours and 4 minutes. You will also observe the rule of astronomical calculation, which warns that whenever, from such a composition of hours, a time emerges that exceeds 24 hours, those same 24 hours must be removed from the total aggregate and completely discarded, and the remainder preserved. Fifth, these hours and minutes, which result from the combination of the two times, or remain after the removal of 24 hours, look for them in the table of houses constructed for the latitude of your region in its left margin.

[Eastern and Western Houses]
In its left margin, for the regions to the right, the beginnings or cusps of the six houses of the sky will present themselves, namely the tenth, eleventh, twelfth, first, second, and third houses. However, with the eastern hemisphere thus described, the six western stations are known from the opposite signs. In the proposed example, the Sun's place is 8 degrees, 54 minutes of Gemini, which in this matter is safely estimated as the completed ninth degree. I therefore look for this ninth degree of Gemini in the table constructed for a latitude of 42 degrees (for the common ephemerides do not have a closer one) under the title of the tenth house, and I find on the left side of the table the corresponding time elapsed from noon, namely 4 hours, 29 minutes, which, added to our afternoon time, that is, 3 hours, 47 minutes, makes 8 hours, 16 minutes, which number...

[House Cusps and Ascensions]
In the left part of the table, the time is found not exactly, but approximately greater, that is, 28 hours, 17 minutes. From this region, the eastern hemisphere is described, namely, the cusp of the tenth house is 2 degrees, 2 minutes; the eleventh house is 7 degrees, 0 minutes; the twelfth house is 7 degrees, 0 minutes; the first house is 27 degrees, 2 minutes; the second house is 22 degrees, 0 minutes; the third house is 24 degrees, 0 minutes. The remaining six western houses are known from the opposite signs, as I said. The method of erecting a figure of the heavens according to the author's opinion follows. At the given time, first find the right ascension of the Midheaven, which, as we said, is composed of the right ascension of the Sun's place and the degrees of the equinoctial generated by the time elapsed from noon. Then, to this right ascension, add a quadrant of the circle, that is, 90 parts, and the oblique ascension of the Ascendant will result. From the table constructed for the latitude of your place, extract the corresponding part of the ecliptic, and then calculate the semi-diurnal and semi-nocturnal arcs of that already found part of the ecliptic, and take the third part of each calculated arc. Now, to establish the cusps of the six eastern houses, first, to the right ascension of the Midheaven, add in order three times the third part of the semi-diurnal arc, so that the right ascensions of the eleventh, twelfth, and first houses are produced. Then, to the right ascension of the first house, add in order twice the third part of the semi-nocturnal arc, and you will establish the right ascensions of the second and third houses. Finally, since the horoscoping part is known from the preceding, it remains for us to extract the appropriate parts for the remaining five houses, for all of which the table of right ascensions serves. Having found these, we will have the centers of the eastern houses readily available, and we will establish the remaining six western houses through the opposite points as before. As in our example, the right ascension of the Midheaven is 123 parts, 55 minutes. If we add a quadrant of the circle to this, that is, 90 parts, we will find the oblique ascension of the Ascendant to be 213 parts, 55 minutes. To this, in the table constructed for a latitude of 41 parts, corresponds 26 parts, 44 minutes of Libra. The semi-diurnal arc of this horoscoping part is 80 parts, 52 minutes, and its third portion is approximately 26 parts, 57 minutes. And the semi-nocturnal arc is 99 parts, 8 minutes, and its third portion is approximately 33 parts, 3 minutes. These being so, if you add in order to the right ascension of the Midheaven 26 parts, 47 minutes, which is the third portion of the semi-diurnal arc of the horoscoping part, you will produce the right ascension of the eleventh, twelfth, and first houses. And indeed, for the eleventh, 150 parts, 42 minutes; for the twelfth, 178 parts, 29 minutes; and finally, for the first, 205 parts, 16 minutes. Now, if the third part of the semi-nocturnal arc, containing 33 parts, 3 minutes, is added twice in order to the right ascension of the first house, the right ascension of the second and third houses will result. And indeed, for the second, 238 parts, 19 minutes; for the third, 271 parts, 22 minutes. Moreover, in the table of right ascensions, to the right ascension of the eleventh station corresponds 28 parts, 45 minutes of Gemini; to the twelfth, 28 parts, 40 minutes of Virgo; to the second, 0 parts, 30 minutes of Cancer; and finally, to the third, 1 part, 14 minutes of Leo. The culminating and horoscoping parts of the Ecliptic never change, but are always found and remain the same in all methods, that is, those of Regiomontanus, the Arabs, the author, and Campanus. I omit here the method of constructing figures of the heavens according to Campanus and Gazulus because it is erroneous. I could have omitted the author's method for a similar reason, had it not been necessary for the exposition of the text. For here we only approve the opinion of Regiomontanus, which is also Ptolemy's, or certainly approaches his opinion as closely as possible. If there is anyone who also wishes to reduce Campanus's speculation on the twelve houses to numbers, let him consult problem 16 of Regiomontanus. The first figure of the author. May 21, 3 hours, 47 minutes past noon. Pole 41. Regarding the motions of the seven planets and the head and tail of the Dragon, to be found for any hour of the day and placed in the figure of the heavens. A complete figure of the heavens consists, first, of the description of the houses, namely, the 4 cardinal, 4 succedent, and 4 cadent houses. Second, of the inscription of the true places of the seven wandering stars, also the head and tail of the Dragon. Third, the notation of fixed stars of the first and second magnitude, whose latitude from the Ecliptic is not great. Fourth and finally, of the inscription of the lots and other parts. And thus far it has been said how, for any moment of time, the figure of the heavens, according to the opinion of both Regiomontanus and other astrologers, should be formed, so that it may be seen which signs are above the earth and which lie below the earth, and which parts of the dodecatemoria fall on the cusps of the individual houses. It remains, therefore, for us to briefly complete the remaining members of the celestial figure. It has been said above that all motions written in the usual ephemerides are calculated for the meridian of the day to which their number adheres. However, since the same motions vary at every moment of the days due to the continuous motion of the spheres, it is necessary to present a method by which the motion of any planet for any moment of time should be calculated from the ephemeris. Therefore, take the motions of the planets that are assigned opposite your proposed day, and write them out in order. Then, immediately place above them the motions of the same planets for the immediately following day, and subtract the smaller from the larger, for the excess or the difference of the numbers will show the diurnal motions of the planets, as is evident in the following example of the proposed nativity. May 22, 9 degrees, 46 minutes, 9 degrees, 10 minutes, 28 seconds, 23 degrees, 27 minutes, 29 seconds, 29 degrees, 1 minute, 27 seconds, 504, 21 degrees, 25 minutes, 22 seconds. May 21, 8 degrees, 44 minutes, 25 seconds, 6 degrees, 28 minutes, 16 seconds, 27 degrees, 16 minutes, 29 seconds, 21 degrees, 26 minutes, 38 seconds, 3 degrees, 12 minutes, 25 seconds. Super. or 0 degrees, 57 minutes, 14 seconds, 14 degrees, 40 minutes, 40 seconds, 4 degrees, 7 minutes, 0 seconds, 13 degrees, 0 minutes, 2001, 12 degrees, 1 minute, 9 seconds, 0 degrees, 3 minutes, 1 second. Diurnal motion. After this, one must proceed according to the rule of proportions in this way: In the first place, set 24 hours, into which the entire natural day is divided. In the second place, place the differences of the motions or the diurnal motions. The third place should be occupied by the hours elapsed from noon, together with the minutes, for which the motion is proposed to be sought. The fourth number, which is elicited from the progression of the rule of proportions, is to be added to the motion of your complete day if the planet is direct, that is, when its motion written in the ephemeris increases. But if it is retrograde, that is, if its motion written in the ephemeris decreases, it must be subtracted from the same, so that the motion corresponding to the proposed time may result. Here it must be observed that the Sun and Moon are swifter than the other wanderers, and therefore never retrograde. Likewise, the Head of the Dragon always progresses in antecedents.

[Planetary Positions and Time Correction]
Having observed all these things, add the complete month of May, 3 hours, 47 minutes. You will collect the true longitudes of the planets from Stoffler's ephemerides, calculated as follows: Sun 8 degrees, 58 minutes; Moon 27 degrees, 19 minutes; Mercury 28 degrees, 17 minutes; Venus 27 degrees, 18 minutes; Mars 29 degrees, 18 minutes; Jupiter 26 degrees, 49 minutes; Saturn 3 degrees, 23 minutes; Dragon's Head 25 degrees, 25 minutes. Lords: Aquarius, Aries, Gemini, Scorpio, Gemini, Cancer, Sagittarius. However, these motions, collected in this manner, still need to be corrected for two reasons. One is the distance between meridians, the other is the equation of days. But just as both are necessary for scrutinizing the true motion of the Moon, so in collecting the motions of the other planets, they are safely neglected due to their slow progression, especially when we extract the motions of the stars from ephemerides not adapted to the Prutenic but to the Alphonsine calculation, and therefore showing the positions of the stars not precisely, but approximately. Therefore, leaving aside the six planets, you must correct the Moon's course as follows: First, in the tables of regions, which are commonly added to ephemerides, under the character (region), the minutes of degrees are noted for any given place, by which the Moon's motion, taken from the ephemeris for the proposed time, must be augmented or diminished due to the meridians or the position of the place, as indicated by the letters A and M added to these minutes. The table of provinces and more recent towns, which precedes Stoffler's ephemerides, does not have our example's location inserted. Therefore, we must refer to a place in Spain whose longitude agrees with or at least does not differ much from the longitude of Valladolid, such as the longitude of the city of Cordoba, from whose region under the character (we find) 42 minutes, which must be added to the Moon's true motion, because the meridian described through Cordoba is so much more western than the Ulm meridian, for which Stoffler compiled the ephemerides. Finally, the minutes, which must always be subtracted from the same motion due to the equation of days, will be indicated by the common angle of the following table, if in its left margin you take the Sun's position for the proposed day, and in the head of the table the sign in which the Sun is located. As in the previous example, ten minutes are indicated, which, being subtracted from the Moon's motion, and those which the difference of meridians produced for us being added to it, the Moon's motion, corrected in all ways, becomes 27 degrees, 51 minutes of Aquarius. The table of the Moon's motion follows. 2437 hours must be subtracted, so that the time starting from midnight remains. For example, in our case for the year 1527, 21 days are proposed as completed at noon, with 3 hours, 47 minutes elapsed from noon. Therefore, if you subtract twelve hours from this, you will have the remaining time starting from midnight: 20 days of May, 15 hours, 47 minutes. And this is the first thing worthy of consideration in our calculation. Secondly, this time must be corrected by the difference of meridians, that is, it must be translated to the meridian of the place for which the motions are calculated. You will achieve this if you subtract the difference of longitude or meridians converted into time from the proposed time, when your meridian is more eastern than the meridian of the tables, or if you add it to the same when it is more western, because eastern places count more hours, and therefore they must be subtracted; western places, however, count fewer, and therefore they must be added, so that the time prepared for the meridian of the tables exists. As in our example of Königsberg in Prussia, for whose meridian the Prutenic tables were compiled, the longitude is 46 degrees, 45 minutes, 1 second. The longitude of Valladolid, however, is 8 degrees, 20 minutes. The difference of meridians is therefore 38 degrees, 25 minutes, 1 second, which converted into time makes two hours, 33 minutes, 3 seconds, to be added to the proposed time, because Valladolid, a city in Spain, is so much more western than Königsberg in Prussia. If you wish to use the table of regions for brevity's sake, and immediately calculate the positions of the stars for a given meridian other than the meridian of the tables, which is what we are doing here, look for your location in the catalog of places, or one closer to it, and add the ascribed time difference to your time if the letter S is present, or subtract it if A is present, so that in this matter of ours you do the opposite of the notes themselves. The location of our example is not noted in the Prutenic catalog, but nearby locations, such as Toledo, Cordoba, Compostela, are, for which a nearly prior time difference is found with the letter S indicating that such a difference must be added to our time for the hours to be adapted to the meridian of Königsberg, contrary to what the notes actually signify. For in the catalog of notable places, the more eastern locations of the tables have the note of addition A, while the more western ones have the note S. For these notes are specifically intended for such calculations, so that the same motion calculated for the meridian of the tables corresponds to different hours of another meridian. Thirdly, by the Sun's position, which the ephemerides will always easily show you, the prothaphaeresis or equation of days should be instituted. As in our example, because the ephemerides showed the Sun's position to be almost in 9 degrees of Gemini, therefore from the first Canon of the Equation of Natural Days, the prothaphaeresis of 9 minutes of days is collected, designated by the title S indicating subtraction. For these titles A and S have a place when apparent time is converted into equality, but the opposite must be done with these titles when equal time is in turn to be converted into apparent. Therefore, the time of our example, corrected both for the meridians and for the equation of days, is as follows: For the complete year 1526, 4 complete months, 20 days, 18 hours, 11 minutes, 4 seconds. Finally, the Julian time, reduced and conformed to astronomical equality by the methods we have discussed, must be adapted to the Alphonsine Abacus from the congruent table, which is found on folio 7 and following. For the Alphonsine method, in which, for collecting the equal motions of the stars, Julian time is reduced to sexagenaries of days and minutes, is shorter and, as it seems, more suitable than others. However, you should seek fuller instruction for this part from the third Prutenic precept, according to whose tenor we have preferred to describe only the diagram of our example here, rather than obscure the matter with a verbose narration in an unsuitable place. Sum: 2, 34, 51, 5, 45, 27, 55. Therefore, the time suitable for Alphonsine calculation will be 2 third sexagenaries, 34 second sexagenaries, 51 first sexagenaries, 5 days, 45 minutes, 27 seconds, 15 thirds. In this way, given the time for use.

[Calculating Equinoctial Precession]
For the calculation of equal motions, you must use the Alphonsine method for collecting the mean motions of the stars. I do not describe it here, as it is clearly demonstrated with examples in the eighth Prussian precept. There follows the calculation of the true precession of the equinox, that is, the computation of the true precession of the equinox. Alphonsus derives these computations from the vernal intersection, which is now known to have changed completely, more than needs to be proven here at length. Hence it is to be seen the reason why Copernicus and Erasmus state that the points of the equinoxes, as well as the nodes of the Moon, Anabibazonta and Catabibazonta, are in precession, and they imagine two equinoxes, namely the mean and the true: the former indeed which recedes regularly, but the latter which recedes anomalously from the head of the starry Aries. Therefore, the precession of the equinoxes is the arc of the Ecliptic intercepted between two great circles, each of which is drawn through the poles of the Zodiac, but one through the first star of the starry Aries, and the other through the point of the equinox, whether true or mean. The difference between the mean and apparent equinox is called the prothaphaeresis of the equinoxes, which is at most 71 first minutes, 22 second minutes, as can be seen in the Canon of Prosthaphæreses of the eighth sphere. Since therefore the points of the equinoxes are not fixed, motions more correctly begin from the head of the starry Aries, that is, a certain and fixed beginning in the eighth sphere. Then, however, they add the precession of the equinoxes to the discovered motions of the stars, so that their true places from the vernal equinox may be collected. Hence it is clear that before any computation of the wandering stars, it is necessary that we first find out by how many parts the apparent equinox has receded in precession from the first star of Aries. The form of the calculation is as follows: For the given time, correctly prepared, collect the equal motion of the mean vernal equinox, or the equal precession, by adding it with its Epoch. For in collecting equal motions, the Epochs of Christ God, or Alexander, or Caesar, or another beginning from which equal motions are derived, must always be prefixed. Then, with the same effort, extract the simple anomaly of the equinoxes, which, when doubled, you then collect the prosthaphæresis of the equinoxes from the convenient Canon of Prosthaphæreses, and correct it by the proportional part if necessary. And if the double anomaly is less than a semicircle, that is, 180 parts, then the Prosthaphæresis is subtracted from the equal motion of precession; if greater, the same Prosthaphæresis is combined with the equal motion of the precession of the equinoxes, so that in the aggregate or remainder the true and apparent precession of the equinoxes for the given time emerges. The rule for the addition or subtraction of the anomaly of the equinoxes therefore agrees with the rule for the addition and subtraction of the Solar Anomaly. According to these, the computation of our example will be as follows: Sexagesimal 1. P. 12. 22. Simple precession of the equinoxes. 0. 26. 50. 37. Simple anomaly of the equinoxes. 2. 46. 48. 3. Doubled anomaly. 5. 33. 36. 6. Corrected Prosthaphæresis. 0. 0. 31. 44. But since this exceeds the semicircle of the double anomaly, it must be added to the simple precession of the equinoxes, after which, the true precession of the equinoxes comes out as six parts, 27.22.12.21.22. The calculation of the solar motion, that is, the computation of the true or apparent solar motion. Erasmus, in his tables, provided two methods for computing the true motion of the Sun. In one, he uses the simple motion of the Sun, which is equally derived from the first point of the starry Aries; in the other, he assumes the motion of the Sun derived from the mean equinox, which he calls the composite motion of the Sun, because he only adds to the simple motion as much as the equal precession of the equinoxes. But we will briefly touch upon only the other method, in which equal motions flow from the first star of Aries, because it is common to the calculation of the subsequent stars. The method of calculation is as follows: FIRST, have these four ready: 1. The true precession of the equinoxes. 2. The simple anomaly of the equinoxes, both of which are taken from the preceding computation of the precession of the equinoxes. 3. The equal motion of the Sun. 4. The motion of the annual anomaly (with their respective established Epochs added in both cases). The motion of the Sun, which depends on the Apogee. SECOND, by means of the simple anomaly of the equinoxes, two things are drawn from the canon of the Prosthaphæreses of the Sun, namely, the prosthaphæresis of the center, and the proportional minutes. This prosthaphæresis of the center, or as it is commonly called, the equation of the argument, is nothing other than the difference between the mean and true Apogee, which, just as it is added before the semicircle of the annual anomaly of the Sun, so it is subtracted from it after the semicircle, to obtain the true and corrected anomaly of the Sun. THIRD, by means of the fourth anomaly, with one and the same entry into the table of the Prosthaphæreses of the Sun, these two are sought: namely, the equation or prosthaphæresis of the orb and the subsequent excess, of which the part corresponding to the proportional minutes reserved a little earlier must always be added to the prosthaphæresis of the orb, so that the absolute or corrected equation may emerge. Finally, if the equated anomaly of the Sun is less than a semicircle, then the corrected equation of the orb is subtracted from the equal motion of the Sun, so that the true place of the Sun from the head of the starry Aries may emerge. To this, if the true precession of the vernal equinox is finally added, discarding the entire cycle if necessary, the true place of the Sun from the apparent equinox will appear in signs, parts, and minutes. As in our assumed example: Sexagesimal P. 1a 2a 3a 4a True precession of the equinoxes 0 27 22 21 Simple anomaly of the equinoxes 2 46. 48 3 51.3 Equal simple motion 0 40.39 27 21 321 Annual anomaly 5 28.58 7 16:53: Prosthaphæresis of the center added 0 0 55 25 33.48. Proportional minutes 0 0 54.35.36 Equated anomaly 5:30 53 32 50 41 T2. The first equation of the orb added 0 0 52 21 38 49. Subsequent excess. 0 0 14 55 0 41. Proportional part of the excess 0 0 13 34 21 to be added. Equated equation of the orb added 0 0 52 34 13 10. Hence the true place from the head 0 41 32 1 34 2. And the true place from the equinox, 1 8 54 22 34 2 apparent. Therefore, the Sun at the time of the proposed nativity falls in 8 degrees, 54 minutes, 22 seconds of Gemini, which motion differs from the motion of Stoffler's ephemerides by almost 5 first minutes. The calculation of the lunar motion. First, these two are assumed from the preceding: 1. True precession of the equinox. 2. Equal simple motion. 1. Elongation of the Moon. Then, from the appropriate tables, two others are extracted: 1. Anomaly of the Moon, or as they call it, the mean argument. (1. Of the second epicycle, or as it is commonly called, the center of the Prosthaphæresis A or S. With these established, the longitude of the Moon from the Sun is immediately doubled, and by that double, that is, by the distance of the center of the epicycle from the Apogee, two things are sought: 2. Proportional minutes. Less is added. And if the doubled elongation, that is, if the center of the Moon is a semicircle, then the Prosthaphæresis of the second epicycle is subtracted from the lunar motion, so that the true motion may be obtained.

[Planetary Placement and Latitude]
158. The difference is filled, however, by observing the series and multitude of degrees. For example, in our case, the Sun's place is 8 degrees, 54 minutes of Gemini. Since this dodecatemorion is found in the eighth house, and its eighth or rather ninth part, following the cusp of the eighth and preceding the cusp of the ninth, falls into the eighth house, we must therefore also insert the Sun's place into the eighth house. And by the same reasoning, the other stars are to be inserted into the remaining stations of the heavens into which they fall, as can be seen in the figures described above. Others pursue these matters more subtly, taking into account not only the longitude but also the latitude of the stars. In this way, if stars endowed with some latitude are found in parts of the signs existing in the middle or around the middle of any house, they will be correctly placed in the same houses, just as if they lacked latitude. But if a planet has a significant latitude, and is moreover near the cusp of some house, artificers show that it can happen that it falls sometimes into the succeeding house, sometimes into the preceding one, due to its deviation to the sides. But those who wish to, should seek an exact judgment of this matter from the 21st problem of Monteregius's tables. To me, this care seems superfluous, and what is more, alien to Ptolemy's opinion. For since Ptolemy neglected the latitudes of the planets in directions, it is consistent that he much more disregarded them in the distribution of stars through the houses of the figure of the heavens, especially since he attributes power to the stars, as will appear more widely below, not from the place of latitude, but from the parts of the Ecliptic, which are defined by lines passing through the centers of the stars and through both poles of the Ecliptic. Just as Palilicium has power around the middle of the fourth part of Gemini, not because the orb of its rays extends so far, but because the line drawn through the center of the star and the poles of the Zodiac passes through the said point of the Ecliptic. And let what has been said here about the constitution of the celestial figure suffice. For concerning the Part of Fortune and other parts to be found, it will be said below in the fifth difference. The fourth part, which is from the ascendant to the midheaven, which is the twelfth, eleventh, and tenth house, is called the eastern, masculine, and approaching fourth part. It signifies the beginning of life; it is called puerile, sanguine, vernal. And the other part, which is from the midheaven to the degree of the western sign, that is, the seventh, which is above the circle of the western hemisphere, which is the ninth, eighth, and seventh house, is southern, feminine, receding; it signifies middle age; and is called the progression of youth, aestival, choleric. The third part also, which is from the west to the degree of the fourth house, which is above the meridian circle below the earth, which is the sixth, fifth, and fourth house, is western, masculine, approaching; it signifies the end of life; and is called autumnal, melancholic, and is senile; and is of middle age. The fourth part, which is from the fourth house to the ascendant, that is, the third, second, and ascendant house, is northern, feminine, receding. It signifies what happens to a person after their death, and to what their body will come, or its disposition from the substance left behind, or what will be said of them, namely, whether they will be praised or blamed. And this part is called senile, defective, phlegmatic, hiemal. And those two parts, which are from the midheaven to the ascendant, and from the ascendant to the fourth house, make up the half which is called the ascending half. The remaining parts, which are from the fourth house to the seventh, and thence to the midheaven, constitute the descending half. And whatever is above the earth from the circle, that is, the upper part of the circle, is called right; whatever lies below the earth, that is, the lower part, is called left. And the ascendant, fourth, seventh, and tenth are called angles, as it sounds more beautiful. And the second, eighth, fifth, and eleventh houses are called succedent to the angles. But the third, sixth, ninth, and twelfth are called cadent from the angles. Therefore, when a planet is in the angles or their succedents, it is said to prosper; and if it is in the cadent houses from the angles, it is said to decline. And each of these houses signifies something about human beings. This is the other member of the distribution of the fortuitous heaven. For after briefly touching upon the division of the heaven into unstable parts, he now intends to describe the familiarity of these parts with the subject matters. He begins this matter with the quadrants of the heaven, which are distinguished by the horizon and the meridian circle, and compares them, as he did above in the exposition of the stable parts of the heaven, to the seasons of the year, the regions of the world, sex, ages, and the four humors. Just as the diurnal or its equinoctial motion, which is the effector and constant commutator of these unstable parts, is opposed to the other motion proper to the stars traversing the zodiac: so also the numbering or series or order of the unstable quadrants of the heaven is opposed to the stable ones, and thence extends to the opposite part of the world. For the stable quadrants, of which we spoke above, which two circles passing through the poles of the zodiac and the cardinal points make, are numbered by consequent signs: but these, which the meridian and the setting encompass, by preceding ones. Hence it results that the quadrant which is from the east to the south is the first: that which is placed between the western horizon and the midheaven, the second: that which is between the west and the middle of the night, the third: finally, that which is concluded by the imum coeli and the eastern setting, the fourth. As for the sex of the quadrants, this author agrees with Ptolemy, for both make the first and third quadrants masculine, the second and fourth feminine. But Ptolemy's words on this matter are these: "And they also say that the stars are feminized and masculinized according to how they are configured with respect to the horizon. For they are masculine as if eastern in the quadrant from the east to the midheaven, and from the west to the imum coeli: but in the other two quadrants they are feminine as if western." Hence it is clear that artificers wish the energy of acting and the sex of the planets to change according to the ascent of the world to the meridian circle or its descent from the same. For first, from the ascendant to the midheaven, they always ascend higher, then from the meridian zenith, inclining towards the west, they descend: and again from the west, approaching the imum coeli, they ascend, and finally from here until they reach the eastern horizon, they descend. By this reasoning, the author also named the first and third quadrants approaching, and the other two receding. The work of the Moon clearly shows us that this way is true, for when it ascends to either meridian, it excites a great influx of marine waters: and conversely, when it descends from the meridian to the western or eastern horizon, it remits them and causes reflux. These things being so, the common error is clear, of calling the two continuous quadrants constituting the eastern half masculine, and the other two feminine because they are placed in the western half. For the half of the world distinguished by the eastern horizon is called the ascending half, and the opposite of this the descending, with respect to the vertical point alone, and not with respect to both meridians, namely the upper and the subterranean, which we particularly consider here. The knowledge of this matter is useful both for recognizing the strength of the stars and for judging the significators of masculine and feminine births. The first quadrant is eastern, the second southern, the third western.

[Ptolemy's Quadrants and Qualities]
The fourth is western, the fourth is northern, as Ptolemy also says. And there is no doubt about the eastern and western regions. But why is the perpetually hidden part of the sky attributed to the north, and the perpetually apparent part to the south, and vice versa, the perpetually apparent midheaven to the southern region, and the perpetually hidden part to the north? This is due to the similarity of qualities. For just as midday is the beginning of extreme heat, so from the southern part and from the torrid zone, the greatest heat proceeds. And midnight is the beginning of the greatest cold, the cause of which is also the north wind. However, the author, comparing these same quadrants to the seasons of the year, ages, and humors, leaves Ptolemy and follows the common and vulgar opinion on this matter. For he likens the first part of the world, situated between the eastern horizon and the midheaven, to spring, the second to summer, the third to autumn, and the fourth to winter. But Ptolemy, on the contrary, makes the first quadrant dry, the second hot, the third humid, and the fourth cold. Indeed, in the second and fourth quadrants, they almost agree, but in the first and third, they differ. For Ptolemy wants the eastern part to be not humid like spring, but rather dry, and the opposite part, which extends from the west to the imum coeli, not dry like autumn, but rather humid, so that the whole day is dry and the whole night is humid. His words are these: "Similarly, the fourth part of the horizon, the fourth of the angles, from which the winds blowing throughout the parts have their origins in the angles, the one towards the east is itself dry, because of the heat that occurs there, drying up the parts moistened by the night; and the winds blowing from it, which are commonly called Apeliotes, are dry and drying. The place towards the south is itself very hot, because of the fire of the midheaven and the inclination of our inhabited world towards the south; and the winds blowing from it, which are commonly called Notus, are hot and rarefying. The place towards the west is itself humid, because of the rain that occurs there, moistening the parts dried by the day; and the winds blowing from it, which are commonly called Zephyrus, are moist and humidifying. The place towards the north is itself very cold, because of the cold that occurs there."

[The Four Angles and Their Qualities]
In the same way, the four terminating angles are also distributed, from which the winds occupying those entire parts arise. The East indeed has a great deal of dryness, because when the Sun is there, the humidity excited during the night begins to dry up. And the winds blowing from there, which are commonly called subsolani, are dry and desiccating. But the southern region is very hot, both because of the Sun's inflammations in the middle of the sky, and because the Sun's culminations in the location of our regions incline most towards the south. The winds also blowing from there, which we generally call nothos, are hot and rarefying. However, the western place is humid, because when the Sun illuminates it, the humidity exhausted during the day then first begins to spread. The winds also erupting from there, which they call favonios, are soft and moist. But the northern place is cold, because, due to the position of our earth, it is very far from the Sun's culminations that produce heat, just as the Sun, while ascending the subterranean south, is very far from us. And the winds that blow from there, commonly called aquilones, are cold and congealing. From all these, both the tradition of the Arabs and Ptolemy concerning the nature of the four quarters of the world, as well as their agreement or disagreement, can be made manifest. But how this diversity of opinions is to be reconciled, or if it cannot be reconciled at all, how one opinion is to be rejected and the remaining one approved and defended by reasons consistent with nature, is not so clear or readily apparent; indeed, it seems very difficult. For it is not probable that Ptolemy, in these suddenly changeable parts of the sky, which are distinguished by the southern and terminating circles, departed without some grave cause from the usual order received from nature, which he faithfully observed not only in the four quarters of the year, but also in the configurations of other stars to the Sun. For just as he made spring hot and humid, summer hot and dry, autumn dry and cold, winter cold and humid, in the same way he afterwards taught that the stars from the first emergence of the Sun to the quadrangular configuration with the Sun are humid like spring; from the first quarter to the opposition, hot like summer; from the opposition to the second quarter, dry like autumn; and finally from there to the occultation, cold. This natural order plainly seems to require that in the same way the first of the four angles of the world be believed to be humid and hot; the second, hot and dry; the third, dry and cold; and finally the fourth, cold and humid. But Ptolemy not only neglects this law, but completely inverts it. For he makes the East, which according to this reasoning ought to be humid, dry, and he says the West, which by common tradition is dry, is humid. On the contrary, the Arabs seem not only to observe the customary order here, but also to be able to confirm their opinion by the authority of men most skilled in nature. For Hippocrates, and all his school of Asclepiads, compares the first quadrant of the day, which is contained by the eastern horizon and the terminator, to spring and sanguine humor; the second, to summer and bile; the third, to autumn and melancholic humor; the fourth, to winter and phlegm. To these, among many other things that can be objected to Ptolemy, this is also very serious, that he assigns only one quality to these parts of the day, and indeed in such a way that wherever you begin to enumerate the series of qualities, you are always thrown into an order repugnant to natural actions, namely from hot to dry, from dry to humid, from humid to cold, from cold to dry: which process is absurdly instituted against the law of natural actions. For we see matter first dissolved and slightly heated by ordered heat; then it becomes hot and dries out, and finally, having dried out, it becomes cold. Thus, bodies do not dry out because of cold, but they become cold because of dried-out humidity. And children do not become humid because they are hot, but because they are humid, they therefore become hot. These individual points can so disturb an unperturbed reader that he always doubts whether Ptolemy or the Arabs are more to be believed. Pontanus also, in his copious exposition of this passage, brought forward nothing but a bare exposition of the words. And Hali noted nothing worthy of observation here, except that he gathers from Ptolemy's words that the qualities of the southern region are changed into the qualities of the northern part by reason of the inhabitants. "Ptolemy," he says, "said very well when he said with respect to our dwellings: Because if we suppose that there is some population in the southern part, the north will be to those who dwell there in the manner that the south is to us. Because to us the south is hot and burning with respect to our dwellings, because the Sun is in the direct part of that region all year, and it makes many reverberations of rays." Only Cardanus do I find to have truly looked into the matter in this place. "The meaning of Ptolemy's words," he says, "is that such a time, such a part of the sky, and such a wind, which has its origin from the east to the south, is dry in quality and action, not in substance, nor does it couple another quality to it. The reason is that in things of short duration, one must consider actions, which are vehement, and these produce qualities, not substances. For substance requires concoction, concoction requires time, but time is short and very small: Therefore, planets and fixed stars change qualities, not substances, in parts of the day and sky. Therefore, it is not inconvenient for an action to pass from dry to hot, or from hot to humid. But here perhaps someone will doubt how primary qualities, insofar as they are substances, are to be distinguished from primary qualities, which are accidents and impressions, that is, how, for example, hot is sometimes transferred to a quality, and sometimes to a body or substance. I answer that the names of qualities are sometimes transferred anomymously or denominatively to the whole body from inherent qualities. For we call a body hot according to substance, a subject body receiving alternately and by turns contrary qualities, that is, heat, coldness, humidity, and dryness. Hence, when we name much or little hot, we refer to the quantity of the substance. For example, if there are two equal vessels, but filled with unequally tempered liquid of water and wine: then we would rightly say that one vessel contains more wine and more heat, and the other more water and less heat. For wine is hot by its nature. It is similar if two vessels are unequal in magnitude, but nevertheless are supposed to be filled with equally tempered liquid. But indeed, when we look not at the abundance of substance or subject, but only at the quality, we say that there is more heat in the former, and less in the latter, which must be understood differently. For example, if two equal vessels are filled with unequally heated liquid, then we would say that the hotter one contains more heat, and the colder one less. And here there is a clear catachresis in the word 'more', if indeed properly and better 'more' or 'less', 'more vehemently' or 'more weakly', and similar words are accommodated to qualities: but 'little' and 'much' are rightly observed only in the quantity of the substance. Similarly, it is manifest that both substances and qualities are subject to alterations. For to speak first of qualities, the substance of oil now receives heat infused from continuous motion or elsewhere; now again, due to rest or otherwise, the quality of heat is expelled and it becomes cold. This same substance, when it consists of a vaporous, oily, and well-concocted air, if subjected to fire, feeds a tranquil flame. But if, striving to preserve the entire quantity of the substance, one restores the parts of the oil consumed by heat through continuous addition of watery parts, that substance will always become worse, and the flame, due to the impure and crude matter acceding, will multiply its crackling, and finally

[The Nature of Heat and Cold]
The concocted parts will be extinguished by the crudity of the unconcocted parts. This natural action is also observed in our bodies. The substance sustaining vital heat exists in its purest form from the beginning, for it consists of well-concocted seminal and watery menstrual fluid, and for this reason, it participates in very little earthly substance. But since it is necessary for the parts consumed by vital heat to be continuously restored, and indeed for the underlying substance itself to be amplified, and those parts, daily consumed through the organs of nutrition and added to the bodies, so that the lost may be restored, are more impure and more earthy, it is clear that the substance or subject of that vital and innate heat is continuously attenuated, until at last, having become completely earthy and desiccated, it denies nourishment to the vital flame, and renders the body itself subject to death. Hence Hippocrates rightly stated, in Aphorism 14, that children are warmer with innate heat than those who are in the vigor of youth or have fallen into old age. Lycus, therefore, by transferring the word 'hot' to quality, falsely slandered Hippocrates, contending to show that a more vehement heat is present in a consistent age than in childhood. For Hippocrates did not deny this here either, who did not refer to the quality, but to the substance of heat, and wished the bodies of children to possess not a sharper quality of heat, but a greater abundance of innate substance, and one apt to sustain vital heat. For, as we just said, those who are in a consistent age have a more earthy body, and although it may be very vehemently hot, it has very little of the substance of innate heat; otherwise, we would say that those seized by a feverish heat have a great deal of innate heat, when they not only do not have more, but not even an equal amount to the former. Just as in our bodies, so also in celestial bodies, we must consider and understand these alterations of both substances and qualities. And indeed, the substances of celestial bodies, just as they consist of the multitude, density, and purity of light, so they are varied by the same difference of light. Qualities, however, proceed either from the strength or weakness of substances, or from a similar quality: just as heat proceeds from much heat or from hotness; cold, however, from little heat or coldness. But someone might say, how can heat produce coldness? I believe that those who diligently adhered to Aristotle's doctrine of the nature of things could never extricate themselves from these ambiguities, especially Cardano, who for that reason, having abandoned the chorus of Aristotelians, presented the nature of qualities differently: There are, he says, only two qualities: one active and celestial, and it is called heat, since in the stars nothing exists besides heat, to which coldness is opposed, which is nothing else than the privation of heat. The other quality, namely humidity, is not in the heavens, but in the globe of the elements. For humid and dry are the affections of the subject bodies. To humidity, however, dryness is opposed as a privation. Therefore, a hot body is one that is compacted from abundant light; a cold one, however, where there is little light and heat. And the body of the elements is humid, which abounds in well-concocted vapor; and dry, which is deprived of that humid substance. But every star, by its light, produces heat and tenuity, or humidity, in the subject matter, so that you may consider and understand that cold and dry in the actions of the stars are not qualities contrary to hot and dry, but names of the measure of hot and humid. For example, the Sun, with its abundant, dense, and clear light, significantly heats the air. If it mixes its light strongly with the light of the Moon, the air will no longer seem to us to grow warmer, but rather colder, just as hot water strongly mixed with lukewarm water reduces the intensity of heat. From these things, you can understand that it is not contrary to nature for cold to arise from little heat. But humidity comes from much humid or from a humid quality. And finally, dryness is derived from little humid in the substance or from a dry quality. From what has been said, it can be understood how the substance of the stars, by the cognate temperament of the signs in which they remain longer, is strengthened, diluted by a contrary crasis; and how the same stars in the angles of the world, which they constantly change due to the rapid conversion of the world, can intensify or remit not the substance, but only some quality. Hence it follows that one and the same star, at one and the same time, can be said to be humid and dry with respect to different aspects. Cardano gives such an example of this. Taurus is a very temperate sign, having much hot and humid, because it is a vernal and middle sign: yet a planet existing in it will be called dry, if it is found in the eastern part of the sky, but dry according to quality, that is, one born under such a planet, although it may have much natural heat and vital humidity, which is the same as its substance: yet that humid will be drier and not so pure nor so fervent according to quality, as would be the humid of one born in Taurus setting. On the contrary, one born under a planet in Libra setting has purer and more sincere humid according to quality, yet it will not be so dense or abundant, but, so to speak, watery. And in this way, Ptolemy indicates that the powers of the planets are to be distinguished by the familiarity of the signs and angles, in these words, with which he concludes the chapter on the general constitution of the signs and the nature of the four angles: "The understanding of these things is useful for judging the temperaments in every way. For it is clear that such positions, whether of the signs, or of the Moon, or of the angles, somehow affect the productive power of the stars. And if they are in their proper positions, they have a more unmixed quality and a stronger inherent power, as those that are hot by nature in hot places, and those that are humid in humid places; but in contrary positions, mixed and weaker, as those that are hot in cold places, and those that are humid in dry places, and similarly in others according to analogy, and the quality is mixed with a little mixture." That is, the knowledge of these things is useful for judging.

[Planetary Qualities and Effects]
It is easy to understand that the very efficacy of the stars varies in such constitutions of the four seasons, ages, or angles. And indeed, in constitutions congruent with the quality, they acquire a purer and greater efficacy, as in hot things, those that heat, and in moist things, those that moisten. In alien things, however, they are weakened by the admixture of diversity, as in cold things, those that heat, and in dry things, those that moisten, and similarly in others according to the proportion and mixture of qualities. Furthermore, the author seems to be reciting certain opinions rather than establishing doctrine regarding the ages with the quadrants (οικιώod or familiarity). For while some teach that the entire life of a human is contained in the three prior quadrants, so that the first quadrant signifies the first age, the second the middle, the third the end and last part of life, and finally the fourth signifies fame following life: others, however, accommodate the same period of life, divided into four spaces, to the quadrants in the same order: he seems to mix or insinuate both opinions here. But neither of these opinions agrees with the doctrine of Ptolemy, who, although he states that the entire state of life is contained not only in the complete revolution of the world but also in the revolution of individual planets, nevertheless seems to divide the entire life of a human by the ratio of quarters, neither into thirds nor quadrants, but at least into halves, so that the two eastern quadrants, and these compared both to the Sun and to the ascendant, obtain half of life, and the remaining two western quadrants obtain the remaining half of life. For example, in the Moon, two quarters from the new moon and full moon signify half of life, while the remaining quarters opposite these signify the remaining half of life. Similarly, it must be said of the three superior planets. Venus and Mercury, however, although they never come to opposition with the Sun, nevertheless measure the entire life in one period in a small circle, which he calls an epicycle. For their conjunction with the Sun, which occurs while the planet is retrograde, takes the place of the opposition of the three superior planets. But their true synod with the Sun occurs in the upper part of the epicycle where they are swiftest. Ptolemy wrote in this sense in several places, and in the third Apotelesmatic, immediately at the beginning before the treatise on parents, from the position of the significators in the quadrants.

[House Meanings and Triplicity Lords]
Look at which of them is stronger in essence and place; this one you will rightly make the author of substance and the significator of acquisition. If it is in the midheaven, it will find this from the king; and if it is in the house of faith, or rather trust, it is better. Similarly, the first lord of the triplicity gives substance at the beginning of life, the second in the middle of life, and the third at its end. The third house is of brothers and sisters and relatives and loved ones, of faith and religion, of commands and legacies, of changes and minor journeys: and it signifies the essence of life before death. Alendezgod said: The first lord of the triplicity of the house of brothers signifies older brothers, the second middle ones, the third younger ones, and their dignity and their essence will be according to their places. The fourth house is of fathers, inheritances, the end of things and treasures, and all hidden and secret things, and it signifies the end of the essence of human life. Alendezgod said that the first lord of the triplicity of the house of fathers signifies fathers, the second cities and lands, the third the ends of things and prisons. The fifth house is of children and affections, legacies and donations: and it signifies what will happen after death, namely praise and blame. Alendezgod said that the first lord of the triplicity of children signifies children and life, the second affection, the third messengers or envoys. The sixth house is of illnesses and servants, and it signifies the end of life, and whatever will happen before old age. Alendezgod said, the first lord of the triplicity of the house of illnesses signifies illnesses and health, from illnesses and from evils, and deteriorations: the second signifies household slaves and servants: the third signifies what will happen from them, and their usefulness and works: and it is the significator of beasts and cattle, and all quadrupeds, and their strength, also their multitude and scarcity, their manner in his hand or their departure from that hand: also imprisonment and retention. The seventh house is of women, marriages, contentions also and partnerships and opponents. And it signifies the middle of the end of life towards old age. Alendezgod said that the first lord of the triplicity of the seventh house signifies women, the second contentions, the third mixtures and partnerships. The eighth house is of death, it signifies fear and death and Almauerith, that is, the substances or inheritances of the dead, which the heirs ought to possess after their death: and it signifies the end of the years of life after old age. Alendezgod said that the first lord of the triplicity of the house of death signifies death, the second precepts and ancient things, the third Almauerith, that is, what is to be inherited from the dead. The ninth house is of peregrinations, journeys, faith and religion, wisdom, philosophy and books, also letters and legacies, narratives or rumors and dreams: and it signifies the beginning of half of life. Alendezgod said that the first lord of the triplicity of peregrination signifies peregrination and everything that happens to it: the second signifies faith and religion and their health and their manner: the third is the significator of wisdom, dreams and stars and auguries and truth, those who practice them, and falsehood. The tenth house is of the queen, and of works, of exaltation or elevation, also of kingdom and memory and voice, that is, of empire, and of magistracies and mothers, and it signifies half of the years of life. Alendezgod said that the first lord of the triplicity of the royal house signifies work and exaltation, namely of the seat, elevation and the highest dwelling: the second signifies the voice of empire and boldness in the same: the third signifies its stability and durability. The eleventh house is of trust and praise and fortune, also of friends and ministers and helpers. And it signifies the end of the years of the middle of life. Alendezgod said that the first lord of the triplicity of the house of trust signifies trust: the second friends: the third their usefulness or profits. The twelfth house is of enemies and labor and sadness, envy and whispering, the quality of talents and beasts, and it signifies the end of life, and what happens to mothers in their conception, good or bad. Alendezgod said, the first lord of the triplicity of the house of enemies signifies enemies, the second labors, the third beasts and cattle. These are what the twelve houses signify. We will consider the strengths of the houses or the twelve stations of the sky in three different ways here. First, the places of the sky themselves have greater strength the more amply and purely they are illuminated. For this reason, the tenth house holds the first place, the first the second, the seventh the third, the eleventh the fourth, the ninth the fifth, and there is no doubt about these. The twelfth holds the sixth, because it ascends, the eighth the seventh, the second the eighth, because it is in the morning twilight. For example, in Cologne, where the northern pole is elevated 51 degrees, with the beginning of Aries in the ascendant, the cusp of the second house falls at 24 degrees of Taurus, and beyond two hours, 29 degrees of the same Taurus rises. Since this is the time of morning twilight, it is clear that the part of the second house, which is from 24 degrees of Taurus to 29 degrees of the same Taurus, is contained in the morning twilight. Therefore, stars found in the second house acquire strength both because of the position of the second house in the crepuscular light, and indeed primarily because the second house is in a trine radiation with the tenth house, which, being the ruler of the sky, has the greatest strength. Therefore, Ptolemy also attributes great strength to it, although in his region, due to the very small elevation of the pole, a very small part of it is contained in the morning twilight. The sixth house holds the ninth place, because it is in a trine radiation with the tenth, and everywhere it is enclosed in the evening twilight. All other houses, which are below the earth and from the end of the second to the beginning of the sixth, are contained in light, and for this reason lack strength in themselves. Here, if someone asks why all strength is taken away from the fifth house, since it seems to embrace a part of the twilight no less than the second. To this it must be answered that the fifth house, being unconnected to the tenth, and not associated with any scheme, and that twilight, as it is evening and ending in darkness, is far weaker, and finally the part that is contiguous to the sixth is not the beginning of the fifth, but rather the end, due to such weakness it is rightly neglected. However, since Cardanus says it is placed in light, it has some strength, but not to be considered.

[The First House and Nurturing]
The DIFFERENCE, as the Arabs are accustomed to infer and pronounce concerning the outcome from each house, according to their opinion, signified by the affection and disposition of the triplicity rulers in general. The first house signifies 'anabibes', that is, nourishment, life, and the health of the body, both good and bad. Therefore, to understand, for example, what pertains to education and nourishment, they inquire into the lords of the triplicity of the first house, that is, of the sign that ascended in the nativity. If it was a diurnal nativity, they consider the diurnal triplicity ruler; if a nocturnal nativity, the nocturnal triplicity ruler in the first place, then the next one, and finally the participant. From the position of all of them in the celestial chart, they infer about good or bad nourishment. For if all are found in angular houses and are strong, they declare a robust life and strong nourishment for the first four years. But if some occupy angular or succedent houses, and others have fallen from angular houses, then each triplicity ruler signifies particular thirds of nourishment, taken in proportion to the order of the triplicity rulers. For to the first triplicity ruler is attributed the first third of nourishment, to the second the second, and to the third the third. As in the example of the superior diurnal nativity, in which the primary lord of the triplicity in the western angle indicates the first third of nourishment to be safe. This is followed by Jupiter, and this by Saturn, both in the succedent place, namely the eighth, in the celestial chart of equal houses. Therefore, they also declare the second and third quarters of nourishment to be safe, although weaker. But if all were cadent and afflicted, and the nativity was diurnal, they look to the triplicity rulers of the Sun's place; if nocturnal, to the triplicity rulers of the Moon's place, in the way I have already said concerning the ascendant. And if even here sufficient causes for nourishment do not appear, they resort to the place of the conjunction or opposition immediately preceding the nativity. Finally, they look to Jupiter and Venus, one of which, if found in angular or succedent houses, free from evils and impediments, promises nourishment to the native. To this place belongs the statement of Hermes 72: 'Take what pertains to education from the triplicity rulers of the ascendant; what pertains to life, from the triplicity rulers of the conditioned luminary.'

[Hermes' Dictum on Life and Nurturing]
The dictum has two parts. The first is about finding the time of nourishment, whether it is granted by heaven to the native to be nourished or not nourished. The second is about those things that are sought after the time of nourishment is known, and which pertain to the entire life decreed by the stars. What pertains to nourishment must be sought from the triplicity rulers of the ascendant sign, as I have just said. For since the ascendant is the beginning of our life, the beginning of education is not absurdly referred to the lords of its triplicity. But what pertains to the rest of the entire life, if a longer life is granted to the native, so that you may know, you must similarly contemplate the triplicity rulers of the place of the conditioned luminary. The luminary is called 'aigale' or 'conditioned' that which follows the condition of the time. For just as the Sun is more potent and effective by day, so is the Moon by night. Therefore, if the nativity was diurnal, the triplicity rulers of the Sun's place must be looked at; if nocturnal, the triplicity rulers of the Moon's place. If all of these are fortunate, strong, received, in good places and their dignities, they decree prosperity and riches for the native throughout all the days of his life. For example, in the following example of Albohal's nocturnal nativity, the first triplicity ruler of the Moon's place, traversing the sign of Scorpio, is Mars, the second is Jupiter, the third is Saturn, all in angular houses. Therefore, the native of this constellation was always fortunate and eventually rose to the greatest eminence.

[Nocturnal Nativity and Life Status]
Nocturnal nativity with Scorpio ascending. If one was fortunate, but the others unfortunate, the fortunate one promises substance and riches to the native during the age distributed to him; the unfortunate ones, however, threaten calamity and indigence during the age divided to them. The first triplicity ruler signifies the beginning of life, or rather the first third part of a person's life; the second administers the second; and finally, the third administers the third third of a person's life. As in the following nocturnal nativity, where the Part of Fortune falls in the horoscope at 2 degrees, 30 minutes of Libra, and the positions of the planets are as you see, the status of life is signified by the triplicity rulers of the Moon's place traversing the sign of Cancer, whose first triplicity ruler was Mars, the second Jupiter, both cadent in the sixth and third houses, and thus they threaten a bad state of life for the native. But the Moon, which was the luminary of the time, placed in the midheaven, and moreover the last lord of the triplicity, and of the nature of Venus, decrees beauty and good fortune for the native at the end of life. For when the triplicity rulers of the conditioned luminary are from angular houses...

[Ptolemy vs. Arabs on Horary Astrology]
From these examples, a clear disagreement between Ptolemy and the Arabs is evident regarding three major points. First, in the structure and distribution of the heavens. For the Arabs, dividing the zodiac into twelve equal parts, follow an equal method. On the contrary, Ptolemy divides not the zodiac, but the equinoctial into equal dodecatemoria, and with respect to the zodiac, he creates plainly unequal domiciles, as has been extensively explained above. Secondly, it is clear that the Arabs, for determining the place of the Part of Fortune in diurnal nativities, cast it from the Sun to the Moon, but in nocturnal nativities, they cast it from the distance from the ascendant, contrary to what Ptolemy does. Ptolemy universally prescribes that in both diurnal and nocturnal nativities, the distance from the Sun to the Moon should be taken, and that it should be counted from the ascendant, so that the end of the counting shows the place of the Part of Fortune. In the preceding example, since it is a nocturnal nativity, the place of the Part of Fortune, according to the opinion of the Arabs, is noted at the fifth part of Taurus, while according to Ptolemy's calculation, it falls at the beginning of Aquarius. Thirdly, it appears from a comparison of the precepts that the Arabs derive indications of effects from lighter causes, while Ptolemy derives them from more potent and stronger causes. For example, here, where the danger of nourishment is questioned, Ptolemy considers the principal causes of life, namely the affliction of the luminaries and cardinal points by malefic stars. The Arabs, on the contrary, do not so much consider the luminaries themselves, as the condition of the luminaries and the relationship of the triangulators of the ascendant to the angles. Similarly, we will find that Ptolemy considered only the more potent and stronger causes for all other effects, omitting lighter conjectures. Indeed, he not only omits these idle questions and empty lots, but also often severely criticizes them. From what we have said about the first house, it is easy to judge and understand the author's narration concerning the other houses. For the triangulators of the other houses and their significations are judged no differently than those of the first house. For example, in marriage, which they attribute to the seventh house, the triangulators of the seventh place and Venus are considered; in journeys, however, the triangulators of the ninth house and the place of Mars; and similarly in other matters, other triangulators are considered, as is obvious to anyone in the books of the Arabs.

[Colors of the Twelve Houses]
The twelve houses also signify colors, and these are they: For the ascending or first and seventh houses are white; the second and twelfth are green; the third and eleventh are saffron; the fourth and tenth are red; the fifth and ninth are honey-colored, that is, they have the color of honey; the sixth and eighth are black.

[Joys of the Planets in Houses]
And each planet has a certain power in each of these houses, from the accidental powers, which is called joy. Because Mercury rejoices in the ascendant, the Moon in the third house, Venus also in the fifth, Mars in the sixth, the Sun in the ninth, Jupiter in the eleventh, Saturn in the twelfth. Here, he also assigns colors to the houses of the sky. Just as Saturn agrees particularly with blackness, Jupiter and Venus with whiteness, Mars with redness, etc., according to their temperament; so also he assigns whiteness to the horizon, and thus consequently always two celestial stations equally distant from the horizon to one color, namely the second and twelfth to green, the third and eleventh to saffron, the fourth and tenth to red, the fifth and ninth to honey-colored, the sixth and eighth to black. They say that knowledge of this is useful for knowing which colors of clothes, horses, and such things will be fortunate or unfortunate for the native. For cusps illuminated by benefics and free from malefics declare fortunate colors, but damaged ones declare unfortunate colors. But Ptolemy never mentions this in his Apotelesmatics. However, what he says, that each of the planets has a certain accidental power in some of the said houses, which is called joy, can be excused by reasons and deduced from Ptolemy's sayings. For first, since Mercury is the significator of the mind, which most strongly affects the mind in the ascendant, it is rightly said to rejoice in the ascendant. Then, since the third house is of short journeys, the Moon, which is itself wandering and unstable, will not absurdly be considered familiar, although for this reason it seems more appropriate to the ninth and especially the seventh: for placed there, it wonderfully incites the native's mind to undertake journeys. The fifth house loves Venus, the second and prolific star, because from this place alone the Arabs primarily infer the number and quality of children. Ptolemy also does not neglect this in this matter, but attributes more important parts to the tenth and eleventh. The sixth place is that it is the place of health and misfortune. The ninth place is attributed to the Sun, because both signify religion. The eleventh house signifies fortune, and is called by Ptolemy agathodaimon or agathopoios daimon: therefore, Jupiter is attributed to it, just as the twelfth, which is unfortunate, and for that reason is called by Ptolemy kakodaimon or kakos daimon.

[Strength of the Angles]
And it is said in the signification of the houses that the angles signify strength and perfection, while those falling from the angles signify weakness and detriment, except that the ninth and third signify an open and revealed matter with fame, while the twelfth and sixth signify concealment and retention and worthlessness of things. But the angles and the lords of the angles signify greatness of honors, value, and fortune, strength and exercise, and prolongation from fall. And their presence in cadent houses is contrary to fortune, that is, disgrace and fall. Concerning the succedent houses of the angles, the house that succeeds the tenth, that is, the eleventh, signifies strength and moderate fortune of friends, and from that part in which there was trust. And that which follows the fourth, that is, the fifth, signifies moderate fortune through gifts and venerations, and the cause of children with veneration, joy, and gladness. But that which succeeds the ascendant, which is the second, similarly signifies moderate fortune from the cause of substance and servants. But that which succeeds the seventh, that is, the eighth, similarly signifies moderate fortune from Almauerith, that is, from substance inherited from the dead and hidden things.

[House Strength and Planetary Power]
This entire chapter can be understood from what was said a little earlier about the strengths of the celestial stations. The angles are the strongest, to which the succedent houses approach most closely in strength. However, the cadent houses signify weakness and detriment, except that, as the author says, the ninth house with the third signifies an open and revealed matter with fame: for they are, as he said a little earlier, the joys of the luminaries. Similarly, the twelfth and sixth are said to signify concealment and worthlessness of things, because they are the joys of the infortunes. Therefore, he makes three states of strength in the houses: the angles are the strongest; the places succeeding the angles have moderate strength; and the cadent places are entirely devoid of strength. Among these primary states, there are infinite intermediate ones, the judgment of which is left to the sagacity of the prudent Astrologer. Some have tried to explain the variety of states in individual houses by this kind of similitude. A planet in the first part of an angle or even another house has its total power, which is like 8. But when it is distant from the cusp by 5 parts, it loses one power; if it is distant by 10 parts, it loses two; if by 15 parts, it loses 3; if by 20, it loses 4; if by 25, it loses 6. Furthermore, the remaining 5 degrees partake of the nature of the following house. For each house consists of 30 parts, namely 5 parts before the beginning of the house, and 25 parts immediately following the cusp, and this, if we follow Ptolemy, is to be understood primarily of the parts of the equinoctial circle, as we said above.

[Signification of Angle Lords]
These are also the significations of the lords of the angles when they are present in the angles. The presence of the lord of the ascendant in the ascendant signifies his fortune through himself and through his acquisition: and they signify the presence.

[Lord of the Tenth House]
Its presence in the tenth house signifies higher offices through the king; when it is in the seventh, through conventions, agents, and wives; its presence in the fourth also signifies fortune through inheritances and the affairs of fathers, and through the production of waters and populations or plantations, that is, buildings, from ancient and radical things. The lord of the tenth house also signifies, through its presence in the same tenth house, fortune either through the king or through a great kingdom and higher offices; and through its presence in the seventh, it signifies a kingdom through victory in contentions arising from the affairs of wives; and through its presence in the fourth, it signifies a kingdom through the friendship of the king's nobles, and through the affairs of tribunes, and through the king's dispensers, and the cultivation of lands, and the building of cities, and through the division of rivers, and the guardianship of cities, and from ancient things; and through its presence in the ascendant, it signifies a kingdom through ingenuity and through the king's proximity to the affairs of the common people. The presence of the lord of the seventh in the seventh signifies fortune through negotiations and conventions; also through nourishment and women, and through agents; its presence in the fourth signifies through conventions of women and through negotiations, and through the affairs of fathers, inheritances, and cultivation of the land; its presence in the ascendant through conventions and negotiations, through the affairs of medicine and astronomy, and through spiritual works, geniuses, and similar things; and its presence in the tenth signifies fortune through conventions, negotiations, and through wives and through the king's affairs. The presence of the lord of the fourth in the fourth signifies fortune from the cultivation of the land and its fruit, through the affairs of fathers, and ancient things; its presence in the ascendant signifies fortune from the cultivation of the land and its fruit through ingenuity and depth of counsel; and its presence in the tenth signifies profit from the cultivation of the land and its fruit through the affairs of the king and magistrates. And its presence in the seventh signifies fortune from the cultivation of the land and its fruits through the affairs of wives, and sisters, and through negotiations. These are what the lords of the angles signify by their presence in their own angles. You will do similarly for the presence of the lords of the other houses, and we have therefore introduced only the lords of the angles, so that they might be an example for the others. In this chapter, the decrees of the lords of the angles, if they are found present in those very angles, are recited in plain and extensive discourse, so that this may teach, as it were by example, that the universal governors or significators of things, as they say, are to be determined not only according to their own nature, but also according to the property of all the celestial stations in which they are found. For greater clarity, let one of the author's examples be repeated. He says that the lord of the fourth, if it is powerful, signifies fortune from agriculture and the other fruits of the earth.
