Gemini: 9, 10, 22, 23, 27. Cancer: 7, 12, 15, 24, 27, 30. Leo: 10, 27. Virgo: 7, 12, 22, 24, 28. Libra: 1, 12, 17, 22, 24, 29. Scorpio: 4, 9, 24, 27, 28. Sagittarius: 2, 12, 17, 26, 30. Capricorn: 5, 12, 17, 23, 26, 30. Aquarius: 2, 6, 13, 15, 22, 23, 28. Pisces: 2, 5, 16, 13, 8. I believe that bright, dark, smoky, empty, putrid, and similar places derive their names from the presence or absence of bright stars: which, since they pass into other places through the motion of the eighth and ninth spheres, it is easy to judge how much importance they can have in judgments, if they are accommodated to such dodecatemoria partitions, and not to the eighth sphere. CONCERNING AZEMENA DEGREES, that is, degrees of bodily debility. And there are certain degrees in the signs that are called azemena degrees, that is, degrees of bodily debility. For azemena is a certain temporary bodily debility, such as deafness, blindness, loss of a limb, and other such things, which a person will always have as long as they live, unless miraculously healed. Therefore, when the Moon is in these degrees in the nativity of a child, the aforementioned azemena will happen to them according to the signification of the place and aspects or places of the planets. Azemena is also signified by the stars in various ways, as you will find in books of nativities. The azemena degrees are these, which are described in this table. Aries: 6, 7, 8, 9, 10. Taurus: 9, 10, 11, 12, 13, 14, 15. Gemini: 18, 27, 28. Cancer: 19, 29. Leo: 1, 7, 8, 18, 19. Virgo: 10, 20, 27, 28, 29. Libra: 18, 19. The word azemena signifies the same to the Arabs as 'vitium' to the Greeks, that is, a defect or injury and not a disease: for these, according to Ptolemy, are separate. For, he says, a defect is inflicted once and corrupts a limb once, and does not subsequently bring an intensification of suffering, such as blindness, deafness, lameness, and such things, which, once inflicted, cause pain initially, but never afterwards. A disease, however, brings either continuous or reciprocal suffering to the body: for there are two kinds of diseases, of which reciprocal ones are those that both depart and return at intervals, such as asthma, epilepsy, gout: non-reciprocal ones, such as pleurisy, dysentery, strangury, and similar ones, all of which are of such a nature that they can be separated from the body, and while present, continuously torment the body. On the contrary, defects are inseparable and do not afflict after the initial onset. Such things are said to be affected here when the azemena part falls into the ascendant cardine, especially if the Moon is conjoined to it or to the lord of the horoscoping sign, and indeed in that limb of the native which is subject to the same sign. Likewise, if in someone's nativity, Saturn is found in an azemena degree, there will be a defect in the right eye; if the Moon, in the left. But I advise consulting and listening to Ptolemy concerning the causes that produce defects and diseases. CONCERNING DEGREES THAT INCREASE FORTUNE. And in this circle, there are certain degrees that are said to increase fortune, which are described in this table. Aries: 19. Taurus: 3, 15. Gemini: 6. Cancer: 1, 3. Leo: 2, 3, 7. Virgo: 5, 14, 20. Libra: 7, 18, 20. Scorpio: 3, 20. Sagittarius: 4, 15. Capricorn: 12, 13, 19. Aquarius: 7, 14, 16, 17, 20. Pisces: 13, 20. Hali says that the degrees that increase fortune are those in which fortune and the luminaries are exalted, such as the 19th part of Aries, the 3rd part of Taurus, the 27th part of Pisces, although the author's table has the 20th part of Pisces: and those in which the bounds of both benefics are joined: such as the 13th part of Sagittarius, and the 13th part of Pisces: and those that are the terms of fortunes, and finally those with which fixed stars of a benefic nature are connected. But it seems more in line with Ptolemy's doctrine to judge those parts in which the bounds of both benefics are joined, and as it were, mix their natures, to be the most fortunate. Just as the beginning of the seventh part of Aries is the beginning of Venus's term, and the end of Jupiter's term, similarly, to the letter, there are unfortunate parts in which the bounds of both malefics cohere, as is evident in the following descriptions, of which the former is derived from Ptolemy's terms, the latter from the Egyptian terms.

[Sun and Moon in Nativity]
Each of the remaining planets differs in the quality of its nativity, just like the Sun and Moon.

[Qualities of Nativity]
Each of the remaining qualities of nativity differs, as do the Sun and Moon.

[Planetary Influence on Appearance]
The Western planet will make the color black, with sparse bodies, simple and flat hair, black eyes, small, somewhat thin, and of moderate limb proportion, but with a colder and drier temperament. Similarly, each of the other planets also tempers all these things according to its nature, when they are the rulers of the said places of the Sun. But if several rulers are found together, that which is formed from the mixture of different natures must be prudently gathered here and everywhere. For if they agree harmoniously in signifying, we will simply increase the signified things; if they disagree, then we must consider their strengths. For if they are unequal, judgment must be made according to the nature of the victor and the excess by which it surpasses the contrary significator: but if they are equal, they will mutually cancel each other out and decide nothing. Unless we hold and follow this method of intensification and remission, we will never be able to grasp the true strengths and true effects of the stars. The same Saturn, he says, signifies depth of counsel, and the rest that follows there. Again, it must be established that Saturn only effects this if he is the principal ruler of the qualities of the places assigned to the soul, that is, of the places. For the power that is proper to the mind and reasoning depends on the place of Mercury; but morals and lower powers depend on the place of the Moon. Just as the motions of the soul are varied and manifold, so they are not apprehended by one simple consideration, but by many and varied conjectures derived from the difference of the signs containing the places and their rulers, and from the configurations of the rulers themselves, both to the ascendant and to the centers: which could not be conveniently treated in this place, but are copiously and diligently described by Ptolemy in the third book, chapter on the quality of the soul. He calls 'magisterium' actions, that is, actions, study, craft or exercise, by the profession of which men are accustomed to lead their lives and acquire reputation. In this question, we first consider whether the native will have any 'magisterium', or whether he will live idly without a profession. Contemplating the planet, and he who, casting off the Sun's rays, rises nearest before the Sun, and he who occupies the midheaven, and if both happen to one planet, so that the preceding Sun first casts off its rays, and holds the midheaven, he alone will be the master of actions and arts, and especially if he has also applied himself to the Moon: similarly, when neither has both, one must be content with him who has either, that is, who has either first cast off the Sun's rays, or has stood in the midheaven. But if different planets have different conditions from these, as if one first casts off the Sun's rays, and the other is either in the midheaven or conjoined with the Moon, both or all are to be taken into counsel: however, we will give the first parts to him who has obtained more votes, and will rule the place of the midheaven. But if no one suitable for this office is found, neither seen in the morning, nor placed in the midheaven, we must take the lord of the midheaven, so that we may know the 'magisterium' to which the native will be called. For Ptolemy, as it were, despises him who separates from the Moon, and prefers to him the one who holds the place of the culminating planet, that is, the lord of the midheaven. But such, for whom no planet first casts off its rays, nor is placed in the midheaven, are often born inactive, that is, either entirely devoid of exercise, or certainly not treating art remarkably or diligently. In this way, after we have recognized the star suitable for forming the actions and craft of the native, we then immediately inquire what kind of actions these will be. This, however, is not taken, as above, from any planet, but only from the proper power of the three stars, namely, Mercury, Venus, and Mars. For the two superior heavy planets, namely Saturn and Jupiter, are rightly excluded from determining the quality of the craft, since every craft desires dexterity of actions, and abhors the slowness and such delay with which they complete their orbits. The luminaries also recede from such dominion, both because of their nobility and because of their common nature. Therefore, the quality of the craft was to be ascribed to the remaining three planets. And since there are three principal kinds of crafts, Mars, the superior, massive, and crude planet, will suitably preside over the laborious and rough work of farmers and blacksmiths: but for those actions that pertain only to the mind, or are performed with light labor, and have their greatest part deposited in the mind, such as calculations, measurements, meditations, disputes, etc., Mercury agrees because of its smallness, just as Venus agrees with delicate, soft, and voluptuous things because of its sweetness and softness. Therefore, in the explanation of this question, first of all, a general significator of the craft must be chosen from all the wandering stars, in the manner we mentioned a little earlier: then it must be seen with which of the three determining the quality of the actions it is mixed. Any of these three can be both the general significator and the quality at the same time. Whenever this happens, if it is not mixed with others, it will simply excite the native to actions consistent with its nature. But if it is mixed with Saturn or Jupiter, it will indeed indicate a simple craft, but varied according to the nature of the mixing planet. But if it is mixed with any of the three of its family, it will produce a mixed and manifold craft. But if none of these three, but Saturn or Jupiter presides, we will not be able to define the quality of the craft, except from the mixtures of Saturn or Jupiter with the other planets determining the quality of the craft. The modes of crafts, about 19, are described by Ptolemy in book 3, chapter on 'magisterium'. From these, the error of those who think that the general significator of the craft should be chosen from the three determining the quality, and then mixed with Saturn and Jupiter, is first evident, since the principal one, as I said, is chosen from all, and then mixed with the three. Then the error of the author, who attributes a peculiar quality of craft to each planet, and then varied by the mixture of others. If Saturn is the significator of fortunes, which happens if he claims the governance of the places of fortunes, he himself, being strong and fortunate, will bestow, he says, riches through agriculture, the substances of the dead, and inheritances, which is most true, and as Ptolemy says, when he has been reconciled with Jupiter, etc. The author also rightly, like Ptolemy, attributes to Saturn cold, melancholic, crude diseases, and therefore long ones, and those that communicate with the spleen. The words of Ptolemy in book 3, chapter on the vices and diseases of the body, are these: "In general, Saturn causes cold bellies, phlegmatic conditions, defluxions, emaciation, weakness, dysentery, coughing, difficulty in expelling, pains of the large intestine, leprosy: and in women, also uterine conditions." That is, Saturn in general makes bellies cold, phlegmatic, prone to defluxions, emaciated, sickly, dysenteric, coughing, difficult to expel, afflicted with pains of the large intestine, leprous: women besides these suffering from the womb. And in book 4, chapter on finding the quality of death, he says: "And if the star of Saturn has obtained the prefecture of death, it causes deaths after long illnesses and wasting and defluxions of humors, putrid fevers, affections of the spleen, dropsy, colicky passions, pains of the womb, and as many as consist of an abundance of cold." And although in this latter place Ptolemy principally deals with diseases that will kill the native, nevertheless this text can and should be transferred to investigate the quality of other milder diseases, which malefics cause by their presence or hostile rays cast into the first, seventh, and sixth houses.

[Planetary Influence on Conception and Life]
It is to be believed that the forces of the planets are changed by their mixture or combination. For Ptolemy, after he had explained the proper nature of each planet, and what it acquires from its relation to the Sun, concludes this treatise with these words: "It is clear that the stars, when mixed with each other, produce many differences in the surrounding heaven, yet so that the peculiar quality of each star generally dominates, and is in some measure restrained by the position of opposing forces." Concerning the order of the planets or their rulership in the conception of children, and how long they have been in the mother's womb, it is to be known that the first month, that is, from the hour of conception, belongs to Saturn, the second to Jupiter, the third to Mars, the fourth to the Sun, the fifth to Venus, the sixth to Mercury, the seventh to the Moon, the eighth to Saturn: and therefore he who is born in the eighth month does not live, because he is born under the power of Saturn: and the ninth to Jupiter. The rulership or ordering of the planets in human life, that is, how it disposes the life of the native, is divided thus. The Moon begins from the native's entry, that is, from the birth of the child, and disposes according to the quantity of nutritive years, which are four years. Then Mercury after ten, then Venus eight, then the Sun nineteen, then Mars fifteen, then Jupiter twelve, then Saturn until the end of life. Ptolemy states that there are two beginnings of human nature's temporality. One is generation, when the fetus is conceived: the other is birth, when the fetus is brought forth into the light by striving. Both of these provide us with subtle physical causes for the variety of effects in the bodies and souls of those born over time. And the principle of generation makes it so that the seed, once shed, takes on such a crasis as the constitution of the heavens then was, and assimilates the nourishment to its nature, and so attempers itself that the entire temperament which grows from it retains that first state and the quality first impressed. Furthermore, the principle of birth, although it brings many things to the infant that were not present in the mother's womb, such as breathing, being nourished through the mouth, using the senses and other faculties, etc., nevertheless it does not show what will happen to the native from itself, but rather because of the similarity and conformity it has with the first principle, namely, that birth is very similar to generation. For nature emits the fetus with that constitution of the heavens which is very similar to the constitution of generation. But since the principle of conception is for the most part unknown, astrologers therefore use the principle of birth, which Ptolemy also says is very important, and is surpassed by the former only in that through it one can know what happens before birth. For these beginnings do not act as mere signs, but show the causes of subsequent effects. For if any force were to be believed in the preceding nativities, nothing would be more fitting than that they should show us what preceded the exit from the womb in seven or nine months. But in this chapter, the general rulers of both courses, namely that from conception to birth, and that which extends from birth to the very last day of life, are enumerated, and first those are ordered to whom the months, which exist from the point of generation to the birth of the infant, are distributed. For this reason, in the first month, the matter of the fetus coagulates and dries according to the nature of Saturn. Next to Saturn, who is higher than the others, is Jupiter, who presides over the second month, in which, according to his nature, the softening and inflated matter usually causes nausea in pregnant women. In the third month, attributed to Mars, the mass is converted into the nature of blood, and winds and nausea subside. The Sun has the fourth month, in which the principal members are believed to be formed and the soul infused. In the fifth month, assigned to Venus, they say that the form is perfected and the sex is figured. In the sixth Mercurial month, the fetus acquires senses with the tongue and appetites for nourishment. In this month, pregnant women feel the children striving with the impulse of hands and feet to come out of darkness into light, but in vain, because they cannot yet break the membranes due to the weakness of their strength. The seventh month is lunar, in which if the infant, since it has then attained full form and has endured the temperaments of all the planets, comes forth into the light, it will confer the best hope of nourishment. In the eighth month, they say that Saturn, having again assumed command, according to his nature, cools the womb and so weakens the vital spirit that the movements of the infant are scarcely felt. Therefore, if it comes forth so dry and congealed, it will for the most part come forth dead. Since Saturn is such a great and such an enemy of human nature, it is not without reason that poets imagine him to bereave parents of their children. The ninth month, again agreeing with the Jovial temperament, excites winds and gives strong movement to the infant. Therefore, then, for the most part, the bonds being broken, through narrow passages, with the sacrum yielding, it bursts into this world, and if it is born by day, it turns its eyes to the Sun: if by night, to the light of a candle, and admires the unaccustomed light, weeping at the same time either for the torments endured in birth, or foreseeing the calamity of this future life: for as Euripides says, "Life is not truly life, but a misfortune." This is the cohabitation of the months of generation with the crasis of the planets. But concerning the state of the later principle, that is, the ages which begin from infancy and end in extreme old age, nay, even death itself, how and with which planets they agree, it has been amply discussed a little earlier when we were dealing with Saturn. I think only this one thing should be added here, that the said division of times of both principles is general, and common to all men equally, and attributed to the planets for no other reason than because of the convenience of their temperaments. These things being so, just as in the seven ages of life, so in the months of generation, we believe that effects corresponding to the disposition of the stars presiding over those times are vainly to be expected. For the cause that makes these times almost different for each individual must be sought elsewhere, as has been abundantly shown above.

[Hours of Day and Night]
If any day or night belongs to any of the planets, the first hour will be of that planet itself, and the second hour of the other planet that succeeds it, and the succession of the others follows in the same order of the circles. For example, on Friday, to which the day belongs, the first hour is of Venus herself, the second of Mercury, the third of the Moon, the fourth of Saturn, the fifth of Jupiter, the sixth of Mars.

[Planetary Hours and Their Calculation]
The seventh hour is of the Sun, the eighth again of Venus, the ninth of Mercury, the tenth of the Moon, the eleventh of Saturn, the twelfth of Jupiter. Behold, you have the twelve unequal hours of the day. Now concerning the hours of the night: The first hour of the following night is of Mars, to whom the entire night belongs; the second of the Sun, the third of Venus, the fourth of Mercury, the fifth of the Moon, the sixth of Saturn, the seventh of Jupiter, the eighth again of Mars, the ninth of the Sun, the tenth of Venus, the eleventh of Mercury, the twelfth of the Moon. And thus you also have the twelve unequal hours of the night. And so the Sabbath day begins, whose first hour is of Saturn, and to him belongs the entire day. Thus, by counting in order, you will find the hours of the days and nights and their divisions among the individual planets, if God wills. CONCERNING MASCULINE AND FEMININE HOURS. It is also said that hours are masculine and feminine: because the first hour of every day and night is masculine, the second feminine, the third masculine, the fourth feminine, and so they succeed in order, one feminine, the other masculine, until the end of the day and night. Every part compared with its whole becomes better known. Therefore, so that the variety of hours may be more correctly perceived, first of all we will compare them with their wholes, that is, with the days. Nor is there any doubt that hours are parts of days. Now, there are two types of days: natural and artificial. A natural day is the time in which the entire equinoctial circle revolves, and moreover, a particle of the same magnitude as corresponds to the arc of the Ecliptic which the Sun has traversed by its own motion. Since the Sun moves unequally in the Ecliptic, and the parts of the zodiac ascend dissimilarly, it is necessary that there be some inequality in these days, which is explained elsewhere. This day, which consists of light and darkness, and is therefore called νυχθήμερον by the Greeks, is first divided into artificial day and night, then into 24 parts, which we will immediately call equal hours. Artificial days are properly called lights, because they begin with light and again end with the setting Sun. The remaining times, during which the Sun has descended below the horizon and returns to the east, are called nights. Moreover, the inequality of artificial days and nights is also commonly manifest. Just as there are two types of days, so also it must be known that there are two types of hours: namely, equinoctial and temporal. An equinoctial hour is a space of time in which 15 parts of the equinoctial circle ascend, that is, it is approximately one twenty-fourth part of a natural day. For we have just mentioned that a natural day is a little longer than 24 hours. They are also called equal, because they do not vary in summer or winter, day or night. Temporal or unequal hours are the twelfth portions of both the artificial day and night, so called because the diurnal hours are unequal to the nocturnal ones. Astrologers call these erratic hours and planetary hours. The rising of the Sun is the beginning of the first, midday the sixth, the setting of the Sun ends the twelfth. The end of the twelfth diurnal hour is the beginning of the first hour of the night, the imum coeli the sixth, the rising of the Sun ends the twelfth. And therefore, they say that Christians sing at the first hour: 'Iam lucis orto sidere' (Now that the star of light has risen), because the beginning of the hours is taken from the rising of the Sun. Temporal hours vary in three ways. First, by reason of the point of the Ecliptic, by which temporal hours that are longer than equal hours are called hours of Cancer; those shorter than these, of Capricorn; finally, those equal to them are called hours of Libra. Secondly, by reason of regions: For just as in one region the temporal hours of one artificial day or night are not unequal, but only differ from those of different days and nights in the year; so again in one day they differ by reason of different regions, so that a temporal hour of the beginning of Gemini in our region contains one equal hour and 18 scruples. But in Venice, where the pole is elevated 45 degrees, it contains 1 hour and 14 scruples. Thirdly, by reason of night and day, for one diurnal temporal hour of the beginning of Gemini contains, as I said, 1 hour and 18 scruples. But a nocturnal hour contains 42 scruples. For even if in a right sphere the diurnal hours compared to the nocturnal are perpetually equal to each other, nevertheless in an oblique sphere diurnal hours are longer than nocturnal in summer, shorter in winter; and only at the equinox are they equal, which Virgil elegantly taught in one verse: 'Libra diei somnique pares ubi fecerat horas' (When Libra had made the hours of day and sleep equal). For it is known that the Romans used temporal hours, which the ancients attributed to the dominion of the wandering stars. They attributed the first morning hour to the star from which the day takes its name; the second to the next; and so on, observing the order of the planets. For example, on the day of the Moon, the first hour of the day from the rising of the Sun is attributed to the Moon, the second to Saturn, the third to Jupiter, the fourth to Mars, the fifth to the Sun, the sixth to Venus, the seventh to Mercury, the eighth to the Moon, the ninth to Saturn, the tenth to Jupiter, the eleventh to Mars, the twelfth to the Sun. Therefore, the dominion of the first hour of that same night will fall to Jupiter, the second to Mars, the third to the Sun, and so on. The Greeks call the lord of the hour διέπων. Finally, the number of parts and scruples of the equinoctial that ascend in any temporal hour are called τὰ ὡριαῖα μεγέθα, or ὡριαῖοι χρόνοι, that is, horary times. Having defined these, we must now explain how the diurnal and nocturnal arc of the Sun's place or any other star, and their horary times, are found. The diurnal arc in any climate or at any elevation of the pole is shown by the oblique ascension of that half of the zodiac which is from the part of the Sun or another star, or point in the Ecliptic in consequence, up to the part diametrically opposite the Sun. For example, in our time on June 25th, the Sun illuminates 13 degrees of Cancer. Now, to have the diurnal arc of the proposed day, or the ascension of the six signs immediately following the Sun's place, namely the last 17 degrees of Gemini, Cancer, Leo, Virgo, Libra, Scorpio, and the first 13 degrees of Sagittarius, and this for Cologne, where the northern pole is elevated 51 degrees. First, with the end of the given arc, which is 13 degrees of Cancer, I enter the table of oblique ascensions made for 51 degrees of latitude, and from the table area I write down 315 degrees 30 minutes. Then, with the beginning of the proposed arc, namely with 13 degrees of Gemini, entering the same table, I find in the common angle 72 degrees 44 minutes. Finally, I subtract this beginning from the end, and the oblique ascension of the desired arc remains, 242 degrees 46 minutes, which divided by 12 yields horary times of 20 degrees 13 minutes 50 seconds. These, converted into time, make one equal hour and 20 minutes 13 seconds 55 hundredths of a second. And such is the unequal hour of the proposed day. Now, 15 parts of the equinoctial make one hour, and one equinoctial part makes 4 minutes 12 seconds. Finally, 15 scruples of the first equinoctial make one first scruple of time. Having found the diurnal arc in this way, namely 242 degrees 46 minutes, converted into time, the time of diurnal light of equal hours is elicited as 16 hours 11 minutes 12 seconds. Furthermore, half of the diurnal arc shows the semi-diurnal arc, which if you divide by 6, you will obtain the same horary times. And you will convert the same half into time no differently than was just said about the entire arc. The nocturnal arc and nocturnal horary times are collected from the ascension of the six signs beginning from the part opposite the Sun or another place: just as the nocturnal arc of 13 degrees of Cancer is the last 17 degrees of Gemini, Cancer, Leo, Virgo, Libra, Scorpio, and the first 13 degrees of Sagittarius. But you will more easily elicit the nocturnal arc if you subtract the diurnal arc from a complete revolution, that is, from 360 degrees. In the proposed example, the diurnal arc of 242 degrees 46 minutes subtracted from 360 degrees leaves a nocturnal arc of 117 degrees 14 minutes, that is, 7 equal hours 49 minutes 13 seconds. The twelfth part of the same arc indicates nocturnal horary times of 9 degrees 47 minutes 1 second 10 hundredths of a second, that is, approximately 39 scruples of time. But having known the diurnal horary times, you will much more easily ascertain the nocturnal horary times if you subtract the times of the diurnal hour from two equal hours, that is, from 30 parts. For a diurnal hour and a nocturnal hour, if they combine, make two equal hours or 30 parts: just as the horary times found a little earlier, 20 degrees 13 minutes 50 seconds, subtracted from 30 degrees, leave nocturnal horary times of 9 degrees 46 minutes 19 seconds 10 hundredths of a second. If anyone understands the use of Regiomontanus' tables, they will reach all these things by an easier and shorter path through the semi-diurnal arcs of the Sun and any star, which are collected as follows: Join the ascensional difference (which is half the difference of the equinoctial days, or the part by which the oblique ascension of a quadrant exceeds or is exceeded by the right ascension) to 90 degrees, if the declination of the star is northern.

[Calculating Diurnal and Nocturnal Arcs]
or subtract it from 90 parts, if the declination is Southern, and the semi-diurnal arc will be formed or remain. Subtracting this from 180 parts will leave the semi-nocturnal arc, and doubling each of these will show the whole arc. For example, in our case, the ascensional difference of 13.69 parts is found to be 31.231 parts, which, when added to 90 parts, produces a semi-diurnal arc of 121.231 parts. Its double, 242.461 parts, gives the whole diurnal arc. Subtracting this semi-arc from 180 parts leaves a semi-nocturnal arc of 58.37 parts, whose double, 117.1412 parts, indicates the whole nocturnal arc. If you divide these arcs, as we advised above, by 15, the number of corresponding equal hours will result. Similarly, if you divide the whole arcs by 12, or their halves by 6, you will obtain the horary times, which, when converted into time, will manifest unequal hours. You will find the same, and more easily, if you convert the ascensional difference into time by dividing by 15, and add the result to six equinoctial hours if the declination is Northern, or subtract it from the same number of six if the declination is Southern. Thus, the arc of semi-diurnal time, or the space of six temporal hours, will result, whose double will count the 12 unequal hours of the whole day. Finally, if you divide the said ascensional difference by 6, and add 15 to the resulting number for a Northern declination, or subtract it from the same 15 if the declination is Southern, you will obtain the equinoctial times that correspond to one unequal diurnal hour. In our assumed example, divide the ascensional difference, which is 31.2312 parts, by 6, and you will get 5.131.5023 parts, which, when joined with 15 parts, will give you 20.131.502 parts, the times corresponding to one unequal hour. From this, it is clear that horary times are most correctly calculated if the sixth part of the ascensional difference is added to 15 parts in a Northern declination, or subtracted from the same 15 parts whenever the declination is Southern. Since not everyone knows how to use Regiomontanus' tables, but many are content with ephemeris calculations, for their sake, we will briefly note a method, descending from tables to ephemerides, by which everything that has been said can be most easily calculated from a single table of the quantity of days. First, to obtain the diurnal and nocturnal arcs and their halves, take the elevation of the pole, or the latitude of your region, at the top of the table of the quantity of days. Then, look for the place of any star on the sides of the same table, descending on the left side, and ascending on the right. For the Proselis, or as it is called, the common angle, will show in hours and minutes the semi-diurnal quantity if the star has traversed a Northern sign, and the semi-nocturnal quantity if it has traversed a Southern sign. Once the semi-diurnal quantity is known, and subtracted from 12 whole hours, the semi-nocturnal quantity remains, and conversely, the semi-nocturnal space subtracted from 12 hours leaves the semi-diurnal arc. The use of this knowledge is also that from the semi-diurnal space, the setting of the Sun, and from the semi-nocturnal space, its rising, are accurately known. The double of each space demonstrates the length of the diurnal light for the semi-diurnal, and the length of the night for the semi-nocturnal. Finally, the diurnal space subtracted from 24 hours leaves the nocturnal space; and vice versa, the nocturnal space subtracted from 24 hours leaves the space of diurnal light. For example, for 13 parts of Cancer, on the right side of the table, the diurnal quantity (for it is a Northern sign) is found in the common angle opposite the polar altitude to be 8 hours and 1 minute, which, when subtracted from 12 hours, leaves 3 hours and 55 minutes, the semi-nocturnal space. Therefore, the space of the entire diurnal light is 16 hours and 10 minutes, and the total space of darkness is 7 hours and 50 minutes, etc. Now, having known the usual time of light or night from the conversion of the diurnal or nocturnal arc, or collected from the table of the quantity of days, you can easily extract the quantity of the unequal hour of the same day or night from the following table in this manner. Look for the whole hours at the top of the table; take the minutes (if any beyond the perfect hours are attached to the diurnal or nocturnal space) on the left side, descending, and the common angle will reveal the calculated space of one unequal hour: diurnal if with the diurnal space, and nocturnal if you have entered with the nocturnal arc. If the minutes of time written on the left side of the table do not correspond to your minutes, consider the difference or excess, and for every minute that you cannot enter into the table, add five seconds to the common angle, so that the true time of the temporal hour comes out. For 12 minutes increase the temporal hour of the day or night by the space of one minute. For example, since the diurnal arc of our example, when converted into usual time, showed us 16 equinoctial hours and almost 11 minutes, look for 16 hours at the top of the table and 0 minutes on the left side, and the common angle will show you 1 hour and 20 minutes. To this, for the remaining 11 minutes, add 55 minutes, and you will get 1 hour, 20 minutes, and 55 seconds, and that is the quantity of the temporal hour in Cologne when the Sun is passing through 13.69 parts. Table of the quantity of temporal hours. Hours, minutes. First, if some unequal hours are to be converted into equal hours, resolve the unequal hours into minutes, and divide them by the minutes of one equal hour of the same denomination. For the number of the section will indicate the number of equal hours to which the proposed unequal hours correspond. Conversely, if you wish to convert equal hours into unequal ones, then all the minutes of the equal hours must be divided by the minutes of one unequal hour of the same denomination. For example: if someone was born on the fourth hour, 30 minutes after noon before sunset, and you wish to know which planet presides over the hour of birth, divide the minutes of the given equal hours, 270, by the minutes of one temporal hour, which, for example, are 82. The number of the section will show 3.421 temporal hours, to which add the 6 hours elapsed from sunrise to noon, and you will form 9.241 temporal hours. Therefore, assign the first to Mars, the second to the Sun, the third to Venus, the fourth to Mercury, the fifth to Jupiter, the sixth to Saturn, the seventh to the Moon, the eighth to Mars, the ninth to the Sun. The tenth, therefore, incomplete, into which the birth falls, will be ruled by Venus. Another example of a nativity assumed by us, which began in the year 1527, on May 21, which is Tuesday, at 3.471 hours after noon, at which time the Sun occupied almost the ninth part of Gemini, under a latitude of 41 parts. Therefore, the semi-diurnal arc is 7.212 hours. This time, combined with the 3.471 hours elapsed from noon, makes 11.812 equal hours, which, divided by the space of one diurnal temporal hour, which is 1.13302 hours, will yield approximately 9.51 diurnal temporal hours elapsed from sunrise. Assign the first to Mars, the second to the Sun, the third to Venus, the fourth to Mercury, the fifth to Jupiter, the sixth to Saturn, the seventh to the Moon, the eighth to Mars, the ninth to the Sun. The tenth, therefore, incomplete, into which the nativity falls, is ruled by Venus. You will also extract the horary times of other planets from the table of the quantity of days as follows. Take from the table of the quantity of days the semi-diurnal or semi-nocturnal arc of the planet in hours and minutes, and multiply it by 2. For the resulting number will show you its horary times, as in the present example, the semi-diurnal arc, which is 7.212 hours, multiplied by 24, produces 18.2212302 parts, which are the diurnal horary times of the Sun's place, because the sign of Gemini is Northern. Another example: I want the horary times of the twenty-fourth part of the sign of Aries. I see under the altitude of the pole 51, for the city of Agrippa, directly opposite the twenty-fourth.

[Calculating Horary Times for Aries]
The difference of the fourth part of Aries is 6 hours and 47 minutes, which I multiply by 24. This results in 15.11718 parts. After the usual resolution and composition, the horary times for the twenty-fourth part of Aries in the said region are 16.571 parts. These are also diurnal, because they are from the ascending signs. If nocturnal horary times are ever needed, and you have found the diurnal times, subtract them from 30, and the nocturnal times will remain, as in the proposed example for the twenty-fourth part of Aries. The diurnal times, which are 16.571 parts, when subtracted from 30 parts, leave 13.31 parts for the nocturnal horary times of the twenty-fourth part of Aries. The reason for this operation is easy to understand. For since one part or 60 equinoctial minutes arises with four minutes of time, if you now multiply 4 minutes of time by the number 6 (for each semi-arc contains that many temporal hours), you will produce 24 minutes of time, that is, a certain semi-diurnal or semi-nocturnal arc, around which 60 equinoctial minutes arise in the space of one temporal hour. Therefore, place 24 in the first place, 60 minutes in the second, and finally the semi-diurnal or semi-nocturnal arc in the third, thus: temporal minutes 24, equinoctial minutes 60, hours 6, minutes 47. Or, reducing the first two numbers to their smallest nomenclature, in this way: 2, 5, 6, 47. Since in practice, we either multiply the second number by the third and divide the product by the first, or divide the second number by the first and multiply the quotient by the third, and by various other methods of operation we arrive at the desired goal, therefore, divide 5 minutes by 2 minutes, and you will get 21 integers (for this is the nature of physical calculation), which, multiplied by 6 whole hours and 47 minutes, produce horary times of 16 parts and 57.12 minutes. From this, it can be understood why in this canon we command to multiply the semi-diurnal or semi-nocturnal arc by 21, so that the sought diurnal or nocturnal horary times emerge. I wanted to explain this more extensively here, because these things are worthy of knowledge and most necessary for understanding the doctrine of the progressions or directions of significators, as they are called, about which more below.
