[Third Difference: Planetary Interactions]
THIRD DIFFERENCE IN WHAT HAPPENS TO THE SEVEN PLANETS AMONG THEMSELVES, AND WHAT HAPPENS TO THEM FROM EACH OTHER. And because, with God's grace, we have now completed what pertains to the planets in themselves, and what they signify, we will proceed to narrate what happens to them among themselves, that is, from some towards others.

[Planetary Affections and Appearances]
We have already stated that the power and influence of the wandering stars are primarily observed in their quality, place, position, and finally, in their mutual comparison and comparison to their own orbs. The first three modes were combined in the preceding distinctions. Therefore, in the third distinction, he now addresses the fourth remaining mode, in which the phenomena of the moving stars, which occur to them either due to the diversity of the orbs in which they are carried or from their mutual respect, are to be examined and explored. All such affections are commonly called passions of the planets, but he calls those passions that accompany the planets in their orbs without any respect to others, accidents of the planets in themselves: such as the stations, directions, declinations, latitudes, ascensions, and descensions of the planets, both in their deferents and in their epicycles. However, he calls the appearances, which are understood only from the mutual collation of the planets, accidents of the planets to each other, which again are twofold. First, he compares the planets to the luminaries themselves, and teaches what is the rising of a planet, what is its setting, what is combustion, what is the persona, which they call Almugea, etc. Then, of the combinations that arise from the mutual respect of the planets, although many thousands could be enumerated, the author only recounts the principal ones and those that are observed due to the evidence of their effects: such as conjunction, application, deflux or separation, prohibition from a body or aspect, obsession, solution, kenodromia, peregrination, feralitas, reception and pulsation, translation of light, contrariety or perturbation, abscission, collection, delation of light, refranation, sublimity and veneration, affection and hatred. Signi THIRD DIFFERENCE. The signification of planets in themselves is, for example, when a planet is ascending in the circle of its apogee, lesser in light, magnitude, and speed, that is, when it is between itself and its apogee less than 90 degrees before or behind: if, however, there are 90 equal degrees between it and its apogee, it will be equal in light, magnitude, and speed: but if it is outside these two places, that is, more than 90 degrees, it will be descending from the circle of its apogee, and will appear greater in light, magnitude, and faster in speed. And if its equated portion is less than 180 degrees, it will be increasing in number: if it is more, it will be decreasing in number: and if its equated portion is 180 degrees or 360 degrees, it will neither be increasing nor decreasing: and if the equated equation is added to the mean course, it is said to be increasing the course or number: and if its equation is decreased, it is said to be decreasing the number. And when any of the three higher planets goes more than its mean course, it is said to be increasing its course: and if it goes less, it is said to be decreasing its course: and if it goes just as much, it is said to be equal in course: and when any of the lower planets goes more than the mean course of the Sun, it will be increasing its course: when it goes less, it is said to be decreasing its course: when it goes just as much, it will be equal in course. To be in the course of the luminaries is like being one of the three higher planets. A planet also becomes northern when it has passed its Genzahar, that is, when it has passed through the path of the Sun going from south to north: that transit, that is, that abscission of circles, is called Genzahar. Therefore, when a planet has passed that abscission, and is between the planet and that abscission less than 90 degrees, the planet will be northern ascending: and when it is from 90 to 180 degrees, it will be northern descending: and when it has passed this number, that is, when it has more going to 270 degrees, it will be southern descending: and when it has passed this number going to 360 degrees, it will be southern ascending. This is what it means for planets to be in themselves. Significa- Of the first kind of passions, by which planets are affected by reason of their orbs. First and foremost, it teaches how to find the ascension and descension of a planet, both in the deferent and in the epicycle. Here, some terms conducive to this matter must be explained. A circle having the same center as the world is called homocentric or concentric, such as is represented in the appended delineation by a circle described from center a, with its extremity designated by the letters y. A deferent or eccentric or ablis cuxorge is one whose center is different from the center of the world, yet encompassing it, in which the Sun or the epicycle of another planet is carried around. This is indicated to you by the inner periphery described around the center and designated by the letters. But an epicycle is an orb or a small circle affixed to the deferent, in which the body of the planet revolves, whose image is represented by a small circle, designated by letters, and described around the center. In both of these, two significant points are to be noted, one of which is called the highest apogee, the other the lowest apogee. The highest apogee is the point of the deferent or epicycle most remote from the innermost point of the universe. Ptolemy calls it apogee, commonly by the barbarous word augem, Pontanus calls it apex, culmination, and the summit of the circle. The lowest apogee is the point of the deferent or epicycle that is lowest and closest to the earth, called by Ptolemy perigee, commonly the opposite of apogee. In our delineation, the apogee of the eccentric is indicated by the letter s, in the epicycle by the letter A: The perigee is represented in the eccentric by the letter, and in the epicycle by the letter u. With these things thus, it is clear that the Sun and the epicycles of other planets descend towards the earth in their deferents until they traverse a hemicycle or 180 parts from the perigee of the eccentric, and are carried to the lowest apogee, and conversely, from here ascending, by reason of their deferents, and are elevated from the center of the world for so long until, having completed one circuit, they are carried to the highest apogee, and again proceed to the same turns. The ascent and descent of the stars in the epicycle must be imagined in exactly the same way. For those descending from the apogees of the epicycles to their perigees are said to descend: but they are said to ascend when, having passed the perigees, they reach the apogees. However, the author here understands planets to ascend when they remain in the upper part of the deferent, that is, when they do not differ more than 90 parts from either side of the highest apogee of the eccentric, either forward or backward. For then, due to their greater removal from the center of the world, they appear smaller in light, magnitude, and speed. Conversely, when they traverse the lower halves of the deferents, they accelerate their course and appear larger in body and light, since all things appear larger when perceived by the senses from a smaller, rather than from a larger, interval. That the planets truly revolve in eccentrics and, in comparison to the zodiac, move slower in the higher half than in the lower half, we will explain with the example of the Sun, which is easily understood. No one is unaware that the zodiac is divided into two equal hemicycles by the equinoctial, so that one inclines to the north and the other to the south. Now, it is again known to all that there are more days from the vernal equinox to the autumnal equinox than from the latter to the former. For the Sun, traversing the northern hemicycle, which contains six summer signs, consumes almost 186 days: but traversing the southern hemicycle, which possesses six winter signs, it completes at least almost 178 days. Therefore, since the Sun traverses the southern half almost 7 days faster than the northern half, it is clear that it progresses slower here, but faster there. For if

[Planetary Distance from Earth]
From this, the ascent and descent of the inferior planets in the epicycle are more easily known than from other signs. I think this is because they tend to ascend in the morning rising and seek the lowest point of the epicycle in the evening. The same planets, when conjoined with the Sun, culminate after their morning rising, but after their evening rising, they precisely occupy the lowest apogee of the epicycle. Finally, I thought it worthy of note when planets are plainly closest to the Earth, or furthest from it, both by reason of the deferent and the epicycle. Therefore, when Saturn is conjoined with the Sun, around the end of Sagittarius; Jupiter, around the sixth part of Libra; Mars, around the end of Leo, it recedes furthest from the Earth for both reasons. Conversely, when Saturn traverses the last parts of Gemini; Jupiter the sixth part of Aries; Mars the last parts of Aquarius, if it is opposed to the Sun, it will likewise be closest to the Earth for both reasons. For Saturn, to speak of only one by way of example, first recedes furthest from the Earth in Sagittarius by reason of its eccentric, and then, conjoined with the Sun, occupies the summit of its small circle. Therefore, in such a state, it is clear that it recedes furthest from the Earth. By the same reasoning, you will judge the greatest recession from or approach to the Earth of the inferior planets, if indeed the places of the apsides in the eccentrics, and the times at which they are carried through the highest and lowest parts of the epicycles, are not hidden.

[Inconjunct Signs and Human Relationships]
The sixth, however, defines no exact measure or aliquot part, as they call it, of the zodiac. For example, from the beginning of Gemini to the beginning of Scorpio there are 150 parts, which do not constitute an aliquot part of the whole zodiac, that is, which, when collected a certain number of times, make up 360 parts, of which the whole zodiac is composed. Hence it is clear that all parts of alien signs are plainly inconjunct. This is the cause of inconjunct signs; it remains for us to explain the method of finding them by three rules. FIRST: Fixed signs, namely, Taurus, Leo, Scorpio, and Aquarius, each have four inconjunct signs: one immediately following, another immediately preceding, and those opposite to both, or those which are found to be sixth before and sixth after the fixed signs. For example, Taurus is disjoined first from Gemini and Aries, which are second from Taurus, and one immediately follows Taurus, and the other immediately precedes it. Then from Sagittarius and Libra, which are opposite to those second signs, and are sixth from Taurus, if we proceed to one by consequent signs and to the other by antecedent signs. SECOND: Mutable signs, namely, Gemini, Virgo, Sagittarius, and Pisces, each have only two inconjunct signs: the succeeding one and the one opposite to that succeeding one, which itself precedes the mutable sign in the sixth place. For example, Aries is disjoined first from Taurus, which is second from it, then from Scorpio, which is opposite to Taurus and sixth before Aries. THIRD: Common or bicorporeal signs, namely, Gemini, Virgo, Sagittarius, and Pisces, similarly find only two inconjunct signs: namely, the immediately preceding one, which is second from it, and the one opposite to the preceding one, which is distant in the sixth place from the common sign. For example, the sign Gemini has as inconjunct first the sign Taurus, which immediately precedes the sign of Gemini; then the sign Scorpio, which is opposite to Taurus and succeeds Gemini in the sixth place. From these it is manifest that opposite places are disjoined from the same places in number and equality. Aries, for example, and Libra are opposite signs, and for this reason are separated from Taurus and Scorpio. Thus Gemini and Sagittarius, when they are diametrically distant, are equally disjoined from Scorpio and Taurus; and so it can be said of the others, a summary of which we have placed in the following figure. INCONJUNCT SIGNS OF MUTABLE SIGNS: Aries, Cancer, Libra, Capricorn. INCONJUNCT SIGNS OF FIXED SIGNS: Taurus, Leo, Scorpio, Aquarius. INCONJUNCT SIGNS OF BICORPOREAL SIGNS: Gemini, Virgo, Sagittarius, Pisces. It is most worthy of relation and observation what Cardan wrote about this place, namely, that the ancient wise men, by that fourfold distinction of signs, insinuated to us a fourfold comparison of men. The first is taken from commanding and obedient signs, and it is of those whose one spontaneously renders obedience to the other, because of nativities constituted in commanding and obedient places. This obedience will be most perfect if the significators of such things have received each other not only from the same sign or degree, but also from a minute part of the sky; for then he who has his significator in an obedient place, even if he is a king or a man of other more excellent fortune, will perfectly obey him whose significator has traversed the commanding point of the sky, especially if they are born in the same region, for in this way they will more easily meet through the administration of affairs or through some other occasion. This perfection will diminish somewhat if the said significators are coupled only by degree, for then one will obey the other if they are equal, or certainly if he who ought to obey is of lesser condition in dignity, wealth, sex, or age. But still, and by far, the obedience will be weaker if that connection of significators arises only from the sign: nevertheless, one will obey the other and comply if he who has the significator in the obedient sign is a servant, and the other, having the significator in the commanding sign, is a master. Hence is clear the reason why Aristotle, unlike jurists, did not say that all men are free by nature, but that some are free by nature, and others are servants by nature. Those are free by nature whom no one can command without force, such as exist when no significators occupy the obedient signs. But those are servants by nature whom no one willingly obeys and only under compulsion, and such are generated when the commanding signs comprehend none of the significators. But among these extremes, few are found, but most are found to be of a middle condition, that is, they command some, and spontaneously obey others. Why? Because most have a part of the significators in commanding signs, and a part in obedient signs; very few are found who obtain all significators of one kind. The second comparison of men is taken from those who behold, and it is of those who, although they agree in manners and some affections, are not for that reason, but for another that follows, more prone by nature to mutual concord or discord. For it was said a little earlier that beholding signs are not efficient of love, but of equality of manners. But it can hardly happen that someone has no one agreeing in manners, since all parts of the zodiac, even minute ones, correspond to beholding parts, with the exception of the tropical points of the sun, which, when they occupy significators, behold no place at all. The reason for proximity explained in commanding and obedient signs must also be observed here. The third comparison of men is taken from signs aspecting each other by trine, square, sextile, or opposition, and it is of those who agree in friendship or disagree in malevolence. Indeed, significators placed in trine and sextile figures conduce to friendship, but in opposition or square to enmity. The fourth and last comparison is taken from inconjunct signs, and it is of those who are plainly connected by no bond, but either do not know each other, or if they meet, fall into great enmities. To this place belongs the 33rd enunciation of the Centiloquium: "Love and hatred are taken both from the concord and discord of the luminaries, and from the ascendants of each nativity; but the obedient signs increase benevolence." By this rule, we are commanded to judge the friendships and enmities of the native not only from his own genesis, but from his concurrence with the nativities of others. And it rightly says that the natural concord and benevolence between two people is caused by the agreement of the principal places, that is, of the luminaries and horoscopes in each nativity. This agreement occurs in four principal ways. First, if the luminaries in both nativities hold the same signs: Then if they behold each other with a favorable light: Third, if they have exchanged places: Finally, if the signs in which they are situated are obedient. For example, if in both nativities, namely of Paul and Peter, the Sun has occupied the sign Sagittarius, we will pronounce that there will be mutual familiarity and friendship between Paul and Peter because of the Sun's presence in one sign in both nativities. The same is also determined by the concurrence of the Moon's places, which, if it is partile, signifies that friendship to be most close and perfect; if not, then according to Ptolemy's proportion of the distance of the solar or lunar places, the bond of benevolence is attenuated, until, by a longer departure of 17 parts, the signification of benevolence vanishes into nothing. But if the places of the luminaries are not in one but in different signs, yet beholding each other with a favorable light, they will similarly reconcile friendship. As if, when Paul is born, the Sun occupies the sign Sagittarius, but in Peter's nativity the same Sun passes through the sign Libra, from which places the Sun's places, connected by trine radiation, generate mutual love between the natives. In the same way, but with less benevolence, sextile places determine, and also antiscia, provided they are not hateful; for these, as I said above, ...

[Planetary Anomaly and Motion]
The meaning is: If the equated anomaly of the orb, or its equated argument, as they call it, of which we spoke a little earlier, is less than 180 parts, then the planet is said to be προθετικός (prothetikos), that is, augmented in number, because then the prosthaphaeresis, that is, the equation of the argument, is added to the mean motion of the planet, that is, to the true motion of the center of the epicycle, so that its true motion may result. If the same equated anomaly of the orb has more than 180 parts, it is called ἀφαιρετικός (aphairesis) of the number, that is, diminished in number, because then the prosthaphaeresis is subtracted from the true motion of the epicycle, so that the excess makes known to us the true place of the planet. For example, above in the calculation of Saturn, the equated anomaly of the orb was found to be 45 parts, 33 minutes, 13 seconds, 2223 thirds, which is much less than a semicircle. Therefore, with Saturn being προθετικός (prothetikos) in number, we added its equated prosthaphaeresis, which is 4 parts, 13 minutes, 1 second, 55 seconds, 2 thirds, to the true motion of the epicycle, which is 3550 parts, 61 minutes, 5 seconds, 2 thirds, to produce the true place of Saturn from the head of sidereal Aries, namely 359 parts, 20 minutes, 12 seconds. To this number, adding the true precession of the equinoxes, we produced the true place of Saturn from the apparent equinox, 26 parts, 42 minutes, 1 second, 20 seconds, 2 thirds. In the same way, if you look at the calculation of Venus and Mercury, you will find them augmented in number. But in the calculation of Mars and Jupiter, since the equated anomaly exceeds 180 parts, you will be able to pronounce both of these planets diminished in number. But if the equated anomaly is exactly 180 or 360 parts, then, since the lines of the true motion of the planet and the true motion of the center of the epicycle unite, it is said to be neither augmented nor diminished, but equal in number. This is described more fully and clearly in the theories of the planets.

[Planetary Velocity and Stations]
Regarding the velocity and slowness of the planets' motion, to which we must add progressions, or as they call them, directions, retrogradations, and stations, and any other affections of the five wandering stars due to the sections that occur to them by reason of their eccentrics and epicycles: a planet is said to be swift in motion when, in the consequence of the signs, its true motion is greater than its mean motion. But it is said to be slow in motion when it retrogrades, or at least moves more slowly than if its mean motion were proceeding. You will judge these affections of the stars most easily if you extract the diurnal motion of the planet from an ephemeris and compare it with its mean motion described here in tables, which must be diligently done in examining the forces of the stars' diurnal motions. For when stars have shone in their morning rising and their true motion has been greater than their mean motion, they are judged by Ptolemy to be most robust. Here it should also be observed that in the three superior planets, the swiftness of motion does not have as much effect as in the three inferior planets. Therefore, in Venus, the swiftness of motion is to be considered and has much effect, more in Mercury, and most in the Moon. Furthermore, a planet is progressive or direct, as they call it, when it moves through the upper arc of the epicycle intercepted between the station points, and when its motion written in the ephemeris increases, from which it also received its name, so that being thus affected, and leaving the preceding parts, it invades the succeeding ones, and is called πολικός (polikos). There is, however, a clear difference between the swiftness of course and direction. For every planet swift in course is at the same time direct, but not every planet that is direct is necessarily swift in course; indeed, around the beginnings of directions, the mean motion usually surpasses the true motion in swiftness. A planet becomes retrograde or retrograding when it moves through the lower apogee of the small circle, and its motion written in the ephemeris decreases, and again it finds a name, so that in this way, leaving the succeeding places of the sign and returning to the preceding signs, it is called ἀποκγαιικός (apokgaikos).

[Planetary Stations and Anomaly]
It is called stationary when, falling into the station points, it seems to stand armed as if in a cage, and to inhibit its course. You will recognize this whenever you observe no variation in parts and scruples in the ephemerides. In addition, the syllables DI and RE inserted in the columns of the ephemerides for the five planets indicate the beginnings of directions and retrogressions. But also two stations are observed, namely the first and the second. The first station, like the ωριγμός (horismos), is the point of the epicycle from which the planet, descending, begins to retrograde. The second station, the δεύτερο ωριγμός (deutero horismos), is the other point of the epicycle into which the planet, falling from retrogradation, begins to become direct. We will also be able to calculate all these affections of the planets, of which we have just spoken, very easily, if, by entering the canon of planetary stations through the equated anomaly of the eccentric, we appropriately extract the numbers of both the first and second stations, and then immediately compare them to the equated anomaly of commutation. For if we find the number of the equated anomaly of commutation equal to the number of the first station, we will also pronounce the planet to be lingering at the point of the first station, from which it descends through retrogradation to the perigee of the epicycle. But if the same number of πραλλάξεως (parallax) is equal to the number of the second station, we will similarly say that the planet is stationary at the other point of the epicycle, from which it ascends through direct motion to its highest point. Finally, if the anomaly of commutation is unequal to the number of either station, the planet will indicate either ὑπολλῆι κόμ (hypolleikom) or προκγατικόμ (prokgaikom): and ὑπολλῆι κόμ (hypolleikom) indeed, when the number of the anomaly of commutation is less than the number of the first station, or greater than the number of the second station: but προκγατικόμ (prokgaikom), when the same anomaly is greater than the number of the first station, and less than the number of the second station. At the time of our example in calculation 1, the equated anomaly of commutation is seen to be 45 parts, 33 minutes, 1 second: and the equated anomaly of the eccentric is 114 parts, 32 minutes, 19 seconds, to which in the canon of stations under the title 'h', corresponds the number of the first station 114 parts, 30 minutes, 14 seconds, and of the second station 245 parts, 30 minutes, 1 second. Since the number of the anomaly of commutation does not agree with either of these numbers, and is much less than the number of the first station, therefore Saturn at this time is outside the limits of the stations.

[Planetary Motion and Latitude]
The second type of planetary affections follows, which includes the affections of the stars that they acquire from their relation to the equinoctial and ecliptic of the Sun. The declination of the stars is nothing other than their distance from the equinoctial circle towards the north or south. And latitude is nothing other than the departure from the middle of the zodiac; which is similarly twofold, namely southern and northern. Declination is calculated by a line drawn through the poles of the world and the true place of the star. Latitude, however, is calculated by a line drawn through the cardinal points of the zodiac and the true place of the star. It is clear, however, that although the Sun has declination, it never acquires any latitude, because the plane of its deferent always remains in the plane of the ecliptic. For the other planets, whose apsides or deferents intersect the plane of the ecliptic at the center of the world, and whose deferents depart from the same at certain limits, the latitude is manifest, but so involved with the deviations of the apsides and the inclinations and deflections of the epicycles, that it can hardly be explained briefly without obscurity. There is, however, this law for the three superior planets, that just as the Moon, so their individual apsides recede from the Ecliptic at a certain interval, and are intersected by it at the center of the universe, so that those hemicycles of the apsides which sustain the apogees, are removed to the northern part of the world, and the remaining ones which contain the perigees, to the south, at a certain and minimally variable distance: in Saturn indeed almost two and a half parts; in Jupiter almost one and a half parts; and in Mars finally one part. But the nodes of the Moon differ from the intersections of these apsides with the ecliptic in this, that there they move daily by three scruples into preceding signs, but here into consequent signs no faster than the apogees are transferred by the motion of the eighth sphere, as they call it, so that the apogees describe equally distant peripheries of the ecliptic in the north, and the perigees in the south, and never approach the nodes of their apsides as in the Moon. All intersections, conjunctions, or nodes, or syzygies of all apsides with the ecliptic are called extended, but Ptolemy in the third book of the Almagest appears to have specifically named the conjunction of the Moon a syzygy, and the other planets' nodes as kampia. Furthermore, since this latitude of the apsides differs from experience, therefore, in order to satisfy the phenomena, another ratio of latitudes is added to it, derived from the inclination of the epicycles to their apsides, and it is such: When the center of the epicycle occupies either of the nodes, then the plane of the small circle is entirely along the ecliptic circle, and the culminating point and the opposite point fall on the intersection of the deferent and the ecliptic. But when the center of the epicycle is at the nodes, the perigee of the small circle declines from the node, that is, it faces that part of the world to which the hemicycle of the apsis, in which the epicycle resides, has receded: but the apogee of the same circle reflects just as much to the opposite part. These things being so, it is clear that the inclinations of the perigees increase the latitudes of the apsides, and those of the perigees diminish them. For example, if the center of the epicycle, having left the ascending node, moves northward, and the perigee of the small circle by a certain ratio similarly inclines northward from the plane of the apsis, and the apogee of the same deflects just as much towards the ecliptic in the opposite direction, it is clear that the planet, moving through the perigee of the epicycle, and wandering outside both planes, will acquire a greater latitude than the distance of the eccentric from the ecliptic: but much less, if it travels through the apogee or the upper parts of the epicycle, and remains enclosed in the middle of the deferent and ecliptic planes. In the same way, when the center of the epicycle, having passed the descending node, slides beyond the ecliptic to the south, the perigees of the epicycle similarly inclined to the south, if the planet then passes through them, will acquire a greater latitude than the distance of the apsis from the ecliptic: but less, if it is carried through the apogee of the same epicycle: then it will remain between the deferent and the ecliptic circle. From all these, it is clear that the latitudes of the individual parts of the apsis vary in many ways according to the varied inclination of the epicycle. The latitudes described in the tables of motions, however, only show that variation which occurs when the center of the epicycle moves through the limits of the stars' greatest latitude. We have also included here for you the latitudes of the apogees and perigees of the three superior planets in their epicycles, from which the greatest variation of the deferents' distance from the ecliptic, which is caused by the inclination of the apogees and perigees, is manifest. SATURN, with the center of its epicycle holding the greatest latitude: Northern limit, Southern limit. Culminating or joined to the Sun, has latitude north 2.31. Depressed to the bottom of the epicycle or opposite the Sun, has latitude south 2.31. Culminating or joined to the Sun, has latitude north 3.31. Depressed or opposite the Sun, has latitude south 3.12. JUPITER, with the center of its epicycle illuminating the greatest latitude: Northern limit, Southern limit. Culminating deviates to the north 1.4.10. Depressed deviates to the north 2.5.1. Culminating deviates to the south 2.8.18. Depressed deviates to the south 1.6.14. MARS, with the center of its epicycle illuminating the greatest latitude: Northern limit, Southern limit. Culminating deviates to the north 1.6.1. Depressed deviates to the north 2.1. Culminating deviates to the south 0.24. Depressed deviates to the south 7.30.1. The remaining

[Planetary Latitudes and Anomalies]
The remaining changes in latitudes, which accompany the position of the planets outside the apogee line of the epicycle, and which occur when the center of the epicycle wanders beyond the limits of the greatest latitude, must be sought from tables. And according to the astrological principle, as the center of the epicycle deviates much or little from the mean limits, it must be derived through the anomaly of the eccentric and the anomaly of commutation. Indeed, by the coequated anomaly of commutation, the number of the latitude itself is obtained, and by the absolute anomaly of the eccentric, the proportional minutes are to be extracted. And from these, the proportional minutes corresponding to the number of the latitude are to be retained for the sought latitude of the star. And lest you err, and that you may rightly understand the whole substance of this matter, you must keep in mind the difference between the places of the apogees and the places of the limits of maximum latitude, for these are different. From the apogees of the apsides, the stars, approaching the earth more closely, descend; but receding from the limits of the greatest latitude, they proceed to the nodes. However, the apogee points of Mars are very far removed from the ecliptic, and therefore coincide with its points of greatest latitude. But the limit of the greatest latitude of Saturn precedes the apogee of the eccentric, and is removed from it by 50 parts of the heavens in the direction of the signs, so that the center of its epicycle, advanced through the consequent signs, passes the limit of the greatest latitude before the apogee of the eccentric. But the limit of the greatest latitude of Jupiter follows the apogee of its eccentric, and recedes from it by 20 parts of the heavens in the direction of the signs. Therefore, by adding 20 parts to Jupiter's apogee, and subtracting 50 parts from Saturn's apogee, the places of all the limits and nodes are easily described, as you see. NODES: ascending, descending. P. 8, 26, 2, 2, 8, 26, 27, m, 28, 15, 28, 003. Limits of greatest latitude: North, South. P. 8, M, +, P. 8, 4, P. 4, P. 26, 1, P. 272, P. 27, P. 1ST, II, P. 28, m, P. 28. Therefore, since in Mars the apsides of the eccentric and the limits of the greatest latitudes exist simultaneously, it is for this reason that by the coequated anomaly of the eccentric, similarly as in the Moon, by its true motion in latitude, we easily deduce the quality of its latitude, whether it is northern or southern. For when the coequated anomaly of Mars' eccentric is either nothing or a full semicircle, it indicates that the center of the epicycle is at the limits of the greatest latitude; northern, indeed, when that anomaly is nothing, and southern when it is a semicircle. But when it is equal to three-quarters of a circle, the center of the epicycle occupies the ascending node, from which it gradually moves northward; but when it is equal to a quadrant, the center of the epicycle likewise occupies the other node, from which it descends southward from the ecliptic. Thus, Mars ascends in latitude from the perigee to the north, and descends from the apogee to the south. Venus and Mercury do almost the same, but the difference is made by the diverse departure of the apsides, which in Venus is always northern, and in Mercury always southern. Furthermore, the stars of Saturn and Jupiter plainly reject this law, because the limit of the greatest northern latitude is placed for Saturn 50 parts before the apogee of the eccentric, and for Jupiter 20 parts beyond the apogee. And this is the reason why in Saturn's tables it is prescribed that the southern latitude is to be extracted by its anomaly of commutation when the coequated anomaly of the eccentric is greater than 40 parts and less than 220 parts; but northern for the remaining hemicycle of the eccentric anomaly. Similarly in Jupiter, the southern latitude is taken by the anomaly of commutation when the coequated anomaly of the eccentric is greater than 110 parts and less than 290 parts; but northern for the remaining hemicycle of the eccentric. Therefore, the latitude of Saturn and Jupiter does not ascend northward from the perigees of their eccentrics, as Mars does, but rather from the southern limits of greatest latitude; and similarly, it does not descend southward from the apogees, but rather from the northern limits of latitude. In our example, in the calculation of Saturn, the coequated anomaly of the eccentric was found to be 114 degrees, 32 minutes, 18 seconds, 142 thirds, to which correspond proportional minutes of 57, 14, 50, 29. The coequated anomaly of commutation was found to be 1 sign, 15 degrees, 33 minutes, 18 seconds, 22 thirds, by entering which into the table of southern latitude (because the anomaly of the eccentric greatly exceeds 40 parts and is less than 220 parts), we find the corresponding number to be 2 degrees, 9 minutes, 1 second. This, multiplied by the proportional minutes of 57, 1, 50, 2, yields the numbers 2 degrees, 41 minutes, 20 seconds, 2, 30 thirds, for the southern latitude of Saturn, descending from the northern limit of latitude beyond the ecliptic to the south, and distant by scarcely 15 parts from the southern limit. This is indicated by the number 1641, which is less than a semicircle, formed by the coequated anomaly of the eccentric with the 50 parts by which Saturn's northern limit precedes the apogee of the eccentric. From this, our common and irrevocable error in the first difference, published in so many copies, can easily be corrected, where, calculating the same example, we carelessly said that Saturn was southern and descending from the apogee of the eccentric, which is indeed very true, but wrongly applied to the descent of latitude, which has different limits from the apogees. Hence, multiplying the proportional minutes of 25, 10, 28, 2, 22, 3, corresponding to Jupiter's eccentric anomaly, by the latitude of 1 degree, 7 minutes, 1 second, corresponding to Jupiter's commutation in the table of southern latitude (for Jupiter's coequated eccentric anomaly, being 264 parts, is greater than 110 parts and less than 290 parts), we produced 0 degrees, 28 minutes, 1 second, 26, 2, 38, 3. This is Jupiter's southern latitude, ascending northward from the southern limit, which is clearly indicated by the number 224, less than a hemicycle, remaining after subtracting 20 parts from the eccentric anomaly. Finally, multiplying the minutes of 7, 1, 56, 28, 26, 3, corresponding to Mars' eccentric anomaly, by the numbers of southern latitude corresponding to Mars' commutation, namely 6 degrees, 35 minutes, 18 seconds, 2, 28, 30, 3, we produced 0 degrees, 32 minutes, 13 seconds, 16, 23, 54, 3, which is Mars' southern latitude, having recently passed the descending node and progressing southward from the ecliptic. This is indicated by the number of 108 parts of the eccentric anomaly, exceeding a quadrant and falling short of a semicircle. Having explained the reason for the latitude of the three superior planets, it remains to explain the same for the two inferior planets. However, since their latitudes are found to be even more variable, they are distinguished by a triple ratio or difference, one of which is attributed to the eccentrics, and the other two to the epicycles. The first latitude, which the departure of the eccentric from the ecliptic causes, is almost such as in the three superior planets; but the difference is in this, that the apsides of the three superior planets maintain a constant distance from the ecliptic, while the apsides of the two inferior planets, like cradles constantly agitated on a diameter connecting the nodes, sometimes unite with the ecliptic, and sometimes, receding from it and then approaching it again, are accustomed to be distant from the ecliptic by plainly unstable intervals. For when the centers of the epicycles occupy the nodes, the apsides are conjoined with the ecliptic without any departure. Then, when the centers of the epicycles, having left the nodes, proceed to the auges of the equant or the limits of maximum latitude, then the apsides themselves, leaving the centers of the epicycles and having left the ecliptic, gradually depart to its sides, and having traversed a quadrant, similarly approach the ecliptic again. However, this departure of the apsis, agitated here and there, as I said, from the ecliptic, in Venus is always northern, and when it is greatest, it contains 10 minutes; but in Mercury it is always northern, never exceeding an amplitude of 45 minutes, 12 seconds. For it has been found that the center of Venus's epicycle is never carried otherwise than towards the north, both around the apogee and around the perigee; but Mercury's only towards the south. This is the first reason for the latitude of Venus and Mercury, which Ptolemy calls the inclination of the eccentric, and our artists call the deviation. Of the remaining two reasons attributed to the epicycle, one, which Ptolemy calls the inclination of the epicycle, and our artists call the declination, they have in common with the three superior planets, by which, as has been said, latitudes are changed by the inclination of the epicycle's apogee and perigee from the plane of the deferent; the other is proper and peculiar, called by Ptolemy the oblique.

[Calculating Planetary Latitudes]
which by our authors is called reflection, or rather obliquation, by which the latitudes themselves are varied through the permutation of the points of the mean epicyclic longitude. Furthermore, in the tables of the motions of these latitudes, each is explained by peculiar canons, having their own proportional scruples attached. Therefore, the method of operation by which the latitude of Mars is derived, for each latitude, must be repeated once and for all, three times before all else. As for the quality of the latitude, the deviation of Venus, as we said a little earlier, is always northern, while that of Mercury is southern. The canons inscribed above indicate what kind of latitude the declinations and obliquations are. Having calculated these, if all the ratios of latitude are of one affection, the sum of all will give you the desired latitude: if not, we must compare the sum of two of one affection to the third remaining latitude, by subtracting the smaller from the larger. For the desired latitude will be the excess, retaining the name of that whose excess or remainder it was. Example: In the calculation of Venus, the equated anomaly of the eccentric is found to be sign 11, part 22, 34, and the anomaly of commutation is sign 1, part 12, 50. By means of these arcs, the deviation of Venus was found to be northern, 7 scruples, 13, 48, and the declination 6 scruples, 1, 33, southern: finally, the obliquation 57 scruples, 14, 25, northern. Furthermore, since the reflection agrees in name with the deviation, which is always northern, we must therefore combine these latitudes: having done so, we will produce part 1, 51, 13, from which, subtracting the declination latitude, which is southern, 6 scruples, 1, 33, there remains the latitude still northern, 58 scruples, 12, 40, of ascending Venus. In the same way, for Mercury, by the equated anomalies of the eccentric and commutation, of which the former is sign 6, part 10, 51, and the latter sign 4, part 5, 54, the deviation, which is always southern, was found to be 51 scruples, 18, 48, and the southern declination 17 scruples, 18, 51, and finally the northern obliquation was first calculated as part 2, 37, 20, and afterwards, by the addition of its tenth part, it became part 2, 53, 4. For in the calculation of Mercury's latitudes, the tenth part of the obliquation is rejected when the anomaly of the eccentric is either less than a quadrant or greater than three quadrants of a circle: but it is added through the remaining semicircle, that is, when the anomaly of the eccentric is greater than a quadrant and less than three quadrants, as in our example. Furthermore, the first two southern latitudes aggregated make part 1, 9, 39, which, rejecting the latitude of obliquation, we make the remaining latitude still northern, part 1, 43, 19, 25, of descending Mercury. In ephemerides, letters are also placed on the characters of the five planets, which show the ascent of the latitude to the northern limit, and the descent to the southern: MA for meridional ascending, SA for septentrional ascending, MD for meridional descending, SD for septentrional descending. If these letters are found in the middle of the columns, they indicate that around that day the planet changes from northern to southern latitude, or vice versa, or that having finished its ascent, it descends, or having reached the limit of its descent, it rises again. Finally, at the foot of the table of planets, the latitudes for the first, tenth, and twentieth day of the month, denominated by the letters just explained, are noted, which if one wishes to adapt to each intermediate day, the difference between the two immediately written latitudes must be divided by the number of intervening days: for the number of the section will indicate how much must be added or subtracted for each day, so that the certain latitude for the desired days is obtained. Concerning the latitude of the Moon to be found by the true motion of latitude, it has been said above. Although it is mostly omitted in the description of ephemerides, it is nevertheless easily extracted daily from the tables of the Moon's latitude in all ephemeris books described for this purpose, in the following manner. From the ephemeris, describe both the true motion of the Dragon's Head and the Moon in signs and parts of signs. From this, subtract the true motion of the Head from the true motion of the Moon, assuming, if necessary, twelve signs, so that the distance of the Moon from the Dragon's Head remains, which in Alfonso's and most other tables is called the true argument of the Moon's latitude, whose common signs are at the head or foot of the present table, and the parts on the right side, descending if those signs are at the top, but ascending on the left side if those signs are at the bottom of the table. For the number in the common angle, as they call it, will show you the desired latitude of the Moon: whether it should be called ascending or descending, northern or southern, the titles of the table clearly declare. In our example, the true motion of the Moon found is sign 10, part 27, 52, and the true motion of the Head is sign 8, part 25, 31. Now, subtracting the latter from the former, there remains the argument of the Moon, true, of 2 signs, 2 parts, 21, which in the table will show the Moon's latitude to be northern ascending, 4 parts, 25 scruples. And this is the Alfonsine method of the Moon's latitude, counting the argument from the ascending node of the Moon, and used by most who have compiled ephemerides. TABLE OF THE MOON'S LATITUDE. If, following the calculation of Ptolemy, Copernicus, and Salueldensis, you wish to measure the Moon's latitude from the northern limit, then from the Alfonsine argument of the Moon's latitude, which we have just designated as an example, sign 2, part 2, 21, you must subtract a quadrant or three common signs, adjusting with a whole circle if the matter so requires, so that you make the true motion of the Moon's latitude from the northern limit remain, which in the present example is sign 11, part 2, 21. By means of this, from the canon of latitude described in full at the end of the Prutenic tables, both the quantity and quality of the Moon's latitude are easily and no differently found than by the Alfonsine argument a little earlier extracted from the present table, namely, part 4, scruple 25, northern ascending. This latter method of finding the Moon's latitude was used by the most learned and diligent mathematician, our intimate friend Johannes Stadius, in his ephemerides recently calculated from the Prutenic tables and published in the year of the saving birth 1555. But, as I believe, being occupied with other matters, which often happens to the most learned and diligent men, he erred in this, that for obtaining the true motion of the Moon's latitude from the northern limit, he prescribed adding three signs to the Alfonsine argument, which, as I said, ought to be subtracted.

[Planetary Phases and Almugea]
The three higher planets, after they emerge from the Sun's rays, are called oriental, and are said to increase in strength up to 30 degrees from the Sun. After this, up to another 30 degrees, they are called strong or stronger oriental. When they have passed the Sun by 60 degrees, they are called oriental planets moving towards weakness, until they become retrograde. Then they are named oriental retrograde, until they are in opposition. After this, they will be occidental retrograde when they have passed opposition, until they reach direct motion. Then, from direct motion up to 60 degrees after direct motion, until their distance from the Sun is 30 degrees, they are called occidental moving towards weakness. Then they become weak occidental until they enter under the Sun's rays. The inferior planets, from the time they separate from the Sun, and are retrograde, are called weak oriental. They remain in this state until they reach their direct motion. Then they become strong oriental, until their distance from the Sun is as the Sun's distance from them was when they began to weaken, moving towards retrogradation. Then they become weak oriental until they are under the Sun's rays. After this, they are conjoined, then combust, moving towards appearance until they are seen. And from the time they separate from the Sun in direct motion up to 60 degrees, they are stronger. And from 60 degrees up to the hour of their retrogradation, they will be strong occidental. Until they are with the Sun, they will be weak. And when a planet emerges from under the Sun's rays and is not conjoined to any other planet, it is said to be in its own light. This is the state of planets with the luminaries. The third type of planetary condition is the collation of planets to the light, etc., the luminaries. And first, concerning the 'persona' or aspect of planets to the luminaries. For the Chaldean word 'Almugea' here signifies nothing other than such a configuration of planets with the Sun or Moon as their domiciles have with the domiciles of the luminaries. This occurs when there is as much interval between the planet and the Sun/Moon as the house of the planet is distant from the house of the Sun/Moon. This dignity is also approved by Ptolemy and is called 'idioprosopon'. His words are these: 'They are said to bear their own persona when each is configured with the Sun or Moon in the same way that their domiciles are with the domiciles of the luminaries.' For example, if Venus is in a sextile configuration with the luminaries, but in such a way that it is occidental to the Sun and oriental to the Moon, according to the original houses. Therefore, a planet is said to be in Almugea when there are as many signs from the Sun to the planet as there are from the Sun to the principal house of that planet according to the order of the signs. Otherwise, it would not be occidental, which is absolutely required in Almugea. For example, Saturn is in Almugea when it occupies the fifth sign after the Sun. The author says the sixth, which is to be understood inclusively, as if it were in Aries and Saturn in Capricorn, Saturn is said to be in the Almugea of the Sun. Similarly, if Jupiter is in the fifth sign from the Sun, Mars in the fourth, the Sun is said to see it face to face. For this is how the common people call this dignity. But it is also said to bear its own persona when it is in the following sign after the Sun, wherever the Sun may be, as when the Sun is in Aries and Venus in Virgo. Finally, Venus is said to be in Almugea when it has obtained the second sign, or rather the third inclusively, from the Sun, that is, when it is distant from the Sun by a sextile radiation, being occidental. This aspect can never occur exactly, since it never recedes from the Sun by so many signs. Therefore, this must be understood as an aspect 'in latitude'. Almugea of the Moon occurs for oriental planets remaining in the signs preceding the Moon's place, so that from the planet to the Moon, as many signs are counted as there are between Cancer and the secondary house of the planet against the succession of the signs. For example, Cancer and the secondary house of Saturn, namely Capricorn, are distant by a space of six signs. When, therefore, six signs are counted from the Moon according to the succession of the signs, there will be, as they call it, a 'face-to-face' vision between them, all of which is evident from the description provided for the eye. Almugea of the Sun: When from the Sun, inclusively, against the succession of the signs, there are 4 signs to the planet. Almugea of the Moon: When from the Moon, inclusively, according to the order of the signs, there are 4 signs to the planet. But Ptolemy, in Book 3, chapter 'On Parents', where he prefers occidental planets to the Sun and oriental planets to the Moon, seems to contradict this tradition, in which oriental and occidental are required from the Sun. We respond: It is true that planets are disposed as oriental to the Sun and occidental to the Moon. But if it ever happens that they are occidental, their best disposition, which they can achieve in such a state, will be when they are distant from the Sun by as many signs as their houses follow or precede the Sun. They call a planet 'victorious' when the star is in places similar to its own nature, which they call Hayz, and is in one of its angles, and moreover, the conditioning luminary is placed in an angle from it, so that by day the planet is oriental to the Sun, and by night occidental to the Moon. This will almost always happen if by day the planet is in the tenth sign from the Sun, for then, according to the equal distribution of houses, it can be in an angle from the Sun and oriental. By night, however, in the fourth sign from the Moon according to the order of the signs. For in this case also, it can be in an angle from the Moon and set after the Moon. But an opposite angle cannot apply here, for a planet opposite the luminaries is correctly said to be neither oriental nor occidental. However, I do not find this dignity in Ptolemy. And every planet placed under the rays, called 'subradiatus', 'plasmatis', which word, because it seemed harsher, the moderns preferred to call a planet affected in this way by the Sun's rays 'combust'. According to common opinion, a planet is said to be combust when it is less than half its orb distant from the Sun, or precisely so much before and after. But the author thinks more correctly and agrees more with Ptolemy, who states that planets are combust from the point of occultation to the point of emergence, where they first shine forth and appear, shedding the Sun's rays. However, this time or interval during which planets lie hidden under the Sun's rays is not constant due to the unequal ascent and descent of the signs, nor, as the author believes, always concluded by 12 parts before and after, but is variable many times due to different planets and signs. As can be seen in the following canon of occultation and emergence, translated here from the Prutenic tables, which indicates when both inferior and superior planets begin to appear or are occulted, which is very important to know, since a planet recently emerging into view from the Sun's rays obtains an excellent state, but when occulted, it lacks all strength. The use of the table is as follows: First of all, from the second face of the Ephemerides, or if it is possible to use the calculation of tables, the type of rising or setting must be known through the equated anomaly of commutation or epicycle, according to the doctrine to be explained. Afterwards, by the distance of the same star from the Sun, it must be investigated whether it is visible or not visible, whether it emerges, or whether it hides itself in the Sun's rays, in this way: Enter the canon of occultation and emergence with the sign in which the planet is, and extract the arc corresponding to that type of rising or setting. For if this arc is smaller than the planet's distance from the Sun, the planet will be visible. If it is larger, it will lie hidden within the Sun's rays. If it is equal, it will emerge or be occulted, as its distance from the Sun increases or decreases in the following days. In our example, the equated anomaly of Jupiter is 37 degrees, 13 minutes. Therefore, since it is clear from this that Jupiter is not far from the apogee of its epicycle, it is manifest that it is either visible a little after sunset, or hidden, oppressed by the Sun's rays at evening setting. The comparison of the longitude by which Jupiter and the Sun differ to the arc of vision easily resolves this hesitation. And it is

[Jupiter's Evening Occultation]
Jupiter in the 27th degree, 12th minute of Gemini, distant from the Sun by 18 degrees, 19th minute. The canon for the beginning of Gemini, under the title of Jupiter's evening occultation, shows 10 degrees, 16 minutes, an arc of vision smaller than Jupiter's distance, and therefore clearly indicates that Jupiter will soon enter the Sun's rays, still visible in the evening after sunset. Furthermore, since the canon assumes that planets hold the beginnings of dodecatemoria, when we find a planet traversing further parts of the dodecatemoria, by using the part corresponding to the degrees, we will arrive at an exact knowledge of this arc. In the present example, 30 parts of Gemini in the arc of Jupiter's evening vision make a difference of 1 degree, 28 minutes, of which the part corresponding to 27 parts is 1 degree, 6 minutes, which, combined with the arc assigned to the beginning of Gemini, produces an exact arc of vision of 22 degrees, 13 minutes, which itself declares Jupiter, though near its occultation, to be visible after sunset. Finally, it must be known that these arcs vary so much in each climate that a more exact investigation of these matters would require far more extensive canons. When a star is in the same degree as the Sun, and is distant from it by as much space as the apparent semidiameters of the Sun and the star combined, both in longitude and latitude, or less, it is said to be ἐν τῷ καρδίᾳ τοῦ ἡλίου εἶναι, that is, in the heart of the Sun, or, to use a foreign but common term among us, to be in Cazimi of the Sun. But if it is less distant than the said semidiameters in longitude, but more in latitude, it nevertheless remains combust. An example of such a conjunction is this: Let Venus be in 15 degrees, 44 minutes of Capricorn in mean longitude, where its apparent semidiameter is 4 minutes without latitude; and let the Sun be in 15 degrees, 24 minutes of the same Capricorn, where its apparent semidiameter is 16 minutes, 14 seconds, 30 thirds. They are therefore distant by 20 minutes. And because the Sun's semidiameter is 16 minutes, 18 seconds, 30 thirds, and Venus's apparent semidiameter is 4 minutes, the combined longitude from both semidiameters will be 20 minutes, 19 seconds, 30 thirds, which is more than the minutes of distance, which are at least 20 minutes. Given these conditions, we will say that the bodies of the Sun and Venus almost touch. For it is scarcely possible to know such a contact precisely, as Monteregius rightly warns in his tables of directions. Judge the same for the bodily conjunction of planets with the Moon, or of fixed stars with other planets, in all of which both latitude and longitude must be considered. For when they agree in longitude and latitude, they will effect a bodily conjunction, as it is called, and when they are more distant than their semidiameters by reason of latitude, they will effect at least a conjunction of longitude, as will be said shortly: Folio 196. We have already explained the apparent magnitudes of all stars above, at the beginning of the fifth difference. From this, the following section on the rising and apparent setting of wandering stars is closely related. A star is said to rise in the morning when it begins to be seen before the Sun's emergence; in the evening when it is seen after sunset. And a star is occulted in the morning when it ceases to be seen around dawn; in the evening, when it ceases to be seen after sunset. Given these conditions, it is clear that there are four general forms of this appearance: two of rising, and as many of setting; the summary of which we have described in a figure. Rising. Morning rising, when they begin to be seen before sunrise. Evening rising, a little after sunset. Morning occultation, when they begin to be occulted before sunrise. Evening occultation, in the evening after sunset. Furthermore, the three superior planets, like the fixed stars, from the conjunction with the Sun to its opposition, while descending from the apogee of the epicycle to its perigee, are called oriental (ἔῳοι καὶ προηγούμενοι), left behind by the Sun in precedence; but from the opposition to the conjunction, while ascending from the perigee of the epicycle to its apogee, they are called occidental (ἑσπέριοι καὶ ἑπόμενοι), and as if placed by the Sun in the following parts of the zodiac. Hence it is clear that they emerge from the Sun's rays in the morning, or as the Greeks say, ἐπιτέλλομεν, a little after the Sun's conjunction, when they have receded somewhat into the following parts (εἰς τὰ ἑπόμενα) from the apogee of the epicycle; and conversely, they are occulted and vanish in the evening (κρύπτεσθαι καὶ ἀφανίζεσθαι) a little before the Sun's conjunction, when, having been lifted from the perigee of the epicycle, they are not far from its apogee. Besides these two forms, they admit no others, except that when placed in the Sun's diameter, they are peculiarly called ἀκρόνυκτοι or ἀκρόνυχοι, because at the beginning of the night, when the Sun sets, they rise exactly opposite in the eastern horizon, whereas before, remaining beyond and within the first station, they always rose at night. But the Moon observes at least two forms, one of rising, the other of setting, contrary to the forms of the three superior planets. For in its very swift motion from conjunction to full moon, it rises in an evening rising unknown to the three superior planets; and then, having departed from opposition, it hastens to a morning setting likewise unknown to the three superior planets. But the two inferior planets, because they are, as it were, around the Sun.

[Translation of Light]
TRANSFER of nature from a lighter to a heavier planet. 6. If, however, neither of these is joined to the other, but each is joined to a third, then if that third planet aspects some place in the circle, it returns their light to that place: this is called the return of light. 7. Again, if one planet is not joined to another, but a third planet has transferred light to both, then this conjunction is also called the return of light. 8. Next follows prohibition, and it occurs in two ways. One, namely, by conjunction, that is, when there are three planets in one sign, but in different degrees: if the heavier planet has more degrees, then the one in the middle prohibits the former, that is, the one with fewer degrees, from joining the heavier until it passes it. In the second way, if two planets are in one sign, and the lighter joins the heavier, and another also joins the same heavier planet by aspect: then the one that is with it in one sign prohibits the aspecting planet from conjunction with the heavier, if the degrees of the one joining and the one aspecting are equal, that is, of the same number. But if the one aspecting is closer in degrees to the heavier, it will be the conjunction of the aspecting planet. 9. And if a planet is joined to the ruler of the sign in which it is, or to the ruler of its exaltation, or to the ruler of its other dignities, it is said to 'strike', that is, to send the nature of that ruling planet, namely, of the same dignity, to it. 10. And if a planet is in one of its dignities, and is joined to another planet which also has a part of dignity in the same place: it also sends to it both natures, its own and that of the one to which it is joined: and this entire sending is called Alcochod, that is, reception. 11. Now follows the return, that is, when a planet returns virtue to another planet that is combust, that is, under the Sun's rays, or retrograde, because of its own weakness, which it cannot retain. Then if both planets are in an angle or succedent houses, the return will be with profit: similarly if the one joining it is only in an angle, and the one to which it is joined receives it. But if the planet that joins is cadent from an angle, and the one to which it is joined is in an angle or succedent house, or if both are cadent, the return will be with detriment. 12. Next follows Almenen, that is, refranation, which occurs when a planet wants to join another, but before it joins, retrogradation occurs, and thus its conjunction is destroyed. 13. This is followed by Alichorad, that is, accidental contrariety. This occurs when a light planet has many degrees in a sign, and another, heavier than it, has fewer degrees, and a third, lighter than the first, wants to join the heavier: but before it joins it, the lighter one with more degrees becomes retrograde, and joins and applies to that heavy one, and passing it, also joins another lighter planet: and thus the conjunction of the former with the heavy one is destroyed. 14. Next follows Alfazim, that is, frustration. This also occurs when a planet seeks the conjunction of another planet, but before it reaches it, this one changes into another sign, and there will be some planet in few degrees aspecting that sign, and its rays will be at the beginning of the sign. And when the following planet leaves the first sign, it joins this aspecting one, and the conjunction it had with that one, namely with the first, is annulled. 15. Hence follows the cutting off of light, that is: when a planet seeks the conjunction of another, and is in the second sign from the sign of the other planet to which it is joined: but before it joins it, the one in the second sign first becomes retrograde, and joins it and cuts off its light from the planet that previously wanted to join. 16. Similarly, if a light planet is applying to the conjunction of another planet, and that other planet to which it wants to join seeks the conjunction of another planet heavier than itself: but before the light planet reaches the degrees of the heavier one, the heavier one joins another planet heavier than itself, and cuts off its light from the first lighter planet. 17. Next follows the fourth kind of passions that happen to planets when compared to each other. The first event of this kind is syzygy, or conjunction: which occurs when stars are either in one region of the sky, or in the radiation of fire, or finally, when they aspect each other by antiscion. For the word syzygy is general, and signifies not only application or coition, but also testimony by aspect. And Ptolemy's phrase in the fourth book of his Apotelesmatics sufficiently indicates that these words are to be distinguished in this way, where, speaking of the geometricians, he wrote: 'nor do the rhomboidal ones, which are distant from the anaphas, receive good wives by conjunction and by testimony, if the malefics are contrary.' Furthermore, of conjunctions, some are partile or perfect, others are platical or imperfect. But of those, etc. imperfect. And of partile conjunctions, some are of longitude, others of latitude, the most absolute of all conjunctions. And in a partile conjunction of longitude, stars are joined when they are found either in one circle described through the points of the zodiac, or in different ones, but nevertheless aspecting each other partilely through parts of the ecliptic, even if they are separated by latitude towards the sides of the world. As if the Sun is at the beginning of Leo, and Mars is there with its maximum southern latitude, then Mars is said to be partilely joined to the Sun according to longitude, even if they are separated by a space of seven degrees of latitude in the sky. Similarly, one should judge concerning the conjunctions of aspects. But stars cohere in a partile conjunction of latitude when, besides the agreement of longitude, they also agree in latitude. This is observed by Ptolemy, neglecting aspects in the bodily conjunctions of the Sun and stars, and also, as it seems, in diametrical oppositions, whence it deserves to be called a bodily syzygy. And although the bodies of the stars pass to opposition in at least one case: nevertheless, it is manifest that they can meet in bodies in several ways. First, if, as the author says, in the application of conjunction, tending simultaneously to one side of the world, what latitude they acquire. As if the Moon and Mars, agreeing in minutes of longitude, and tending simultaneously to the maximum northern limit of latitude, or thence to the ascending node, have equal latitude, they will conjoin most perfectly both in longitude and in latitude. Then stars can be corporally and most perfectly conjoined, even if they travel to different limits or nodes, provided their latitudes are of the same form, that is, northern or southern and equal: and this is what the author says, that in this case it must happen that the latitude of one is ascending in the north, and that of the other descending in the north: or of one ascending in the south, and of the other descending in the south. For example, if the Moon and Saturn agree in parts and minutes according to longitude such that Saturn is near its ascending node, and having entered the north, has already acquired a northern latitude of three minutes: and the Moon, descending from the northern limit, is approaching its descending node, decreasing its northern latitude, and is similarly distant from the Ecliptic by three minutes, a bodily syzygy of the Moon and Saturn will be effected, that is, a most perfect conjunction of bodies, as far as longitude and latitude are concerned, so much so that the Moon in such a meeting of those stars would deprive us of their sight, unless a very large parallax, or, as they call it, diversity of aspect, prevented it. Then, from the bodily coition, beyond and on this side, forwards and backwards, by the space that the apparent semidiameters of both luminaries compose, receding, they maintain a platical conjunction as long as, as will be said presently, their rays are mixed. From what I have said, what a bodily syzygy of stars is, is clear. It remains for us to explain the bodily configuration, that is, the bodily conjunctions of aspects, of which the diametrical one not only has diverse and equal, but also contrary latitudes, that is, one northern, the other southern.

[Table of Trigon and Hexagon Latitudes]
2. A table explaining the latitude of the trigon and hexagon of stars more than 8 parts distant from the Ecliptic. This is a clear ratio of bodily conjunctions, not involved in any obscure ambiguities, and if you consider the matter correctly, it almost agrees with Ptolemy's tradition concerning the equicrural figure explained above. However, Blanchinus and many other astrologers, by no means common, apply this theory not only to the conjunctions of bodies, but also to all other aspects and simple radiations, and they direct such empty places for stars, just as they observe the latitude of stellar bodies significantly distant from the Ecliptic. And since the circles of latitudes passing through the poles of the Ecliptic and the limits of radiations having latitude do not coincide at the limits of radiations simply calculated on the Ecliptic in the manner described above, but extend beyond the hexagon and within the trigon, therefore, in order to establish some equation of aspects, they use "prothaphairia" (scruples), the multitude of which varies according to the latitude of the planets whose aspects are being considered, as the series of numbers described here indicates to you. Latitude of planet. Scruples of prothaphairia. Latitude of planet. Scruples of prothaphairia. These scruples are added to hexagons and subtracted from trigons. For example, Mars, located at 15 degrees 49 minutes 1 second of Capricorn, with a southern latitude of 7 parts, has a sinister sextile aspect falling at 15 degrees 49 minutes 1 second of Pisces. If you add 111 scruples, corresponding to seven parts of latitude, you will find the equated sinister aspect of Mars falls exactly at the end of 16 degrees of Pisces, with a southern latitude of 3 degrees 29 minutes 19 seconds 37 parts 28. However, the sinister trine radiation of the same Mars, which, simply calculated by longitude, falls at 15 degrees 49 minutes 1 second of Taurus, when it omits the same 111 scruples due to the equation, ends at 15 degrees 48 minutes of Taurus, with a northern latitude of 3 degrees 29 minutes 1 second 37 parts 2. The same judgment applies to dexter radiations. I, however, could find no cause worthy of mention that could or should have impelled practitioners to follow this laborious and troublesome operation, which seems to display much ostentation and no fruit. Some have presented it as fortified by the authority of Monteregius. But not carefully enough. For Monteregius rejects this method of operation as weak and useless, so far is he from approving it, and teaches how to translate the bare and simple radiations of stars endowed with latitude to the Ecliptic without any observation of latitude, in this way: Every star, like all others, also emits those most efficacious rays which we observe in an orbicular manner, so that for a hexagonal radiation we have the periphery of that circle, which is described by one side of a large celestial hexagon inscribed in such a way that one extremity of the said side, ending at the center of the star, remains fixed, and the side itself, together with the limiting extension, is rotated so that it returns to the place from which it began to move. Similarly, one must judge concerning all other radiations, except that Monteregius, no differently than Blanchinus, wished the place of a square radiation to always be distant from the place of the planet's longitude by a quadrant of the Ecliptic, and the opposite radiation to end at the diameter's terminus. These things being so, it is clear that a hexagonal ray does not penetrate the Ecliptic in any part of itself if the star's latitude exceeds 60 parts; but it intersects the Ecliptic in two places if the star's latitude is less than 60 parts. Example: Let it be decreed to explore to what parts of the Ecliptic Mars projects its sextile rays, being placed at 15 degrees 49 minutes 1 second of Capricorn, with a southern latitude of 7 parts. If you now wish to clearly experience this on a celestial globe, first of all find the place of Mars, both in longitude and latitude, on the instrument. This done, then with a compass, opened to the interval of the side of the hexagon calculated on the Ecliptic, or what is the same, on the equinoctial, describe from the place of Mars as a center the periphery of a circle, representing the hexagonal radiation of Mars, which periphery, intersecting the Ecliptic in two places equally distant from the star on either side, that is, 59 degrees 45 minutes 1 second, will define to the left the limit of the sinister sextile radiation, and to the right the terminus of the dexter sextile radiation. In the same way, one must think about the termini of both trine and square radiations. The table of rays inscribed here, which defines the places of both trine and sextile radiations, explains to you the variety of these intervals, which different stellar latitudes create. TABLE OF RAYS for the hexagonal and trine radiation of Monteregius. Arc of Latitude, Ecliptic. If, therefore, according to Monteregius's opinion, you wish to equate the radiations or aspects of stars endowed with noteworthy latitude, and to prepare them for deductions, which they call directions, within the table of rays with the planet's latitude, an arc of the Ecliptic will immediately present itself to you, defining the longitude of the dexter and sinister hexagonal radiation to be calculated from the place of the star's longitude. When this is subtracted from 60 parts, the scruples of equation will remain to be subtracted from the longitude of both sextile radiations, but to be added to the longitude of both trine radiations, so that the equated longitude of all these aspects may appear. According to this method, therefore, Mars, constituted at 15 degrees 49 minutes 1 second of Capricorn, with a northern latitude of 7 parts, extends its dexter hexagonal ray to 15 degrees 34 minutes 18 seconds of Scorpio, and its sinister ray to 15 degrees 34 minutes 1 second of Pisces. But it projects its dexter trine radiation to 16 degrees 41 minutes of Virgo, and its sinister to 16 degrees 41 minutes of Taurus. The longitudes of square radiations, whose dexter extends to 15 degrees 49 minutes 1 second of Libra, and sinister to 15 degrees 49 minutes 1 second of Aries, as has been said, require no such equation. Similarly, neither does the longitude of the diameter, which ends at 15 degrees 49 minutes 1 second of Cancer. However, stars lacking latitude, or rather, not more than 1 degree 4 minutes 1 second distant from the Ecliptic, universally maintain the longitude of all radiations without any equation. From these, the difference between the operations of Blanchinus and Monteregius is clear. For in one, the longitude of the hexagons is increased, while in the other, it is diminished. A similar contradiction is observed in the equation of trine radiations. Leaving it open which of these opinions is truer, for two main reasons it seemed best not to approve either at present: first, because however great the latitude, it can make little difference to the aspects, whether taken from the Ecliptic or by the said methods, so much so that eight parts of latitude make scarcely 20 scruples of difference in places in the ray. The second and most important reason is the authority of Ptolemy, who stated that latitudes should be observed not in the aspects of rays, but in the bodily conjunctions of stars, and then only if the stars meet in one sign. Example: If the Moon is 16 parts distant from the Sun, having a latitude of 7 or 8 parts, it will certainly be outside the Sun's rays. The reason why it is not appropriate to observe latitudes in aspects, he says, is that the rays of the planets are not considered as they come from one to the extremity of the world to the other, but as both rays come to the center of the earth, and form an angle in them. His words at the end of the first book of Apotelesmatics are these: "And generally, those that precede are said to be conjoined with those that follow, and those that follow are said to be separated from those that precede, when there is a great distance between them. And this is taken easily, especially if it happens according to the given calculations, except that for the bodily conjunctions and emanations themselves, and latitudes, one must observe."

[Planetary Latitudes and Aspects]
However, universally, preceding planets are said to apply to succeeding ones. Succeeding planets, however, are said to flow from preceding ones, provided they are not separated by a long interval. This applies to both their bodily conjunctions and configurations, except that in the applications and flows of bodies, it is useful to observe their latitudes. For only those conjunctions are admitted which are found in the same parts of the Ecliptic.
