In configurations, however, such care is superfluous, since all rays are carried to the same point, that is, to the center of the earth, and thus, emitted from anywhere, they always concur. From these words of Ptolemy, it seems to follow that in lunar conjunctions, latitude must be observed, but not in any other aspects. And although Cardan explains this text in this way, and the matter itself holds true (for a bodily conjunction of stars cannot be effective unless they have equal latitudes, and not only in one circle of latitude, but also situated in the same parts of the Ecliptic), nevertheless, we believe that in other aspects too, the observation of stellar latitude, if it refers not to the variety of intervals but to the strength of the aspects, has great importance. For if, for example, an opposition of Mars and Venus occurs, who can doubt that such an opposition will be far more effective and powerful if Mars and Venus have contrary latitudes, than if they both traversed the north or south? Otherwise, what was said above from Ptolemy's opinion about the figure of the equicrural triangle could have no place. And to pass over in silence the trine radiation, there is no doubt that all other bodily aspects gain strength if they are carried from one part of the Ecliptic, and conversely become weaker if they have contrary latitudes. For this reason, it is written in Ptolemy that one of the causes why anaretic places do not always universally have the power to kill is the contrary latitude of the Apheta and Anareta's body. "For the anaretic part is impeded," he says, "if both bodies, that which meets and that which governs, do not have the same latitude or latitude in the same parts." Hence it is clear that the encounter becomes stronger if the Apheta and Anareta agree in southern or northern latitude, but weaker if they disagree. From this, it is evident that Ptolemy here does not entirely reject the conjunctions of stars made in both parts of the Ecliptic, but attributes greater and more powerful force and perfection to conjunctions made in the same parts of the Ecliptic. Indeed, if the latitude is from the same part of the Ecliptic, but unequal, a lesser effect will follow, because body can never penetrate body. But the exact observation of latitudes changes not the intervals, as I said, of radiations, but the strength of the stars. For just as the fixed stars, so also the planets, the closer they are to the Ecliptic, the more evident effects they produce; the more remote, the more obscurely they act, always universally, whether they are near the Ecliptic or very far from it, intending one end of action and one kind of effects, since the natural action of all celestial bodies is rightly referred to the orbit of the Sun, who is the ruler of Olympus, the source of life and light. But why do the latitudes of the stars not change the intervals of configurations in the Ecliptic? Because, as Ptolemy says, all rays are carried to the center of the earth, and thus, emitted from anywhere, they always concur. For greater clarity, let Jupiter be in 15 degrees of Cancer with 2 degrees of southern latitude, and in 15 degrees of Scorpio with 7 degrees of southern latitude. I say that the bodily rays of Mars and Jupiter, concurring at the innermost point of the earth, are equidistant from all sides, that is, by the space of one right angle and a third part of it, or what is the same, by 120 degrees. To understand this more deeply, imagine the orbital radius of each planet, one drawn through a line extended from the center of the earth to the body of Mars, and completely revolved through both poles of the zodiac; the other drawn through another line similarly extending from the center of the earth to the body of Jupiter, and circumducted through the same poles of the zodiac. These things being so, it is manifest that the two hemicycles of the great circles intersect each other on the axis of the Ecliptic passing through the center of the earth, and that in any part beyond or within the Ecliptic, observed equality of angle, they are distant from each other. Similarly, one must think about all other radiations, namely, that they are hemicycles ending at the poles of the zodiac. Since these things are so, it is clear that neither the opinion of Blanchinus, who imagines aspects on the periphery of a circle inclined to the Ecliptic, nor that of Monteregius, who thinks that rays diffused from the bodies of stars as centers are orbs, agrees with Ptolemy's view. As has been shown, Ptolemy transfers all rays into hemicycles intersected at the poles of the zodiac, whose intervals calculated in the Ecliptic are plainly similar to any intervals proceeding towards either pole of the zodiac. And this is the greatest praise of Ptolemy's opinion, that through it, planets are connected not only to stars closely adjacent to the zodiac, but also to all others that weave the entire sky, in one circuit, which no other imagination can achieve. For example, the star shining in the left hand of Perseus and the head of Medusa, called Gorgonion and the head of the Gorgon, has a northern latitude of 23 degrees, which Mars never reaches. And yet, according to this reasoning, these stars will rightly be said to be conjoined when Mars travels through 20 degrees of Taurus, for such is approximately the distance of the Gorgon from the apparent equinox in our time. Such a conjunction will be far more powerful if Mars is in the north with the greatest latitude it can attain. And that Ptolemy approves of such conjunctions can be proven by many of his sayings. For example, in the place cited a little earlier, where he teaches how to infer the number of wives, he writes thus: "If the Moon is in a bicorporeal or multiform sign, or applies to several stars existing in such a sign, it indicates that men will frequently repeat marriage." Here, the signs δίσωμα (bicorporeal) and πολύμορφα (multiform) must be distinguished. For some signs that double the number fall within the zodiac, such as Gemini, Pisces, and Sagittarius, and are called δίσωμα. Others, however, reside outside the zodiac, such as Centaurus, Auriga, Bootes, Hercules, Hydra, and are properly called πολύμορφα, that is, multiform or multiple, none of which, due to their great distance from the Ecliptic, can have the Moon's five parts of latitude.

[Planetary Conjunctions and Separations]
It can enter, never exceeding 338 degrees of latitude, and yet it is said to reside in them when they are in the same longitude. It should not seem strange to anyone that we have dwelt at length on this, because of the constant dispute among practitioners regarding the measurement of ray intervals, the distinction of which through the said hemicycles, if it truly agrees with Ptolemy's opinion, certainly the opinions of Bianchini and Monte, and all others who in any way equate longitude, can not only be considered unnecessary for the practice of the art, but also erroneous. The very method of directing, as they call it, proposed by Ptolemy in Book 3, Chapter on the Length of Life, helps us, where he explicitly states that the strength or power of the stars changes only by reason of latitude, and that the times defined by progression do not vary. For although a star may have little or great latitude, yet for the completion of a direction, it always assumes the hourly times of the Ecliptic part of longitude, that is, of that part of the Ecliptic which the hemicycle passing through the poles of the zodiac and the star defines, just as if the star were traversing the Ecliptic. For if he had thought that the latitudes of the stars could bring any other variation to the times to be defined by direction, that man of immense diligence would undoubtedly not have passed over so great and important a matter in silence. But now it is time to explain the remaining types of conjunctions. Platic conjunctions are those which similarly exist either in one sign or in conjoined signs, but are said to occur according to a broader coherence of rays. This is subdivided into κόλλησιν καὶ ἀπόρροια, that is, agglutination or application and defluxion, which they call separation. Application occurs when the lighter planet follows the heavier, and approaches a partial conjunction with it. Defluxion or separation occurs whenever the lighter planet, existing in more parts, recedes from the heavier: it is sufficient, however, that the lighter, if it is not in more parts, at least recedes by one scruple or more, since their bodies very rarely come together due to the dissimilarity of latitudes: as if it is in 28 degrees 6 minutes, and in 28 degrees 7 minutes of the same sign, the Moon is said to recede from the Sun. But judges disagree as to how much space the mixed force of the rays coheres. The author believes that application and defluxion last as long as the body or primary ray of the lighter planet is 6 degrees distant from the heavier or its ray. The common opinion is that the conjunction of planets lasts as long as their rays are mixed, that is, cohering by touching each other by half of both orbs. In this way, existing in 5 degrees 2 minutes, applies to Saturn traversing 29 degrees of the same sign, and thus in 6 degrees, applies to Saturn residing in 27 degrees, for the difference is 21 degrees, which is made up of the semidiameters of the composite orbs, whose spaces are indicated above: the semidiameter of the Sun is 15 degrees, of the Moon 12 degrees and 49 minutes, of 8 degrees and 7 degrees. Therefore, if is in 3 degrees, and in 26 degrees, then the Sun will be said to apply and go to Mars, and this continues until, by its faster motion, it reaches a part similar to the 5th part of Leo in which Mars is located, and joins it partially. Haly wished that applications occur from three degrees before, and separations one degree beyond the heavier, yet he did not neglect the application according to the quantity of the orbs. Guido says: When one planet projects its rays upon the rays of another, and is distant from it by the space that the semidiameters of both orbs make up: then the lighter is said to seek the conjunction of the heavier, and signifies the effect of the matter, unless another planet destroys such a conjunction. But if they are distant by half of the orb of the other, the conjunction already completed will likewise signify the effect of the matter, unless it is impeded elsewhere. Finally, if the half of the orb of the faster touches the body of the heavier, then the heavier is said to be in the parts of the lighter. It is undoubtedly true that Cardan weighed these limits more diligently and defined them more accurately and truly according to Ptolemy's opinion: therefore, I think he, as an expert, should be followed here above all. Furthermore, the spaces of the sky in which the bodies of the stars and their rays cohere, as Ptolemy indicates, are in 17 degrees, Moon 12 degrees, 4 degrees, 12 degrees, 10 degrees, 7 degrees, 8 degrees, and 7 degrees. Therefore, when the planet to which the application is made, or from which the defluxion occurs, is not more distant from the one applying or separating than the parts assigned in the table, it is called an application or defluxion, so that those parts are understood of the planet to which the application is made, or from which the defluxion occurs, and not of the one that flows away or applies: diligently observing that the application is stronger than the defluxion, and the closer the application is by aspect or body, and the less the separation has receded, the more effective the conjunction is to be judged. For as Cardan wrote, the forces of aspects are like the labor of bending a ballista. For a conjunction or aspect from two parts is ten times that which occurs with a distance of ten parts: and that which is from the same part, ten times that which occurs with a distance of two parts: and that which is by six minutes, ten times that which occurs by a distance of almost one part, whence the greatest events. Also observe that for the application of the lighter to the heavier to occur, it is necessary for the heavier to have completed more degrees in its sign, and the lighter fewer. The heavier or grave planets are called such by the slowness of their motion, such as the three superiors: the lighter ones by their speed, such as the three inferiors. From this it follows that a retrograde or diminished in course lighter planet becomes heavier by nature. In this way, retrograde Mercury is considered heavier than Venus, and this slow in course is considered heavier than the Sun. From what has been said, the first type of application and separation is the simplest and easiest. The second type of application is when both planets are retrograde, but the lighter is in more parts, and by moving faster, overtakes the heavier, as if is in 9 degrees retrograde, and in 8 degrees retrograde. Here, by retrograding faster, pursues Saturn, who is retrograding more slowly and tardily, and finally joins him. But if, with both retrograding, the lighter is in fewer parts, a violent or averse separation occurs, as if is in 8 degrees retrograde, and in 9 degrees retrograde. Here Venus separates from by contrary motion, because she moves faster than Saturn into the preceding signs. The third type of application and defluxion is called mutual. For mutual application occurs when two planets, one which is anterior by direct motion, and the other which follows by retrograde motion, meet each other, as if is in 1 degree.

[Difference in Obstruction]
The difference is that in the first case, the lightest planet is at the beginning of the following sign, and the other two are near the end of the preceding sign. In the case of contrariety, which was mentioned a little earlier, all three planets are in the same sign, and the retrograde planet encounters not only the heavier but also the lighter one. These two forms do not differ in any other respect, so you may correctly conclude both under one name, either contrariety or perturbation or interposition. Similarly, when the light planet seeks the heavier, but before it reaches it, the heavier planet joins another still heavier one, and thus the heaviest planet cuts off the light of the medium from the light one. For example, if Mars is in the 10th degree of Aries, Jupiter in the 9th degree of Aries, and Saturn in the 4th degree of Aries. Here Mars seeks Jupiter, but before it can join Jupiter, Jupiter conjoins Saturn, who cuts off the light of Jupiter from Mars. But this occurrence was called a prohibition above, for no distinction appears between these two. Therefore, since one of these cases pertains to perturbation and the other to prohibition, the cutting off of light still remains to be defined more accurately and properly. Therefore, the cutting off of light is when three planets are within their bounds, and the lightest is in fewer degrees, but by its course, surpassing the others, it overtakes the middle one and reaches the one in more degrees before the middle one joins it. Then that light and swift-moving planet is said to cut off the light of the middle one from the last, which is in more degrees. For example, if Jupiter is in the 28th degree of Taurus, Mars in the 14th degree of Taurus, and the Moon in the 7th degree of Taurus. Here Mars was going to Jupiter, but the Moon, with swifter motion, passing Mars, reaches Jupiter before Mars does, and cuts off the light of Mars from Jupiter. For although the Moon has not yet reached Jupiter, nor is it interposed between Mars and Jupiter, yet it must be judged as if it had already reached it, because everything that is potentially such obtains such power in actuality, from which source all these precepts have flowed. Similarly, the cutting off of light by the rays of another planet, but which is much weaker, is to be understood. For example, if Jupiter is in the 1st degree of Gemini, Mars in the 8th degree of Gemini, and the Moon in the 6th degree of Virgo, casting a ray into the 6th degree of Gemini. Here Mars was going to seek a conjunction with Jupiter, but the Moon, moving faster, passing Mars, having fallen from a square aspect to Jupiter, cuts off the light of Mars from Jupiter, etc.

[Fortunate and Unfortunate Places]
There are also places for these planets in which they are strengthened, and in which they are weakened, and places in which fortunes occur, and places in which misfortunes occur. But the places in which fortunes occur are these: that they are in the aspects of benefics, that is, in some useful aspect, namely a sextile or a trine aspect, because some call the hexagonal and trigonal radiation thus. And that malefics are cadent from them, separated from misfortune and joined to fortune; or that they are besieged by benefics or their rays, that is, that they are between two benefics or between the rays of benefics. This is indeed obsession, that a planet has a benefic or its rays before it, and another benefic or its rays behind it. And some call this 'altifert', that is, veneration. Or that they are united with the Sun in one degree, or that they are in its sextile or trine aspect, or a similar aspect of the Moon. The Moon is then fortunate, and that they are swift in course, augmented in light and number, or that they are in their dignities or in their Hayz, that is, in their similitude, so that a masculine planet is in a masculine sign, and a feminine planet in a feminine sign, and a diurnal planet in the day above the earth, and in the night below the earth, a nocturnal planet in the night above the earth, and in the day below the earth, and so on. That they are in signs in which they have their dignities or powers, or in their joys, or in bright degrees, received. And from their fortitude it is that they are ascending in the north, or are northern, or are ascending in the circle of their apogee, or in the second station, that is, when they are in the station where they are directed from retrogradation, or are emerging from under the Sun's rays, or are in an angle, or in its succedent. Or that three are higher and oriental from the Sun: for if they aspect with a sextile or trine aspect, this will be an increase of strength for them, or if they are in masculine quarters. And the Sun, when it is in those masculine quarters, or in masculine signs, will also be strong, unless it is in Libra, because there it falls. And from the strength of the three inferior planets it is that they are occidental from the Sun, or in feminine quarters. And from the strength of the Moon, that it is in the night above the earth, and in the day below the earth, in a feminine place, or in a feminine sign, when it is in the exaltation of the Sun. Regarding the misfortune of planets. From the misfortune and destruction of planets it is that they are in conjunction with malefics, or in opposition to them, or in their square, or trine, or sextile radiation. Or if there is between them and the body of a malefic or its rays less than the term of the planet, or if they are in the terms of malefics, or their houses, or if malefics are elevated above them from the 10th or 11th house from their places, and much worse if they do not receive them. Or if they are in conjunction with the Sun, or its opposition, or in a square radiation, or if they are in the heads of their Genzahar, or with their tails, or with the head of the Dragon or the tail, and there are between them and any of these places 12 degrees or less: and especially if the Moon is in any of them. Similarly, the Sun is then more impeded by them, that is, by the head or tail, when there are between it and one of them even 3 degrees before or after. Or if planets are besieged between two malefics, that is, that a planet is in some sign and with it a malefic, or its rays before it, and a malefic or its rays behind it; or it is separated from a malefic by conjunction, or by aspect, and joins another malefic in such a way: or if a malefic, or its rays, are in the sign that is before it, that is, in the second from it: and in the sign that is after it, that is, in the twelfth from it, there is another malefic, or its rays. Similarly, it is said of signs that they are besieged. But if a benefic or the Sun aspects the same besieged planet, or besieged sign, with a trine or sextile aspect: and there is between them a conjunction of less than seven degrees, that malice or obsession is resolved. And from this, that is, in misfortune, it is also that a planet is retrograde, or combust under the Sun's rays, or cadent from the ascendant. And from their debility it is that they are slow in course, or in the first station, that is, when they stand as if retrograde, or if they are in dark degrees: or if they are masculine in feminine signs: and in feminine degrees, in the day below the earth, and in the night above the earth, or if they are feminine in masculine signs: and in masculine degrees, in the night below the earth, and in the day above the earth.

[Planetary Strength and Weakness]
Planets that are combust, retrograde, slow, peregrine, afflicted by the malefic rays of malefics, and finally, cadent from the angles, are considered unfortunate and weak. If they have received equal votes from both sides, that is, if they are partly endowed with strength and certain prerogatives, but partly depressed by misfortunes, they are considered indifferent or mediocre. A similar brevity of this passage is found in Ptolemy, whose words in the beginning of Book 2 of the Apotelesmatics are these: "We consider whether they are ruling monarchically, both cosmically and natally, or the opposite. For they are most active when cosmically they are in their own or proper places, and again when they are oriental and in direct motion; but natally, when they precede the center in their return, and especially the first, I mean those in the ascensions and midheaven. But they are cosmically inactive when they are in alien places, or their own, and occidental, or in retrograde motion; and natally, when they decline from the centers." Therefore, the strength and weakness of planets according to Ptolemy are judged from four circumstances. First, in comparison to the world, when they are in their own, proper, alien, or non-domestic places. Second, in comparison to the Sun. For oriental planets are strong, occidental ones weak. Third, in comparison to their orbits. For swift planets in direct motion gain strength. Conversely, slow, retrograde planets lose it. Fourth, in comparison to the genesis or figure of the heavens. For in the centers, and especially in the ascendant and Midheaven, and also in succedent houses, they are strong, but in cadent houses, they are weak. I also thought it necessary to add here that Jupiter and Venus, when powerful and fortunate, have the greatest efficacy; but when weak and unfortunate, they are equated with mediocre planets and achieve little or nothing. Again, even fortunate planets placed in their splendors do less harm than when they are outside their prerogatives, unfortunate and weak. But mediocre and strong and fortunate planets are equated with benefics, while unfortunate and weak ones portend no less detriment than the malefics themselves. This opinion is confirmed by the torment of the 7th and 8th houses, he says: "When the lord of the ascendant is conjoined with the body in Leo, unless Mars himself has some dignity in the ascendant, or not even in the eighth house, the native will be burned." From this it is clear that planets do not only harm in places where they rule. We note the same in evil princes, who do not treat their own subjects as badly as they do strangers. Finally, the twelve cusps, planets, and lots are made fortunate by the presence and all good aspects of benefics, and by the good aspects of mediocre planets. But they are condemned and made unfortunate by the conjunctions and bad aspects of malefics, and of mediocre planets, and this if the aspects occur within the limits, or as most have taught, from the midpoint, and within the midpoint of their orbs. For example, if the cusp of the seventh house is at 12 degrees of Aries, and Jupiter is at 19 degrees of Aries. Here Jupiter, by its presence from the midpoint of its orb, which is 7 degrees, makes the cusp of the seventh house fortunate. The same is achieved by intermediate degrees. Nor is there any other judgment concerning aspects, as if Jupiter projects a ray to 21 degrees of Aries, there from the midpoint of its orb it would make the same cusp of the seventh house fortunate. Concerning the fortune and misfortune of the Sun and Moon, it has been said above. Concerning the friendship and hatred of the planets. And it is said among the planets that some of them love and hate each other: for concerning those who love, some of the ancients said that Jupiter loves all planets and is their friend, and they are his, except Mars. The friends of Venus are all planets, and all love her except Saturn. But the friends of Saturn are Jupiter, the Sun, and the Moon; his enemies are Mars and Venus, and she herself hates him more. And the friend of Mars is Venus, the other planets hate him, and Jupiter and the Sun more. But the friends of the Sun are Jupiter and Venus, and his enemies are Mars, Mercury, and the Moon. The friends of Mercury are only Jupiter, Venus, and Saturn, and his enemies are the Sun, Moon, and Mars. But the friends of the Moon are Jupiter, Venus, and Saturn, and her enemies are Mars and Mercury. The friends of the Head of the Dragon are Jupiter and Venus, and its enemies are Saturn and Mars. But the friends of the Tail are Saturn and Mars, and its enemies are the Sun, Moon, Jupiter, and Venus. And there are other kinds of enmity: when two planets have their houses opposite, like Mars and Venus: when they are conjoined, it is called the opening of the gates. A third kind of enmity is when the exaltations of two planets are opposite. But the stronger friendship of planets is when a planet agrees with a planet in nature, quality, substance, and power, just as Mars agrees with the Sun, because both agree in heat, dryness, sharpness, and swiftness, and Mars is the lord of its exaltation, in which its strength appears: and just as the Moon and Venus agree in coldness and humidity, and Venus is the lady of its exaltation. But when two planets agree in nature and substance, like Jupiter and Venus, they are friends. Jupiter's friends are all except Mars. Venus's friends are all except Saturn. Saturn's friends are Jupiter, Sun, Moon; enemies are Mars and Venus, and Venus hates him more. Mars's friend is Venus; enemies are all others, especially Jupiter and Sun. Sun's friends are Jupiter and Venus; enemies are Mars, Mercury, Moon. Mercury's friends are Jupiter, Venus, Saturn; enemies are Sun, Moon, Mars. Moon's friends are Jupiter, Venus, Saturn; enemies are Mars, Mercury. Head of the Dragon's friends are Jupiter and Venus; enemies are Saturn, Mars. Tail of the Dragon's friends are Saturn, Mars; enemies are Sun, Moon, Jupiter, Venus. Enemies by reason of opposite houses. Enemies by reason of opposite exaltations. Friends by reason of agreement in nature, quality, substance, and power. End of the third difference.

[Eclipse Effects and Planetary Stations]
DIFFERENCE & places of effects collected from the defects of the luminaries of the Sun. However, in another circumstance, that is, in the judgment of places, besides the Eclipse, it seems to take the stations and heliacal risings of the three superior planets, and indeed at least those which fall into the time of the Eclipse, and of those planets which preside over the defect. This seems to be able to be elicited from his own words: “The first chapter of this prediction contains the foreknowledge of places and cities, to which, besides or particularly, both the defects of the luminaries and the concurrent monthly constitutions of the wandering stars, such as exist in stationary Saturn, Jupiter, and Mars, bring significations of events.” From this it seems to follow that places subject to future events are to be found from the dodecatemoria containing the defects, and also from the stations of the three superior planets when they preside over the defective places, that is, when they are lords of the place of defect or placed in the place of the Eclipse, or aspect it, or finally ascend the horizon at the hour of the Eclipse. But neither is this a sufficiently firm consequence deduced from Ptolemy's words: For the causes of great events are to be taken both from defective places and from the stations and risings of the wandering stars, it follows that both of these indicate to us places subject to such effects. And what is more, Ptolemy, in the consideration of the regions to which the effects pertain, expressly makes no mention of the wandering stars, and accommodates the significations of defects at least to those places which are coupled with the defective dodecatemorion. Hence it is clear that, when we think that places, which by sympathy or affinity of the dodecatemoria containing the stars dominating the defect, are in some part subject to the significations of the Eclipse, he either completely disregarded them, or at least insinuated them to us in passing. As for the words, “τὰς ἔμμένους στάσεις” we believe to mean the same as the Latin “menstruas constitutiones”. There are those who read “ἐπιμένεις στάσεις”. Both readings proceed well. For monthly stations are long-lasting, and familiar to the three superior planets, and alien to the inferior ones, who, since they scarcely consist due to the swiftness of their motion, are very far from menstruation. However, that the times of great events are not to be calculated from the conjunctions of Saturn and Jupiter, but rather from the defects, can be established from this, that the effects of the former, extended to very long times, must be changed and varied almost infinitely by the transits of so many of them and other stars through different regions of the sky. But the decrees of these, since they can never exceed two and a half years, remain simpler, and usually expire before the heavier planets significantly change their places in the sky. Moreover, Ptolemy in this matter considers not the inherent power of the three superior planets, but that which is derived from the defects and brought to effect. For he says that the “κατὰ καιρὸν ἐμμήνους στάσεις” of the wandering stars, that is, the concurrent monthly constitutions, are to be observed. And if you wish to pursue the conjunctions of the heavier planets in the same way that Ptolemy treats the defects of the luminaries, the very difficulty of the matter, since the human intellect cannot comprehend the exact time of the conjunction of the superior planets, will easily repel you from your purpose. For even if they move with the swiftest motion they can have, it has already been shown by experts that Jupiter departing from Saturn never exceeds a difference of six first scruples in diurnal motion. Since this distance is smaller than can be detected by calculation or any instrument, such a conjunction will be so far from a certain hour and figure of the sky that not even its day can be considered certain. When the doctors of great conjunctions noticed this, they were therefore forced to devise another remedy for this ailment, which the author mentions in this place. For when the ascendants of great conjunctions are named, they mean those signs which the Sun, having reached the vernal intersection, or as they say, entering the beginning of Aries, and that in that year in which that conjunction is to occur, is observed ascending. For example: In the year of Christ our Lord 1563, a great conjunction of Jupiter and Saturn will be celebrated, and according to the calculation of the Alphonsine tables, on the 26th day of September, around 6 hours after noon, in the 4th part, 8th scruple, 1st degree of Leo; but according to the Prutenic calculation, on the 25th day of August, around the tenth hour after noon, in the 29th part of Cancer. Furthermore, the horoscoping part in the annual revolution, or as they call it, the part at the moment of time, when the Sun reaches the first point of Aries in that year, the ascendant, will be, according to their calculation, the 16th part of Sagittarius. For this reason, Sagittarius will be called the ascendant of that great conjunction by them, although there is a similar uncertainty about the ascending sign of the annual revolution, and no less disagreement in calculations. But it is also established by many arguments that this has no semblance of truth and is completely empty and without reason. For the beginning of the natural year, which is certainly accommodated to the Sun's entry into the beginning of Aries, is simply uncertain. For although it is consistent with truth that the Sun's return to the cardinal points of the zodiac constitutes the beginning of the natural year, nevertheless it brings with it no small hesitation as to which of these should be used as the primary principle, especially since each of these parts has something excellent, why it seems to claim the beginning of the year or the new moon by right for itself. Hence it happened that of those who commented on these matters, one established one beginning of the year, another another, as each was impelled by reasons or his own nature to establish this or that. Ptolemy also, since he could not resolve this hesitation, therefore transferred the discussion of the causes making the variety of individual years, not to one, but to all four, so that the vernal equinox varies spring, the summer solstice summer, the autumnal equinox autumn, the winter solstice winter. Therefore, since all doubt about the beginning of the natural year, how can we be certain about the sign ascending at its beginning? Finally, even if the Sun's return to the vernal intersection made the new moon of that year, nevertheless the signs ascending at the Sun's return to that beginning would still escape us, because that time can hardly be calculated so exactly as to serve the perfection or structure of the celestial theme without gross error. And this is the reason why Ptolemy prescribes to consider for the change of the four seasons of the year, not the Sun's return to the cardinal points, but rather the conjunctions or oppositions of the luminaries immediately preceding the Sun's entry into the cardinal points: whose times, since they can be calculated most exquisitely, provide us with the most perfect fabric of the celestial theme.

[The Vanity of Vulgar Astrologers]
Since these things are so, the vanity of those men of our time who compile vulgar calendars is manifest. Neglecting true doctrine, they babble publicly about floods in regions, destruction of cities, wars, famine, earthquakes, sterility, and such public calamities, solely from the Sun's passage to the vernal intersection. They fill the world with their lies and mere trifles, and render this most excellent profession infamous, bringing great opprobrium even upon those who care for truth. But they proceed to even greater folly or impudence when they attempt to strengthen their prophecies, as if they had not woven a fabulous history of the future without cause, by means of the annual progressions of great conjunctions, described a little below even by Alcabitius, or by the change of some great orb, no more certain than Plato's great year existing in the true nature of things. Therefore, either no, or a more learned, conjecture about the causes producing these greater effects in the world could not undeservedly be desired in republics. This is that which penetrates through all media from the first and most powerful natural causes to the most proper or particular. It is not, as we said a little earlier concerning Ptolemy's opinion, a prior or more potent cause of such events than that which is derived from the defective conjunctions of the luminaries. This is followed by the method by which the general constitution of each quadrant of the year is derived through the syzygies of the luminaries preceding the Sun's passage into the cardinal points. From this, the annual ephemeris, that is, the monthly method, by which the future constitutions of individual months of the year are produced, must be considered. Then follows the consideration of certain more subtle significations pertaining to the quadrants of each month. Inferior to these is the method by which we are taught to gather the quality of the air on any given day from the rising and setting of illustrious stars. The last place is held by the method which, through each hour of the day, hunts for the quality of the air's constitutions from the passage of the luminaries through the cardines. This is Ptolemy's method of conjecturing about the constitutions of the air, translated from the greatest to the least, which requires both more labor and far greater skill and erudition in things than is contained in all our calendar makers combined into one mass.

[Ascendant Correction from Adverse Events]
Next, calculate the years since birth during which adverse bodily events occurred. Having found these in the Ephemeris, if any misfortunes, such as bodily ailments, occurred on a certain day when the native's body or a malefic ray struck the ascendant degree itself or near it, these will prove the true ascendant of the nativity. Observe diligently that Saturn, passing through the ascendant with its body or malefic ray, causes melancholic infirmities of both mind and body, while Mars causes anger of the soul, wounds, burns, and similar Martial infirmities. Similarly, the transits of the lord of the ascendant and the lord of the geniture through the bodies and noxious rays of malefics must be attended to. Since such accidents occur not only through transits over the ascendant but also through other progressions of the Apheta to anaretic parts, for this reason, in the second place, it teaches how to find the true ascendant from such effects by means of profections, in this manner: See whether the annual profection of the ascendant, about which more below, can reach the bodies or noxious rays of malefics. If so, calculate the mean days between the day of birth and the day of the accident, and multiply them by six, so that the sixths of the days emerge, which, divided by 73 sixths of days (for 73 sixths of days make one degree in annual profections, when they progress by 30 degrees per 365 days), will yield the remaining degrees and minutes. If you subtract these from the place of the promissor of the accident, you will obtain the true degree and minute of the ascendant. For greater clarity, let's take an example. Schonerus was born in the year 1477, on January 16th, at 11 hours after noon, in latitude 50 degrees. In his 58th year, on the first day of January, he writes that he fell from a height, the cause of which was retrograde Saturn in 20 degrees 32 minutes of Scorpio in the tenth house. Through this significant fall, he thus examined the ascendant degree in his geniture. He first noted that in that year, the annual profection of the ascendant had reached 20 degrees 32 minutes of Scorpio, and before the completion of the year, it had reached 21 degrees 42 minutes of Scorpio, where Saturn was in the radix. He counted 349 days from the nativity to the time of the accident, that is, the first of January, which, multiplied by 6, yielded 2094 days. These, divided by 73 days, yielded 28 degrees 40 minutes, which he then subtracted from the place of the promissor, that is, Saturn, which was 20 degrees 32 minutes of Scorpio, and there remained 21 degrees 42 minutes of Scorpio, which are almost the degrees of the true ascendant, namely Libra, which ascends in Schonerus's geniture, as follows: 20 degrees 32 minutes of Scorpio. 1477. January 16th: 11 hours. Plus 30 degrees. 20 degrees 32 minutes of Scorpio. Finally, by directions of the ascendant to unfortunate parts, the ascendant degree is to be proceeded with thus: From the oblique ascension of the promissor of the accident for the given latitude of the region, subtract the degrees and minutes due to the completed years and days from birth up to the year and day of the accident (for each year makes one degree, and six days make one minute in primary directions, as will be explained below). What remains will be the oblique ascension of the ascendant of the same region, which, if you put it into the table of oblique ascensions, you will obtain the true horoscoping degree. The same Schonerus narrates that when he was 40 years old, with 192 days completed, he was afflicted with scabies, ulcers, and various melancholic disturbances, which are manifest effects of Saturn. Therefore, by diligent calculation, it was found that around these days, the direction of the ascendant applied to the square of Saturn, falling in 20 degrees 32 minutes of Scorpio, and inflicted such evils on his body. Knowing these things beforehand, it is not difficult to obtain the true ascendant degree by backward calculation. For if from the oblique ascension of the said square aspect of Saturn, which is 250 degrees 46 minutes, you subtract 40 degrees 32 minutes, which correspond to 40 years and 192 days, there remains the oblique ascension of the true ascendant, namely 210 degrees 14 minutes, to which, in the table of ascensions constructed for latitude 50 degrees, corresponds approximately 21 degrees 40 minutes of Scorpio as the true ascendant of the nativity. Nor indeed are the other significators and promissors with their accidents to be treated differently, so that they may yield the truly horoscoping part. The third method of finding the true ascendant is Ptolemy's, which the author calls Annimodar, that is, which is taken from the dominion of the conjunction or opposition, which they call prevention, immediately preceding the nativity, and is explained here by the author. Here, however, before explaining the method of operation, he raises a very difficult question for us, namely, whether the number of degrees that the planet ruling in the place of the luminaries has made in the sign can truly show the ascendant degree. For if this method, by which the ascendant degree is said to be found by numbers, is universally true when it agrees with the ascendant degree taken by the Astrolabe, why is there a need for observation by the astrolabe or other examinations? But if these two do not generally agree, and yet both are to be trusted, it seems to follow that the birth of a human has two ascendants and twin decrees of the stars, differing in kind, quality, magnitude, and time, which cannot be. Before answering this hesitation, it must first be judged what the principle of human nature is. For man is not born in one moment, as many have thought, but first, turning in the womb, he breaks the cotyledons and the chorion, that is, the membrane in which the infant is enveloped, commonly called the secundine, and opens it. Then, if he is born naturally, he first comes out headfirst, then the middle, and finally the whole. Finally, the umbilical cord is cut or broken during birth, all of which actions from beginning to end never conclude in less than three hours; often, one and sometimes three days are involved. Now, therefore, will all these have force, or only one? If all, we will have nothing certain; if only one, it can rightly be doubted whence it should be taken. For when the head emerges, he is not yet born, and for the whole to emerge seems more voluntary than natural; indeed, it depends on the will of the midwife. Finally, the space from the emergence of the head to the emergence of the whole is too long to be considered the beginning. But here, according to Cardano's opinion, we must respond that the time in which an infant is produced is not a moment, but just as in the art of healing diseases and in all other human cases there is a certain known latitude, so also the nativity has some delay, distinguished by three principal moments, namely, access, consistency, and end, in which whole space the infant receives power from the stars, it must be believed. Access is the first principle, Consistency the second, which, since it is unequally distant from the extremes, and sometimes approaches very close to the end or the beginning, cannot properly be called the middle. The third moment is the end of the whole nativity. But these principles are to be taken from the infant's principal action, namely, respiration, so that the beginning of birth is established when the infant begins to breathe. The middle and principal beginning is when he has breathed so that he can live by himself. The end is when he has already drawn in so much air that he spontaneously ceases to breathe and the umbilical arteries move. Therefore, each principle acquires the powers of the whole nativity, but not equally, but so that each part has its own signification, and the beginning signifies about the beginning, the middle about the middle, and finally the end about the end. These things thus explained and understood, the answer to the question proposed at the beginning will also be clear, namely, that the time which must be taken by the astrolabe is that time which we have said to be principal and almost middle. But the time which the author and Ptolemy teach to find by numbers sometimes coincides with this principal time, from which the most true prognostics of astrologers are taken; sometimes, however, it coincides with the beginning by which wills and the beginnings of things are signified, which usually follows the effects; sometimes, finally, it falls into the end of the nativity, from which the quality of the end of the matter is gathered. And this is what the author says, that by Annimodar the true ascendant degree is not always found, but the most dignified of all degrees of the circle after the ascendant degree at the same hour according to the course of nature.

[Two Corollaries from Cardano]
It is better and safer to note the principal beginning by astrolabe, but since this is often not available, the natural beginning of the princes must be taken by Annimodar, which, although sometimes imperfect, yet, as has been shown, always exists as part of the whole nativity and can never be idle or useless, and is rightly assumed as the true beginning of the nativity. TWO COROLLARIES FROM THE SAME CARDANO. First, it follows from what has been said that those for whom the ascending number is similar to the number found by Ptolemy's method, or at least very close, as happened in the example we assumed, have very similar and conforming beginnings, middles, and ends: but for those where the true ascendant differs greatly from the ascendant sought by Annimodar, their beginnings, middles, and ends will be very dissimilar, especially if they differ greatly. For such people will sometimes suddenly fall from the summit to the lowest depths of the same thing, and conversely, sometimes, having been plunged into the depths and deprived of all hope, they will suddenly emerge as if aided by divine power, contrary to all expectation. Secondly, it is clear that, according to the opinion of Ptolemy and the author who described this passage from Ptolemy, both beginnings must be considered, the one found by the astrolabe and the natural one. For even if you cannot have the true beginning by astrolabe, you can nevertheless find it by the consensus of many progressions, which you will find true by experience, as we showed above. But now it is time to explain the ephod itself, which depends entirely on the syzygies of the luminaries immediately preceding the geniture. If the conjunction of the luminaries immediately preceded the geniture, no one fails to see by what degree one should operate. But if the opposition of the luminaries immediately preceded the nativity, there, because the luminaries hold opposite degrees, it can be doubted which degree, whether of the Sun or the Moon, is the place of prevention. To this hesitation, the author, according to Ptolemy's opinion, responds that the degree of that luminary which was above the earth at the time of the opposition has greater prerogative. But if one luminary touches the eastern horizon and the other the western cardinal point, then he says that the degree in the east is to be preferred to the western degree. Ptolemy's words on this matter are these: We must take the syzygy that immediately preceded the geniture, whether it was a conjunction or a full moon; and having accurately determined the degree, in the case of a conjunction, of both luminaries, but in the case of a full moon, of that one which was above the earth, we must consider the stars that exercise dominion over that part, which consists in these five: namely, the trigon, domicile, exaltation, term, and irradiation or configuration, that is, when one or more of these, or even all, are held by that place with respect to the ruling star. Therefore, if we find one star suitably disposed to all or most of these, then, having accurately noted the part which it occupies at the time of the geniture, in the sign in which it dwells, we shall judge a part similar in number to it to rise in that sign whose parts, by computation or by the calculation of ascensions, are found to be most similar to the part of the ruler. But if we find two or more aspiring to the principality of dominion, whose part at the time of the geniture was more similar, or rather closer in number, to the part ascending according to the rule of ascensions, we shall use the number of its parts. But if two or more agree in that proximity of number, then we shall follow the one who agrees more with both the angles and the condition of the time. But indeed, where the difference of the parts of this dominion, with respect to the horoscope placed at the estimated time, is greater than with respect to the culmination of the heavens, then, using that very same number of parts to constitute the culminating part, we shall constitute the remaining cardinal points according to its guidance. The meaning of this passage, therefore, is this: If, having erected the figure of the heavens for the estimated time (which is when they think the child was born, if it was not observed by very accurate instruments), you have already certainly known which sign (for unless the sign is certain, this rule could easily lead you into gross error) touched the eastern horizon at the hour of birth, and you have at least hesitated about the horoscoping degree, and wish to be more certain about it: Then, he says, if the conjunction immediately preceded the hour of birth, diligently see which among the planets in the place of conjunction had more and greater prerogatives, which, according to Ptolemy's opinion, as appeared, are contained in the trigon, domicile, exaltation, term, and irradiation or configuration, that is, irradiation or configuration, and most diligently seek out its true motion for the estimated time of birth: having done this, look at the theme of the heavens conformed to the estimated hour of birth, and consider whether the place of the ruler of the interlunium is more similar or closer in number, that is, according to the quantity of the disjunction or arithmetic, to the horoscoping degree or to the midheaven. For if it is closer to the eastern cardinal point, that part of the rising sign is to be advanced into the horoscope which agrees with the degrees and parts measured by the ruling planet of the interlunium in its sign. But if the same ruling planet is closer to the midheaven, so many parts of the sign dwelling there are to be placed in the culmination of the heavens, and then the remaining places are to be conformed to the true position of either the horoscope or the midheaven, in the manner which I will explain a little below. Nor indeed is the horoscope of the preventional genitures, as they call them, examined otherwise, by the degree of the luminary dwelling in the upper hemisphere: therefore, we must now make the matter easier to understand by the calculation of examples. And so, to speak first of the geniture described by us as an example throughout this work, it is manifest that it followed the opposition of the luminaries, and indeed such an opposition in which the Moon was found above the earth, having recently entered the fourth part of Sagittarius, where Jupiter has the carpentum by reason of domicile, trigon, and terms. And since Jupiter at the time of the geniture was found in the 27th part of Gemini, which number, departing farthest from the culminating part, approaches closest to the number of the horoscoping parts, and on account of this indicates that the 27th part of Libra is to be advanced into the eastern horizon according to our rule, which plainly agrees with the ascendant of the time, whether it was observed by astrolabe or corrected in some other way. It is also consistent that the two more powerful rulers of the preceding conjunction of the luminaries, if they agree with one of the principal cusps, more effectively fulfill the intention of the rule. Here, however, as everywhere else, the more powerful one takes precedence, and especially if it also follows the condition of the time, as happens here, and the right of the trigon, which it exercises in the place of the conjunction of the luminaries, was heretical. The same effect is produced if two more powerful ones

[Determining the Ascendant and Midheaven]
The two rulers of the house, one of the cusp of the tenth house, the other of the first, agree. The evidence for this is clear in our example, if in the prevention you prefer the place of the Moon to the place of the Sun, or at least compare it simultaneously with the beginning of Gemini, which Saturn and Mercury, ruling with great power, seem to obstruct, so that the number of parts of the former agrees with the cusp of the first house, and that of the latter with the second. Another example concerns two aspirants to leadership: At some estimated time, let the 18th degree of Cancer culminate, and the 14th degree of Libra ascend, and let the conjunction of the luminaries immediately precede this nativity in the 10th degree of Libra, where Saturn, residing in the 15th degree of Capricorn, has the first prerogative. Venus, traversing the eleventh degree of Scorpio, has the second prerogative. For each of these two planets has a chariot in the said place of the preceding conjunction, Saturn indeed because of exaltation and trine, and because he illuminates the same place from the 15th degree of Capricorn with a square ray: Venus indeed because of house and bounds. But because she is very remote from the similarity of the number of angles, therefore Saturn is to be preferred to her, who is also more powerful than Venus, and has made the number of parts closer to the parts of the ascendant in his sign. Let the Midheaven again be in the 15th degree of Aquarius in the nativity, and the ascendant in the 7th degree of Gemini, and the place of the conjunction immediately preceding the nativity in the 22nd degree of Libra, in which, since the oriental Saturn, traversing the 13th degree of Virgo, has the first prerogative, and Mercury, traversing the 5th degree of Scorpio, has the second, it is clear that the number of Mercury approaches the number of the ascendant, and the number of Saturn approaches the number of the Midheaven equally: however, since Saturn is more powerful, both because of the prerogatives in the place of the immediately preceding conjunction, and because of the condition of the day, therefore the 15th degree of Aquarius should be promoted to the Midheaven rather than the 5th degree to the ascendant. The author seems to think that the proximity of the ruler to the angles should be taken according to continuous quantity. As if the ascendant of the estimated nativity is the 5th degree of Taurus, and the Midheaven the 4th degree of Capricorn, and the ruling planet is the Sun, passing through the 14th degree of Aries, then according to continuous quantity the Sun is closer to the Midheaven than to the ascendant, for the distance from Aries to Capricorn is greater than to Taurus, and therefore the 14th degree of Taurus should be promoted to the cusp of the Midheaven, and the other places conformed to the position of the same Midheaven. But discrete quantity pushes the 14th degree of Capricorn to the ascendant, because the number 14 is closer to 5 than to 4. But indeed, that this matter is to be understood of discrete and not continuous quantity, the very words of Ptolemy, recited by us a little earlier, speak openly. Another fourth method is commonly handed down for finding the ascendant degree by the duration of the native in the mother's womb, which they call the balance of Hermes. For Hermes, the most ancient writer on celestial matters, corrects the estimated times of nativities from the moment of the sperm's fall into the womb, and seeks the true rising degree, because that place of the sky, in which it is at the time of conception, falls into the rising angle at the time of birth. For he wanted the Moon to have such a conformity to the birth of man, that the place of the Moon at the fall of the sperm ascends at the time of birth, and vice versa, the place of the Moon at the time of birth, if it is the ascendant of the fall of the sperm into the womb: so if it was in the 3rd degree of Taurus when the woman conceived the fetus, he supposes the same degree to rise when she gives birth. Again, if at the time of conception the first degree of Scorpio emerges, the child will not be born unless the Moon comes to the first part of the same. Sometimes, however, he stated that not the same sign, but the opposite, ascends, which they want to be true when at the time of coitus it is found in the western angle. His words are these: “In what sign is the nativity at the time of conception, make that the ascendant; and in what sign it was found at conception, make that or its opposite the ascendant at birth.” This aphorism being posited and conceded, by the altitude which the Moon has at the time of birth in our hemisphere, they find the rising degree at the time of someone's birth in this way: First, construct the celestial chart, and find the true place of the Moon for the time when they estimate the infant to have been born. Second, find the distance of the Moon from the ascendant or the western angle: from the ascendant if it is below the earth, but from the seventh if it is above the earth. You will calculate such a distance in this way: Subtract the signs and parts of the angle from the signs and parts of the Moon, adjusted, as astronomical calculation requires, by a full revolution, as often as the lower cannot be subtracted from the higher: this done, the removal of the Moon from the angle will remain. Third, seek the found removal of the Moon from the angle in the table of the duration of the fetus in the mother's womb, and you will find the days of duration in the first series of numbers, if the Moon was below the earth at the time of birth; but in the latter, if it was in the upper hemisphere. Fourth, to have the found time of conception, count back the found duration from the time of birth, or, which is the same, subtract it from the time of birth, which is intercepted between the beginning of the Roman year and the point of birth, adjusted by a full revolution of the preceding year, if subtraction cannot be done, diligently observing the bissextile year in both cases, and the number of days of conception of the same or the preceding year will remain, when namely you have adjusted one period of the preceding year, whether common or bissextile. As in our example, the Moon's motion is 10 signs, 27 degrees, 52 minutes, 11 seconds, and the ascendant is 6 signs, 26 degrees, 44 minutes, 1 second. Subtracting these from the Moon's motion, the Moon's distance from the ascendant remains, namely 4 signs, 1 degree, 8 minutes, 1 second, to which in the first series of the table correspond 283 days, during which the native was detained in the mother's womb. However, since from the beginning of the year 1127, at least 1417 days have elapsed until the time of birth, which are fewer than to allow subtraction of the days of duration in the womb, therefore I will add to them a full revolution of the preceding and common year, that is, 365 days, and I will produce a sum of 5064 days, from which, subtracting the 283 days of duration in the womb, 223 days remain. With these, to recognize the day of the month of the preceding year, enter the table of months of the common year, in which opposite July, 212 days, the next smaller number, will present themselves. Subtract these from 223 days, and 11 days will remain. Therefore, the estimated day of conception according to this method is found to be the twelfth of August, at the end of which the Moon traversed the 20th degree, 16 minutes, 1 second of Libra, as the Ephemerides tables show you.
