Concerning the Duodenaries of Planets and Houses. 1. And from this the duodenaries of planets or houses: that is, that you look at how much the planet has traveled in its sign, in whatever it may be, or the degree of the house you wish, in degrees and minutes: and you will multiply this by 12. After this, you will add to it those degrees and minutes which you multiplied by 12. And what is collected, from it you will cast off from the beginning of the degrees of the same sign: giving to each sign 30 degrees: and where the number is finished, there will be the duodenary of the planets and houses. Concerning their Novenaries. 2. And from this Anaubarach, which are the novenaries. The knowledge of which is, that you know how much the planet has traveled in its sign in degrees and minutes, or the degree of the house whose novenary you wish to know. After this, divide the sign into nine divisions, and each division is of three degrees and a third part of one degree. After this, you will look in which novenary out of the nine novenaries the degree of the planet or house falls. After this, you will give the first novenary from the signs to the lord of the movable sign of the same triplicity, and the second novenary to the lord of the sign whose sign it is that succeeds: until you come to some novenary, from which the degree is, and the planet will be the lord of its novenary. For example: if the sign from which that degree was, was from the triplicity of Aries: then the first novenary of the same sign will be Mars, lord of Aries, and the second novenary will be Venus, lady of Taurus, and the third novenary will be Mercury, lord of Gemini. Similarly up to the ninth novenary. And if the sign is from the triplicity of Cancer, its first novenary will be the Moon, the second novenary will be the Sun, lord of Leo: and the third will be Mercury, lord of Virgo. And if the sign is from the triplicity of Libra, its first novenary will be Venus, lady of Libra, and the second will be Mars, lord of Scorpio, and the third will be Jupiter, lord of Sagittarius. Similarly in the triplicity of Capricorn, its first novenary is Saturn, lord of Capricorn: and the second similarly Saturn, lord of Aquarius, and the third Jupiter, lord of Pisces, then what follows according to the order of succession of the signs. For example: a planet or some house out of the twelve houses was in the 19th degree of Aquarius, if the sign is divided into nine divisions, the 19th degree will be in the sixth division, and because the sign of Aquarius is in the triplicity of Libra: its first novenary is placed as Venus, lady of Libra: the second Mars, lord of Scorpio, and the third Jupiter, lord of Sagittarius. The fourth Saturn, lord of Capricorn, and the fifth also Saturn, lord of Aquarius, and the sixth Jupiter, lord of Pisces, and Naubahar, that is, the novenary of the 19th degree of Aquarius, will be Jupiter. Method of finding the lord of the decan. 3. And from this Adorogen, that is, that you divide the ascendant into three parts, and each division is of 10 degrees: and you will give the first division to the lord of the ascendant, and the second to the lord of the fifth sign from it: and the third to the lord of the ninth: for the ascendant, 5th, and 9th are always one triplicity. For example: from the beginning of Aries up to the tenth degree of it, is Adorogen, that is, the decanate of Mars. And if it is from the 10th degree up to the 20th degree, its Adorogen will be the Sun, lord of Leo. And if it is from the 20th up to its end, its Adorogen will be Jupiter, lord of Sagittarius. 4. And from this the lord of the orb in nativities, that is, that you look at the lord of the hour in which the native is born, and you will give the ascendant and the lord of the hour in the first year of his nativity, and of the sign.

[Planetary Rulerships and Dignities]
Thus, these rulerships are exchanged, so that it is very easy to describe all these in a table. For the dodecatemoria of the planets, the decan lords, and the 84 salia are much more easily drawn from tables, which, as to how they should be composed by anyone, since we are sufficiently clearly admonished here, their description seemed superfluous to me, especially since the tradition of such things, as we have forewarned, differs greatly from the precepts of true art. This last chapter also appears to be such, containing nothing else but a very easy precept for constructing the tables of Ferdaria, that is, the years or times by which, according to the doctrine of the Arabs, the planets generally govern and rule the life of the native in order. The full years of Ferdaria for each planet are these: 111, 412, 07, 010, 8, 513, 9, 003, 2. The beginning of the rulership, however, is from the luminaries. For in a diurnal nativity, the Sun will principally govern for 10 years, to whom the next planet in order succeeds in rulership, that is, Venus, and she presides from the tenth to the eighteenth year of the native: after which Venus succeeds in rulership for 13 years. Then Mercury for 9 years. Then Saturn for 11 years. Then Jupiter for 12 years. Then Mars for 7 years. Then the Moon for 3 years. Finally, the Ascendant for 2 years. This is the complete circuit of planetary power in a diurnal nativity, containing 75 years, after which the rulership will return to the beginning and dominion of the Sun. Furthermore, in nocturnal nativities, the nocturnal luminary will assume rulership for the first 9 years, to whom the other succeeding planets will follow in series and order, as we have said. But truly, both in diurnal and nocturnal nativities, the rulership is distributed among these planets in such a way that each one governs alone for the seventh part of its years, but in the remaining seven parts of the rulership, it admits the six immediately succeeding planets as colleagues. For example, in a diurnal nativity, the first ten years are ascribed to the rulership of the Sun, but the Sun itself will govern the native for the seventh part of these years, that is, one solar year and approximately 156 days. In the second seventh part of its years, that is, from the 116th day of the native's first year to the completion of two years and 313 days, it will allow Venus as a partner in rulership: and in the third seventh part, the Sun, having dismissed Venus, will take Mercury as a colleague, and so on for the others that follow, as can be seen in tables, both in Ephemerides and widely described in Arabic books. Furthermore, this distinction of the Arabs seems to have been established in imitation of the primary Ptolemaic distinction of times, of which four exist concerning the four-part construction of the year. And from this Almanar, that is, pre-eminence, by which it is said that a planet goes above another planet: that is, so that you may observe the mean course of the planet and its equated place: if its equated place is less than its mean course, it will be ascending from the middle of its circle to the summit of the circle: and if its equated place is more than its mean course, it will be descending from the middle of its circle to its lowest point. And if its equated place is equal to its mean course, it will be in the middle of its circle. Then you will subtract the planet's place from its mean course. After you have most certainly known this, that is, the equated place: or conversely, subtracting the smaller from the larger of them: and you will multiply what remains by seven, you will divide by XXII, and what comes out of the division will be for every planet, according to the quantity of its ascent or descent. For Venus and Mercury, we take what is between both places, that is, what is between the place of the Sun and Venus and Mercury, and we do as above: because by this we know which of them is stronger in the circle of the apogee: but Venus and Mercury, when one of them is oriental, and its equated place is less than the Sun's place: you will take the remainder, which is between the Sun's place and its place: you will do with it as you have done, as was said for the higher planets by multiplication and division. And the signification of the planets will be more proper and stronger when they go above each other: that is, when one goes above the other in conjunction: but in opposition and in the fourth aspect, their signification will be less apparent and weaker. And yet when one of them is ascending and the other descending: then the ascending one goes above the descending one. But when both are descending: the one with the lesser descent goes above the one with the greater or more descent. But if both are ascending: the one with the greater ascent goes above the one with the lesser ascent. It is also said in another way, that is, so that you may know the latitude of the planets: because the northern one goes above the southern one. And if both are northern: the one with the greater latitude goes above the one with the lesser latitude. But if both are southern: the one with the lesser latitude goes above the one with the greater latitude, and therefore it is said that the Moon goes above Jupiter in these two operations. Similarly, if one of the two planets is in the middle of the circle, having no latitude: then the ascending northern one goes above the one that lacks latitude: the one that is without latitude goes above the descending southern one. And from this, the opening of the gates is said, when an inferior planet joins a superior planet, and their houses are opposite. And from this, concerning the eminence, superiority, or sublimity of the planets, which is universally to be understood with respect. For a planet is eminent when it is carried at the highest point of the shorter circle, which is called an Epicycle by the Greek name, while the other planet holds lower parts. For when a planet is held at the culmination of its epicycle, it seems to reign there in a certain way, sitting on a throne and dispensing justice: but the other, which is further from the apogee of the epicycle, and declines more from the summit of the circle, seems to sit at its feet and venerate the superior one. The Greeks call this power καθυπέρ-τέρησιμ, but more recent authors call it elevation, but quite ineptly: for the word 'elevare' in Latin means to depress or cast down, and the opposite word is 'extollere': to 'elevate' the affairs of an adversary is to depress them by diminishing them with words, and by depressing them, to render them more contemptible. But καθυπόρῶμ in Greek is the same as 'superare', 'to be superior', and 'to be extolled' in Latin. First, however, we are taught here by what reason we recognize one planet to be superior and more eminent than another, and that from the tables of motions, but somewhat confusedly as it appears: if indeed it calls the mean course of the planet the true motion of the center of the epicycle, which today in planetary theories are distinguished as follows. The mean motion of a planet or epicycle is the arc of the zodiac from the beginning according to succession, up to the line of the mean motion of the planet, which, drawn from the center of the world to the zodiac, is equally distant from the line extending from the center of the apogee to the center of the epicycle. The true motion of the epicycle is the arc of the zodiac calculated from the beginning up to the line of the true motion of the epicycle, which the author here calls the mean course of the planet. Finally, the true motion of the planet is the arc of the zodiac, which, beginning from the beginning, extends up to the line of the true motion of the planet, which goes from the center of the world through the body of the planet to the zodiac. They call the mean anomaly the arc of the epicycle calculated from its mean apogee to the body of the planet. But its true and equated anomaly is the arc of the epicycle, which extends from the true apogee of the same epicycle to the body of the planet, to which the corresponding arc in the zodiac is called προσθαφαίρεσις or the equation of the anomaly, to be added to the true motion of the epicycle, if the line of the true motion of the planet precedes the line of the true motion of the epicycle: or to be subtracted, if it follows the same, so that finally the true motion of the planet is elicited. Furthermore, the entire equation of the anomaly and the line of the true motion of the planet precedes the line of the true motion of the epicycle, when the planet is carried from the highest point of the epicycle to the lowest, and follows the same when in the other half it strives from the lowest point of the epicycle to its summit. With these things known, as they can be most easily recognized by inspecting the figures of the Theories, the author's meaning is clear. If, he says, the equated place of the planet is less than its mean course, that is, if the arc of the true motion of the planet is smaller.

Calculation of the true motion of the five wandering stars, namely Mars, Jupiter, Saturn, Venus, and Mercury. Before all else, these four things should be at hand. 1st. The true precession of the equinoxes. 2nd. The simple equal motion: These two are assumed from the preceding. 3rd. The planet's 'allaxis', that is, its commutation, or as the Alphonsines call it, the mean argument of the planet, that is, that arc of the epicycle which extends from the mean apogee to the planet through consequent signs. 4th. The apogee of the planet in the second sense, as it is commonly spoken of, which is numbered from the first star of Aries. Therefore, if you were to add the precession of the vernal equinox to this, the apogee from the vernal equinox for the given time would become known. However, the apogee of Venus always occupies one and the same place under the fixed sphere, and is distant from the first star of Aries by 0 sexagesimal parts, 48 scruples, 21.1, according to Copernicus. These four things provide us with the full calculation of the five wandering stars. For since both the motion of the anomaly of commutation and of longitude agree with the motion of the Sun by a certain ratio and harmony, it is not necessary that, besides the simple motion of the Sun, both the motion of anomaly and of longitude of the planet be laboriously sought from tables, since one of these is sufficient. For the motion of anomaly subtracted from the mean motion of the Sun leaves the planet's equal motion in longitude, and this in the three superior planets. But the simple motions of Venus and Mercury perpetually agree with the simple motion of the Sun: for they are 'sun-runners' and 'equal-runners' and 'same-runners', as these things are copiously explained in the elements and hypotheses of motions.

[Planetary Motion and Epicycles]
If the true motion of the epicycle is such that the planet ascends from the middle, that is, from the lowest point of its small circle or epicycle to its highest point, such a position causes the argument's equation, which follows the line of the epicycle's true motion, to be subtracted from the epicycle's true motion, so that the planet's true motion remains. Therefore, it is then said to be diminished in number. But if the equated place is greater than the mean course, that is, if the planet's true motion is greater than the true motion of the epicycle's center, it will be descending from the middle, that is, from the apex or apogee of its small wheel down to its lowest point. Such a position of the planet in the descending half of the epicycle causes the argument's equation, which precedes the line of the epicycle's true motion, to be added to its true motion, so that the planet's true course emerges. Therefore, it is then said to be augmented in number, etc.

[Planetary Weakness and Elevation]
Planets are rendered weak and outside their dignities when they are placed in locations dissimilar to their nature. Hence Ptolemy, in the third book of his Apotelesmatics, discussing the future manners of nativities, says that planets are elevated above the lords of the soul's qualities primarily when those lords are weak or outside their proper and dissimilar places. Thirdly, when Saturn is in a square, or even a trine or sextile to the Sun, such that the Sun is on the right and Saturn on the left; otherwise, Saturn could not be more occidental than the Sun. For example, if the Sun is in Libra and Saturn in Capricorn. A similar effect occurs if, while the Sun travels through an obedient sign, Saturn is found in an imperious sign. For example, if the Sun is in 10 degrees of Aries and Saturn in 20 degrees of Capricorn. Fourthly, if Saturn ascends to the apogee of its eccentric while the Sun descends to its apogee. However, ascent or descent is most observed in epicycles, as we have said. Furthermore, a ponderous occidental planet is said to be elevated above the Sun or Moon, or another lighter planet, because the Sun or Moon in such a state moves towards that planet, and the superior planet never moves towards them. And just as a man who awaits others at home seems nobler than those who come to his house, so a more ponderous planet, when occidental, is said to be elevated above another, especially if one or more of the aforementioned principal conditions are present. However, do not neglect the reason for declinations, latitudes, and finally the motion of the entire world, by which stars are carried from the east to the most eminent meridian place by reason of the horizons. Always, northern declination and latitude are more efficacious and higher than southern ones. Given these conditions, Porphyry's tradition regarding the supereminence of stars, who stated that oriental stars are higher than occidental ones, can no longer hold. His words on supereminence are these: "Every star placed in the right trine or square, or in the left, and to which it approaches, overcomes. For example, a star placed in Capricorn is overcome by stars traversing Libra, Virgo, and Scorpio. Furthermore, they say that supereminences, which occur from trine or square, are more powerful than others. For a star thus elevated gains strength, whether it be benefic or malefic, and especially if it is found in an angle with this. And if it is benefic, it shows an illustrious nativity; if malefic, an obscure one. In general, every star placed to the right overcomes the left to which it is carried." This means: "Every star placed in the right trine or square, or in the left, and to which it approaches, overcomes. For example, a star placed in Capricorn is overcome by stars traversing Libra, Virgo, and Scorpio. Furthermore, they say that supereminences, which occur from trine or square, are more powerful than others. For a star thus elevated gains strength, whether it be benefic or malefic, and especially if it is found in an angle with this. And if it is benefic, it shows an illustrious nativity; if malefic, an obscure one. In general, every star placed to the right overcomes the left to which it is carried."

[Opening of the Gates]
The seventh topic is about the opening of the gates, which are so named from their great and evident effects in the surrounding heavens. There are two types of gate openings: great and small. A small opening of the gates occurs as described here, namely when an inferior planet conjoins a superior one, and their houses are opposite. Such conjunctions, if you disregard the Moon, are found to be three: The first is of Saturn and the Sun, which causes very turbulent and cold air. The second is of Jupiter and Mercury, which stirs up winds. The third is of Mars and Venus, which produces rain and lightning. Each of these three conjunctions produces three forms, namely conjunction, opposition, and square, of which opposition is the most powerful, then conjunction, which has more strength than the square. The great opening of the gates is defined thus: When the Moon, separating from an inferior planet, applies to a superior one by conjunction, opposition, or square, such that the planets to which the Moon conjoins have opposite houses, a great opening of the gate or valve occurs, in which there is a great and evident change in the air, which reflects the nature of the superior planet, and especially if the superior and inferior planets aspect each other. For example, if the Sun is in 29 degrees of Scorpio, Saturn in 29 degrees of Aquarius, and the Moon in 27 degrees of Leo. Here the Sun aspects Saturn by square and applies to it. The Moon, receding from the Sun and approaching the diameter of Saturn, opens the greatest gates from the Sun to Saturn, and makes the weather cloudy and cold, especially in those places whose cardines the Moon traverses. If such a configuration occupies watery signs, it will produce great rains, especially if Saturn dominates the cardines established at the moment of the square radiation. A nearly similar configuration occurs if the Moon, separating from Saturn, joins the Sun. For universally, the planet to which the Moon first applies is said to open the gate over it, to which the Moon, flowing away, secondly conjoins, provided they have opposite houses. However, the significance is greater if it occurs from an inferior to a superior planet, for that is truly the opening of the great valves. A smaller and shorter change in the air occurs, and the turbulence quickly changes to a tranquil state when the Moon, flowing from a ponderous planet, applies to a lighter one. The great opening of the valves occurs when the Moon separates from a planet and applies to another, or vice versa. In every lunar flow, Mercury must be particularly observed, for if it joins the planets, one of which opens the gates to the other, or if it aspects them, it will cause instability in the air and winds. And from this, Albuzic, and it is a thing that the Indians greatly observed. For they count 12 hours after the conjunction of the Sun and Moon, and apply them to the Sun, and divide the 12 hours into three parts, namely into the haim of the Sun, and they judge over all four hours according to the judgment of the lords of the triplicity of the Sun's hour of conjunction. Then they give to Venus after 12 hours, and divide them again into three, and judge over every three-part division according to the lords of the triplicity of Venus's hour of conjunction. After this, they do similarly with Mercury and other planets in succession, until the orb returns to the Sun after 84 hours, and they do not cease to do so frequently until the second conjunction arrives. And some say that Albuzic is this: that after the conjunction there are 12 unequal hours, which are called combust; and no one should begin any work in these. And after the 12 hours, there are 72 hours incombust; in which the beginning of works is useful. And after these 72 incombust hours, there are 12 combust hours similarly until the conjunction that follows. After this, they divide these 12 hours into three divisions, that is, into parts. They said that when someone began to walk or wage war in the first four hours, the loss of his soul would be feared; and in the second four hours, the loss of his body without the loss of his soul would be feared; and in the last four hours, the loss of his substance, his possessions, and everything he owns would be feared. Regarding fortunate and unfortunate hours according to the opinion of the Indian people. The Indians, he says, divide the periodic month into hours, and name the first twelve hours, which begin from the conjunction of the luminaries, the combust hours of the Sun, to which they assign the lords of the triplicity of the place of the luminaries' coitus. The first lord presides over the first third, the second over the second, and the third over the third. The following 12 hours they assign to Venus, who follows the Sun in order, and to these they assign the lords of the triplicity of the place that Venus held in the synod of the luminaries, in the same way as was said for the Sun. And so on, the following hours are distributed to the following planets, until after the lapse of 84 hours of the month, the Sun repeats its former circuit of dominion. The rest are easy. The combust hours are those that have the Sun as their lord. Likewise, they call the loss of the soul the loss of life in their manner, etc.

[Parts of the Houses and Other Parts]
The eighth house is the part of death, and it is taken day and night from the Moon to the degree and minute of the eighth house. It is added from above that Saturn has walked in its sign, projected from the beginning of Saturn's sign. The part of the planet that kills is taken by day from the lord of the ascendant degree to the degree of the Moon, and by night, conversely, etc. The part of the year in which death, poverty, impediment, and destruction are feared for the native is taken day and night from Saturn to the degree of the lord of the house of conjunction or prevention that occurred before the nativity, etc. The part of imprisonment and whether one will be freed from it or not is taken by day from Saturn to the Part of Fortune, and by night, conversely. The ninth house is the part of peregrination; it is taken day and night from the lord of the ninth house to the degree and minute of the ninth house, and it is projected from the ascendant. The part of a journey by water is taken by day from Saturn to the 15th degree of the sign of Cancer, and by night, conversely, etc. If Saturn is in the 15th degree of Cancer, the degree of the part will be the degree of the ascendant. The part of religion is taken by day from the Moon to Mercury, and by night, conversely, and it is projected from the ascendant. The tenth house is the part of region and kingdom, and what work the native does; it is taken day and night from Saturn to the Moon, and it is projected from the ascendant. The part of the king and kingdom is taken by day from Mars to the Moon; by night, conversely, and it is projected from the ascendant. And there is another part similar to the father of fathers. The part of work and kingdom according to Velius is taken day and night from the Sun to the degree of the Midheaven, and it is projected from the ascendant. The part signifying whether there is a cause for a kingdom or not is taken day and night from the Sun to the degree of the Midheaven, and it is projected from Jupiter. The part of Mars is taken by day from Venus to the Moon, and by night, conversely, and it is projected from the ascendant. The eleventh house is the part of friends, and it is taken day and night from the Moon to Mercury, and it is projected from the ascendant. And Alendez said that it is taken by night, conversely. The part signifying the effect of friends and friendship is taken by day from the Part of Fortune to the part of future things, and by night, conversely, etc. The twelfth house is the part of enemies according to Hermes, and it is taken day and night from the lord of the house of enemies, up to the degree and minute of the house of enemies, and it is projected from the ascendant. And according to some ancients, it is taken day and night from Saturn to Mars, etc. And these are the parts of the 12 houses, etc. These are other parts that the masters of astrological judgments use frequently. Among these is the part that signifies the falsehood of rumors, when it falls in a crooked or mutable sign, or if the lord of the house is retrograde or impeded, or if malefics aspect it or are conjoined with it. And it signifies the truth of rumors if it falls with benefics or in their houses or terms, or if they aspect it or are in a direct sign; and it is taken by day from Mercury to Venus, by night, conversely, and it is projected, etc. And according to Albumazar, by day from Mercury to the Moon, and by night, conversely, etc. The part of the reason for the profundity of sense is taken by day from Saturn to the Moon, and by night, conversely, etc. The part of wisdom and discipline is taken day and night from Saturn to Jupiter, projected, etc. The part of war and battles is taken day and night from Saturn to the Moon, and it is projected from the ascendant. The part of peace and concord of armies is taken day and night from the Moon to Mercury, and it is projected from the ascendant. The part of looking in the revolution of the year is taken by day from the Moon to Venus, and by night, conversely, and it is projected from the Sun. Concerning the parts of revolutions in matters of kingdoms. And because, with God's help, we have introduced these parts that are relevant in particular matters, let us proceed to the parts that are relevant in the revolution of the years of the world and the conjunctions signifying the causes of the kingdom, its stability or duration. Among these is the part called the part of the kingdom or empire, which is exercised in the revolution of the world; and it is taken from Mars to the Moon, and it is projected from the ascendant of the conjunction that signifies the change of the kingdom. It is also taken in another way, namely, from the degree of the ascendant of the conjunction to the degree of the conjunction, and it is projected from the degree of the ascendant of the revolution. Likewise, it is done in another way: it is taken from the degree of the Midheaven of the revolution up to the Sun, and it is projected from Jupiter, i.e., from the degree of Jupiter. The part of the king's time, that is, how long it should last, is taken at the hour of the king's election from the Sun to the 15th degree of the sign of Leo, and it is projected from the Moon; then it is taken from the Moon to the 15th degree of the sign of Cancer, projected from the Sun. Another part concerning the time of the king's election: it is taken at the hour of the king's election by day from Jupiter to Saturn, and by night, conversely; it is projected from the ascendant of the revolution of the year in which the king arose. If Jupiter is in a common sign, and the revolution is divine, and Jupiter is falling from the angles, then it is taken from Saturn to Jupiter, and 30 degrees are added from above, and it is projected from the ascendant. But if Saturn and Jupiter are opposite each other, and both are falling from the ascendant, what comes between them is mediated, i.e., half of the degrees between them are taken; it is projected from the ascendant. And if Jupiter is in its exaltation, and the revolution is at night, it is counted from it to Saturn, and it is projected from the ascendant. There are two main parts from which the time of the king's election and its duration will be extracted. The first of these is to look at the hour of the king's election, when the perfection of the year arrives from the conjunction of the triplicity that signified the sect, from the number by which 30 degrees are given for each year, and for every two and a half degrees, one month. And when you know in what degree or sign it is, keep it here, because this is the place from which you will equate the first part: when you want to adapt it, you will adapt the ascendant of the revolution of the year in which the elected one arose. After this, look from the oriental planet from the Sun, from Saturn and Jupiter in that year, up to the degree of the equation of the first part that you kept; project it from the ascendant of the revolution of the year; and where the number arrives, that is the place of the first part. The second part is found thus: take at the conjunction in which the king arose or began to reign, to what sign and degree the progression of the year arrives from the number by which 30 degrees are given for each year, and two and a half degrees for each month, up to the day on which the king arose; that will be the place of the equation of the second part, also keep it. After this, look from the occidental planet from the Sun, from Saturn and Jupiter, up to the place of the equation of the second part that you kept; project it from the ascendant of the revolution: where it arrives, that will be the place of the second part. These are the parts that signify the strength of the king and his duration. There are also other parts that are used in the revolutions of the years of the world: by these, it is known what is heavy in the market, what will be light in price, or what is precious or vile, much or little; that is, you look at where the part falls, that is, in whose planetary house, exaltation, term, or triplicity: if that planet is retrograde, or combust, or in a bad place, that thing will become vile and of little value. But if it is in a place of strength, or in an angle, and especially in the Midheaven, that thing will become heavy and of great value; if the lord of the house reaches the place of its fall, that thing will become vile. And look at the aspects of the benefics and malefics to these, and also to the Moon and its lord; and see how and who aspects that part: if benefics and the Moon aspect that part, that thing will multiply, and if malefics aspect it, it will suffer detriment. The part of the disposition of the year from the Moon to Mercury. The part of water from the Moon to Venus. The part of barley from the Moon to Jupiter. The part of chickpeas, which is a kind of legume, from Venus to the Sun. The part of lentils from Mars to Saturn. The part of beans from Saturn to Mars. The part of millet from Saturn to Mars. The part of onions from Saturn to Mars. The part of wheat from the Sun to Mars. The part of nuts from Mercury to Mars. The part of sugar from Mercury to Venus. The part of dates from the Sun to Venus.

[Calculating Planetary Positions]
FIRST. 153 are applied. These four being thus constituted, subtract the equal common motion of the planet from the equal simple motion of the Sun, so that the equal motion of the planet's longitude from the head of the starry Aries may emerge, and that only in the three superior planets. For Mars, Jupiter, and Saturn, as we just said, always obtain the same or common equal motion of longitude with the Sun. From this longitude motion, now ascertained, immediately subtract the Apogee of the planet in the second signification, and there will remain the Anomaly of the Eccentric, or as it is commonly called, the mean center. 1. The Prosthaphæresis or equation of the Eccentric, that is, the arc between the line of the mean and the true motion of the epicycle. This, indeed, is nothing at the apogee or perigee of the epicycle's center, but is greatest when passing through the mean longitudes. (2. Proportional minutes. If the equation of the Anomaly of the Eccentric is less than a semicircle, it subtracts from the mean motion of longitude; if greater, it adds to it, so that both may be equalized, as the titles themselves indicate. The opposite, however, must be done to obtain the true Anomaly of commutation. For they alternately exceed and are exceeded by each other, since the arcs of the equation of the Anomaly of the Eccentric and the commutation of the epicycle are proportional and similar to each other, and therefore, one being taken from the tables, the other is also obtained. Afterwards, by the commutation of the planet, 1. The Prosthaphæresis or equation of the orbit. 2. The subsequent excess, whose part corresponding to the proportional minutes must always be added to the equation of the orbit, so that the parallax or absolute equation of the orbit may emerge. And if the Anomaly of commutation is less than a semicircle, subtract the Prosthaphæresis of the orbit from the co-equated longitude, so that the true place of the star from the horn of Aries may emerge. If you add the true precession of the equinoxes to this, the true place of the planet from the apparent equinox will emerge. We could have omitted the co-equation of the anomaly of the eccentric when calculating the stars' motion in longitude. However, we have everywhere added this co-equation because, through the co-equated anomaly of the eccentric and the absolute anomaly of commutation, besides many other things to be explained elsewhere, the latitudes of the five wandering stars must also be calculated according to precepts 81 and 82 of Prutenic. Thus, in our example, the latitude is calculated in this way: Saturn's southern latitude, descending from the apogee of the eccentric, is approximately 2 parts, 4 minutes, 12,20,22 seconds. Jupiter's southern latitude, ascending, is 0 parts, 28 minutes, 12,272 seconds. Mars' southern latitude, descending, is 0 parts, 52 minutes, 12,17,22 seconds. Venus' northern latitude, ascending, is 0 parts, 39 minutes, 12,14,3 seconds. Mercury's northern latitude, descending, is 1 part, 46 minutes, 12 seconds. Finally, you will find the true places of the fixed stars, both in longitude and latitude, as follows: From their canonical description, the star's longitude from the head of the starry Aries must be extracted, and then the true precession of the vernal equinox must be added to this longitude, so that the star's longitude from the apparent equinox may emerge in parts. If we divide these parts by 30, we will easily discover in which dodecatemorion and in which part of it the star is located. For example, let us find the part of the sky in which Lampadias, called Palilicium by the Romans, is located at a given time of birth. Its longitude from the head of Aries in the constellation Taurus is noted as 36 parts. Its southern latitude is 5 parts, 10 minutes, 1 second, which never changes in fixed stars, and therefore needs no further correction. But the true precession of the equinox, found above, is 27 parts, 22 minutes, 12,21,22 seconds. Therefore, this, combined with the star's longitude, makes 63 parts, 22 minutes, 12,21,22 seconds, which, divided by 30, yields two signs with 3 parts, 22 minutes, 12,21,22 seconds. Therefore, Palilicium falls into the 3rd part, 22 minutes, 12,21,22 seconds of Gemini. Likewise, Basiliscos, that is, Regulus, has a longitude from the head of Aries of 115 parts, 50 minutes, 1 second, with a northern latitude of 0 parts, 10 minutes, 12 seconds. Adding the true precession of the equinox to the star's longitude, you will produce its distance from the apparent equinox of 143 parts, 12 minutes, 12,21,22 seconds, which, divided by 30, makes 4 dodecatemoria, with 23 parts, 12 minutes, 12,21,22 seconds remaining. Therefore, Regulus falls at the given time into the 23rd part, 12 minutes, 12,21,22 seconds of Leo. Similarly, the true longitudinal places of the other fixed stars can be calculated. Finally, when you insert the exactly calculated places of the stars into the celestial chart, consider which celestial stations complete the dodecatemoria in which the stars are located. For each star must be described in the houses whose cusps embrace the parts of the dodecatemoria in which it is located.

[Parts of Various Things]
The Part of Honey from the Moon to the Sun. The Part of Rice (which is a kind of grain) from Jupiter to Saturn. The Part of Olives from Mercury to the Moon. The Part of Grapes from Saturn to Venus. The Part of Cotton from Mercury to Venus. The Part of Alonica (a kind of white seed, similar to flaxseed, used by doctors to make a useful ointment) from Mercury to Jupiter, or from Saturn to Venus. The Part of Melons (which are large and yellow) from Mercury to Saturn. The Part of Sour Foods from Saturn to Mars. The Part of Sweet Foods from the Sun to Venus. The Part of Foods with the taste of celery or similar herbs from Mars to Saturn. The Part of Bitter Foods from Mercury to Saturn. The Part of Sharp Medicines from Saturn to Jupiter. The Part of Salty Medicines from Mars to the Moon. The Part of Poisonous Medicines from Genzahar (that is, from the Head of the Dragon) to Saturn. And all these are to be cast from the Ascendant of the year's revolution. These are all the Parts that have come down to us. We have also introduced these very last Parts, even if their narration is weak, so that we might not omit anything that could be introductory to the mastery of astrological judgments. Friends: Table of friendship and enmity of the signs. 447 2 x II Enemies. Digitized by Google. FIFTH. 149. ON THE FIFTH DIFFERENCE. Ptolemy removes natural effects from dodecatemoria, novenaries, Parts, and other such non-natural but artificial divisions of the zodiac. For he calls them 'arithmos kai anaitiologeta' (numbers and uncaused). And Alcabitius himself testifies that the signification of the Parts is very weak. Having therefore set forth in order, as he promised, all the necessary precepts by which the natures of the parts of the sky, the temperaments of the stars, and other affections are contained, and all the variety and force of the terms of this divine art are explained: now in the last part of his Isagoge, he enumerates in order and strictly the principles of all the Parts or Lots, from which they originate, and the ends into which they terminate, lest it seem to anyone that he has left anything pertaining to the elements of this part of Mathematics. First, he collects the Parts of nativities, and then those of the Revolutions of the years of the world. But before all else, he delivers a general rule, by the guidance of which one can easily know all the Lots of any celestial chart. Having known, he says, the places, that is, the beginning and end of any Lot, inquire into the arc or intermediate degrees: having found these, cast them from the Ascendant or other places that will be indicated, that is, starting from the Ascendant or the degree of another place, count the subsequent degrees until that sum of intercepted degrees is finished. For in whatever degree it finishes, that degree will show the place of the sought Lot. He explains this rule with the example of the Part of Fortune, which he instructs to be collected by day from the place of the Sun to the Moon, but by night from the Moon to the Sun. For example: in a diurnal nativity, let the ninth degree of Aries ascend, with the Sun in 8 degrees 54 minutes of Leo, and the Moon in 24 degrees of Capricorn. If you proceed by counting through the subsequent signs from the Sun to the Moon, you will find that the Moon is 169 degrees distant from the Sun. Now, proceed from the horoscope, that is, from the 9th degree of Aries, counting through the subsequent signs until the sum of 169 degrees ends: it will end in 28 degrees of Virgo, which will be the place of the Part of Fortune. Therefore, if the luminaries are in the same degree of the sky, the horoscope will share the Part of Fortune. Let there be again a nocturnal nativity, in which we imagine the places of the luminaries and the same 9th degree of Aries to ascend. If you now proceed from the Moon to the Sun through the succeeding signs, you will find the Moon to be 131 degrees distant from the Sun, which if you extend from the Ascendant counting through the subsequent signs, you will find the place of the Part of Fortune to be in the 20th degree of Libra. Digitized by Google. 470. DIFFERENCE. So that this calculation of Parts may be easier and more expeditious, resolve the places from which and to which the Parts extend, such as the places of the seven planets, the Ascendant, the second, eighth, ninth, midheaven, twelfth houses; also the places of the Part of Fortune and the Part of Spirit, and the Sun and Moon, which immediately preceded the figure, into numbers of signs, degrees, and minutes: having done this, subtract the place from which the author instructs the progression to begin from the place to which it is to be continued, accommodating a full revolution or 12 signs, as often as the subtraction cannot be performed, and add what remains to the signs, degrees, and minutes of the place from which it is to be cast, so that the true place of the sought Lot may emerge. In this way, we will subtract the place of the Sun from the place of the Moon in diurnal nativities, and what remains of the signs, degrees, and minutes, we will add to the Ascendant, and we will produce the true place of the Part of Fortune. As in our example of a diurnal nativity, the place of the Ascendant is 6 signs, 26 degrees, 44 minutes. The place of the Sun in 8 degrees, 54 minutes of Leo, resolved, makes 2 signs, 8 degrees, 54 minutes. The place of the Moon in 27 degrees, 52 minutes of Capricorn, resolved, makes 10 signs, 27 degrees, 52 minutes. From these, the motion subtracted leaves 8 signs, 18 degrees, 58 minutes, which joined to the eastern cardinal point, which has 6 signs, 26 degrees, 44 minutes, produce 3 signs, 15 degrees, 42 minutes, and demonstrate the place of the Part of Fortune to fall in 15 degrees, 42 minutes of Cancer. Conversely, if it had been a nocturnal genesis, then the place of the Moon, that is, 10 signs, 27 degrees, 52 minutes, would have to be subtracted from the place of the Sun, that is, 2 signs, 8 degrees, 54 minutes: which done, there would remain 3 signs, 11 degrees, 2 minutes, which joined with the horoscope, that is, 6 signs, 26 degrees, 44 minutes, make 10 signs, 7 degrees, 48 minutes, and project the place of the Part of Fortune into 7 degrees, 48 minutes of Aquarius. Ptolemy instructs that the place of the Part of Fortune is always to be collected by day and night from the Sun to the Moon. His words in Book 3, Chapter 'Of the Times of Life', are these: "The Lot of Fortune, the number collected from the degrees, both by day and by night, from the Sun to the Moon, and subtracting the same from the horoscope according to the succeeding signs of the zodiac, where the number falls, that degree and dodecatemoria, and that place, we say, holds the Lot of Fortune, so that it has the same ratio and configuration to the ascending horizon as the Moon has to the Lot of Fortune, and is as it were a lunar horoscope." From these words, the reason is also clear why the Part of Fortune is deservedly attributed an effect by Ptolemy, because, namely, it is as it were a lunar horoscope, and with the same or a similar ray with which the Sun, which is the vital source, illuminates the horoscope, so also the Moon, the source of natural power, illuminates the Part of Fortune: which signifies external things and external accidents, whence it is also called the Part of Fortune. This is the reason for the Lot, which can have no place in other planets, because they are neither luminaries nor do they reflect the light of the Sun. Therefore, in the examination of Lots, it seemed to me neither necessary nor useful to dwell here any longer. Glory to God. Errors are thus corrected. Fol. 3, line 32, read 'tosanikō'. [Planetary Powers and House Meanings]
177 are worthy, except among the more northerly. Secondly, we consider not the light proper to the houses, but the powers that the stars acquire in their individual stations, about which Ptolemy wrote at the end of the fourth book of his Apotelesmatics. In that place, as we noted above, he wants the power and quality of the stars to be judged from these four things. First, from the star's own inherent nature. Secondly, from the inherent nature of the dodecatemoria in which they are located. Thirdly, from the natural disposition of their configuration, that is, whether they are oriental or occidental, and whether they are swift or slow in their course, which accident we have always referred to their own orbits rather than to the Sun. Fourthly, from the natural disposition of their configurations to the angles. The three former modes of powers will be discussed elsewhere. Only the fourth mode contributes to the explanation of the present topic, and Ptolemy commanded judgment concerning it in these words: "The qualities of each star are to be sought from its position, or rather its relation to the horizon, in this manner. For in the midheaven, or in the place succeeding it, they are most powerful. They have a second degree of power in the eastern horizon and the place succeeding it. But they are more powerful in the horizon, and less so in the imum coeli, or in a place otherwise configured to the eastern horizon. But found outside all these, they are considered weak and utterly infirm." Cardan thus explains these words: "The first place is occupied by the heart of the sky, then the eleventh or first and succeeding or second, then the seventh, then the eighth." He seems, he says, to attribute powers to the imum coeli and the fifth and ninth places. To me, however, this not only seems so, but I also state it for certain, since the words themselves mean the same thing. He understands the places excluded from the eastern horizon to be the twelfth, sixth, and also the third, because it is below the earth and cadent. The twelfth and eighth places are also excluded from the ascendant: but these, as has been said, have power by themselves due to the abundance of light, even if they are not configured to the ascendant. Indeed, the eighth always surpasses the ninth in power, except in the matter of life, in which, according to Ptolemy, as we shall see in its proper place, the ninth precedes the eighth, because it is in a trine radiation of the ascendant and is sublime, and the Sun existing there produces the greatest heat. After these, Cardan orders the sixth place, then the fourth, which, although it has no power by itself, as stated above, yet the planets constituted there, when they illuminate all the cardines with very strong rays, acquire great powers. From this it can be understood that the fourth place, being deprived of all light, is indeed weaker by itself than the twelfth or sixth, but is stronger than them if a comparison is made to the planets, etc. Thirdly, the places of the sky are considered as they are connected by a certain familiarity and sympathy with inferior things. For just as in the essential distribution of the zodiac, or rather of the entire sky, we said that the dodecatemorion of Aries is particularly associated with Germany, Leo with Italy, etc., and Saturn with the spleen, Venus with the genitals, Mercury with the tongue, etc., so in this fortuitous partition of the zodiac, we say that certain places agree with certain inferior things by a peculiar familiarity. For example, only places illuminated by light favor life, as will be seen below, namely the tenth, first, seventh, eleventh, and ninth places. For even if the twelfth and eighth places are in light and signify life, they cannot communicate so much power to a planet constituted there that it can moderate the life of the native, much less, by Hercules, will the second place be able to accomplish this, although it exercises principal power and authority in other matters: as in the matter of riches, the second place is preferred not only to the eighth and twelfth, but also to all others. And the same is more potent in honors than the others, except for the angles and the eleventh and ninth. Similarly, each of the remaining houses respects individual things inherent in the body or mind, or coming from elsewhere, and affects them peculiarly by consent. Moreover, in the accommodation of these things to the domiciles of the sky, the Author and the other Arabs do not agree with Ptolemy in all things, but dissent in many. For example, Ptolemy judges the status of the father principally from the position of the Sun, and that of the mother from the position of Venus. On the contrary, the Arabs principally infer matters concerning the father from the fourth house and its lord. To this they then add, in a diurnal nativity, the consideration of the Sun, and in a nocturnal nativity, the consideration of Saturn, and besides these, the planet or planets placed in the fourth house. Likewise, they command the Part of Fortune of the father and its lord to be contemplated. Similarly, they take their principal conjectures concerning the mother's affairs from the midheaven and its lord. Hence, also taking into account the diurnal or nocturnal condition. Finally, from the planets occupying the tenth house, and from the Part of Fortune of the mother and its rulers: all such places Ptolemy scorned in his treatise on parents, and made no mention of them, and transferred this entire conjecture to the luminaries, Saturn, and Venus. Indeed, Ptolemy wrote in the third book of his Apotelesmatics, chapter on twins, that twins or more are born if the luminaries and the ascendant possess bicorporeal signs, or indeed if, with the luminaries, not the angle of the ascendant, but in its place the culmination of the midheaven falls into bicorporeal signs, such mothers usually give birth to twins or more. From this statement of Ptolemy, it seems to follow that he wanted the tenth house to be that of the mother. Hence, they seem to have judged the imum coeli to belong to the father. But this is not sufficiently clear: for if the tenth belongs to the mother, it does not immediately follow that the fourth, below the earth, belongs to the father. To this, since Ptolemy in the said place conjectures about the number of children, the tenth house will be much more rightly accommodated to children than to the mother for such a reason, especially since he himself in the fourth book of the Apotelesmatics attributed the tenth place to children: but in the treatise on the mother, he made no mention of the tenth place. Similarly, the Arabs ascribed many things to the other houses contrary to Ptolemy's opinion, and many things also pertaining to vain questions, the commemoration of which and comparison to Ptolemaic doctrine we will now omit for the sake of brevity, and will at least explain by example, as in the chapter on children.

[Planetary Dignities and Almuten]
DIFFERENCE 192: Saturn, however, because of the reason of his exaltation, has four powers there, and by reason of his triplicity, three; finally, by reason of his terms, two, collecting in total nine powers, and by this prerogative he surpasses not only Venus but also all other planets. Therefore, in this example, the Lord of the Ascendant is Venus, and the Ruler is Saturn, who will be the principal dominator of the matter for the author, if that degree in which the planet obtains rulership pertains to the house that signifies the matter in question, especially if it also obtains the same rulership in other places signifying the same matter. For whatever planet obtains the greatest number of dignities collected together in the places of all the significators of a certain matter, is called the principal dominator of the matter and the Almuten of the significators by the Arabs. The author makes that planet a participant in the signification which contains the nature of the matter in question. Therefore, if (to use the author's example) the Part of Aries 5 falls on the cusp of the second house of a diurnal nativity, and the Part of Substance falls on Leo 10, and finally the Part of Fortune falls on Sagittarius 6: then the Sun, because it has exaltation and the right of triplicity on the cusp of the same second house, in the place of the Part of Substance, and indeed the house and trigon; and finally obtains the trigon in the place of the Part of Fortune, surpassing all other planets by the multitude and sum of testimonies it collects in the said places, will be the principal significator of substance, and its participant in signification will be Jupiter, who by his nature signifies substance and lawful gains. Thus, the significator of a kingdom is he who has more powers or dignities in the Midheaven and other places signifying the same thing; its co-significator is the Sun, because by its nature it signifies a kingdom. For individual things in nature, as shown above, are signified not only by the signs of the zodiac and celestial domiciles, but are also distributed to individual planets. In this way, Saturn rules fathers, old men, monks, agriculture, hidden treasures, and things acquired through long journeys and with difficulty. Jupiter, however, has religious prelates, kings and leaders, substance and gains: Mars has barbers, surgeons, doctors, lictors, executioners, butchers, bakers, soldiers, who are commonly called martial. Similarly, other stars have their significations in these and many other persons and things, as described by the author in the following difference. But most collect the dominator, or as the Arabs call it, the Almuten, not only from dominion in the places of the zodiac, familiarity connected to the type of question, but also they add to these the dominions of the places in which the co-significators are found. In all these places, they call the planet that surpasses the other planets in the number of votes the Almuten of the significators of a certain matter. For example, if the Almuten of the significators of substance or wealth is to be investigated, these places of the zodiac must be considered: the place of the second house, and the place of its lord; the place of the natural co-significator of wealth, which is always Jupiter; the place of the Part of Fortune, and the place of its lord; the place of the Part of Substance, and the place of its lord; finally, the three places of the triplicities of the second house. In all these places, they call the planet that excels all others in the multitude of testimonies the Almuten of the significators of substance, and they add to it as a participant in signification another planet which, although inferior in strength, approaches the Almuten most closely in the number of testimonies among all the others. But here we approve the method of Ptolemy, of which we will make mention in the second difference at an opportune place, since we have been sufficiently prolix in the exposition of this place. CONCERNING THE ACCIDENTAL POWERS of the planets. Among the accidental powers of the planets is also Alhaiz, that is, similarity. And this is when a diurnal planet is above the earth by day, and below the earth by night; and a nocturnal planet is above the earth by night, and below the earth by day, and with this, if a masculine planet is in a masculine sign, and a feminine planet in a feminine sign, it is said to be in its similarity, that is, in its Alhaiz. And its strength will be like the strength of a man in the place of his progress, that is, of acquisition and fortune. The last chapter of the Author, which is entitled: On the accidental powers of the planets, is not the beginning of the narration concerning the adventitious strengths of the stars, since those things which are said concerning the stars in their various positions in the sky are also rightly referred to the accidental powers of the planets: but it is rather a continuation of such a narration, which the words themselves also indicate. Among the accidental powers of the planets, he says, is Haiz, as if to say, to the other accidental powers of the stars mentioned above, that property of the stars which agrees with the time is also referred. For a star agreeing with the time is called Haiz by the Arabs. For example: Two places, one of which is of the Sun, the other of Saturn, are called family fathers, or significators of the father. If, therefore, it is a diurnal nativity, the place congruent with the time, or Haiz, will be the Sun; but if nocturnal, Saturn. He says that a planet is found in its Haiz, or in its similarity, in three ways. First, by reason of the sex of the signs, namely, when a masculine planet is in a masculine sign, and a feminine planet in a feminine sign. Secondly, by reason of the feminine or masculine quarters. Thirdly, according to the agreement of the temperatures of the time and the stars. But that in the night diurnal planets prefer a position below the earth to a position above the earth; and that in the day nocturnal planets prefer a position below the earth to a position above the earth, lacks all reason, and contradicts the most true opinion of Ptolemy, who universally wished stars, both diurnal and nocturnal, placed above the earth to always be more efficacious, so that even diurnal stars constituted above the earth during nocturnal time receive greater strength than if they are carried through the lower hemisphere at the same time. For every star rejoices in its like, as far as it pertains to its congruent nature, so that nocturnal stars are better tempered by the night, and diurnal stars by the day. Therefore, a diurnal planet will be better by day than by night, and a nocturnal planet obtains a more convenient temperament by night than by day. Such a sympathy or augmentation of temperatures cannot arise from the star's stay above or below the earth. For all rejoice in the place above the earth, and in it they become better and stronger. Indeed, neither is it consonant with truth what he says, that masculine planets assume strength in masculine signs and quarters: and feminine planets in feminine signs and quarters, since all are weakened and made stronger by the Sun and the masculine places of the signs. End of the First Difference.

There are also two stars in the head of Aries of the fifth magnitude, of the nature of Venus and a little Saturn, of which the more northern's longitude is 31 parts with a half, having a northern latitude of 7 parts. The more southern's longitude is 23 parts and a half, with a northern latitude of 6 parts. Those located in the hind foot are: in the tail, Venus. The constellation of Taurus consists of notable stars. And those four stars which are in the section or cutting off of Taurus, are all of the fourth magnitude, and have a similar mixture to Venus and a little Saturn, that is, they are cold and moist. Of these, the most northern today is precisely 47 parts distant from the vernal intersection, with a southern latitude of 6 parts. The next after it has a longitude of 46 parts, 40 minutes, 13 seconds, with a southern latitude of 7 parts, 15 minutes, 1 second. The third's longitude is 45 parts, its southern latitude is 8 parts, 30 minutes, 1 second. The fourth's longitude is 45 parts, 10 minutes, 1 second, with a southern latitude of 9 parts, 15 minutes, 1 second. On the back of Taurus, which are called Atarage by the Babylonians, in Greek Pleiades from 'plein' (to sail), because by their rising they indicate the time for navigation: in Latin they are called Vergiliae, because they rise at the end of spring when summer begins. They are also called Atlantides, because they are the seven daughters of Atlas, among whom Maia is the mother of Mercury, and not rarely is taken for all of them. Virgil in the Georgics, concerning the sowing of wheat: "Many began before the setting of Maia, but the expected harvest deluded them with empty ears." Six stars of this constellation are seen, but the seventh, because one of the daughters of Atlas mingled with a mortal, is scarcely perceived by the senses, as the German verses of Caesar in the interpretation of Aratus warn: "Seven are said to be in number, but one is snatched away, the eye failing to distinguish small bodies." Of these, the preceding has a longitude of 52 parts, 50 minutes, 1 second, with a northern latitude of 4 parts, 30 minutes, 13 seconds. The middle one's longitude is 53 parts, 10 minutes, 12 seconds, northern latitude 4 parts, 40 minutes, 1 second. But the following and last has a longitude of 54 parts, 20 minutes, 1 second, with a northern latitude of 5 parts, 20 minutes, 1 second. All are of the fifth magnitude, and are benefic stars: indeed, as Ptolemy relates, they follow the nature of the Moon and Jupiter, that is, they are very humid and hotter than the Moon, but colder than Jupiter. If therefore a cluster of stars at the hour of birth should appear with the ascendant, it will make the native great and famous, especially if it radiates to them: for then he will be pleasing to princes, and will lead an army, but nevertheless will finally perish by a bad death, having offended a prince. For notable fixed stars have significant indications of fortune in human nativities, if they are found in the angles and especially in the ascendant or Midheaven, with the Sun or Moon, or with the Part of Fortune, they decree the greatest felicity, but often with a calamitous outcome. Therefore, the great gifts of the fixed stars are neither sufficiently stable nor sufficiently reliable, unless they are confirmed by the suffrage of the wandering stars, as Hermes warns in his 29th statement: "The fixed stars bring irrational and admirable felicities, which nevertheless they mostly mark with calamities, unless the planets also agree to the felicity." Furthermore, to relate more about the Pleiades as more notable stars, although they are benefic, nevertheless whoever has them struck by malefics in the western angle at the time of their rising, will die by shipwreck. But if benefic stars look upon them thus constituted, they will nevertheless threaten the native with sudden death from intemperance and an afflux of phlegm. Likewise, if anyone has the Moon void of light in the Pleiades, or hostilely struck by them, they say the native will be deprived of sight. But now let us also examine the other stars of the constellation. The stars around the Pleiades are of the nature of the Moon and a little Jupiter. In the face or head of Taurus is located another cluster of stars, which the ancient Latins called Succulae, for 'sus' (pig) means the same to them. But the Greeks named them Hyades from 'hyein' (to rain), because they do not rise without rain. Of these, one huge and reddish star shining in the very eye of Taurus, called Aldebaran in Chaldean, Lampadias in Greek, and Palilicium in Latin, is of the nature of Mars and of the first magnitude. It belongs to the third part with a third of the dodecatemory of Gemini. Its southern latitude is 5 parts, 10 minutes, 1 second. Finally, in the highest horns are Martian stars. Castor and Pollux are thought to be Gemini. Some wish them to be Apollo and Hercules. Thus, the bright star in the head of the preceding Gemini, called the star of Apollo or Castor, has a longitude of 104 parts, that is, it belongs to the 14th part of the dodecatemory of Cancer. Its latitude is 9 parts with a half, and it is of the second magnitude, of the nature of Mercury. The other star shining in the head of the following Gemini is named Hercules, also Pollux. It has a longitude of 107 parts, 10 minutes, 13 seconds, with a northern latitude of 6 parts, 15 minutes, 1 second, and is of the second magnitude and of the nature of Jupiter. Ptolemy's words about these stars are these: "The two bright stars in the heads, the one in the preceding has a similar appearance to Mercury, and is also called Apollo, and the one in the following is called Hercules. Of the rest, those in the feet have shared a quality similar to Venus and a little Saturn, and the bright ones in the thighs are like Jupiter." In the breast of Cancer is found a nebulous cluster, called Praesepe. This was once in the dodecatemory of Cancer, but today its middle has advanced to the end of the first part of the dodecatemory of Leo. It has a northern latitude of 0 parts, 40 minutes. And it is nebulous, of the nature of Mars and the Moon. Of the two following and placed on either side, which are called Asini, the northern one has a longitude of precisely 121 parts, like Praesepe, with a northern latitude of 2 parts, 40 minutes, 1 second. But the southern Asinus has a longitude of 122 parts, with a southern latitude of only ten minutes. Both are of the fourth magnitude, of the nature of Mars and the Sun. The stars in the eyes of Cancer are like Mars and Venus, and a little Mercury. Those which are in the arms or claws resemble the nature of Saturn and Mercury. In the head of Leo are two stars of the third magnitude, of the nature of Jupiter and a little Mars, but the more northern has a longitude of 135 parts, with a northern latitude of 12 parts. The southern one has a longitude of 134 parts, 50 minutes, 12 seconds, that is, it falls into 14 parts, 50 minutes, 1 second, with a northern latitude of 9 parts, 30 minutes, 12 seconds. Three in the neck are Saturnine and a little Martian. Of these, the first has a longitude of 140 parts, 50 minutes, 1 second, with a northern latitude of 11 parts, of the third magnitude. The middle one is of the second magnitude, and has a longitude of 142 parts, 50 minutes, 1 second, with a northern latitude of 8 parts, 30 minutes, 1 second. The third has a longitude of 141 parts, 20 minutes, 1 second, with a northern latitude of 4 parts, 20 minutes, 1 second, and is of the third magnitude. In the middle of the constellation of Leo shines a bright star of the first magnitude, of the nature of Mars and Jupiter, which is commonly called Cor Leonis and Regulus, the Latins call it Regia, the Greeks Basiliskos, and it is in the 23rd part, 10 minutes, 18 seconds of the dodecatemory of Leo, with a northern latitude of only ten minutes. This is believed to be the most efficacious of all stars in the sky, because existing near the Ecliptic, it is greatly aided by the light of the Sun. Therefore, those who have it in the ascendant or placed in the Midheaven at the time of birth with the waxing Moon, will be promoted to great wealth and great empires. However, whenever the Sun and Moon reach it, it will stir up quarrels and contentions and serious wars for him. Those in the loins, and the bright one in the tail, are like Venus and Saturn. Those in the thighs are Venerean and a little Mercurial. Moreover, the one that shines at the end of the tail is of the first magnitude, and has a longitude of 165 parts, 10 minutes, 1 second, that is, it is in the 15th part, 10 minutes, 1 second of the dodecatemory of Virgo, with a northern latitude of 11 parts, 50 minutes, 19 seconds. Among the unformed stars around Leo, between its extremities and the Great Bear, the nebulous involutions which they call Coma Berenices are to be diligently noted. For the Moon, when it conjoins with these, is accustomed to wonderfully afflict the eyes of natives, as Ptolemy wrote in Book 3 of his Apotelesmata, chapter on injuries and bodily affections. Their position in our age is as follows: The luminous one, which looks most to the north, has a longitude of 165 parts, 30 minutes, 1 second, with a northern latitude of 30 parts. Therefore, in October in the evening, as the Kids descend from the region, they emerge achronically: and therefore the poet calls them asterias, that is, appearing in the evening.
